# 参同契阐幽

**Preface to *The Kinship of the Three Elucidating the Obscure***

*By Zhu Yuanyu of the Qing Dynasty*

The Great Dao is fundamentally beyond words and names, beyond forms and appearances. Vast and undifferentiated, none know its beginning. Yet without borrowing words and names to express it, the Dao would never be revealed. In ancient times, Fuxi’s *Book of Changes* directly pointed to Qian and Kun, while Laozi’s *Classic* fully expounded the Dao and its virtue. Through these two sages, the primordial chaos was pierced, and the root of life and nature within each person found its place. Thus, the Great Dao became luminous!

These two works sprang from the same source, but later, alas, they diverged into the Confucian and Daoist schools. Those who followed the *Changes* became petty scholars of images and numbers, while those who adhered to the Daoist path became longevity-seeking alchemists. The study of returning to the root and restoring life nearly vanished. Who could unite and penetrate both? Only *The Kinship of the Three*!

This text emerged in the Han dynasty, where the patriarch Wei Boyang employed the trigrams and hexagrams to reveal the root of nature and life. Nature is the primordial spirit, indestructible through ten thousand kalpas; life is the primordial qi of the Void, the original essence of utmost purity. Grasping one implies two, lifting two implies three, and uniting three returns to the One. Thus, when speaking of spirit, essence and qi are present. Essence and qi are not coarse; when speaking of essence and qi, spirit is present, yet spirit is not essence. When speaking of nature, life abides; life is not mired in existence. When speaking of life, nature abides; nature is not lost in nothingness. These two words alone contain the true teaching—they may be divided or united, expanded or gathered.

In the *Changes of Fuxi*, Qian and Kun are the parents of all hexagrams; in Laozi’s *Classic*, the Dao and virtue are the master of all phenomena. Later masters and schools, in all their varied discourses, never strayed beyond the scope of these two words. Following them forward is called "Heaven’s mandate is nature"; reversing them is called "Exhausting principle and fulfilling nature to reach life." The grand Dao converges in the three teachings, and the thousand sages share a single destination. All else is but side paths and winding trails.

This method of reversal and return is not inherently complex. The author, out of compassion, would surely wish to point directly to the truth. Yet fearing that those who understand would be few and unable to bear its clarity, he had no choice but to craft words from the wordless and establish names and images from the nameless and imageless. With painstaking effort, he concealed the mother and spoke of the child, weaving the two-word truth into seven scrolls.

Thus, he divided governance, nurturing nature, and dietary refinement into three gates, and further separated medicinal substances, the crucible, and the firing times into three schools. Within each gate lie three gates, and within each school lie three schools. Through threefold interpenetration, the ninefold transformation is complete—this is the meaning of *kinship* in *The Kinship of the Three*.

Only through *participation* do the proper alignments of nature and life become visible; only through *unity* does their non-duality appear; only through *harmony* is the essential return of exhausting principle, fulfilling nature, and reaching life revealed. Within the seven scrolls, there is constant division and union, expansion and gathering—roughly, the earlier sections emphasize division and expansion, while the later ones emphasize union and gathering. Interwoven and ever-changing, the text naturally forms its structure—this is why *The Kinship of the Three* is not easily known or spoken.

Yuanyu, in his youthful years...

In his childhood years, he yearned for the Dao. Initially, he paid homage to Master Zhang Bixu of the Northern School, who pointed out the mysterious pass, allowing him to gain an entry into this book—though he had yet to glimpse its inner sanctum. From then on, he traversed the Five Peaks and visited masters far and wide, yet few could dispel his accumulated doubts. Finally, deep within Zhongnan, he was fortunate to meet the Elder of Lingbao, who unlocked the *Yi* within his heart, revealing all within and without with perfect clarity. Only then did he realize that a single grain could contain the world and a mote of dust could turn the Dharma-wheel—these were no empty words. Yet the subtleties of this higher mechanism cannot be lightly discussed.

He still recalled the parting exhortation of the Elder at their crossroads, urging him to broadly ferry others across so the lineage would not be severed, for the karmic conditions lay mostly south of the great river. Thereafter, he packed his belongings and journeyed south, entering the circle to cultivate the Dao. Relying on the strenuous support of fellow practitioners in Piling, he roughly resolved the great matter. In the *dingyou* year, he brought his disciple Pan Jingguan to Huayang to practice quietude and peruse the Daoist Canon. Casually drawing out a volume of the *Kinship of the Three*, he read it several times with delight, like a pauper discovering a treasure trove, overjoyed beyond measure.

All his life, he had privately reflected that this book’s origins ran deepest, serving as the primal ancestor of alchemical classics and the lifeblood of all perfected ones. When the Patriarch Wei personally transmitted this book to Xu, the Attendant of Qingzhou, Xu concealed his name and wrote a commentary on it—now lost to time. Later commentators proliferated, many misconstruing its meaning. Some even led it astray into the forge-fires of heterodox schools, further obscuring the Patriarch’s intent.

Yu, deeply moved by this, resolved to lift its obscurity. Thus, he secluded himself for winter retreat. Each day, he recited one or two chapters of the original text, exploring its profound meaning with his disciple Pan, who recorded their insights. In the stillness of deep mountains and quiet nights, they sat by lamplight around the hearth, delving into the text’s innermost secrets. When understanding eluded them, even spirits seemed to offer guidance, until at last, clarity dawned in a sudden breakthrough. After eighty dawns and dusks, the draft was complete. He titled it *Unveiling the Obscure*, declaring: "This book, long buried in the ninefold depths of earth, now ascends to the ninefold heavens above; long submerged in the dark valley, it now bathes in the radiance of the Salty Pond." After finishing the draft, he and Pan revised it several times, stripping away all superficial layers to preserve only the truth, like a secret treasure hidden beneath one’s pillow.

In the *dingwei* year, Xu Jingdu petitioned for its circulation, making it available to fellow seekers. Zhang Jingjian assisted in this endeavor. Thus, craftsmen were commissioned, and patrons eagerly responded, bringing the seven fascicles to completion in sequence. They requested a preface from me, so I briefly recount what I received from my teacher, offering it for the scrutiny of those well-versed in the Dao.

I also wish that readers of this book will not cling to mere words or rigid concepts. Seek understanding only from within its teachings: first, to encompass the transformations of creation, and then to shatter the emptiness of the void. What need is there to dissect governance, nurturing life, or the ingestion of elixirs? What need to distinguish between medicinal substances, crucibles, or the timing of the fire? Even the duality of *xing* (nature) and *ming* (destiny) need not be established. If one grasps it thus, it may suffice to repay the kindness of Patriarch Wei, Master Xu, and all the ancestral masters above them; it may suffice to repay the kindness of Fuxi, Laozi, and all the sages above them. And the gratitude for all beings—sentient and insentient, the mountains and rivers, the earth, and all creatures—will likewise be repaid. The Dao is equal in all things, manifesting in every particular. One will see that both the sentient and insentient alike radiate the great treasury of luminosity, dispelling darkness and illuminating the obscure. From this, I too shall remain silent, utterly forgetful of words.

Respectfully composed on the first day of the second lunar month in the *jiyou* year of the Kangxi reign by Zhu Yuanyu, disciple of the Longmen lineage of the Northern School.

---

*The Kinship of the Three* by the Perfected Wei Boyang of the Eastern Han.
*Unveiling the Obscure* by the Cloud Yang Daoist Zhu Yuanyu.

*The Kinship of the Three* was composed by the Perfected Wei Boyang of the Eastern Han, using the principles of the *Yijing* to elucidate the Dao of alchemy. The essence of the *Yijing*’s Dao lies in nothing beyond the interplay of yin and yang, and the application of the alchemical Dao likewise lies in nothing beyond the interplay of yin and yang. When yin and yang unite, they form the *Yijing*, and the Great Dao resides therein.

*Can* (參) refers to the interweaving of the three; *tong* (同) means convergence; *qi* (契) signifies mutual resonance. The text is divided into three chapters—upper, middle, and lower—each encompassing the three schools of governance, nurturing life, and ingesting elixirs. Only by interweaving these three schools, merging the *Yijing*’s nature and emotions, the Yellow Emperor and Laozi’s nurturing of life, and the crucible’s workings into one, does one resonate with the Great Dao of fulfilling nature and destiny. To grasp one aspect is to possess all three. The final fascicle’s "Three Resemblances" clarifies this principle.

**Upper Chapter (First Fascicle: On Governance, comprising five sections—this is the upper portion of the upper chapter)**
This fascicle focuses on governance, with the nurturing of life and ingestion of elixirs already implicit within it. The so-called "governance" presents the symbolic forms of *qian*, *kun*, *kan*, and *li*, subtly embodying the hierarchical structure of ruler and minister. The ruler governs through non-action, while the minister acts—this is the principle underlying both the nurturing of life and the ingestion of elixirs. Thus, the final fascicle also refers to the *Yijing*’s emotions and nature.

**Chapter One: The Gate of Qian and Kun**
*Qian* and *kun* are the gates of the *Yijing*, the parents of all hexagrams. *Kan* and *li* form their framework, turning the hub and aligning the axle. The four trigrams of the *pin* and *mu* serve as the bellows. Enveloping the Dao of yin and yang, they resemble a craftsman wielding the inked cord, holding the reins, aligning the compass and square, and following the tracks. Ruling from the center to govern the exterior, their numbers are recorded in the pitch pipes and calendars. The lunar phases consist of five and six, weaving the warp and weft at the sun’s command. Combined, they form sixty, with hardness and softness manifesting both internally and externally. At dawn, *zhun* presides over affairs; by dusk, *meng* assumes its duty. Day and night each correspond to one hexagram, applied in sequence. Before reaching the end of the month, the cycle concludes and begins anew. The sun and moon set the measure, their movements and stillness varying in timing. Spring and summer rely on the inner body, from *zi* to *chen* and *si*. Autumn and winter engage the outer function, from *wu* to *xu* and *hai*. Rewards and punishments align with spring and autumn; dawn and dusk follow cold and heat. The line statements embody benevolence and righteousness, expressing joy and anger in accordance with the seasons. Thus, they harmonize with the four seasons, and the five phases attain their principle.

This chapter first presents *qian* and *kun* as the gates encompassing all transformations, serving as the guiding principle of the entire text.

*Qian* and *kun* are the gates of the *Yijing*, the parents of all hexagrams. *Kan* and *li* form their framework, turning the hub and aligning the axle.
This passage speaks of the Dao of yin and yang as nothing beyond the scope of *qian* and *kun*. In the realm of heaven and earth, this single body of yin and yang is called the Dao; its transformative mechanism is called the *Yijing*; its numinous application is called the elixir. The *Yijing*’s yin and yang do not extend beyond *qian* and *kun*; the alchemical Dao’s yin and yang do not extend beyond *xing* (nature) and *ming* (destiny). *Qian* and *kun* are *xing* and *ming*. Yet one must thoroughly investigate the workings prior to their emergence to understand that heaven and earth originate from this, and human life begins from this. The alchemical Dao is founded upon this.

Originally, before the primal chaos, when the single *qi* had yet to manifest, it could neither be spoken of nor named. Vast and empty, without form or boundary, this is called true emptiness. Yet within emptiness, there is non-emptiness—this is called wondrous existence. Only by recognizing existence within emptiness can the beginningless beginning be forcibly named the origin of heaven and earth. Only by recognizing emptiness within existence can the beginning of existence be forcibly named the mother of the myriad things. Existence within emptiness is the Supreme Ultimate without the Ultimateless; emptiness within existence is the Ultimateless within the Supreme Ultimate. The essence of the Supreme Ultimate is originally neither movement nor stillness. Movement without movement is why *qian* constitutes heaven, and the light and pure have their root.

Stillness without stillness—this is why *kun* constitutes earth, and the heavy and turbid have their foundation.

Between one movement and one stillness lies humanity as the heart of heaven and earth, wherein the ceaseless generation of the *Yi* resides. Though the embryo is formed, primal chaos remains undivided; thus it is said the Supreme Ultimate contains the Three.

When stillness reaches its extreme and movement arises, *qian*’s solitary yang pierces straight through the nine depths of earth, and *kun* receives it. Within yin, yang is enfolded—solidified, it becomes *kan*, and this is called “Heaven One generates Water.” In the earth it is water; in the heavens it is the moon.

When movement reaches its extreme and stillness returns, *kun*’s solitary yin ascends straight to the nine heavens above, and *qian* governs it. Within yang, yin is contained—ruptured, it becomes *li*, and this is called “Earth Two generates Fire.” In the world it is fire; in the heavens it is the sun.

Thus from the Supreme Ultimate arise the Two Modes, and from the Two Modes arise the Four Images. Heaven and earth are not revealed without sun and moon; *qian* and *kun* do not circulate without *kan* and *li*. Therefore, in the Dao of the *Yi*, *qian* and *kun* serve as the body, while *kan* and *li* serve as the function.

Why is this so? As a thing, *qian* is concentrated in stillness and direct in movement. All sixty-four hexagrams’ yang lines enter and exit through *qian*’s gate, yet ultimately they are but the original solitary yang. As a thing, *kun* is contracted in stillness and expanded in movement; all sixty-four hexagrams’ yin lines open and close through *kun*’s portal, yet ultimately they are but the original solitary yin. One yin and one yang—this is called the true *Yi*.

*Qian*, knowing the great beginning, is truly the father of all yang, thus the Dao of *qian* completes the male, manifesting as *zhen*, *kan*, and *gen*. *Kun*, forming things, is truly the mother of all yin, thus the Dao of *kun* completes the female, manifesting as *xun*, *li*, and *dui*. From this interchange and transformation, generation ceaselessly arises, layering into sixty-four hexagrams and branching into four thousand ninety-six. Is this not what is meant by “*Qian* and *kun* are the gates of the *Yi*, the parents of all hexagrams”?

Why, then, among the Six Children, are only *kan* and *li* employed? Because *zhen*, *xun*, *gen*, and *dui* each receive a partial aspect of *qian* and *kun*, while *kan* and *li* alone receive their true essence. In the primordial arrangement, *qian* is positioned in the south and *kun* in the north; only by exchanging their central lines do *kan* and *li* emerge. In the later arrangement, the hexagrams reverse, placing *li* in the south and *kan* in the north, yet *qian* and *kun* still envelop them externally, the framework of heaven and earth remaining unmoved, while *kan* and *li*—the sun and moon—naturally revolve within.

In the small, this is day and night, waxing and waning; in the greater, it is the seasons of spring and autumn, cold and heat; in the greatest, it is the epochs of cosmic cycles. It is like driving a chariot: the hollow center is the hub, and the two turning ends are the axle. The chariot cannot move itself; it relies on the axle at both ends, and the axle, in turn, relies on the hub at the center to rotate. The chariot turns by means of the axle, and the axle revolves by means of the hub—only then is its function complete. Such is the relationship of *kan* and *li* to *qian* and *kun*. Is this not what is meant by “*Kan* and *li* frame the structure, turning the hub and aligning the axle”?

Laozi said: “Thirty spokes share one hub. It is where there is nothing that the usefulness of the chariot lies.” This is what is meant.

This chapter serves as the guiding principle of the entire work, and this passage, in turn, is the guiding principle of the whole chapter. In the alchemical practice of the Dao, the gates of *qian* and *kun* become the furnace and crucible, while the framing of *kan* and *li* becomes the medicinal ingredients; the fire-times emerge from within. The four trigrams of the female and male serve as the bellows and flute. They enwrap the Dao of yin and yang, just as a skilled charioteer follows the marking line, holds the reins and bit, aligns the compass and square, and follows the wheel tracks. By abiding in the center to govern the external, the calculations are recorded in the pitch-pipes and calendars.

This passage speaks of *qian* and *kun* transforming into *kan* and *li*, thereby enveloping the Dao of yin and yang. *Qian* is originally pure yang, the male. Yet when its middle line transforms to produce *li*’s lesser yin, the male turns into the female. *Kun* is originally pure yin, the female. Yet when its middle line transforms to produce *kan*’s lesser yang, the female turns into the male. When *kun* shifts into *kan*, beneath the ninefold earth, its depths are unfathomable; the qi mechanism moves and ever arises—this is the bottomless bellows. When *qian* shifts into *li*, above the ninefold heavens, a single thread subtly connects; its fundamental nature is empty yet inexhaustible—this is the perforated flute. Laozi said: “Is not the space between heaven and earth like a bellows and flute?” This is what he meant.

Thus it is said: “The four trigrams of the female and male serve as bellows and flute.” The two *qi* of *kan* and *li* move back and forth, entering and exiting between heaven and earth, so that day and night, darkness and dawn, spring and autumn, cold and heat never deviate by even a hair’s breadth. Though called four trigrams, in truth they are but one *kan* and one *li*. Though called two trigrams, in truth they are *kan* and *li*, with one yin and one yang between them. They encompass the entire body of the sixty-four hexagrams and the full function of their three hundred sixty lines, all enveloped within. Is this not like a skilled charioteer who holds the reins according to the marking cord, aligns the wheels with the compass and square, and follows the tracks? The horse has its bit and bridle; the chariot has its ruts and tracks. The standards are manifest before one, each to be observed and followed. Externally, one holds fast to the standard and aligns with it, yet within there must be the charioteer who governs from the center—precisely the one mentioned above who turns the hub and aligns the axle. The method of governance is neither too swift nor too slow, adapting to every direction and matching every rhythm. A numerical order exists within, which is the measure of the fire’s timing discussed below. The twelve pitch-pipes of the *lü* and the twelve celestial stems of the calendar are nothing but six yin and six yang, circulating and revolving without the slightest deviation. When the fire’s timing and regulation attain this standard, this becomes the guiding principle of the celestial revolution. Thus it is said: “Governing the external from the center, the numbers are recorded in the *lü* and calendar.”

The lunar phases have five and six; warp and weft serve the sun’s command.
Combined they make sixty, with firm and yielding in outer and inner.
At dawn of the new moon, *Zhun* takes up its duty; by dusk, *Meng* receives it.
Day and night each govern one hexagram, used in proper sequence.
Before reaching the last day’s darkness and dawn, the cycle ends and begins anew.

This passage speaks of the waxing and waning, darkness and dawn, measured by the lunar month—the fire’s timing of the lesser celestial revolution. The *Book of Changes* has sixty-four hexagrams; excluding *Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, and *Li*, which correspond to the furnace, crucible, and elixir, the remaining sixty hexagrams and their three hundred sixty lines precisely match the degrees of the celestial revolution. The middle lines of *Kan* and *Li* govern one day and one month, grasping *Qian* and *Kun* as they move within the three hundred sixty-five and a quarter degrees. The guiding principle of the celestial revolution never exceeds this scope. The sun is the great yang; the moon is the great yin. The yang number takes five as its center; the yin number takes six as its center. Doubling six yields the twelve *lü* and celestial stems as their standard. Multiplying five by six gives thirty, the number of a lunar month. As the sun and moon weave their warp and weft, they form the waxing and waning, darkness and dawn. The moon’s waxing and waning, its fullness and emptiness, follow the sun’s revolution, as if receiving its mandate. Thus it is said: “The lunar phases have five and six; warp and weft serve the sun’s command.”

Day and night divide the warp and weft of the sun and moon. Within these thirty days, combined they form sixty hexagrams, from *Zhun* and *Meng* to *Jiji* and *Weiji*, with all hexagram images complete. Within each hexagram, the inner and outer bodies are invariably opposed. Opposed forms include *Zhun* and *Meng*, *Xu* and *Song*, and the like; paired forms include *Zhongfu* and *Xiaoguo*, and the like. Some have firmness on the outside and yielding within, others yielding on the outside and firmness within—*Zhun* and *Meng* serve as examples. In *Zhun*, one yang moves below, resembling dawn; in *Meng*, one yang rests above, resembling dusk. Day and night alternate, yet the two hexagrams are but one. At dawn of the new moon, the cycle begins with *Zhun* and *Meng*, and by the last day of the month, it concludes precisely with *Jiji* and *Weiji*. The sixty hexagrams complete one lunar cycle, then end and begin anew, circulating without end.

The sun and moon set the measure; movement and stillness have their times.
Spring and summer rely on the inner body, from *zi* to *chen* and *si*.
Autumn and winter engage the outer function, from *wu* to *xu* and *hai*.
Rewards and punishments correspond to spring and autumn; darkness and light follow cold and heat.
The line statements embody benevolence and righteousness, expressing joy and anger in due season.
Thus responding to the four seasons, the five phases attain their principle.

This passage speaks of the two solstices and two equinoxes, measured by the solar year—the fire’s timing of the greater celestial revolution. “The sun and moon set the measure” means the sun governs the day, positioned at high noon. From the first stirring of one yang to the fullness of six yang belongs to the sun’s seasonal *qi*. The moon governs the night, positioned at midnight. From the first stillness of one yin to the fullness of six yin belongs to the moon’s fire timing. “Movement and stillness have their times” means the stirring of one yang and the advance of fire correspond to *Zhun* and occur early; the stillness of one yin and the retreat of fire correspond to *Meng* and occur late. Know that the measure of one day is the measure of one month, and the measure of one month is the measure of one year. Also know that the movement and stillness of one year do not exceed those of one month, and the movement and stillness of one month do not exceed those of one day. These two sentences connect the preceding and following, serving as the guiding principle of the entire passage, which then elaborates on the year’s seasonal timing.

The inner and outer bodies of the hexagrams encompass the four seasons. Take *Zhun*, for example: beginning with the first line, the advance of fire marks the *zi* hour when one yang first stirs. Progressing to the top line, it becomes pure yang at *si*. Moving from the inner body to the outer function, it corresponds to spring and summer. In *Meng*, beginning with the top line, the retreat of fire marks the *wu* hour when one yin first stills. Progressing to the first line, it becomes pure yin at *hai*. Returning from the outer function to the inner body, it corresponds to autumn and winter. This describes the seasonal timing of the winter and summer solstices and their union.

The great yang at *mao* corresponds to the spring equinox, embodying virtue with the image of punishment. The great yin at *you* corresponds to the autumn equinox, embodying punishment with the image of virtue and reward. Thus it is said: “Rewards and punishments correspond to spring and autumn.” The sun rises in *yin* and sets in *shen*; fire is born in *yin*, resembling heat. The moon rises in *shen* and sets in *yin*; water is born in *shen*, resembling cold.

Thus it is said: “Dusk and dawn follow cold and heat.” Benevolence governs expansion, righteousness governs contraction; the lines’ explanations each have their governing principle. This still reflects the image of following cold and heat. Joy inclines toward reward, anger inclines toward punishment. They arise in accordance with the season, never exceeding their proper measure. This still reflects the image of corresponding to spring and autumn. This speaks of the bathing phase of the spring and autumn equinoxes.

In this way, water, fire, wood, and metal each hold to one seasonal qi, and within them the true earth harmonizes and blends. The fully complete qi of creation’s harmony coalesces to form the elixir. Thus it is said: “In this way, they correspond to the four seasons, and the five phases attain their principle.”

The preceding section spoke of the lesser celestial cycle’s firing times corresponding to a month; this section speaks of the greater celestial cycle’s firing times corresponding to a year. One must understand that the operations within are all methods of gathering and concentrating: gathering the year into months, months into days, days into hours. It all comes to rest in a single moment, distinguishing movement and stillness. Within this, the ebb and flow depend entirely on the bellows and key of Kan and Li—what is called “the Dao of covering and veiling yin and yang.”

This chapter uses the images of creation to clarify the functions of Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li. The human body contains a small heaven and earth, and its images are likewise. Qian is the head, the image of father and heaven; Kun is the abdomen, the image of mother and earth. Zhen is the feet, Xun the thighs—near the earthly realm, the images of eldest son and eldest daughter. Gen is the hands, Dui the mouth—near the heavenly realm, the images of youngest son and youngest daughter. Kan is the ears, Li the eyes, moving at the center of heaven and earth, uniquely occupying the human position, the images of middle son and middle daughter. The remaining four limbs and hundred bones, the three hundred sixty joints, the eighty-four thousand pores—these are the scattered distribution of the many hexagrams and lines.

Yet all this is what has form and name, known to all. Who knows that which is formless and nameless? Before father and mother were born, it was perfectly complete, all-pervading, utterly empty, spiritually penetrating, and fundamentally unstained. No cultivation or verification was needed; in emptiness there was non-emptiness, the true master of the void. This is what is called the single Supreme Ultimate in its entirety.

Then a single spark of numinous light came from the Great Void, suddenly sensing and attaching, entering straight into the central palace of the spirit chamber to become the master of the body. This is what is called each possessing a Supreme Ultimate. Once the master resided in the spirit chamber, it connected above to the heavenly hub and below to the sea of qi. Before essence and life were divided, it was still the original, undivided face.

When the central palace’s ebb and flow began to stir, it drew in the qi of the Great Void through the two apertures, passing straight through the heavenly hub and descending to the sea of qi. Qian below joined Kun, and one line within Kun became solid, forming Kan—this was the root of life. Once Kun became Kan, its single yin line followed the heavenly qi upward to the heavenly hub. Kun above joined Qian, and one line within Qian broke, forming Li—this was the root of nature. Then, with a *he* sound, the umbilical cord was cut, and nature and life were divided into the upper and lower arcs.

This is what Lü Zu meant when he said: “Exhaustively seek the moment of receiving qi at birth; marvel not that the celestial mechanism is entirely revealed.” From this point onward, the postnatal takes charge. There are gates and doors, never departing from Qian and Kun, and the bellows’ operation depends entirely on Kan and Li. Kan sinks into the sea of qi, primordial essence deeply hidden in the great abyss, its depths unfathomable beneath the ninefold earth—this is the bellows’ function. Li ascends to the heavenly hub, numinous light penetrating the Great Void, piercing straight to the summit of the nine heavens—this is the key’s function.

The sun emerges, the moon enters; breath comes and goes, precisely at the center of heaven and earth’s eighty-four thousand *li*. One opening and closing divides day and night; one ebb and one rest determines the dark and new moons; one gloom and one ease distinguishes cold and heat; one joy and one anger corresponds to spring and autumn. The four seasons and five phases are all fully present, and creation lies within one’s own body.

Thus, those who study the Dao, if they can open their own gates and doors, may recognize the Qian-Kun furnace and cauldron. If they can operate their own hub and axle, they may gather the Kan-Li medicinal substances. If they can work their own bellows and key, they may practice the sixty hexagrams’ yang fire and yin tally. This is what is meant by: “What follows to give life to humans, when reversed, forms the elixir.”

Chen Xiyi said: The sun is the heavenly qi descending from the west to join the earth; the moon is the earthly qi ascending from the east to join heaven—the image of male and female uniting in essence. Heaven and earth cannot produce cold and heat; cold and heat arise from the sun and moon’s distance. Heaven and earth cannot produce the four seasons; the four seasons arise from the sun and moon’s north and south. Heaven and earth cannot produce day and night; day and night arise from the sun and moon’s rising and setting. Heaven and earth cannot produce the dark and new moons; the dark and new moons arise from the sun and moon’s conjunction. Though yin and yang are subtle, they are not beyond the sun and moon. Though creation is vast, it is not beyond Kan and Li.

Thus, though the changes of the many hexagrams are not uniform, they do not depart from the middle lines of Kan and Li—like a wheel’s thirty spokes sharing a single hub.

**Chapter Two: The Two Uses of Kan and Li**

Heaven and earth establish their positions, and the *Yi* moves within them. Heaven and earth are the images of Qian and Kun. “Establish their positions” means arranging the positions of yin and yang in pairing. The *Yi* refers to Kan and Li. Kan and Li are the two uses of Qian and Kun. These two uses have no fixed line positions; they circulate through the six voids. Their comings and goings are not fixed, nor are their positions above or below constant. Hidden and submerged, they transform within. They enfold the ten thousand things, serving as the guiding principles of the Dao. Using the formless to govern the formed, the vessel’s use is emptiness.

Thus, in tracing their ebb and flow, Kan and Li vanish. Words are not idly made; theories do not arise in vain. They are drawn from verification and effect, measured against the numinous and bright. By analogy and structural composition, the original principles are confirmed.

Kan is the lunar essence of Wu, Li the solar radiance of Ji.
Sun and moon form the *Yi*, hard and soft in mutual balance.
Earth reigns through the four seasons, weaving the beginning and end.
Blue, red, white, and black each dwell in their quadrant,
yet all draw from the Central Palace, the work of Wu and Ji.
This chapter reveals that the two uses of Kan and Li do not depart from the One Center—
it fulfills the opening chapter’s theme of the hub turning the axle.

Heaven and Earth establish their positions, and the *Yi* moves within them.
Heaven and Earth are the images of Qian and Kun.
“Establishing positions” means arranging the positions of yin and yang in pairing.
The *Yi* refers to Kan and Li.
Kan and Li are the two uses of Qian and Kun.
These two uses have no fixed line positions; they circulate through the six voids.
Their comings and goings are not fixed, nor are their positions above or below constant.
Hidden and submerged, they transform within.
They enfold the ten thousand things, serving as the guiding principles of the Dao.
This passage speaks of the wondrous workings of Kan and Li as residing within the fixed positions of Qian and Kun.

In the *Yi*, they are Qian and Kun; their modeled images are Heaven and Earth.
In the *Yi*, they are Kan and Li; their modeled images are sun and moon.
These are the postcelestial Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li—formed and named.
Before Heaven and Earth, sun and moon existed, there was only the undifferentiated Great Void.
Within this Great Void, originally nothing existed—round, luminous, and vast—this was the precosmic Qian.
Within it, a thing formed in undifferentiated wholeness, pervading and filling all—this was the precosmic Kun.
From the Void, qi arose as the utmost yang qi, and within this utmost yang lay the reverent, utmost yin.
This ascends from Kun.
The Void contains Being—this is Li within Qian.
Qi congeals into essence as the utmost yin essence, and within this utmost yin lies the blazing, utmost yang.
This descends from Qian.
Being contains the Void—this is Kan within Kun.
One ascends, one descends; the pivot lies entirely in the center.
When the pivot moves, Heaven and Earth divide.
Once Heaven and Earth divide, their positions are fixed.
Naturally, Heaven takes its place above, Earth below;
the sun emerges in the east, the moon arises in the west.
Thus, in Fu Xi’s precosmic round diagram, the Qian trigram resides in the south, the Kun trigram in the north—
Heaven above, Earth below, encompassing all images.
This is the fixing of Heaven and Earth’s positions.
The Li trigram resides in the east, the Kan trigram in the west—
sun and moon face each other, traversing the center of Heaven and Earth.
Water and fire do not oppose each other.
Yet only after the bodies of Heaven and Earth are established do the functions of sun and moon operate.
Thus, the *Xici Zhuan* states: “Heaven and Earth establish their positions, and the *Yi* moves within them.”
This is a direct pointer.
Master Wei feared that later generations would not know what Heaven and Earth are, nor what the *Yi* is,
so he added this annotation:
Heaven and Earth are not the external images of Heaven and Earth, but rather the natural, spiritually empty images of the one Qian and one Kun—
the “gate and door” referred to in the previous chapter.
“Establishing positions” does not mean physical positions, but rather the natural pairing of one yin and one yang—
the “vast expanse” mentioned in the previous chapter.
The *Yi* is not the *Yi* of trigrams and lines, but rather the natural movement of the one Kan and one Li, the true breath coming and going—
the “bellows” referred to in the previous chapter.
Are not the transformations of Heaven and Earth the very transformations of our own bodies?

What is meant by “Kan and Li are the two uses of Qian and Kun”?
Qian is originally old yang, but its center transforms into young yin.
The one yin within Li is the true essence of Kun’s origin.
Thus, Li turns from east to south.
The precosmic Qian position becomes the postcosmic Li, turning one into nine, making the head the tail.
Thus, the line statement bears the image of “having no head.”
Qian’s “use of nine” is the use of Li.
Kun is originally old yin, but its center transforms into young yang.
The one yang within Kan is the ancestral qi of Qian’s origin.
Thus, Kan turns from west to north.
The precosmic Kun position becomes the postcosmic Kan, turning six into one, making the end the beginning.
Thus, the line statement bears the auspiciousness of “eternal steadfastness.”
Kun’s “use of six” is the use of Kan.
This mutual concealment of sun and moon is why they are the ancestral origin of the *Yi*—
the wondrous workings of true water and true fire interacting.
Sun and moon, from ancient times to eternity, rise and set within the Great Void,
moving above, below, and all around—nowhere they do not operate.
Just as in one hexagram, the six lines each have their fixed positions,
yet the two uses of Kan and Li circulate through the six positions, present everywhere.
Their workings are numinous.
Thus it is said: “These two uses have no fixed line positions; they circulate through the six voids.”

The sun departs, the moon arrives; the moon departs, the sun arrives.
How can their comings and goings be fixed?
Li is the yin within Heaven, ever desiring to draw near below.
Thus, the sun travels from east to west, descending to meet Earth.
Kan is the yang within Earth, ever desiring to draw near above.
Thus, the moon travels from west to east, ascending to meet Heaven.
How can their positions above and below be constant?
Within Li lies true water, enveloped by double yang.
Water concealed within fire—dark within, bright without—bears the image of hidden submergence.
Within Kan lies true fire, encased by double yin.
Fire concealed within water—bright within, dark without—bears the image of concealed submersion.
Water and fire mutually conceal each other, transforming endlessly.
All occurs within that single hollow point at the center, bearing the image of “transforming within.”
From here, one lifts Heaven and Earth, grasps Qian and Kun—
the great Dao does not exceed this scope.
Thus it is said: “They enfold the ten thousand things, serving as the guiding principles of the Dao.”

The above all elucidates the two uses of Kan and Li,
rightly revealing the meaning of “the *Yi* moves within them.”
“Using the formless to govern the formed, the vessel’s use is emptiness.”
Thus, in tracing their ebb and flow, Kan and Li vanish.
This passage focuses entirely on the wondrous workings of Kan and Li.

The two uses of Kan and Li originally have no line positions.
They circulate through the six voids—this is the Void.
Yet they enfold the ten thousand things, serving as the guiding principles of the Dao,
showing that the Void suffices to govern Being.
All tangible vessels in the world have solid forms.

Ultimately, what is solid and existent cannot function on its own.
Only by relying on what is empty and nonexistent can it be governed.
As Laozi said: “Clay is shaped into vessels, yet it is their emptiness that gives them their use.”
So it is with Kan and Li—using the nonexistent to govern the existent, their wondrous function lies entirely in the central void.
Thus it is said: “Using the nonexistent to govern the existent, the vessel’s use is emptiness.”
From nonexistence entering existence is called *xixi*—the moment for advancing the fire, when Kun’s three lines transform into Qian.
From existence entering nonexistence is called *xiaoxiao*—the moment for withdrawing the tally, when Qian’s three lines transform into Kun.
From the new moon, when the Zhen trigram takes effect, passing through Dui to Qian;
from the full moon, when the Xun trigram takes effect, passing through Gen to Kun—
in this process, the line positions of Kan and Li are nowhere to be seen, which is called the vanishing of Kan and Li.
Yet they do not truly vanish; they circulate through the six voids, flowing without cease.

Words are not idly fabricated, nor are theories baselessly conceived.
They are drawn from verification and effect, calibrated to the workings of the numinous.
By analogy and structural alignment, principles are established as evidence.
To know the *Yi* in the sun and moon is precisely such analogy and structural alignment—
this is the image of calibrating to the numinous, with clear verification.
Thus it can be taken as evidence of principle, not mere idle discourse.
This passage merely introduces what follows.

Kan is the essence of the moon, Li the radiance of the sun.
Sun and moon embody the *Yi*, with firmness and softness in perfect balance.
Earth reigns over the four seasons, weaving through beginning and end.
Green, red, white, and black each occupy their respective directions,
yet all are governed by the central palace, the work of Wu and Ji.
This passage speaks of the union of these two entities, never departing from the true earth of the central palace.

Kan is the moon, containing within it the Wu earth.
Wu earth originally derives from Qian, yang submerged in yin, its essence concealed within—
what is called “dim and obscure, yet within it lies the essence.”
Li is the sun, containing within it the Ji earth.
Ji earth originally emerges from Kun, yin attached to yang, its radiance outwardly expressed—
what is called “elusive and indistinct, yet within it lies the substance.”
When the radiance of the sun and the essence of the moon converge in the central yellow path,
they unite to form the primordial Great Simplicity.
This is precisely because within them, one Wu and one Ji—firmness and softness—are inherently paired and perfectly matched.
Thus it is said: “Sun and moon embody the *Yi*, with firmness and softness in perfect balance.”

Wu and Ji earths can be divided or united.
In terms of the four seasons: wood flourishes in spring, with Chen earth concealed within;
fire flourishes in summer, with Wei earth concealed within;
metal flourishes in autumn, with Xu earth concealed within;
water flourishes in winter, with Chou earth concealed within.
Wood, fire, metal, and water, from beginning to end, are all enveloped within the true earth of the center.
Thus it is said: “Earth reigns over the four seasons, weaving through beginning and end.”
In terms of the four directions: the Azure Dragon, bearing the virtue of wood, resides in the east;
the Vermilion Bird, bearing the virtue of fire, resides in the south;
the White Tiger, bearing the essence of metal, resides in the west;
the Dark Warrior, bearing the essence of water, resides in the north.
Thus it is said: “Green, red, white, and black each occupy their respective directions.”
North (1) and west (4) combine to form five—this is Wu earth, within which lies the obscure essence.
East (3) and south (2) combine to form five—this is Ji earth, within which lies the elusive substance.
Relying on this true earth of Wu and Ji to harmonize water and fire, to blend metal and wood,
the five phases and four images converge within the central yellow, and the Great Elixir is formed.
Thus it is said: “All are governed by the central palace, the work of Wu and Ji.”

The sun is firm, the moon is soft—they balance each other to become the Great Simplicity.
Thus the *Yi* is called Kan and Li—how could this be idly fabricated?
It is analogy and structural alignment.
The five phases and four seasons all derive from the earth of the central palace.
Thus the *Yi* moves within them—how could this theory be baseless?
It is established by tracing principles as evidence.
This passage summarizes the overarching meaning of the entire chapter.
The opening states, “The *Yi* moves within them,” and further, “transformation occurs within”—
this precisely refers to the true earth of the central palace.
The discussion of the two entities, Kan and Li, never departs from the true earth, thereby forming the Three Families.
To speak of the two entities is to include the four images within them; to speak of the Three Families is to include the five phases within them.
All medicinal substances and fire phases are contained therein.
The great function of Qian and Kun culminates in Kan and Li;
the wondrous function of Kan and Li returns to Wu and Ji.
The key to the entire *Cantong Qi* lies here.

---

Appendix:
Master Tan said: “Kneading emptiness into clods, one sees the clods but not the emptiness—this is earth after the opening of Heaven and Earth.
Grinding clods into emptiness, one sees the emptiness but not the clods—this is earth in the primordial chaos.
How wondrous!”

Chapter Three: The Sun and Moon Contain the Tally
*(The four lines beginning “The sun contains the essence of the five phases” were mistakenly included in the “Ruler and Minister Govern the Polity” chapter in common editions; this has now been corrected according to the collated藏本.)*

The *Yi* is imagery.
No imagery is more luminous than the suspended images of the sun and moon.
The sun contains the essence of the five phases; the moon receives the regulation of the six pitch-pipes.
Five and six make thirty degrees; when the degrees are complete, they begin anew.
Exhausting the numinous to know transformation: when yang departs, yin arrives.
Like spokes converging at the hub, the wheel turns—entering and exiting, coiling and uncoiling.
The *Yi* has three hundred eighty-four lines; drawing from the lines, one extracts the tally—
the tally refers to the sixty-four hexagrams.
From the dark moon to the dawn of the new moon, Zhen comes to receive the tally.
At this moment, Heaven and Earth unite their essences, sun and moon mutually sustain each other.
The male yang broadcasts the primordial bestowal; the female yin transforms the yellow embryo.
Chaos intermingles and exchanges, establishing the root and foundation.
Cultivating and nourishing the bud, concentrating the spirit to form the body—
all creatures emerge by treading this path, and none that wriggle and move do not follow it.
This chapter particularly illuminates the function of the sun and moon,
investigating the origin of the medicinal substances.

The *Yi* is imagery.
No imagery is more luminous than the suspended images of the sun and moon.
The sun contains the essence of the five phases; the moon receives the regulation of the six pitch-pipes.
Five and six make thirty degrees; when the degrees are complete, they begin anew.

To fathom the numinous and comprehend transformation: when yang departs, yin arrives.
Like spokes converging at the hub or a wheel in ceaseless turn, they emerge and enter, coil and unfurl in alternation.
This passage speaks of the sun and moon’s conjunction, whose numinous transformations arise from spontaneity.

The preceding chapter having clarified the dual functions of *kan* and *li*,
it revealed the two entities of sunlight and lunar essence,
yet did not exhaust the principle of their conjunction.
Master Wei thus reiterates the words of the *Yi* to expound it further.
For the sun and moon as *Yi* constitute the pivotal key of the entire *Cantong qi*.
This *Yi* is the Great *Yi*; this image is the imageless image—
what the world can neither see nor know.
To know the imageless *Yi*, one need only look within;
to know the imaged *Yi*, one must gaze upward and downward to attain it.

Among the images formed in heaven, none are more manifest than the sun and moon.
Thus the *Xici zhuan* states: “*Yi* is imagery,”
and further: “No suspended image is more luminous than the sun and moon.”
Why are the sun and moon uniquely called great?
The sun bears the essence of the great yang fire, its fundamental body luminous and all-pervading;
the single dark speck within is the true water of great yin.
Yang conceals yin within: outwardly white, inwardly black—hence it assumes the *li* image.
The moon embodies the essence of great yin water, its fundamental body purely dark and lightless;
the single bright speck within is the true fire of great yang.
Yin conceals yang within: outwardly black, inwardly white—hence it assumes the *kan* image.

The yang essence is fire, and fire possesses light.
The yin essence is water, and water only reflects images.
Thus the moon itself has no light; it shines only where the sun’s rays strike.
Light arises where the sun illuminates; the lunar *po* arises where it does not.
At the conjunction of *hui* and *shuo*, sun and moon share the same palace.
The moon lies beneath the sun, the sun above the moon;
the moon’s body is enveloped by the sun, its illuminated half facing heaven,
its dark half facing earth—hence it is called *hui*.
When the moon moves twenty-five degrees from the sun, a faint light appears to humanity—called *zaishengming*.
When it moves over ninety degrees, half its light becomes visible—called *shangxian*.
When sun and moon occupy opposite degrees, the moon above and the sun below,
their illumination mutually reflects: the dark half faces heaven,
the bright half faces earth, its light full and universally radiant—hence it is called *wang*.
After *wang*, as their opposition gradually shifts,
when the moon is twenty-five degrees from the sun, a faint darkness appears—called *zaishengpo*.
When it is over ninety degrees away, only half its light remains visible—called *xiaxian*.
From this point, its light gradually wanes until, its body concealed and light extinguished, it is called *hui* again.

Thus the moon’s body is not inherently round or crescent;
its phases arise solely from the sun’s illumination.
In truth, the moon waxes and wanes, while the sun knows no fullness or deficiency.
Just as the postnatal fate of mortals—birth, aging, sickness, death—shifts unpredictably,
only the single spark of prenatal nature-light remains perfectly luminous, crystalline, and enduring through ten thousand kalpas.

The celestial sphere spans 365¼ degrees.
The great yang sun traverses one degree per day, completing one circuit in a day and night—
hence a day and night together are called “one day.”
After thirty degrees, it meets the great yin moon.
The great yin moon moves slightly over thirteen degrees per day;
after slightly over twenty-nine days, it meets the sun again—
hence the cycle of *hui*, *shuo*, *xian*, and *wang* is called “one month.”

“Sun contains the essence of the five phases”: the sun, as great yang,
possesses the essence of fire, and within it the crow embodies the essence of water.
It receives the essence of wood to nourish its *qi*,
and the essence of metal to radiate its light.
At its center, it embraces the essence of earth to encompass its beginning and end—
only then is its luminous body and function complete.

“Moon receives the regulation of the six pitch-pipes”: on the *shuo* day,
the first yang establishes *zi*, and the pitch-pipe corresponds to *huangzhong*.
By *wang*, the three yangs are exhausted, corresponding to *zhonglü*.
Yang reaches its limit, and yin arises.
On the *wang* day, the first yin establishes *wu*, and the pitch-pipe corresponds to *ruibin*.
By *hui*, the three yins are complete, corresponding to *yingzhong*.
Yin reaches its limit, and yang arises again.
The six pitch-pipes encompass the six *lü* within.
Five is the center of yang numbers; doubling it yields the ten stems.
Six is the center of yin numbers; doubling it yields the twelve branches.
Five days make one *hou*; six *hou* make one *qi*.
Five multiplied by six equals thirty, precisely matching the degrees of sun-moon conjunction.
*Hui*, *shuo*, *xian*, and *wang* form an endless cycle; when the degrees are complete, they begin anew.

What is meant by “to fathom the numinous and comprehend transformation: when yang departs, yin arrives”?
Master Zhang said: “Unity gives rise to the numinous; duality gives rise to transformation.”
Judging by the luminous suspended images of the sun and moon, they appear as two entities.
Yet the single speck of yin *po* within the great yang is true water,
and the single speck of yang *hun* within the great yin is true fire.
In body, the sun and moon are *Yi*; in function, water and fire mutually conceal each other—
this is the unfathomable numinousness of yin and yang.
Thus one must trace the origin of the numinous to know whence transformation arises.
For when the sun departs, the moon arrives; when the moon departs, the sun arrives.
Their ceaseless alternation is one yet never fails to be two.
In truth, the *qi* of the great yang is concealed within the moon,
and the essence of the great yin is concealed within the sun.
Though called “departure” and “arrival,” there is in reality no departure or arrival—
two yet never fails to be one.

Whenever yin and yang stand in opposition, each departure and arrival is called transformation.
The numinous, however, is wholly present within, utterly still and unmoving, without departure or arrival.
To know transformation is to follow the sequence of what departs;
to fathom the numinous is to know inversely what arrives.
The sun and moon’s alternation endures eternally without cease—
like spokes converging at the hub or a wheel turning the carriage,
one emergence and entry distinguishing day and night,
one coiling and unfurling determining *hui* and *shuo*.

The cyclical shifts of the four seasons’ cold and heat, the ascending and descending movements of a single *yuan*-cycle—all are contained within this. Only because the numinous is unfathomable does transformation remain inexhaustible.

Within our bodies, the sun’s radiance and the moon’s essence mutually nourish and transform, converging entirely in the central palace. The unmoving primordial spirit alone can embrace both, fully sharing in the workings of creation. The *Yijing* contains three hundred and eighty-four lines, from which the *fu*-symbols are drawn—*fu* referring to the sixty-four hexagrams.

From the dark of the moon to the dawn of the new moon, *Zhen* arrives to receive the *fu*. At this very moment, heaven and earth commingle their essences, sun and moon mutually sustain one another. The potent yang scatters primordial bestowal; the receptive yin transforms the yellow womb. Chaos intermingles and conjoins, the first sprouts take root. Cultivation nurtures the budding node, spirit congeals to form the body. All beings tread forth from this, and every creeping, moving creature arises from it.

This passage speaks of the sun and moon’s conjunction producing the first yang. The sun and moon are the *Yi*, the root of creation. The three hundred and eighty-four lines are the functioning of the celestial revolution. Now, the *Yi* contains sixty-four hexagrams; excluding the four cardinal hexagrams *Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, and *Li*—which correspond to the furnace, cauldron, and elixir materials—the remaining sixty hexagrams yield three hundred and sixty lines, precisely matching the degrees of the celestial revolution, neither more nor less, like tallying tokens.

“Drawing the *fu*-symbols from the lines” means that within the sixty hexagrams, each must have one ruling line that holds the *fu*. For example, in *Zhun*, the ruling line is at the first position; in *Meng*, it is at the top. From the perspective of the *Yi*, it is called a hexagram; from the perspective of the elixir, it is called a *fu*.

The transition from the dark of the moon to the new moon is like the passage from *hai* to *zi* in a single day. Between the dark and new moon, sun and moon conjoin. At the northern region of *Xu* and *Wei*, yin reaches its extreme and yang is born—one yang returns, precisely corresponding to the first line of *Zhen*. Thus it is said: “From the dark to the dawn of the new moon, *Zhen* arrives to receive the *fu*.”

At the moment of their conjunction, heaven enters earth, the moon envelops the sun. Heaven entering earth presents the image of essence-commingling; the moon enveloping the sun presents the image of mutual sustenance. *Qian* governs the scattering of essence, its true yang within the primordial descending to earth; *Kun* governs reception and transformation, its true earth within the yellow womb sequentially embracing and enveloping it. Thus it is said: “The potent yang scatters primordial bestowal; the receptive yin transforms the yellow womb.”

One primordial, one yellow, mutually enveloping, their form resembles an egg. At this time, the sun and moon halt their wheels and return to chaos. Within this chaos, they spontaneously intermingle and commingle, producing a single true seed. The foundation of the elixir is established from this. Thus it is said: “Chaos intermingles and conjoins, the first sprouts take root.”

Once this single true seed is obtained within *Kun*, it becomes the budding node. It must be cultivated and nurtured, never allowed to scatter and dissipate. Over time, it gradually congeals, and the primordial spirit begins to form an embryo. The first yang of *Zhen* emerges to receive the *fu*. Thus it is said: “Cultivation nurtures the budding node, spirit congeals to form the body.”

This single true seed is the root of all life in the great earth. Not only is it the source from which we receive the *qi* of our bodily existence, but even the tiniest creeping, moving creatures with consciousness arise and are nourished from this single point. Thus it is said: “All beings tread forth from this, and every creeping, moving creature arises from it.”

This Dao, which creation follows to generate beings, is what we must reverse to generate ourselves. As it is said: “Following it makes one human; reversing it makes the elixir.” The conjunction of the dark and new moon is the living *zi* hour; the primordial bestowal and yellow womb are where the medicine is produced. Cultivation is the gathering and uniting; the budding node is the primordial spirit.

The sun’s departure and the moon’s arrival are nothing but the true fire and *fu*-timing. To seek the primordial true seed, one must establish the root in chaos.

*Baoyizi* says: The potent yang is the dragon; the receptive yin is the tiger. “Scattering primordial bestowal” is the dragon ascending the primordial heavens and descending as rain. “Transforming the yellow womb” is the tiger entering the earth and producing metal. Heaven above entering earth below is the image of chaos intermingling and conjoining. From this, the first sprouts take root, and through cultivation, the budding node is nurtured. Once the spirit congeals, the body naturally forms. From the vastness of heaven and earth to the tiniest creeping, moving creatures with consciousness, all emerge from this.

Producing this single point externally is the descent of the root and the flow of the branch, the inexhaustible Dao of generation. Producing this single point internally is the return to the root and restoration to the origin, the Dao of long life and transcendence.

**Chapter Four: The Advance and Retreat of Heavenly Symbols**

Here, Confucius praises the vast chaos, the virtue of *Qian* and *Kun* pervading emptiness. Investigating antiquity to the primordial sovereign, *Guanju* establishes the beginning.

Capping and marriage, *qi* intertwines; the first year then sprouts and flourishes. The sage is not born in vain, observing above to reveal the heavenly symbols. The heavenly symbols advance and retreat, bending and extending in accord with the seasons. Thus the *Yi* unites the heart of heaven, the hexagram *Fu* establishes the beginning’s sprout.

The eldest son inherits the father’s body, relying on the mother to establish the first signs. Waning and waxing respond to the seasonal rhythms, ascending and descending according to the pivot of the Dipper. On the third day, clarity emerges, *Zhen* and *Geng* receive the west. On the eighth day, *Dui* receives *Ding*, the first quarter is level as a string. On the fifteenth, the *Qian* body is complete, fullness flourishes in *Jia* of the east. The toad and the hare’s spirit, the *qi* of sun and moon shine doubly bright. The toad gazes at the hexagram’s node; the hare spits forth its radiance.

Seven and eight mark the Dao’s completion, bending and lowering in descent. On the sixteenth, the mandate turns, *Xun* and *Xin* appear at dawn. *Gen* stands in the southwest, the last quarter at twenty-three. *Kun* and *Yi* on the thirtieth day, the yang path loses its companion. The node is exhausted, and succession passes on; the embodied one is reborn as the dragon. *Ren* and *Gui* pair with *Jia* and *Yi*, *Qian* and *Kun* encompass the beginning and end.

Seven and eight make fifteen; nine and six likewise correspond. These four combine to make thirty, the yang *qi* searches for extinction and concealment. The eight trigrams array the luminaries, their movements never losing the center. The primordial essence is subtly wondrous and difficult to perceive; through calculation and measurement, its symbolic proofs are revealed.

In stillness, observe its images, modeling and likening its forms. Erect gnomons as standards, divining and awaiting to determine fortune and misfortune. Issue decrees in accord with the seasons, never missing the moment when lines move. Above, observe the patterns of the *Hetu*; below, examine the flows of earth’s forms; in the center, investigate the human heart—comparing and verifying the Three Powers.

In movement, follow the nodes of the trigrams; in stillness, rely on the judgments of the *Tuan*. When *Qian* and *Kun* are put into practice, the world is then governed. This chapter speaks of the celestial tally’s advance and retreat, which serves as the model for the firing times of the Golden Elixir.

Here, Confucius praises the *Hongmeng*, the virtue of *Qian* and *Kun* pervading the void. Investigating antiquity reaches back to the Primordial Sovereign; the *Guanju* establishes the beginning. Cap and marriage intertwine *qi*; the first year then sprouts growth. This passage marks the inception of the firing times.

The previous chapter spoke of the interval between the dark and new moon, when the single yang receives the tally, specifically marking the time when the medicine is produced—yet the waxing and waning of the Golden Elixir’s firing times were not fully revealed. Here, they are entirely disclosed.

Of the great ways of Heaven, none surpass the Five Phases. Of the great ways of humanity, none surpass the Five Classics. These may mutually elucidate one another, each having its own origin. The *Yijing* is the head of the Five Classics, and the two trigrams *Qian* and *Kun* are the head of the *Yijing*. The two trigrams *Qian* and *Kun* are split from the Supreme Ultimate, and this Supreme Ultimate itself, when united, is the *Hongmeng* single *qi*; when divided, it is the two trigrams *Qian* and *Kun*. *Qian* and *Kun* unite their virtues, their bodies containing the myriad transformations, their functions penetrating the Great Void.

Thus, Confucius praised them, saying: "Great is the origin of *Qian*! Supreme is the origin of *Kun*!" Is this not the beginning of yin and yang? Confucius, in editing the *Book of Documents*, began with the Two Canonical Texts, first recording the passage on investigating antiquity. Investigating antiquity reaches back to the Primordial Sovereign—this is the beginning of the *Book of Documents*. In editing the *Book of Songs*, he began with the Two Southern Courts, first listing the *Guanju* chapter. The *Guanju* establishes the beginning—this is the beginning of the *Book of Songs*. Ritual values the completion of a person, with cap and marriage as the beginning of procreation, hence it is said: "*qi* intertwines." The *Spring and Autumn Annals* records years, with the first year as the beginning of the annual sequence, hence it is said: "then sprouts growth." This immortal elder borrows worldly classics to illustrate the Daoist method.

The *Hongmeng* is the single *qi* of emptiness and nothingness. *Qian* is the cauldron that conceals the root of nature; *Kun* is the furnace that conceals the stem of life. Between them, the image of the sun and moon traversing the void. The Primordial Sovereign alludes to the primordial ancestral *qi*; the *Guanju* alludes to the mutual resonance of the two substances; "intertwining" alludes to the merging of the two *qi*; "the first year sprouts growth" refers to the initial stirring of the single yang, when the true seed is born.

The sage is not born in vain; above, he observes the manifest celestial tally. The celestial tally has advance and retreat, bending and extending in response to the seasons. Thus, the *Yijing* unites the heart of Heaven, and the *Fu* trigram establishes the beginning of sprouting. The eldest son inherits the father’s body, relying on the mother to establish the foundation. Waxing and waning respond to the seasonal pitch-pipes; rising and descending are based on the pivot of the Dipper. This passage precisely points to the return of the single yang as the foundation for making the Elixir.

The sage here refers to the sage who composed the *Yijing*. "Not born in vain" expresses the idea that he was not born without purpose. The celestial tally refers to the conjunction of the sun and moon, the natural tally of the Way of Heaven—what was called in the previous chapter "selecting the tally according to the lines." In the Dao of the Elixir, this is the timing of advancing and retreating. From the new moon to the full moon is the advance of yang fire, yang extending and yin bending, corresponding to the six hours from *zi* to *si*. From the full moon to the dark moon is the retreat of yin tally, yin extending and yang bending, corresponding to the six hours from *wu* to *hai*. In the Dao of the Elixir, movement and stillness, bending and extending, each has its proper time.

The sage silently observes the primordial transformations, knowing that the time must not be lost. He always commits his will to emptiness and nothingness to respond to it. As the *Yin Fu Jing* says: "Observe the Way of Heaven, grasp the movements of Heaven." The Way of Heaven, through the conjunction of the sun and moon, has advance and retreat, bending and extending. The Dao of the Elixir also takes the conjunction of the sun and moon; its advance and retreat, bending and extending are none other than the *Yijing*. Yet the sun and moon as the *Yijing* are truly governed by the heart of Heaven. The heart of Heaven is the master within creation—it is the Supreme Ultimate. The precelestial Supreme Ultimate creates Heaven and Earth in formlessness; the postcelestial Supreme Ultimate moves Heaven and Earth in form. In Heaven, it is precisely between the north and south poles; in humans, it is between the functions of *kan* and *li*. One *kan* and one *li*, combined, make the *Yijing*, governed by the heart at the very center of Heaven and Earth. Thus it is said: "The *Yijing* unites the heart of Heaven."

The heart of Heaven unites all things, yet it is seen only in the *Fu* trigram. Why? The essence of the heart of Heaven is originally neither movement nor stillness. The function of the heart of Heaven, however, is precisely in the alternation of movement and stillness. At the interval between *hai* and *zi*, when stillness is at its utmost, the sun and moon conjoin, the mysterious mechanism halts its turning. This heart is perfectly centered, without the slightest trace to be seen. When emptiness reaches its extreme and stillness is most profound, the myriad transformations return to the root—suddenly, from nothing, being is born; from extreme stillness, movement arises. From the depths of utmost yin, a single speck of true yang bursts forth, revealing the face of *Qian*’s origin. And from this, the foundation of the Elixir is established.

Thus, Confucius praised the *Yijing*, saying: "*Fu*—is this not the manifestation of the heart of Heaven and Earth?" Master Shao’s poem says: "At the winter solstice, the midpoint of *zi*, the heart of Heaven does not change." This is what is meant by: "the *Fu* trigram establishes the beginning of sprouting." The *Fu* trigram has *Zhen* within and *Kun* without. The single yang of *Zhen* obtains the original body of *Qian*; though it receives the true seed from the father *Qian*, it truly relies on the nurturing of the mother *Kun*. Like an infant just conceived, its form is complete though small, still not yet emerged from the mother’s womb. Thus it is said: "The eldest son inherits the father’s body, relying on the mother to establish the foundation."

Once the single yang has returned, it waxes and wanes, first corresponding to the Yellow Bell among the six pitch-pipes. When the single yang first stirs, it descends and then ascends, as the Dipper’s handle points precisely to *Yuanxiao*. The alchemist who attains this blows the pitch-pipes of his body, and water and fire naturally harmonize. He turns the Dipper’s handle of his body, and metal and wood naturally merge. Is this not what is meant by: "Waxing and waning respond to the seasonal pitch-pipes; rising and descending are based on the pivot of the Dipper"?

This marks the moment described in the previous chapter when *Zhen* arrives to receive the tally. On the third day, clarity emerges as *Zhen* (☳) and *Geng* receive from the west. On the eighth day, *Dui* (☱) receives *Ding*, and the first quarter hangs level as a taut cord. By the fifteenth day, the celestial stem’s body is complete, fully ripened as *Jia* faces east. The toad and the rabbit’s spirit—sun and moon’s *qi*—shine in dual brightness. The toad observes the hexagram’s nodes; the rabbit spits forth radiant light. When seven and eight of the Way are fulfilled, it bends and descends in gradual decline. On the sixteenth, the turning point shifts governance, as *Xun* (☴) and *Xin* meet the dawn’s level glow. *Gen* (☶) stands straight in the southwest; the last quarter arrives on the twenty-third. On the thirtieth day of *Kun* (☷) and *Yi*, the yang path loses its companion. When the node is exhausted, succession is passed on, and the embodied dragon is reborn.

This passage expounds the *Eight Trigrams’ Reception of the Celestial Stems* to verify the advance and retreat of the elixir’s firing times. As stated earlier, the return of the single yang occurs at the *hai-zi* hour in a day, at the winter solstice in a year, and at the dark-new moon in a month. To understand the firing times of the lesser celestial cycle in a month, one must carefully examine the *Celestial Stems’ Reception* in the *Former Heaven Eight Trigrams*. At the cusp of dark and new moon, sun and moon conjoin their tally. *Qian* and *Kun* remain unsevered; primal yellow is not yet divided.

The yang light is enveloped by the yin essence, concealed and unseen. This is the moment when our body returns to the root and restores life. Once the union is complete, the moon gradually drifts apart from the sun, and within the yin essence, yang begins to stir. By the third day, a faint glow first appears above the *geng* direction. The *Zhen* trigram receives *geng*, advancing to embrace a single yang—this is the initial manifestation of the primal nature, and the lead cauldron grows warm. Thus it is said: *“On the third day, it emerges as clarity; Zhen receives geng in the west.”* By the eighth day, the yang spirit gradually strengthens while the yin essence wanes; within the yin, yang now holds half, and at dusk, it appears in the south—this is the first quarter. The *Dui* trigram receives *ding*, advancing to embrace two yangs—here, the primal nature reveals itself further, and light pierces the curtains. Thus it is said: *“On the eighth day, Dui receives ding; the first quarter is level as a cord.”* By the fifteenth day, sun and moon face each other; the yin essence is entirely dispelled, and the yang spirit flourishes in fullness. Its light is complete, appearing at dusk in the east. *Qian* receives the six *jia*s, advancing to pure yang—now the primal nature shines forth, and within the cauldron, a single luminous spark glows day and night. Thus it is said: *“On the fifteenth, the Qian body is complete; in fullness, jia appears in the east.”*

Yet this lunar essence must unite with the solar spirit to achieve its radiance, truly corresponding to the two images of toad and hare. The toad symbolizes the essence of the great yang, while the hare’s essence symbolizes the light of the great yin. The toad, lurking at the water’s bottom, gazes intently, ceaselessly inhaling the golden essence of the sun into its belly. This illustrates the solar spirit infusing its essence into the moon, drawn inward from without. All hares in the world are female, bearing no males; gazing afar at the jade hare in the moon, they conceive, and when they give birth, they do so from the mouth. This illustrates the lunar essence receiving the sun’s light and releasing it from within. *Li* (☲) and *ji* govern the sun’s light, inherently bestowing. *Kan* (☵) and *wu* govern the moon’s essence, inherently transforming. The sun bestows virtue; the moon unfurls its light. Thus, from the last quarter to the dark-new moon, the moon emerges in the west at the *you* position, its entire body absorbing the sun’s essence and qi. From the first quarter to the full moon, the moon waxes in the east at the *mao* position, its entire body revealing the sun’s radiance. Thus it is said: *“Toad and hare’s essence—sun and moon’s qi shine as one.”* The reason for invoking the toad and hare lies in the toad’s gaze and the hare’s ability to birth from its mouth. The waxing and waning of the moon’s light wholly depend on its regard for the sun’s light to advance or retreat.

A single yin arises in *Xun* (☴), its light gradually withdrawing until the dark moon—this is the mechanism of creation’s inward turn. A single yang arises in *Zhen* (☳), its light gradually unfolding until the full moon—this is the mechanism of creation’s outward turn. At the cusp of dark and new moon, the sun’s light is absorbed into the moon’s essence, swallowing and devouring each other, stirred to conception. Only when the third day’s *geng* emerges is the light, once swallowed, regurgitated, turning from west to east, from *geng* to *jia*, until the full moon, when its radiance is complete. Thus it is said: *“The toad gazes at the trigram’s node; the hare births light by spitting.”*

After the fifteenth day of the full moon, yang reaches its zenith above; fullness cannot endure, and what rises must descend, what expands must contract—yang fire transforms into yin tally. Thus it is said: *“The seven-eight path is spent; bending, it descends low.”* From the sixteenth day onward, yang becomes the guest, yin the host. The yang spirit submits to the governance of the yin essence, and within the spirit, yin begins to stir. At dawn, it appears in the *xin* direction. The *Xun* trigram receives *xin*, retreating to embrace a single yin—this is the beginning of nature returning to life. Thus it is said: *“On the sixteenth, succession is passed on; Xun and xin appear at dawn.”* By the twenty-third day, the yin essence gradually strengthens while the yang spirit wanes; within the spirit, yin now holds half—this is the last quarter. At dawn, it appears in the *bing* direction. The *Gen* trigram receives *bing*, retreating to embrace two yins—this is nature halfway returned to life. Thus it is said: *“Gen aligns with the southwest; the last quarter is the twenty-third.”* By the thirtieth day, *Gen*’s single yang, lost in the northeast, now resides in the *yi* direction of *Kun*’s earth, embodying the image of the northeast’s lost companion. The single yang spirit is wholly gathered into the yin essence—this is nature returning to life, the primal yang reverting to chaos. Thus it is said: *“On the thirtieth day of Kun and yi, the yang path loses its companion.”* Yet yang cannot be entirely eroded; when the trigram’s node is exhausted, what wanes must cease, what descends must rise. The end of *Bo* (剥) is the beginning of *Fu* (复); the end of the dark moon is the beginning of the new. *Zhen*’s single yang, having inherited the body of the father *Qian*, now re-establishes its foundation in the mother *Kun*. Above *geng*, light is once more birthed by spitting. Thus it is said: *“When the node is exhausted, succession is passed on, and the embodied dragon is reborn.”*

*Ren* and *gui* pair with *jia* and *yi*; *Qian* and *Kun* encompass the beginning and end. Seven and eight make fifteen; nine and six are likewise balanced. These four combine to thirty, and the yang qi seeks concealment. The eight trigrams array the luminaries, their movements never losing the center. This passage concludes the beginning and end of the *Celestial Stems’ Reception*.

The *Eight Trigrams’ Reception of the Celestial Stems* originally derives from the *Former Heaven* circular diagram, most profoundly abstruse. *Kan*, the middle son, receives *wu*, yin enfolding yang—the body of the moon. *Li*, the middle daughter, receives *ji*, yang enfolding yin—the body of the sun. *Zhen*, the eldest son, and *Xun*, the eldest daughter, receive *geng* and *xin*. *Gen*, the youngest son, and *Dui*, the youngest daughter, receive *bing* and *ding*. Between them, each yin and yang pair matches the other. The father *Qian* alone receives *jia* and *ren*; the mother *Kun* alone receives *yi* and *gui*. From origin to end, key to beginning and conclusion, they encompass the six children within. Thus it is said: *“Ren and gui pair with jia and yi; Qian and Kun encompass the beginning and end.”* The six children are lesser yin and lesser yang; lesser yang is seven, lesser yin is eight, together making fifteen.

Qian and Kun are old yang and old yin. Old yang is numbered nine, old yin is numbered six, together also making fifteen. This precisely corresponds to the upper and lower crescents, forming the image of the full moon. Thus it is said: *“Seven and eight make fifteen; nine and six are likewise equivalent.”* The two lesser and two elder yin-yang pairs respond to the qi of the two crescents, mutually waxing and waning. Therefore, from new moon to full moon, yang grows while yin naturally wanes. From full moon to dark moon, yin grows while yang likewise wanes. At the dark moon, yang qi wanes and recedes, seeming to vanish and disappear, nearly exhausted. Yet who knows that a single point of primordial essence lies deeply hidden within the cavernous void, cycling endlessly without cease? Thus it is said: *“The four combine to thirty; yang qi wanes and hides away.”*
