The eight trigrams are arrayed in a circle, sun and moon unite as jade disks to generate light. The three yang and three yin mutually wax and wane, seemingly without fixed positions for Kan and Li. Yet as they circulate through the six voids, ascending and descending, it is none other than the central qi of Kan and Li that shifts and moves between them. This is why the sun and moon are the Changes, governing the celestial mind and serving as the standard for the advance and retreat of the three yin and three yang. Thus it is said: *“The eight trigrams array their luminaries; their movement never loses the center.”*

The primordial essence is wondrous and difficult to perceive; one infers and measures it to verify its signs. In stillness, observe its image and model its form. Set up a gnomon as a standard to divine auspices and determine fortune. Issue commands in accord with the seasons, never missing the moment of a moving line. This passage speaks of the correspondence between every movement and stillness and the celestial tally.

The trigrams and lines have movement and stillness; so too does the firing time of the golden elixir. When the appointed time has not yet arrived, one should wait in emptiness. For when Kan and Li unite, between them naturally resides a single point of primordial essence—this is the true seed of prior Heaven. It is that of which it is said: *“Vast and obscure, within it lies essence.”* This entity is most numinous and wondrous, beyond sight or hearing, difficult to conjecture. One can only infer the waxing and waning of the received celestial stems as verification of the celestial tally’s advance and retreat. Thus it is said: *“The primordial essence is wondrous and difficult to perceive; one infers and measures it to verify its signs.”*

The advance and retreat of the celestial tally have no form; within the formless and imageless, it seems to exist yet not exist. One should simply empty the mind and experience it, modeling its form and carefully awaiting its ebb and flow. Thus it is said: *“In stillness, observe its image and model its form.”* When the appointed time nears, one should move to respond. For between the dark and new moon, yang desires to arise yet has not departed from yin; the mechanism has stirred yet not departed from stillness. From within stillness, one must observe with utmost care. Like astronomers who set up gnomons to measure solar halos, or diviners who observe omens to determine fortune, not a single error can be made. Thus it is said: *“Set up a gnomon as a standard to divine auspices and determine fortune.”* This speaks of the moment before movement.

When the pivot is triggered and Heaven and humanity respond in unison, one must then apply the work of gathering. Like the court issuing great decrees at the proper time, or the seasons of creation being timely enacted, not a single moment’s delay is permissible. Thus it is said: *“Issue commands in accord with the seasons, never missing the moment of a moving line.”* This “time” is the “time to partake” in the *Yin Fu Jing*, referring to the living midnight hour between the dark and new moon. When the first yang stirs at the winter solstice, it belongs to the proper midnight hour.

Observe above the patterns of the River Chart, examine below the flows of earthly forms, and investigate between them the human heart, synthesizing and considering the Three Powers. In movement, follow the nodes of the trigrams; in stillness, rely on the judgments of the hexagrams. Qian and Kun put their functions into effect, and only then is the world governed. This passage speaks of the principle of every movement and stillness pervading the Three Powers.

Above is Qian, below is Kun—this encompasses beginning and end, the positions of Heaven above and Earth below. The central qi of Kan and Li shifts and moves between them, the position of humanity in the center. Here the images of the Three Powers are already fully present. This principle of movement and stillness thus penetrates Heaven and Earth, encompassing the River and Luo. The patterns of the River Chart refer to the Dragon Diagram; the numbers of the River Chart, fifty-five in total, cycle endlessly without cease, round to resemble Heaven’s movement. Observing above the patterns of the River Chart is to look up and contemplate the celestial patterns. The flows of earthly forms refer to the Luo Writing; the positions of the Luo Writing, four cardinal and four ordinal directions, are governed by the central five, square to resemble Earth’s stillness. Examining below the flows of earthly forms is to look down and inspect earthly principles.

Humanity is the heart of Heaven and Earth. Between Heaven and Earth lies the human heart—this is what Shaozi meant by *“Between one movement and one stillness lies the utmost subtlety of Heaven, Earth, and humanity.”* For this heart is neither movement nor stillness, yet can move and be still, uniting Heaven and Earth as the pivot of creation. Thus it is said: *“Investigate between them the human heart, synthesizing and considering the Three Powers.”*

In movement, respond to Heaven: yin and yang advance and retreat, necessarily following the nodes of the trigrams’ lines. Thus it is said: *“In movement, follow the nodes of the trigrams.”* This is what the *Xici Zhuan* means by *“In movement, observe its changes and contemplate its omens,”* and also the meaning of the earlier phrase *“issue commands in accord with the seasons.”*

In stillness, respond to Earth: hardness and softness have exterior and interior, never exceeding the words of the trigrams’ lines. Thus it is said: *“In stillness, rely on the judgments of the hexagrams.”* This is what the *Xici Zhuan* means by *“In stillness, observe its images and contemplate its words,”* and also the meaning of the earlier phrase *“model its form.”*

When stillness reaches its extreme, true yang stirs above the nine heavens—this is Qian’s primal yang employing nine, and the primordial spirit ascending to Qian’s crucible. When movement reaches its extreme and returns to stillness, true yin hides below the nine earths—this is Kun’s primal yin employing six, and the primordial qi returning to Kun’s furnace. The primordial spirit is nature; the primordial qi is life.

With nature perfected and life established, the celestial heart sits in solemn dignity at the central pole. The hundred joints and myriad spirits all converge and return to life—is this not what is meant by *“When Qian and Kun are employed in action, the world is then ordered”*? The first chapter states, *“Qian and Kun are the gate and door of the Changes,”* while the second chapter says, *“Heaven and Earth take their positions.”* Here, the opening reveals *“the virtue of Qian and Kun pervades the empty void,”* the middle speaks of *“Qian and Kun encompassing beginning and end,”* and the conclusion declares *“Qian and Kun employed in action.”* Thus, from beginning to end, it is only Qian and Kun that serve as the essence—making the meaning of the “gate and door” all the more evident.

The first chapter states, *“Kan and Li frame the structure, turning the wheel and aligning the axle.”* The second chapter says, *“Kan and Li are the two functions of Qian and Kun.”* This chapter first speaks of *“the qi of sun and moon shining in dual brightness,”* then of *“their movement shifting without losing the center,”* and finally reveals *“the two functions.”* Thus, from beginning to end, it is only Kan and Li that serve as the function—making the intent of “framing the structure” all the more transparent.

Master Baoyi said: *“The toad is the essence of metal qi, thus it waxes gradually according to the hexagram’s phases. The jade hare is the spirit of mao-wood, thus it emits light when gazing upon the great yang.”* This chapter is profoundly abstruse. The wondrous meaning of *na jia* derives from the ancient *Hetu* and the Primordial Circular Diagram, serving not only as the great linchpin of the entire *Cantong Qi* but also as the very marrow of the *Changes* of Fu Xi. The two images of the toad and the hare’s spirit are especially rare and perilously sublime. The Venerable Wei nearly coughed up his heart and liver over this—from now on, commentators and authors may look at one another and smile.

**Chapter Five: The Sovereign and Minister Administering Governance**
How could one not be cautious?

At the forefront of administering governance lies *(renewing the old and reforming the decayed)*. It encompasses the subtle and the meticulous, unfurling and bestowing treasures. The essential Dao and its pivotal handle, the unifying thread of transformation—these are the warp and woof of cosmic order. When the lines and images stir within, fortune and misfortune arise without. The five planets move in错综 or orderly paths, responding to the seasons in mutual resonance. When the four and seven fall into discord, they diverge and converge, bowing and rising. The Literary Star governs and records, while the censors and ministers are questioned and held to account. The hundred officials and their bureaus each oversee their respective domains. Tracing beginnings to endings, one discerns the threads of survival and demise.

Should the sovereign grow arrogant and indolent, swelling with excess and straying from the Dao; or should ministers turn deceitful and sycophantic, their conduct deviating from the proper path—then the waxing and waning of the lunar phases, the expansions and contractions of celestial cycles, will fall into disorder, bringing forth calamity and blame. Those who uphold the law must admonish and reprove, exposing faults to admonish the ruler. When the Pole Star holds its rightful place, *(receiving)* the center, it moves with ease, entrusting matters to those below. When the Hall of Light enacts governance, the state remains free of harmful ways.

This chapter employs the successes and failures of sovereign and ministers in administering governance as a metaphor for the successes and failures of the fire-phase in the alchemy of Golden Elixir. How could one not be cautious?

At the forefront of administering governance lies *(renewing the old and reforming the decayed)*. It encompasses the subtle and the meticulous, unfurling and bestowing treasures. The essential Dao and its pivotal handle, the unifying thread of transformation—these are the warp and woof of cosmic order. When the lines and images stir within, fortune and misfortune arise without. The five planets move in错综 or orderly paths, responding to the seasons in mutual resonance. When the four and seven fall into discord, they diverge and converge, bowing and rising. The Literary Star governs and records, while the censors and ministers are questioned and held to account. The hundred officials and their bureaus each oversee their respective domains.

This passage employs governance as a metaphor for the caution required in the initial stages of regulating the firing process. The essence of the firing process demands vigilance from beginning to end, guarding against danger and contemplating risk without a moment’s laxity. Just as a ruler must be especially prudent at the outset of governance, so too does the great Dao of the Golden Elixir take the celestial mind as its master and essence-qi as its function—akin to a sovereign’s command over ministers. Hence, it is called "governing administration."

When the adept first enters the chamber, the initial stirring of yang is termed the "primary meridian," much like a ruler’s ascension to the throne and the reform of the calendar, marking the first year. The preceding chapter’s "first year" signifies the sprouting of the germ, echoing this principle. Thus, the immortal master sighs and begins, "How could one not be cautious? At the forefront of administering governance..."

"Encompassing the subtle and the meticulous" refers to stillness and inward guarding, the sealing of all gates. "Unfurling and bestowing treasures" signifies movement in response to opportunity, issuing decrees in accord with the moment. The "pivotal handle" is the Dipper’s handle, for the Dipper is the celestial tongue, measuring and harmonizing primordial transformation, governing the entire heavens. Like a net’s main cord or a garment’s fastening, it is the essential Dao. This illustrates that the celestial mind within the body is the guiding principle of all transformations. The workings of the elixir path rely entirely on the celestial mind’s operation of the Dipper’s handle to advance and shift. Hence, it is said, "The essential Dao and its pivotal handle, the unifying thread of transformation."

Since the celestial mind is the warp and woof of all transformations, movement in alignment brings universal fortune, while deviation invites misfortune. The *Xici Zhuan* states, "When the lines and images stir within, fortune and misfortune arise without"—this is its meaning. In the *Book of Changes*, it is the lines and images; in the heavens, it is the stellar configurations. The sky has three enclosures: the Purple Forbidden Enclosure, home of the North Star, lies deepest within. The Supreme Subtlety Enclosure follows, and the Celestial Market Enclosure comes after. Beyond these, the five planets—Metal, Wood, Water, Fire, and Earth—and the twenty-eight lunar mansions encircle the enclosures. The ruling stars within the enclosures all depend on the Dipper’s handle; every planet and fixed star, whether moving in sequence or retrograding, obeys its command.

When the Dipper’s handle moves in harmony, the five planets and fixed stars all follow their proper courses. Should the handle falter, the encircling planets immediately reverse their paths. Their resonance with the seasons reveals immediate omens of misfortune. The celestial bodies across the heavens likewise deviate from their regular paths, leading to "divergence, convergence, bowing, and rising." This mirrors the human celestial sovereign: when it remains unmoved, the five senses err, and the hundred meridians surge chaotically—what is called the slightest deviation preventing the elixir’s formation.

The aberrations of celestial phenomena cannot be blamed on the myriad stars; nor can a ruler’s misgovernance be attributed to the hundred officials. Each office has its overseer—who, then, is the true master? In the heavens, it is the Literary Star and the censorial ministers. The Literary Star comprises the six stars of the Winnowing Basket within the Purple Forbidden Enclosure, known as the Southern Culmen, recording the register of human longevity. The censorial ministers are the Three Terraces and Four Auxiliaries within the enclosure, corresponding to the three powers and four images, each stationed in its proper quadrant, encircling and upholding the celestial pole.

The Literary Star in the heavens is like the six ministries of a ruler; the censorial ministers are like the chief ministers. The chief ministers assist the sovereign in harmonizing yin and yang, while the six ministries carry out their decrees. Thus, the hundred officials and their bureaus, without need for reproach, naturally oversee their respective domains. This is akin to the firing process: when the heart-sovereign resides at the center to govern the exterior, the Dipper’s handle and the Northern Asterism sit in vigil, the handle revolving to regulate water and fire in perfect harmony. Naturally, the six roots attain great stability, the hundred meridians achieve profound harmony, and no reckless or errant movements occur.

Tracing the origin and contemplating the end reveals the threads of survival and ruin. Should the ruler grow arrogant and indulgent, exceeding bounds and violating the Dao, or should ministers turn deceitful and perverse, acting outside proper norms, the waxing and waning of celestial bodies will deviate, foretelling misfortune. The enforcers of the law will censure and reproach, admonishing the ruler for transgressions. When the celestial pole is rightly aligned, governance flows effortlessly; when the Hall of Light enacts policies, the state suffers no harmful Dao. This passage speaks of the firing process’s essence: the sovereign must remain as vigilant at the end as at the beginning.

Every movement and stillness in the firing process, from start to finish, demands unceasing caution—just as a ruler’s every action, from beginning to end, must be equally vigilant. Vigilance transforms ruin into survival; negligence turns survival into ruin. The threads of survival and ruin diverge here. This great matter is the shared responsibility of ruler and ministers, yet the sovereign alone bears ultimate authority. For ministers obey the ruler as qi obeys intent. When the heart-sovereign is reverent and steadfast in its purpose, no deceitful sounds or perverse sights can intrude. But if the heart-sovereign grows arrogant and self-willed, deviating from the proper Dao, the senses will respond with deceit, and actions will stray from the norm.

The celestial mind and the human mind share a single source. Should the celestial mind falter even slightly, celestial movements immediately turn anomalous. Not only do the five planets err and the fixed stars deviate, but even the moon’s phases—new moon, full moon, waxing, and waning—follow regular patterns. Yet when it should wax, it wanes; when it should wane, it waxes. Eclipses and obscurations bring innumerable misfortunes. The heavens have stars that enforce the law, censuring faults—namely, the Left and Right Enforcers within the Supreme Subtlety Enclosure. The court mirrors this, establishing left and right enforcers to censure transgressions. Yet the fault of violating the Dao lies not with the hundred officials, nor even with the censorial ministers, but with the ruler alone. This is the principle that all transformations follow the mind, returning to the source to trace the origin. Hence, it is said, "The enforcers of the law censure and reproach, admonishing the ruler for transgressions."

The ruler’s mind gains or loses its balance in a single reversal. For only when the sovereign establishes the supreme standard do the people return to that standard. If the mind-monarch can remain still and unmoving, governing with non-action to preserve perfect rectitude, the hundred limbs naturally follow its commands—just as the Northern Apex abides in its place and the host of stars naturally encircle it. Thus it is said: *“When the Apex-star holds its proper position, it rests at ease, entrusting all below.”* Once the mind-monarch sits in solemn dignity within the chamber of spirit, the myriad nodes and spirits all become reverently obedient, just as when the king sits in the Hall of Light to receive the feudal lords, the four seas and nine provinces submit without exception. How then could there be any who emerge to obstruct transformation and harm the Dao? Thus it is said: *“When the Hall of Light enacts governance, the state suffers no harm to the Dao.”*

The Apex-star in the celestial pattern is the Purple Tenuity Enclosure, the abode of the Northern Apex. In the ruler, it is the inner chamber where he retires to rest. The Hall of Light in the celestial pattern is the Celestial Market Enclosure, where the Emperor-star descends. In the ruler, it is the place of court assemblies, the thoroughfare to the nine Yi and eight Man. Where the mind-monarch resides, within lies the Grotto Chamber, without lies the Hall of Light—above corresponding to the celestial enclosures, below mirroring the court’s tranquility. Thus it takes the image of governing the polity.

This chapter clarifies the governance of the Dao through the illumination of the elixir path, making it most evident. The elixir path, from beginning to end, does not depart from the mind-monarch’s operation. Hence, the ruler is likened to the celestial mind, the ministers to the medicinal substances, the Literary Prosperity and Grand Supporters to the Three Fields and Four Images, the law-enforcing ministers to the officials of the ears and eyes, and the hundred officials and functionaries to the essence and qi throughout the body. Fortune arises from receiving qi; misfortune from guarding against qi. *“Presence”* signifies obtaining the medicine in an instant; *“absence”* signifies losing it in a moment.

What is prized in governing the polity is that one must externally repel all perverse influences and internally assist the true sovereign. When the mind-monarch sits in solemn dignity at the Everlasting Apex, the myriad transformations submit to the Hall of Light—is this not the essential path of returning to the true? The first chapter of this work clarifies the gates of Qian and Kun to reveal Qian and Kun as the body; the second chapter clarifies the two functions of Kan and Li to reveal Kan and Li as their use; the third chapter speaks of the conjunction of new and full moons to produce the medicinal substances; the fourth chapter speaks of the celestial tally’s advance and retreat to enact the firing times—all are images of governing the polity. Yet the meaning of governing the polity must be made clear: in the celestial pattern, it is the Everlasting Apex governing the host of stars throughout the heavens; in the court, it is the sovereign governing the hundred officials and functionaries; in the elixir path, it is the mind-monarch governing the essence and qi throughout the body—this is the meaning of governing the polity. Thus this chapter concludes it.

The preceding section (the middle chapter of the upper volume, comprising three chapters on nurturing inherent nature) is the middle of the upper volume. This volume focuses on nurturing inherent nature, with governing the polity and ingesting the elixir already contained within it. For the primordial ancestral nature is still and unmoving, yet responsive and immediately penetrating. It does not depart from the Central Yellow, serving as the master of the myriad transformations. To speak of nature is to include destiny within it; to speak of nurturing inherent nature is to return both primordial essence and primordial qi to the primordial spirit. Only by knowing and nurturing it can one accord with the great Dao of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi—emptiness and spontaneity. Thus the final chapter is again titled *Nurturing Inherent Nature in the Manner of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi*.

**Chapter Six: Refining the Self and Establishing the Foundation**

Within, nurture the self in tranquility and emptiness.
Trace back to the hidden luminosity, inwardly illuminating the body.
Seal the orifices, fortify the numinous root.
The Three Lights sink into obscurity, warming and nourishing the child-pearl.
Though unseen to the eye, it is near and easily sought.
The Yellow Center gradually opens its principles, moistening and reaching the skin.
Begin with rectitude, and the end will be cultivated—once established, it can be upheld.
With the One, all is concealed; the world knows nothing of it.

This chapter speaks of refining the self and establishing the foundation in attaining the One—it is the initial practice of nurturing inherent nature.

*Within, nurture the self in tranquility and emptiness.
Trace back to the hidden luminosity, inwardly illuminating the body.
Seal the orifices, fortify the numinous root.
The Three Lights sink into obscurity, warming and nourishing the child-pearl.
Though unseen to the eye, it is near and easily sought.*

This passage speaks of the initial foundation of refining the self. The preceding chapters on governing the polity merely expounded the transformative patterns of Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li. Only here is the work of refining the self directly pointed out, showing the practitioner where to begin. As Ancestor Lü said: *“The sevenfold return of the elixir lies in man, but first one must refine the self and await the time.”* Master Zhang Ziyang said: *“If one wishes to complete the nine transformations, one must first refine the self and hold the mind.”* Refining the self is nurturing the self. The self is the earth of the center within Li, the abode of the root of inherent nature. Only because the primordial Qian-nature has transformed into the posterior Li, and the primordial spirit has turned into the consciousness-spirit, does the yin qi within the heart flow ceaselessly, easily lost and difficult to uphold—without the primordial supreme yang qi of Kan, there is nothing to restrain it. Yet the primordial One Qi arises from emptiness; without the work of attaining emptiness and preserving stillness, how can one trace back to the origin and return to the root? Thus it is said: *“Within, nurture the self in tranquility and emptiness.”*

At the moment of receiving qi in birth, there was originally a single point of numinous clarity, possessed by all. Only because the posterior takes charge, the root is lodged in the dust, the dust turns into consciousness, and daily one races outward in pursuit, inevitably turning away from enlightenment and joining with the dust, mistaking the servant for the master. Thus one must moment by moment withdraw the gaze and return the hearing, so that the single point of numinous clarity naturally hides and does not reveal itself, deeply concealed as if empty. From this, silently illuminating within, one comes to know that the body of the four great elements is but a temporary aggregation, ultimately a bubble of illusion—immediately one attains liberation. Thus it is said: *“Trace back to the hidden luminosity, inwardly illuminating the body.”*

Dui is the mouth, the gateway of entry and exit for the entire body. All places where primordial qi leaks are called Dui, but they are all governed by the square-inch aperture. The *Yellow Court Classic* says: *“Within the square inch, carefully conceal and store”*—this is the meaning of sealing. Within this square inch lies a single point of the most numinous substance, the root and stock of all generation and transformation.

Thus it is called the "numinous stock." To fortify it is to prevent leakage and wavering. The Three Lights in heaven are the sun, moon, and Dipper; in humans, *Li* corresponds to the sun, *Kan* to the moon, and the Heavenly Heart at the center to the pivot of the Dipper. One *Kan* and one *Li*, meeting north and south. Turning inward to listen and illuminate, the true person hides in the deep abyss; sealing the gates and fortifying the guard, the primordial pearl is obtained from the formless. Thus the Heavenly Heart remains still and unmoving, and the work of refining the self is accomplished. Hence it is said: *“The Three Lights sink into obscurity, nurturing the child-pearl.”*

Yet this original single point of numinous light flickers in and out of existence, neither near nor far, ever present before one’s eyes—yet people do not recognize it. Sought within the body, it cannot be found; sought outside the body, it cannot be found. Thus it is said: *“Invisible when seen, near yet easily sought.”* Gradually the Yellow Center penetrates principle, its moisture reaching the skin. When the beginning is correct, the end will be cultivated; when the trunk stands firm, the branches may be grasped. The One is for concealment—no one in the world knows this.

This passage speaks of the work of refining the self as lying in attaining the One. The *Scripture on Salvation* says: *“Within the center, the five qi harmonize, and the hundred spirits merge.”* Clearly, the Yellow Court’s cinnabar chamber is the gathering place of all transformations. It is like the North Star’s abode, around which all stars naturally array themselves. Those who cultivate the Dao nurture the child-pearl here, neither forgetting nor forcing; over time, spirit and clarity arise of themselves. Gradually, the four directions and eight reaches open, the nine orifices, hundred channels, three hundred sixty joints, and eighty-four thousand pores of the body all become unobstructed, naturally radiant and harmonious, responsive and fully permeable. This is what the *Book of Changes* means by *“excellence within, flowing to the four limbs.”* Hence it is said: *“Gradually the Yellow Center penetrates principle, its moisture reaching the skin.”*

The Dao has a beginning and an end, a root and branches. The beginning is the initial work of refining the self; the end is the completion of the inner chamber’s task. The beginning is like the trunk of a tree, the root; the end is like its branches, the branches. Yet one must know that the very first step is also the final step of completion. As the saying goes: *“Grasp the root, and you need not worry about the branches.”* The first step plants one firmly on the correct path; from there, advancing step by step, the work of cultivation naturally aligns at every stage. The origin can encompass the end, just as the root can embrace the branches. Thus it is said: *“When the beginning is correct, the end will be cultivated; when the trunk stands firm, the branches may be grasped.”*

But then, what is the beginning, and what is the root? It lies in the One alone. Before birth, attaining the One makes one human; after birth, embracing the One completes the elixir. For the One generates the Two, the Two generates the Three, and the Three generates the myriad things. Following the natural course to give birth to humans and things—this is the One. Yet reversing the course, the Three returns to the Two, the Two returns to the One, and the One returns to emptiness. Coming against the current to become a sage or immortal—this, too, is the One. The Most High said: *“Attain the One, and all affairs are complete.”* And again: *“The Valley Spirit does not die—this is called the Mysterious Female.”* The Valley Spirit is utterly empty yet utterly numinous, its wondrousness endlessly generative. From this endless generation emerges the Mysterious Female. Its essence is the One; its function is twofold. The secret lies in the two words: *concealment* and *obscuration*. To conceal is to seal the primordial gate; to obscure is to veil the female portal. Without the One at the center, how could concealment and obscuration be achieved? Yet without concealment and obscuration on the outside, how could it become the One? This subtle aperture’s wonder cannot be grasped without a true teacher’s guidance. Even with wisdom surpassing Yan and Min, none could force a guess—how much less the world’s ordinary people? Hence it is said: *“The One is for concealment—no one in the world knows this.”*

The so-called *Yellow Center* refers to the center of the ancestral aperture. The so-called *One* refers to the One within the ancestral aperture. Knowing the center, one knows the aperture; knowing the One, one knows the aperture’s wonder. Knowing the aperture’s wonder, one knows the original ancestral nature—and thus knows that guarding the center and embracing the One is the first step in nurturing one’s nature.

**Chapter Seven: The Mutual Function of the Two Apertures**
The superior virtue acts without striving, not seeking through scrutiny. The lesser virtue labors without ceasing, its function unending. Closing above is called *having*; closing below is called *nothingness*. Nothingness serves the above, where spirit and clarity reside. This two-aperture method—even the golden qi mutually depends on it. Know the white and guard the black, and spirit and clarity will come of themselves. The white is the essence of metal; the black is the foundation of water. Water is the pivot of the Dao, its number named One. The beginning of yin and yang, it contains the Yellow Sprout. The master of the five metals, the Northern River Chariot. Thus lead is black without but holds golden radiance within—clothed in coarse robes yet hiding jade, appearing as a madman. Metal is the mother of water; the mother conceals the child’s embryo. Water is the child of metal; the child shelters the mother’s womb. The true person’s utmost subtlety is as if present, as if absent—now deep in the abyss, now floating on the surface. Withdrawing, it spreads and distributes, each guarding its own domain.

Gathered, it resembles whiteness; refined, it becomes vermilion. Tempered into a protective barrier, the true resides within the white. Square and round, inch and span, blended and mutually constrained. Born before heaven and earth, majestic and exalted. Surrounded by walls and gates, shaped like Penglai. Encircled and sealed, four directions hesitating. Guarded with utmost secrecy, blocking all treachery and evil. Curved pavilions connect, warding against the unforeseen. Free from thought, yet difficult to weary. Spirit and qi fill the chamber—none can detain them. Those who guard it prosper; those who lose it perish. In movement and stillness, rest and activity, it is ever with the person.

This chapter directly points to the function of the *Kan* and *Li* apertures as the key to the golden elixir. The superior virtue acts without striving, not seeking through scrutiny. The lesser virtue labors without ceasing, its function unending.

When the upper is sealed, it is called "being"; when the lower is sealed, it is called "non-being." Non-being serves to uphold the upper, where the numinous dwells. These two apertures follow this method, their golden *qi* mutually dependent. This passage points to the wondrous function of the two apertures.

The Great Dao is not numinous without the One, nor transformative without the Two. The previous chapter states that the One is for concealment—having already shown people how to attain the One. Yet the marvel of concealment lies in this: its essence abides in the One, while its function does not depart from the Two. For the wondrous function of the golden elixir resides solely in the postcelestial *Kan* and *Li*, and the marvel of *Kan* and *Li* does not transcend the precosmic *Qian* and *Kun*. In the end, it is only the two words: *nature* and *life*.

Nature is the precosmic spark of spirit, the essence of true emptiness. Its essence is perfectly complete and all-pervading, neither diminishing nor increasing. In heaven, it is the primordial *Qian* that initiates all; in humans, it is the original face before parents were born. Thus it is called *superior virtue*. Within it, nothing exists—only the radiance of spirit shines, utterly free from the dust of the senses. If one seeks to measure or deduce it through the mind’s calculations, one strays ten thousand miles away. For nature is inherently natural, beyond all conjecture. It is truly unattainable when sought. Hence it is said: *"The superior virtue acts without striving, not seeking through scrutiny."*

Life is the precosmic primordial *qi*, the wondrous function of being. Its function pivots the Three Powers and encompasses the myriad transformations. In heaven, it is the generative *Kun* that nurtures all; in humans, it is the root of destiny established at the first cry of birth. Thus it is called *lesser virtue*. Within it, the primordial *qi* circulates, hidden in heaven and earth, manifesting without fixed form.

If one clings to the realm of form and sinks into emptiness, guarding stillness, one falls into the poisoned sea. For life belongs to the realm of activity and does not dwell in dead emptiness. A single breath uncirculated means death. Thus it is said: *"Lesser virtue acts with effort, its function unceasing."*

*"When the upper closes, it is called ‘being’"*—this is *Kun* entering *Qian* to become *Li*. The primordial *Qian* is originally the superior virtue, but when a single yin from *Kun* ascends, the yang *qi* of *Qian* closes it from without. This is what is meant by *"the utmost yin, solemn and still, emerges from heaven."* With this one yin within *Qian*, nature transforms into life, responding and thus communicating, becoming the active lesser virtue. People only see the emptiness within the body of *Li* and take it for true emptiness, unaware that this very point of emptiness is the wondrous being within true emptiness, called *"being within non-being."*

*"When the lower closes, it is called ‘non-being’"*—this is *Qian* entering *Kun* to become *Kan*. The primordial *Kun* is originally the lesser virtue, but when a single yang from *Qian* descends, the yin *qi* of *Kun* also closes it from without. This is what is meant by *"the utmost yang, blazing and bright, arises from earth."* With this one yang within *Kun*, life transforms back into nature, utterly still and unmoving, returning to the effortless superior virtue. People only see the fullness within the body of *Kan* and take it for wondrous being, unaware that this very point of fullness is the true emptiness within wondrous being, called *"non-being within being."*

Once *Kun* receives the *qi* of *Qian*, it returns this point of true yang upward to *Qian*. This is called returning to the root and restoring life. From within the primordial chamber of spirit, a single *yinin* is produced—this is the indestructible primordial spirit of ten thousand kalpas. Thus it is said: *"The non-being serves the above, where the numinous dwells."*

The wondrous function of the numinous resides entirely in the central yellow’s rightful position. Yet without the true essence of metal from *Kan* ascending and the true *qi* of water from *Li* descending, with being and non-being interpenetrating and the two communicating in harmony, how could the numinous ever arise? Thus it is said: *"These two apertures follow the method—metal and qi likewise depend on each other."* These two apertures are the wondrous openings of *Kan* and *Li* in their functions, the so-called Gate of the Primordial Female, unknown to the world.

Knowing the white and guarding the black, the numinous comes of itself. The white is the essence of metal; the black is the foundation of water. Water is the pivot of the Dao, its number named One. The beginning of yin and yang, it originally contains the yellow sprout. The master of the five metals, the river chariot of the north. Thus lead is black without but holds golden blossoms within. Cloaked in coarse cloth yet bearing jade, outwardly a madman. This passage directly points to the metal within water as the primordial elixir mother.

Following the above, the so-called numinous does not arise spontaneously but requires a certain process. This process lies entirely in knowing the white and guarding the black. The white is the true essence within *Kan*; the black is the true water within *Li*. If one can thoroughly perceive true emptiness and silently preserve wondrous being, a single point of the numinous naturally arises from the formless. As the *Heart Seal Sutra* says: *"Preserve non-being, guard being—accomplished in an instant."* This alone is the golden elixir, the point where the postnatal returns to the prenatal. Thus it is said: *"Know the white, guard the black—the numinous comes of itself."*

Master Wei, fearing that people might not recognize the original source of the golden elixir, reiterates: the white is the essence of metal; the black is the foundation of water. This white is not tangible metal but the primordial nature within the empty kalpa. The primordial nature is originally pure and unstained white—this is the face of *Qian* before birth, what is called superior virtue. Is not the white the essence of metal? This black is not the water that flows on earth but the one *qi* born of the formless. This one *qi* is originally the undivided primal chaos—this is the root of *Kun* after the first cry of birth, what is called lesser virtue. Is not the black the foundation of water?

The primordial metal-nature is the undifferentiated great Dao, as yet unnamed by the One. Once the Dao generates the One, its signs emerge, and it is called the water of Heavenly One—the pivot of the Dao, wherein the metal-nature is concealed. Thus it is said: *"Water is the pivot of the Dao, its number named One."* The first point of true water conceals true metal, the root of the ceaseless generation of primordial *qi*. Thus it is said: *"The beginning of yin and yang originally contains the yellow sprout."* The yellow sprout takes the image of metal concealed within water, referring to the primordial One.

The primordial One *qi* is precisely the metal-essence of *Qian*, able to govern all transformations and serve as the true sovereign of the postnatal five phases’ ceaseless generation, deeply hidden within the northern pole’s great abyss. Thus it is said: *"The master of the five metals, the river chariot of the north."* The five metals borrow the external refinement of silver, lead, cinnabar, mercury, and earth to illustrate the essence of the five phases within the body. This single thing, being purely black without, resembles lead. Yet within its blackness it contains white, thus also bearing the image of golden blossoms. It is like one cloaked in coarse cloth yet bearing jade within, outwardly a madman yet inwardly a sage—is this not the unfathomable numinous? This distinguishes true lead from common lead. If one can know the white and guard the black, the numinous comes of itself.

The wondrous function of the golden elixir lies solely in the metal within water. This passage particularly reveals its symbolic form. The *Mirror of Entering the Medicine* says: *"The lead of the watery realm is but one flavor,"* and the *Awakening to Reality* says: *"Black within holds white—this is the elixir mother."* This is what is meant. Metal is the mother of water; the mother conceals the child’s embryo. Water is the child of metal; the child harbors the mother’s womb. The true person’s utmost subtlety is as if present, as if absent.

As if the great abyss—now sinking, now floating. Withdrawing, they disperse, each guarding its own domain.

Gathered, it resembles white; refined, it becomes vermilion. Tempered into defense, the true dwells within the white.

This passage further points to the dual function of the metal and water apertures, merging two into one. The previous section spoke of metal within water in synthesis; here, it distinguishes the two bodies of metal and water. The essence of metal naturally generates water—it is water’s mother. True metal within *qian* lies concealed within the *kun* water’s envelope, hence: *"the mother conceals the child’s embryo."* This is what the earlier passage meant by *"closing below, it is called absence."*

Water is born of metal—it is metal’s child. True water within *kun* is hidden within the *qian* metal’s frame, hence: *"the child harbors the mother’s womb."* This is what the earlier passage meant by *"closing above, it is called presence."*

When metal and water interact, it is like the *qi* of the two crescents rising on both sides and uniting as one—from this, the true person emerges. The true person resides in the central palace, neither existing nor not existing, numinous and unfathomable. Thus: *"The true person’s utmost subtlety is as if present, as if absent."*

*"As if the great abyss"*—the true person abides in the deep abyss. *"Now sinking, now floating"*—floating and sinking, it guards the compass within. At the moment of metal and water’s union, both dwell in the center. Once united and withdrawn, the true person remains in the middle, while the two again disperse above and below, one south, one north—*"each guarding its own domain."*

Initially, the metal gathered from the northern *kan* is a sheet of pure white. When forged with the true fire of the southern *li*, it suddenly radiates brilliance—is this not *"gathered, it resembles white; refined, it becomes vermilion"*?

Yet this single true seed, neither existing nor not existing, is of the most subtle essence. It must rely on the central yellow *kun* mother to encircle and nurture it, only then can it securely abide in the chamber of spirit, unmoving and unshaken. Thus: *"Tempered into defense, the true dwells within the white."*

This passage speaks of merging two into one, the image of the medicine entering the furnace. It is what was earlier called *"the absent offers to the above, where the numinous dwells."*

Round and square, inch and span—intermingled, they restrain one another. Born before heaven and earth, majestic and exalted. Beside it stand ramparts and gates, resembling Penglai’s isle. Encircled and sealed, four paths hesitate. Guarded with utmost secrecy, all treachery is barred. Curved pavilions connect, warding against the unforeseen.

Here, thought may cease; sorrow and toil cannot enter. The chamber brims with spirit and *qi*—none can detain it. Those who guard it prosper; those who lose it perish. In movement and stillness, rest and activity, it ever accompanies the person.

This section particularly reveals the symbolic form of the furnace and crucible, with the firing process contained therein. Within the Central Yellow Spirit-Chamber, the space spans no more than an inch—round to symbolize Heaven, square to symbolize Earth, and at its center dwells the True Person. Vast and undifferentiated, its shape resembles a hen’s egg. As the *Yellow Court Scripture* states: *"Square and round, an inch within lies this place."* Thus it is said: *"Round and square, inch and span—intermingled, they restrain one another."*

This inch-wide space is the Primordial Pass. The Primordial Pass is a single aperture that broadly encompasses the Six Directions, yet subtly penetrates the finest dust. Before Heaven and Earth existed, this aperture was already present, known as the Heaven within Heaven. Within it is stored the Primordial Ancestral *Qi*. Is this not what was called *"born before heaven and earth, majestic and exalted"*?

This aperture resides at the very center of Heaven and Earth. To its left and right, the Two Modes divide; above and below, the Three Powers are established. To the left, it connects to the Primordial Gate; to the right, it reaches the Mysterious Portal. Above, it pierces the Celestial Barrier; below, it penetrates the Earthly Axis. Eight-sided and exquisitely wrought, it resembles the isles of Penglai and Fanghu. Is this not what was called *"beside it stand ramparts and gates, resembling Penglai’s isle"*?

*"Encircled and sealed, four paths hesitate"* refers to deep roots and firm stems, securely anchoring the Eight Gates to prevent what is within from escaping. *"Guarded with utmost secrecy, all treachery is barred"* means withdrawing the gaze and returning the hearing, shutting out all distractions to prevent what is outside from entering. Though the numinous aperture is interconnected without obstruction, one must still guard against danger and consider risks. Thus it is said: *"Curved pavilions connect, warding against the unforeseen."*

In concentration, the light of awareness is unbroken, yet one must remain at ease and natural. Thus it is said: *"Here, thought may cease; sorrow and toil cannot enter."* Within the Spirit-Chamber, the Primordial Ancestral *Qi* is inherently complete in every person, originally overflowing and abundant. Yet people themselves cannot long retain it. Thus it is said: *"The chamber brims with spirit and *qi*—none can detain it."*

Once the True Person is settled within the Spirit-Chamber, one must attend to it constantly, guarding it at every moment. If even a single moment is neglected, one risks losing it entirely. Thus it is said: *"Those who guard it prosper; those who lose it perish."*

In this state, one dare not act or rest freely. Instead, one’s every movement and stillness must correspond with the True Person within the Spirit-Chamber—when it moves, one moves with it; when it is still, one is still with it; when it rests, one rests with it. Without forcing or forgetting, continuously and subtly present, the firing process is thereby perfected. Thus it is said: *"In movement and stillness, rest and activity, it ever accompanies the person."*

This section describes the symbolic form of the furnace and crucible while also encompassing the function of nurturing and refinement. It embodies the operation of *"metal and *qi* mutually assisting one another"* as mentioned earlier. This chapter first reveals the aperture of being and non-being as the true furnace and crucible, then distinguishes the functions of the metal and water *qi* as the true medicine, and finally instructs on the nurturing, protective work of the true firing process. The essentials of the Golden Elixir are fully contained herein—one must not overlook them.

---

**Chapter Eight: Discerning the True and False**

The method of traversing the viscera judges right and wrong;
Inner contemplation gives rise to thoughts.
Treading the steps of the Dipper’s stars,
The Six Jia follow the days and hours.
The Yin path scorns the nine-and-one,
Defiling and disturbing the primordial womb.
Ingesting *qi* sets the intestines rumbling,
Exhaling the true, inhaling external evil.
Day and night without sleep,
From new moon to dark moon, never resting.
The body grows daily weary,
Bewildered as if in a daze.
The hundred vessels boil and surge,
No moment of clarity or tranquility.
Piling earth to erect altars,
Morning and evening offering reverence.
Ghosts and spirits manifest forms,
Haunting dreams with stirring omens.
The heart rejoices, the mind delights,
Self-assured of extended years—
Yet suddenly death cuts short the lifespan,
The corpse decays, the bones exposed.
Actions ever go awry,
Rebellious and contrary, the pivot is lost.
The arts are myriad,
A thousand strands, ten thousand more,
Advancing and retreating, they defy the Yellow and the Old,
Twisting and turning, they stray into the Nine Dark Realms.
The wise heed this meaning,
Vastly perceiving the source of error.

This chapter enumerates the errors of heterodox paths to distinguish true from false. To know the rightness of the Great Dao, one must first examine the wrongness of the side paths.

The methods are countless, their deviations too many to list; here, we briefly outline them:

*"The method of traversing the viscera judges right and wrong; inner contemplation gives rise to thoughts"*—this refers to the heterodox path of visualizing the Five Viscera through forced concentration.

*"Treading the steps of the Dipper’s stars, the Six Jia follow the days and hours"*—this refers to the heterodox path of pacing the Big Dipper’s stars and adhering to talismanic arts.

*"The Yin path scorns the nine-and-one, defiling and disturbing the primordial womb"*—this refers to the heterodox path of "nine shallow, one deep," practicing Yin absorption to supplement Yang.

*"Ingesting *qi* sets the intestines rumbling, exhaling the true, inhaling external evil"*—this refers to the heterodox path of swallowing external *qi* and expelling the old to take in the new.

*"Day and night without sleep, from new moon to dark moon, never resting"*—this refers to the heterodox path of moving essence and circulating *qi*, sitting constantly without lying down.

The above five heterodox paths all seek within the body. Through bizarre and forced practices, their harmful consequences ultimately lead to bodily exhaustion, mental confusion, and the hundred vessels surging uncontrollably, leaving no moment of clarity or tranquility. Such are the pernicious effects of seeking within the body.

*"Piling earth to erect altars, morning and evening offering reverence. Ghosts and spirits manifest forms, haunting dreams with stirring omens"*—this refers to the heterodox path of worshipping and refining ghosts and spirits, which appear in dreams and take shape.

*"The heart rejoices, the mind delights, self-assured of extended years—yet suddenly death cuts short the lifespan, the corpse decays, the bones exposed"*—this heterodox path seeks outside the body. Initially, one offers morning and evening reverence, vainly hoping for ghostly assistance to prolong life. Unaware, one becomes possessed by these spirits, falling into the dark arts of Yin demons. Eventually, one may suffer royal calamity or strange illness, seeking longevity only to meet an early death.

The exposed, decaying corpse becomes the mockery of the vulgar world. Those who seek outside the body meet with such dire consequences. The opening words "right and wrong" run through this entire chapter, declaring that the great Dao of the Golden Elixir lies wholly in nurturing one’s nature—it cannot be confused with these heterodox paths. The work of nurturing nature was already expounded in the two preceding chapters. The heterodox paths run counter to this, thus incurring all manner of calamities: every action and step goes awry, defying the pivot of the Dao and losing its fundamental mechanism.

The arts of these paths are myriad—thousands upon tens of thousands—yet all deviate from the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, twisting and turning against the Nine Capitals. The wise heed this meaning and, with clarity, discern the proper course. This passage concludes by exposing how heterodox paths betray the Dao.

The great Dao of the Golden Elixir is none other than nurturing one’s nature, rooted in the Yellow Emperor’s and Laozi’s principle of emptiness and natural spontaneity. Thus, the *Yinfu Jing* and *Daode Jing* directly point to the supreme vehicle of fully realizing nature and destiny, sharing the same pivotal mechanism as the *Yijing* composed by the Three Sages. Yet people, failing to awaken, often stray into heterodox paths. Their movements and choices diverge endlessly, reaching the height of perversity and losing entirely the pivot of the Dao.

The five or six methods listed above—whether sought within the body or beyond it—are but a brief illustration of one corner. Extending this principle, one may infer the myriad strands and countless variations. Generally speaking, if it is not the Yellow Emperor’s and Laozi’s practice of returning to life and reverting to the root, then it is not the profound mystery of the Yellow Emperor’s Nine Palaces and Grotto-Chambers. These practitioners willingly stray onto side paths, like walking backward in search of what lies ahead, expending effort in vain twists and turns. Those with discerning eyes should swiftly arouse faith, seek out a true teacher, and exhaustively investigate the original face of the root-source of nature and destiny. If one can, upon hearing even a single phrase, penetrate the essential teaching, only then will one recognize that there is but one level and unobstructed path, accessible to all. With further dedicated practice—diligently refining and sublimating—one may escape the side paths and transcend to the other shore.

The preceding section (*Lower Scroll, comprising seven chapters on "Subduing and Ingesting"*)—this chapter focuses specifically on "subduing and ingesting," though the governance of vitality and nurturing of nature are already implicit within it. The earlier chapters on governing vitality merely expounded the symbolic patterns of one yin and one yang, while the chapters on nurturing nature merely pointed to the fundamental reality of one nature and one destiny. As for the union of yin and yang, the merging of nature and destiny, there lies a wondrous function. This is why the practice of "subduing and ingesting" serves as the crucial mechanism of the Golden Elixir.

To "subdue" is to employ the mutual restraint of two substances for their function; to "ingest" is to merge two substances into one. It metaphorically uses common lead and mercury as analogies for the true seed of nature and destiny, and borrows the external imagery of the crucible and furnace to allude to the transformative dynamics of body and mind. Manifesting the formless through the formed—this is the essential teaching of "subduing and ingesting." It is not something that can be attained through the heterodox practices of burning reeds or playing with fire.

The medicine within the furnace must be refined with true fire, hence the later chapter speaks of "the affair of the furnace and fire."

**Chapter Nine: The Union of the Two Arcs**

The *Fire Records* are not groundless;
They elucidate the *Yijing* to clarify their meaning.
The crescent models the furnace and crucible,
The White Tiger serves as the pivot of refinement;
Mercury, the sun’s flowing pearl,
Accompanied by the Green Dragon.
Raising the East to meet the West,
Soul and spirit naturally constrain each other.

The upper arc corresponds to *dui*, numbered eight;
The lower arc to *gen*, also numbered eight.
When the essences of the two arcs unite,
The body of *Qian* and *Kun* is thereby formed.
Two eights make one *jin*—
The Dao of Change stands firm and unshaken.

This chapter directly points to the qi of the golden and watery arcs—first separated, then united—to reveal the true medicine. *"The Fire Records are not groundless; they elucidate the Yijing to clarify their meaning."* The crescent models the furnace and crucible; the White Tiger serves as the pivot of refinement. Mercury, the sun’s flowing pearl, is accompanied by the Green Dragon. Raising the East to meet the West, soul and spirit naturally constrain each other. This passage designates the true qi of the two arcs as the material for the Golden Elixir.

In the earlier chapters on nurturing nature, only the great Dao of emptiness and natural spontaneity was discussed, without mention of such terms as Dragon, Tiger, lead, or mercury. Only here are these names introduced. The critical operation at the furnace hinges entirely on the two substances of gold and water—termed the furnace and crucible, lead and mercury, Dragon and Tiger, the upper and lower arcs. These various metaphors all refer to the same thing.

It is commonly said that the ancient alchemical classics include *Six Hundred Fire Records*, and that Wei Boyang modeled his *Cantong Qi* upon them—this is not the case. The *Fire Records* never existed as a separate text; their content is already present in the primordial *Yijing* of Fu Xi. The sun and moon embody the *Yijing*, nothing more than one yin and one yang. In essence, they belong to *Qian* and *Kun*, while in function, they reside in *Kan* and *Li*. All other names derive from these, with *Qian* and *Kun* representing the symbolic forms of the furnace and crucible, and *Kan* and *Li* representing the symbolic forms of the medicine. The remaining sixty hexagrams and three hundred sixty lines embody the symbolic forms of the firing times. Each day corresponds to two hexagrams, and the cycles of a month precisely match the three hundred sixty degrees of the celestial sphere. When a month is further aligned with a year, it yields the *Six Hundred Fire Records*. Ultimately, it is still the sun and moon embodying the *Yijing*—one yin and one yang, nothing more. Hence it is said: *"The Fire Records are not groundless; they elucidate the Yijing to clarify their meaning."*

*Kan* is the true water of Great Yin, inherently the essence of the moon. Yet it must receive the mandate of the sun. At the conjunction of the dark and new moon, when yin reaches its extreme and turns to yang, the spirit is born within the soul, and true yang arises at the new moon. The fire’s power is still weak; by the third day at dusk, when the first yang stirs in the *geng* direction as *Zhen*, the crescent moon’s inverted arc forms the image of the crescent furnace. Within the water of *Kan*, the essence of metal is produced—this is what is meant by *"the Tiger is born from water."* The metal concealed in the furnace must be refined to emerge, forming the upper arc of *Dui*. Thus it is said: *"The crescent models the furnace and crucible; the White Tiger serves as the pivot of refinement."* Here, the furnace and crucible are invoked to encompass the medicine.

*Li* is the true fire of Great Yang, inherently the light of the sun. Yet it must unite with the moon. At the full moon’s opposition, when yang reaches its extreme and turns to yin, the soul generates the spirit, and true yin arises at the full moon. The watery qi remains concealed; by dawn on the sixteenth day, when the first yin descends in the *xin* direction as *Xun*, the moon’s fullness brims, taking the form of flowing pearls. Within the fire of *Li*, the liquid of wood is born—this is what is meant by *"the Dragon emerges from fire."* The nature of wood is to yield to metal, ever inclined to flow downward, forming the lower arc of *Gen*. Thus it is said: *"Mercury, the sun’s flowing pearl, accompanied by the Green Dragon."* Here, the medicine is invoked to encompass the furnace and crucible.

Thereupon, the Dragon of the East is driven to meet the Tiger of the West. The flowing pearl and golden essence, as nature and temperament harmonize, the earthly soul and heavenly spirit—metal and wood—naturally restrain each other. Thus it is said: *"Raising the East to meet the West, soul and spirit naturally constrain each other."* This speaks of the wondrous interplay of the two orifices, the mutual embrace of golden qi.

The upper arc of *Dui* is numbered eight; the lower arc of *Gen* is also numbered eight. When the essences of the two arcs unite, the body of *Qian* and *Kun* is thereby formed. Two eights make one *jin*—the Dao of Change stands firm and unshaken. This passage describes the union of the qi of the two arcs to form the Elixir.

From the single yang of *Zhen* and *Geng* rising as a crescent to the two yangs, it belongs to the upper arc of *Dui*. Its hexagram qi receives *Ding*. At this time, the fetal metal within water holds half the soul, and the soul within metal holds half the spirit—this is what is meant by "the upper arc’s metal, half a *jin*." If inverted, it may also be called "water, half a *jin*," hence it is said: *"The upper arc of Dui is numbered eight."*

From the single yin of *Xun* and *Xin* descending as a flowing pearl to the two yins, it belongs to the lower arc of *Gen*. Its hexagram qi receives *Bing*. At this time, the fetal water within metal holds half the soul, and the soul within water holds half the spirit—this is what is meant by "the lower arc’s water, half a *jin*." If inverted, it may also be called "metal, half a *jin*," hence it is said: *"The lower arc of Gen is also numbered eight."*

Previously, the two substances were taken to restrain each other, thus called metal and wood. Here, they are taken as one body generating the other, thus called metal and water. Their use is one and the same.

The body of *Dui* is originally pure *Qian*; with the top line changed to *Kun*’s single yin, it becomes the Youngest Daughter. The body of *Gen* is originally pure *Kun*; with the top line changed to *Qian*’s single yang, it becomes the Youngest Son. Now, when the two sides ascend and unite as one, pure metal returns to *Qian*’s nature, standing within to form the crucible’s enclosure, while pure water returns to *Kun*’s destiny, dwelling without to shape the womb. A single grain of the Golden Elixir is born within the Yellow Earth’s cauldron. Is this not *"When the essences of the two arcs unite, the body of Qian and Kun is thereby formed"*?

Know that at the time of the two arcs, the whole body is already present. Yet when the whole body is attained, the two arcs are no longer seen. The whole body’s union arises naturally, while the two arcs’ division holds wondrous use. This is what is meant by *"The moon’s fullness lies in the oral instruction."*

Once the two arcs unite, lead is but half a *jin*, and mercury only eight *liang*, precisely matching the weight of one *jin* of the Golden Elixir. The complete body of *Qian* and *Kun* is formed from the divided bodies of *Gen* and *Dui*, while the divided bodies of *Gen* and *Dui* emerge from the central bodies of *Kan* and *Li*. The bodies of *Kan* and *Li* are nothing more than the sun and moon. What was previously called *"the sun and moon as Change"* now attains balance, free from the peril of slanting or decline. Thus it is said: *"Two eights make one jin; the Dao of Change stands firm and unshaken."*

It is the use of the postcelestial two arcs to restore the body of the primordial *Qian* metal—this is the true function of the Golden Elixir. This is what is meant by *"elucidating through the Changes,"* the foremost principle of internal alchemy.

---

**Chapter Ten: Metal Returns to Its Nature**

When metal enters fierce fire,
Its color remains, its radiance undiminished.
Since the world’s opening,
The sun and moon have never lost their brightness.

Metal does not lose its weight,
The sun and moon retain their forms as ever.
Metal is born from the moon,
At dawn it receives the sun’s sign.

Metal returns to its mother,
At the moon’s wane, sun and moon embrace.
Concealing its frame,
It sinks into profound emptiness.

Metal regains its original nature,
Its majestic light blazes within the cauldron.

This chapter directly points to the primordial nature of metal as the foundation of the Elixir’s Dao. The previous chapter addressed the two arcs of metal and water as a dual method; here, the two are merged into one, directly revealing the root of metal’s nature, so that those who cultivate the Dao may know their destination.

Just as worldly true gold, when refined in fierce fire, naturally doubles in radiance without losing its luster, how much more so this original nature of metal, which belongs to the primordial *Qian*, born before heaven and earth, indestructible through ten thousand kalpas—who could steal its radiance? Thus it is said: *"When metal enters fierce fire, its color remains, its radiance undiminished."*

When chaos first split and heaven and earth opened, the single yang within *Qian* broke forth to become *Li*, while the single yin within *Kun* solidified to become *Kan*. *Kan* belongs to the Great Yin, its essence is metal. *Li* belongs to the Great Yang, its light is fire. When the true metal within *Kan* is tempered by the true fire within *Li*, its radiance naturally coalesces and does not scatter. Thus, the sun and moon unite as one body, their light enduring through ancient and modern times without cease. Hence it is said: *"Since the world’s opening, the sun and moon have never lost their brightness."*

When worldly true gold is refined in fierce fire multiple times, its weight never increases or decreases by even a hair’s breadth. How much more so the original nature of metal, which is neither deficient nor excessive? Though it undergoes myriad transformations within the great furnace of *Qian* and *Kun*, its weight remains utterly unchanged. Thus, since the existence of the sun and moon, though they rise and set, wax and wane, enduring countless eclipses, their perfect brightness persists through the ages—only because the nature of metal is indestructible. Therefore it is said: *"Metal does not lose its nature; the sun and moon retain their forms as ever."*

The radiance of metal is fundamentally one, yet the sun and moon each receive a portion. The sun’s light constantly governs external illumination, while the moon’s essence constantly governs internal concealment. Ultimately, the sun and moon are not two bodies, nor are their radiance and essence two things. Though the metal essence within *Kan* seems to reside in the moon, it truly receives its embryo from the sun. People only see that on the third evening, a single yang light suddenly appears from the *Geng* direction, as if metal is born from the moon. Yet they do not know this point of light originally comes from the sun. Only at the meeting of the dark and new moons, when the sun and moon conjoin, does the sun’s spirit reflect upon the moon’s soul, and conception occurs. When at dawn the first yang stirs, the moon’s soul traces back to the sun’s spirit to generate light—*Zhen* arrives to receive the sign. Thus it is said: *"Metal is born from the moon; at dawn it receives the sun’s sign."*

The metal essence within *Kan* originally separated from the *Qian* metal, hence *Qian* is its father. It was also produced from the *Kun* earth, hence *Kun* is its mother. When the moon is at its darkest, it unites with the sun, the two embracing each other. The sun is above, the moon below; the sun’s essence enters the moon, entirely gathered by the Great Yin. The moon’s light is enveloped within the sun, entirely absorbed by the Great Yang. Light is exhausted, the body concealed—utterly dark and without radiance. This is the image of *Kan* metal returning to *Kun* earth.

Thus it is said: *"Metal returns to its mother; at the moon’s darkest, sun and moon embrace."* When metal returns to its mother, the moon is enveloped by the sun, and the yang light retreats, concealing itself in the depths of the northern void—*Xu* and *Wei*—where a single speck of metal essence sinks into the vast emptiness of the North Pole’s Great Abyss. In the workings of creation, this is the union of sun and moon, the celestial mechanism halting its wheel. Within the self, it is the moment when spirit returns to the qi-cavern, the Great Medicine enters the furnace. Thus it is said: *"Conceal its frame, sink into the hollow void."*

Before long, yin reaches its extreme and yang is born anew; the nature of metal revives. At the *Geng* direction, a single yang stirs—within creation, this is the dawn of light; within the self, the Great Medicine is about to emerge, rising from the *Kun* furnace to ascend the *Qian* tripod. Only now does the true metal within *Kan* return to its origin, restoring the nature of its *Qian* father. Radiant, it becomes the elixir, its brilliance illuminating the Great Void. Is this not *"Metal regains its original nature; the tripod’s majestic light blazes forth"*?

This chapter directly points to the nature of metal as the root of creation and the foundation of life. The mystery of creation lies entirely in the *Hetu*: water leads the Five Phases, so Heavenly One generates water. Flowing downward, water generates wood, wood generates fire, fire generates earth, and only then does earth generate metal. Metal stands alone at the end, yet it gathers the qi of all Five Phases—thus creation regards metal as its consummation. Earth concludes the Five Phases, so Heavenly Five generates earth. Tracing upward, earth is generated by fire, fire by wood, wood by water, and water by metal. When metal revives, it opens the source of the Five Phases—thus creation also regards metal as its origin. This is the marvel of ending and beginning anew.

Within the self, metal corresponds to the primordial ancestral nature, which, before the union of father and mother, was as vast as the Great Void. After the fusion of essences, the Four Great Elements—earth, water, fire, wind—provisionally combine to form the illusory body. Only when a speck of true nature from the Great Void descends into it can life be established—thus the self regards metal as its origin. When the illusory body composed of the Four Great Elements ultimately perishes, this metal nature alone does not change with it, enduring through ten thousand kalpas—thus the self also regards metal as its consummation. This is the marvel of no beginning and no end.

Long ago, Fuxi composed the *Yijing*, splitting open the Great Ultimate and cleaving the heart of heaven. The first point to descend formed the *Qian* hexagram. *Qian* is heaven, and Confucius glossed it: *"All things rely on it for their beginning."* *Qian* is metal, and Confucius glossed it: *"Pure and refined."* This is the standard for fully realizing one’s nature and fulfilling destiny through the ages. Śākyamuni attained this and manifested a body sixteen feet tall, hence he is revered as the *Golden Immortal*. The Primordial Beginning attained this and coalesced a millet-sized pearl, hence it is treasured as the *Golden Elixir*. The roots of the Three Teachings are one in this metal nature; beyond this, one falls into byways and crooked paths. Those who study the Dao should ponder this deeply.

From the two words "metal nature," one may fathom where the sages of the Three Teachings stand—this truly exhausts the secrets of heaven. From this perspective, the *Cantong Qi* is a book everyone should read, at all times.

**Chapter Eleven: The Full Work of the Two Earths**

*Midnight and noon unite as three; Wu and Ji are called five.*
*Three and five harmonize—eight stones set the norm.*
*Breathing nurtures each other; stillness (thought) becomes husband and wife.*
*Yellow earth is metal’s father; flowing pearl is water’s child.*
*Water treats earth as its ghost—earth subdues water’s rise.*
*Vermilion Bird is fire’s essence, holding balance to adjust gain and loss.*
*When water prevails, fire is extinguished—both die and return to thick earth.*
*The three natures unite, sharing the same ancestral root.*
*Giant fennel still extends years; the returned elixir can be ingested.*
*Metal’s nature never decays—thus it is the treasure of all things.*
*Alchemists refine and consume it, gaining long life.*
*Earth wanders through the four seasons, guarding boundaries and setting rules.*
*Metal and cinnabar enter the five viscera, mist dispersing like wind and rain.*
*Steaming and permeating the four limbs, complexion becomes joyful and lustrous.*
*Hair turns from white to black; fallen teeth return to their place.*
*The old man regains youth; the aged woman becomes a maiden.*
*Transforming form to escape worldly calamities, one is called a True Person.*

This chapter reveals the work of the Two Earths in creation and elixir formation, showing the essentials of returning to the root.

*Midnight and noon unite as three; Wu and Ji are called five.*
*Three and five harmonize—eight stones set the norm.*
*Breathing nurtures each other; stillness (thought) becomes husband and wife.*

This passage speaks of the two functions of water and fire, which must return to the central earth. The marvelous workings of the elixir path are none other than water and fire; the marvelous workings of water and fire do not depart from *Wu* and *Ji*. Roughly speaking, to mention one is to include two; to mention two is to include three; to unite three is to return to one. Thus, when water and fire achieve balance, their function relies entirely on the true earth of the center.

Water belongs to the due north, the true midnight; within the self, it is the *Kan* *Wu* moon essence, born of Heavenly One, its number one. Fire belongs to the due south, the true noon; within the self, it is the *Li* *Ji* sun radiance, born of Earthly Two, its number two. When these two unite, they form the number three. *Kan* contains *Wu*, which is yang earth; *Li* contains *Ji*, which is yin earth. Within the self, this is the true intention of the central yellow. Earth is born of Heavenly Five, uniquely attaining the number five. Thus it is said: *"Midnight and noon unite as three; Wu and Ji are called five."* United, the three natures are complete.

Water and fire, being of different natures, cannot enter each other. Only by relying on the earth’s virtue of the center, which harmonizes in many ways, can they achieve mutual benefit. Thus, water (one) and fire (two), together with the central earth, form the Four Images, which double into the Eight Trigrams. The four cardinal and four intercardinal directions, arrayed in a circle, form the image of the eight stones.

Is this not the meaning of *"When the three and five harmonize, the eight stones establish the fundamental order"*?

External alchemy aligns the five metals with the Five Phases and the eight stones with the Eight Trigrams. When the elixir head arrives, the five metals and eight stones are all transmuted into true gold. Thus, the immortals borrow external images to illustrate internal work—one must not cling to the images or adhere rigidly to the text.

Once water and fire achieve mutual benefit, their union and separation give rise to the ebb and flow of breath. The exhalation reaches the root, the inhalation reaches the stem, all relying on the true earth of the central palace to contain and nurture them. This breath is not the qi of mouth and nose but the true breath. The true breath moves without interruption, uniting and separating within the central earth, no different from husband and wife in their pairing—this is the true embryo. When the true embryo of the central palace remains unmoving, the one water and one fire naturally breathe within it. Just as the true embryo of the great void remains unmoving, the sun and moon naturally breathe within it. Is this not the meaning of *"Breaths intermingle in nurturance, abiding as husband and wife"*?

This passage directly points to the true breath (original text reads "intention," likely an error) as the mother of the golden elixir. The *Nanhua Jing* states: *"The true person’s breath reaches the heels."* The *Xinyin Jing* states: *"Breath nurtures clarity."* The *Huangting Jing* states: *"Behind lies the secret gate, before the life door; sun emerges, moon enters—breath abides."* All speak of the true breath.

Here, the northern true *zi* is water, the southern true *wu* is fire—this refers to their fundamental nature. Later, the flowing pearl within *Li* is water, the golden essence within *Kan* is fire—this refers to their inverted mutual use.

Yellow earth is the father of metal; the flowing pearl is the child of water. Water treats earth as its ghost—earth subdues water so it does not rise. The Vermilion Bird is the essence of fire, holding balance to adjust victory and defeat. When water prevails, fire is extinguished; both perish and return to the fertile earth. When the three natures unite, their original nature shares a common ancestor. This verse speaks of the wondrous function of true earth, which unites the three families into one.

The two earths, *Wu* and *Ji*, belong respectively to water and fire. Within water and fire, metal and wood are concealed, yet they never depart from earth. From the moment of receiving qi at birth, the true yellow earth of the center is the source of the golden essence—this metal is originally the ancestral nature of the *Qian* family, the unmoving primordial spirit of the central palace. Only when *Qian*’s metal is broken and flows into *Kun* does it solidify into *Kan*. The golden essence within *Kan* belongs to *Wu* earth—this is the so-called golden flower. Since the golden essence within *Kan* is born of the *Qian* father, it is said: *"Yellow earth is the father of metal."* When *Qian*’s one yang enters *Kan* and is replaced by one yin, it breaks into *Li*—this is *Kun*’s true water transforming into the wooden liquid within *Li*, which belongs to *Ji* earth, the so-called flowing pearl of the great yang. Since the flowing pearl within *Li* emerges from the *Kun* mother, it is said: *"The flowing pearl is the child of water."* This speaks of the three natures following their natural order of generation.

When the three natures follow their natural order, they must arise from the earth of the central palace. When they oppose and restrain one another, they also begin with the earth of the central palace. Thus, the work of the elixir path lies entirely in the true intention. Where thoughts arise is the root of human life and death. Following them leads to endless cycles; reversing them halts the wheel of rebirth. One begins here and returns to the root here.

One must know that the true water within *Li* is called one nature, the true fire within *Kan* is called one nature, and the true earth of the center alone is called one nature. Before their return, they are forcibly divided into three natures; after their return, one realizes that the three natures were originally just one nature. At the very beginning, the Supreme Ultimate contained the three in unity, the undivided heart of heaven and earth.
