When the primordial chaos was split, water and fire divided into the upper and lower arcs, joining with the central earth to form the three families—this is the process of unity dividing. Later, the qi of the two arcs reunite, the two earths *Wu* and *Ji* dissolve into the center, and they return to a single ancestor. As Zhang Ziyang said: *"Pursue the two qi along the yellow path, unite the three natures in the original palace."* Thus it is said: *"When the three natures unite, their original nature shares a common ancestor."*

First, it speaks of husband and wife in terms of the union of two natures; then, it speaks of father and child in terms of their origin.

The investigation of the ancestral origin ultimately merges into a single nature. Husband and wife are likened to *Kan* and *Li*, father and mother to *Qian* and *Kun*—these form the Two Modes and Four Images. The ancestral origin is likened to the central primordial earth, which is the place of returning to the Supreme Ultimate, the wondrous point of regaining the root and restoring life. *"Sesame may prolong life, but the Returned Elixir can be ingested. The nature of gold does not decay, thus it is the treasure of all things. Alchemists refine and consume it, and their lifespan attains longevity."* Earth travels through the four seasons, guarding boundaries and establishing rules. Golden sand enters the five viscera, dispersing like mist in wind and rain. It steams and reaches the four limbs, brightening the complexion with radiant beauty. White hair turns black again, fallen teeth regrow in their original place. The old man reverts to a youth, the aged woman becomes a maiden. Transforming form to escape worldly calamities, one is called a *Perfected Person*. This passage speaks of the miraculous effects of *fushi* (refining and ingesting the elixir).

When the three natures unite, they form the Golden Elixir; when swallowed, it is called *fushi*. This is entirely distinct from the heterodox practices of consuming medicinal substances. Even common herbs like sesame can prolong life and extend years—how much more so the indestructible, adamantine nature of gold, which endures for ten thousand kalpas? Would it not be the supreme treasure among all things? If Daoist adepts can refine this primordial *qi*, how could their lifespan not be prolonged? *Wu* and *Ji* earths have no fixed position; they circulate through the four seasons. In the east, they become *Chen* earth; in the south, *Wei* earth; in the west, *Xu* earth; in the north, *Chou* earth. Wood, fire, metal, and water are all within the boundaries of earth. When forging the elixir, one relies on this earth to establish the foundation of the central palace; when refining the elixir, one still relies on this earth to stabilize the boundaries of the four directions. Thus it is said: *"Earth travels through the four seasons, guarding boundaries and establishing rules."*

Golden sand is the Returned Elixir, formed from the union of two substances. "Entering the five viscera" means entering the mouth, referring to the moment of ingestion—this is not the heterodox practice of consuming medicinal substances. From *"dispersing like mist in wind and rain"* onward, all describe the natural effects after refining the elixir. Once the elixir is swallowed, its numinous transformations are unfathomable. The eighty-four thousand pores of the body seem to billow with clouds and scatter like mist, as if a storm were suddenly upon them. The four limbs are naturally steamed, the complexion naturally radiant. White hair turns black, fallen teeth regrow. The old man becomes a youth again, the old woman a maiden. Neither the cycles of kalpas nor the constraints of creation can restrain them. Though the seas may turn to fields, I remain carefree and at ease—is it not fitting to *"call them Perfected Persons"*?

**Chapter Twelve: Uniting with Like Kind**

When lead powder is cast into fire, its color is destroyed and it returns to lead. When ice and snow meet warm broth, they dissolve into the Supreme Mystery. Gold takes cinnabar as its essence, harmonizing with mercury. Transformation arises from its authenticity; beginning and end follow one another. To become an immortal who refines and ingests the elixir, one must use substances of the same kind. Planting grain requires grain, hatching chicks requires eggs. Assisting nature with like kind makes things easily formed and refined. Can fish eyes be pearls? Can fleabane become catalpa? Like attracts like; disparate things cannot form treasures. Swallows do not hatch phoenixes, foxes and hares do not nurse horses. Water does not flow upward in flames, fire does not moisten downward. The world is full of scholars, talented and profound. Yet if they fail to encounter the right opportunity, they waste their fire and squander their resources. Relying on textual interpretation, they recklessly impose their own ideas. Without proper cause or connection, their measures lose control.

Pound and refine chalcanthite, mica, and alum-magnetite.
Sulfur burns the camphorwood, mercury and mud refine each other in flight.
Smelt the Five-Stone Bronze to serve as the pivot’s aid.
Mixed natures of different kinds—how could they dwell as one?
A thousand attempts must end in ten thousand failures; seeking cleverness only breeds folly.
From youth to white-haired age, halfway doubts arise like foxes.
Turning from the Dao to cling to a deluded path, stepping from the straight into crooked byways.
Peering through a tube yields no broad vision—how can one gauge what lies ahead?
Even luck brings no encounter; only the sage truly knows.
This chapter speaks of like kind following like, the only way to refine and ingest the elixir—external alchemy loses its truth.

Lead powder cast into fire, its color ruined, reverts to lead.
Ice and snow met with warm broth dissolve into the Great Mystery.
Gold takes cinnabar as its master, harmonizing with mercury.
Transformation arises from its truth, beginning and end naturally follow one another.
This passage rightly speaks of water and fire, like kind transforming to form the elixir.

What is like kind?
People know only that *Kan* is water, yet do not know that the single yang within *Kan* originally comes from the *Qian* household—it is precisely the true fire of the Great Yang.
Yang and yang are of like kind, thus the true fire within *Kan* ever strives to blaze upward and return to *Qian*.
People know only that *Li* is fire, yet do not know that the single yin within *Li* originally comes from the *Kun* palace—it is precisely the true water of the Great Yin.
Yin and yin are of like kind, thus the true water within *Li* ever strives to descend and return to *Kun*.
This is the meaning of the *Great Change*: “Water flows to the moist, fire moves to the dry.”
That which originates in heaven inclines upward; that which originates in earth inclines downward—each follows its own kind.

The Master first illustrates with worldly examples:
Lead powder, though refined from black lead, upon meeting fire immediately reverts to lead.
Ice and snow, though congealed from cold water, upon meeting broth instantly dissolve into water.
Thus fire returns to fire, water returns to water—original nature cannot be violated.
Those who refine the golden elixir take only the gold within water; the gold within water is the root of life, originally issuing from the *Qian* nature.
From *Qian* it breaks into *Li*; *Li* is the root of essence, containing true yin, receiving the fire-qi of the southern direction—this is the image of cinnabar.
The adept seeking to understand the lineage of life must take essence as the master.
Thus it is said: “Gold takes cinnabar as its master.”
Yet this cinnabar within *Li*, when met with fire, flies upward and is not easily subdued—it still relies on the gold within the northern water to restrain it.
The adept seeking to understand the lineage of essence must take life as the foundation.
Thus it is said: “Harmonizing with mercury.”
Know that cinnabar and mercury are originally one body, differing only in name.
At first, they transform from one body into two things; in the end, they must transform from two things back into one.
Only this true yin and true yang, of like kind, interact and mutually serve.
Thus it is said: “Transformation arises from its truth, beginning and end naturally follow one another.”
The method of transformation is nothing more than flowing *wu* to *ji*, inverting host and guest, so that the postnatal *Kan* and *Li* return to the prenatal *Qian* and *Kun*.
As Zhang Ziyang said: “Yin and yang, when of like kind, interact; the two eights, when matched, naturally unite”—this is the meaning.
To become an immortal who refines and ingests the elixir, one must use substances of the same kind.
Planting grain requires grain, hatching chicks requires eggs.
Assisting nature with like kind makes things easily formed and refined.
Can fish eyes be pearls? Can fleabane become catalpa?
Like attracts like; disparate things cannot form treasures.
Swallows do not hatch phoenixes, foxes and hares do not nurse horses.
Water does not flow upward in flames, fire does not moisten downward.
This passage provides further evidence for the principle of like kind.

The method of *fushi* (elixir refinement and ingestion) relies solely on cinnabar and mercury, transforming these two substances into the elixir. Gold restrains cinnabar—this is the meaning of *fu* (subduing). Swallowing it into the five viscera—this is the meaning of *shi* (ingestion). Without *fushi*, there is no way to become an immortal; without substances of the same kind, there is no way to refine and ingest them. Thus it is said: “To become an immortal who refines and ingests the elixir, one must use substances of the same kind.” These two lines are the key to the entire passage, and all the subsequent analogies and metaphors serve to elucidate the two characters *tonglei* (of like kind). Every sentient and insentient being in the world belongs to its own kind. When things of the same kind come together, it is like planting grain with grain or hatching chicks with eggs—their *qi* naturally assists one another, so that things are easily transformed and refined. If disparate things are forcibly combined, it is like fish eyes that cannot become pearls, fleabane that cannot become catalpa, swallows that cannot hatch phoenixes, or foxes and hares that cannot nurse horses. Their natures are entirely distinct, inevitably leading to discord and making it impossible to form treasures. Moreover, water by nature flows toward moisture and tends downward—once it flows, it cannot be forced upward. Fire by nature seeks dryness and tends upward—once it moves, it cannot be forced downward. This is why *Kan* and *Li* each follow their own kind, and why cinnabar and mercury transform into the elixir. This is the meaning of *fushi*.

Many scholars in the world possess lofty talent and refined skill. Yet if they fail to encounter the proper method, they waste their fire and squander their resources. Relying on texts alone, they arbitrarily impose their own interpretations. Without a clear thread of principle, their measures and efforts go astray.

Pound and refine chalcanthite, mica, and alum-magnetite;
Roast brimstone with camphorwood, blend clay with mercury and sublimate.
Smelt the Five Minerals into bronze, using them as auxiliary pivots.
Yet disparate natures reject kinship—how could they ever unite in one body?
A thousand attempts must end in ten thousand failures; seeking cleverness, one becomes a fool.
From youth to white-haired age, doubt arises midway.
Turning from the Dao to cling to deluded paths, one strays from the straight way into crooked byways.
Peering through a tube, one’s vision is too narrow to gauge what lies ahead.
Though one may hope for luck, the encounter never comes—only the sage truly knows.
This passage thoroughly exposes the fallacies of furnace-and-fire theories:
All that possesses form and substance is not the true essence of kindred nature.

To refine the Returned Elixir, one must gather the proper medicinal substances—
those of singular nature and life, inherently formless and wondrous in their primordial state,
metaphorically called "lead" and "mercury," yet utterly unlike common cinnabar or quicksilver.
To refine the Returned Elixir, one must establish the furnace and tripod,
place the medicinal substances within, and employ primordial true fire for forging—
metaphorically called "furnace and fire," yet utterly unlike burning thatch or playing with flames.
The work of the Returned Elixir relies entirely on the fire-phase for refinement,
followed by gentle nurturing, culminating in transformation.
When a single grain is perfected, it sheds its husk and enters the mouth—
metaphorically called "subdued ingestion," yet utterly unlike consuming metallic or mineral concoctions.

Yet the Great Dao of the Golden Elixir is transmitted but once in ten thousand kalpas,
and moreover, worldly beings, lacking sufficient fortune, seldom meet a true master.
Thus, many drift into false arts.
There are reckless men who, proud of their talents and erudition,
refuse to seek guidance from a true teacher, arbitrarily imposing their own interpretations
and misconstruing the alchemical classics.
They mistake common minerals for "lead" and "mercury,"
take mere smelting for "furnace and fire,"
and regard ingestion of substances as "subdued ingestion."
Ignorant of the proper thread and measure,
they amass the Five Metals and Eight Minerals,
indiscriminately employing the Three Yellows and Four Spirits.
Since these are not inherently kindred substances, how could they ever unite to form an elixir?
It is like mistaking a fish’s eye for a pearl or expecting a fox or hare to give birth to a horse.
Such false arts inevitably lead to ten thousand failures for every thousand attempts,
leaving one with no achievement even in old age.

Minor harms drain one’s wealth and resources, while greater ones cost body and life. Seemingly clever yet truly foolish, they cling to doubt yet embrace falsehood—persisting in delusion, turning from the Dao, straying from orthodoxy into heterodoxy. Unwilling to admit their own errors, they instead misdirect others onto wrong paths. With the narrow vision of "viewing the sky through a tube or measuring the sea with a gourd," they write books and establish doctrines, misleading future generations, blocking the paths of those to come, and blinding the eyes of later seekers. When examined through the Dharma-eye, not a single truth remains in their teachings. Yet such individuals remain oblivious, still hoping for a one-in-ten-thousand stroke of luck—how utterly absurd!

The first chapter reveals the true qi of the two celestial arcs; the second chapter uniquely uncovers the precelestial metal-nature; the third chapter then speaks of the convergence of the three natures. Only then does the elixir enter the mouth, the rank of true person is attained, and the essence of subdued ingestion is fully expounded without residue. Yet世人, deluded by the collateral arts of smelting and refinement, often falsely claim "subdued ingestion" while using substances that are not of the same kind. Thus, this chapter reiterates its warnings to shatter their illusions.

Master Lü’s admonitory verse declares:
*"Not realizing the elixir of return originally has no form,
They foolishly ingest metals and minerals instead!"*
Left implicit yet unspoken—does this not embody the immortal elder’s intent to dispel delusion?

**Chapter Thirteen: Following the Teachings of the Three Sages**

As for the three sages, they culminate in Fuxi, who first drew the eight trigrams, modeling them on Heaven and Earth. King Wen, of imperial lineage, structured their forms and composed the line statements. Confucius, the near-sage and hero, added the Ten Wings to assist them. These three lords were raised by Heaven, succeeding one another to govern their eras. Though their steps differed in excellence, their merit and virtue were not disparate. Their creations followed in succession, their calculations precise down to the smallest fraction. What has form is easily measured, but what leaves no trace is difficult to plan. Their deeds set models for emulation, and for the world they established these *Classic of Poetry* and *Book of Documents*. Possessing no innate faculty of foresight, they awakened through the guidance of teachers.

As if lifting the hem of a gauze curtain, eyes blazing as one ascends the high terrace. The *Fire Records* span six hundred chapters, yet their intent differs not at all. The texts expound with solemn gravity, yet the world fails to ponder deeply. Tracing their origins and currents, the hidden and the manifest originally dwell as one. I dare not speak lightly of this for the worthy, nor dare I take writing lightly. If I were to seal my tongue in silence, cutting off the Way would incur the crime of execution. Yet to inscribe my thoughts on bamboo and silk risks leaking the celestial tally. Wavering, I sigh repeatedly, bowing my head to stitch together this folly.

There are methods and measures to refinement, yet how could all be fully expounded? I shall briefly outline the essential framework, where branches and leaves appear in luxuriant profusion.

This chapter speaks of following the three sages’ *Changes* to elucidate the great Dao.

As for the three sages, they were none other than Fuxi, who first drew the eight trigrams, modeling them on Heaven and Earth. King Wen, of imperial lineage, structured their forms and composed the line statements. Confucius, the near-sage and hero, added the Ten Wings to assist them. These three lords were raised by Heaven, succeeding one another to govern their eras. Though their steps differed in excellence, their merit and virtue were not disparate. Their creations followed in succession, their calculations precise down to the smallest fraction. What has form is easily measured, but what leaves no trace is difficult to plan. Their deeds set models for emulation, and for the world they established this *Book of Changes*.

This passage speaks of the three sages’ creation of the *Changes* as the wellspring of the great Dao.

The body of the Dao is one with the Great Void, fundamentally without name or image. Before the remote antiquity, all was a chaotic undifferentiated whole—oblivious to the Dao, yet nothing was not the Dao! Only after the sages created the *Changes* was this chaos cleaved open, and all celestial mechanisms fully revealed.

The *Book of Changes* began with Fuxi’s drawing of the trigrams, King Wen’s composition of the line statements, and Confucius’ completion of the Ten Wings. Only after these three sages did the Dao of the *Changes* become complete. Fuxi, the sage who opened Heaven, held the cosmos in his grasp and the myriad transformations in his heart. At that time, he observed the heavens above and the earth below, exhaustively grasping the root of creation. Heaven did not withhold the Dao, and thus the Yellow River brought forth the *Chart*, and the Luo River the *Script*, serving as verification. From this, he clearly perceived that the root of creation was but the Supreme Ultimate, and the essence of the Supreme Ultimate was contained within the *River Chart* and *Luo Script*. Within the River and Luo, the Five is the Supreme Ultimate! From this, one yin and one yang divided to form the Two Modes, from the Two Modes arose the Four Images, and from the Four Images arose the Eight Trigrams.

Once the eight trigrams were drawn, their sequence was: Qian as one, Dui as two, Li as three, Zhen as four, Xun as five, Kan as six, Gen as seven, and Kun as eight. Qian marks the origin, Kun the culmination, the two encompassing yin and yang. Zhen is the root of Heaven, Xun the cavern of the Moon. From their midst, creation unfolds. Their positions are: Qian in the south, Kun in the north, Li in the east, Kan in the west, Dui in the southeast, Gen in the northwest, Xun in the southwest, and Zhen in the northeast. The pure and centered yin and yang occupy the four cardinal directions, while the mixed and peripheral occupy the four corners. Heaven is positioned above, Earth below, Qian and Kun fixing the positions of noon and midnight. The sun rises in the east, the moon in the west, Kan and Li arrayed at the gates of dawn and dusk. Mountains stabilize the northwest, marshes flow to the southeast, wind arises in the southwest, and thunder resounds in the northeast—all conforming to the natural patterns of creation.

When doubled into sixty-four hexagrams, their sequence and positions remain largely the same. Before the trigrams were drawn, the *Changes* resided in Heaven and Earth; once the trigrams were drawn, Heaven and Earth resided in the *Changes*. This is what is meant by “modeling them on Heaven and Earth.” This is the *Changes* of Fuxi’s Primordial Heaven.

The *Changes* of Primordial Heaven established its structure but did not exhaust its application. Later, the *Lianshan* began with Gen, and the *Guicang* with Kun. Though the *Changes* of the Xia and Shang each had their uses, their profound meanings were not yet manifest. By the transition from Shang to Zhou, King Wen endured imprisonment in Youli, his person experiencing hardship, and the great application appeared before him. He then reversed Fuxi’s configuration, inverting the transformative mechanisms of Qian and Kun. In their positions, Li fire moved from east to south, replacing Qian’s position, for Qian’s great application lies in Li, the image of turning toward the light. Kan water moved from west to north, replacing Kun’s position, for Kun’s great application lies in Kan, the place of hidden utility. Zhen wood, originally in the northeast, advanced to the east to replace Li, for wood and fire are companions. Dui metal, originally in the southeast, retreated to the west to replace Kan, for metal and water are allies. Qian the father was moved to the northwest, placed before Kan water, embodying Qian’s meaning of knowing the great beginning. Kun the mother was placed in the southwest, positioned behind Li fire, embodying Kun’s meaning of completing things. Gen came to the northeast, occupying the position of Zhen in Primordial Heaven, where the eldest son becomes the youngest, yang having advanced to its limit and then retreating. Xun went to the southeast, occupying the position of Dui in Primordial Heaven, where the youngest daughter becomes the eldest, yin having retreated to its limit and then advancing. The young and intermingling yin and yang occupy the four cardinal directions, while the old and unmingling occupy the four corners. The meaning is taken from *jiaoyi* (exchange) as application. The sequence of the eight trigrams thus begins with “The Lord emerges from Zhen” and ends with “Completion is spoken in Gen,” cycling endlessly, beginning and ending the myriad things. The meaning is taken from *bianyi* (transformation) as application. The sequence of the sixty-four hexagrams begins with Qian and Kun, centers on Kan and Li, and concludes with *Jiji* and *Weiji*. The meaning is taken from *fandui* (opposition) as application.

Once the positions were altered, the images were appended with statements. Those attached to the hexagrams are called *tuan* statements, such as “originating, prospering, benefiting, and persevering.” Those attached to each line are called *yao* statements, such as “the hidden dragon does not act.” The images and prognostications are brilliantly complete—this is what is meant by “structuring their forms and composing the line statements.” This is the *Changes* of the Later Heaven of the Zhou.

Confucius, born after the sages, developed a fondness for the *Changes* in his later years, wearing out the leather bindings of his bamboo scrolls three times. Its meanings grew ever more refined.

The Ten Wings were composed to serve as the feathers and wings of the sacred classic. The *Xiang*, *Tuan*, and *Wenyan* commentaries exclusively expound the line statements of King Wen’s Later Heaven arrangement, while the *Xici*, *Shuogua* also elucidate the images of Fu Xi’s Earlier Heaven. The *Xugua* and *Zagua* reveal the marvels of interconnected resonance and the dynamic mechanism of opposition. Within them lie the profound words of "fulfilling one’s nature to reach destiny," the arcane principles of "exhausting the divine and knowing transformation"—nothing is left unaddressed. This is what is meant by "the Ten Wings to assist it." They guide people from the Later Heaven back to the Earlier Heaven, and thus the Dao of the *Changes* attains its fullest completion.

The Three Sages were all endowed by Heaven, arising in succession to illuminate the great Dao. Though their compositions and transmissions followed in sequence, their merit and virtue admit no hierarchy. Fu Xi’s *Changes* drew from the workings of creation; King Wen’s *Changes* drew from Fu Xi; Confucius’s *Changes* drew from both Fu Xi and King Wen. Whether composing or transmitting, they stemmed from a single source, their distinctions finer than a hair’s breadth. The formless pertains to the Dao of the metaphysical—this is the Supreme Ultimate. The formed pertains to the implements of the physical—these are the hexagrams, lines, images, and numbers. The metaphysical Dao is difficult to fathom, while the physical implements are easier to ponder. Thus, by drawing the hexagrams, appending the line statements, and composing the Wings, the Dao of the interplay of yin and yang is fittingly embodied within.

The Three Sages established this book of the *Changes* precisely so that generations of Dao-seeking scholars might model themselves upon its images. Those without prior understanding rely on teachers to awaken. It is as if lifting the curtain to gaze from a high terrace—bright and clear. The *Six Hundred Fire Records* share the same aim, yet their texts are solemnly expounded, while the world fails to reflect deeply upon them.

Tracing its source and flow, the hidden and the manifest originally dwell as one. How dare I, in addressing the worthy, treat the writing of this lightly? Yet if I seal my tongue in silence, I would sever the Way and incur condemnation. To commit my thoughts to bamboo and silk, I fear, would leak the celestial tally. Wavering, I sigh; bowing and raising my head, I string together these foolish words.

Refinement has its methods—how could I exhaustively set them forth? I shall briefly outline its warp and woof, that its branches and leaves may appear in luxuriant array. This passage speaks of modeling the *Can Tong Qi* upon the *Changes*, directly recounting its source and flow.

The Wei Lord himself declared that the Great Dao is not transmitted without a true teacher. Even the heaven-endowed Three Sages, in their compositions, followed in the footsteps of others—how could I, lacking any prior endowment of knowledge, presume to advance the Great Dao on my own? Fortunate to have encountered a true teacher and awakened, my long-standing obstructions dissolved, my doubts melted like ice, and my eyes became luminously clear, as if lifting the curtain to ascend a high terrace—how joyous!

The *Changes* contains sixty-four hexagrams. Excluding Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li, which correspond to the furnace, tripod, and medicinal substances, the remaining hexagrams proceed at two per day—Zhun in the morning and Meng in the evening. A month of thirty days accords with sixty hexagrams; ten months of three hundred days accord with six hundred hexagrams. Ultimately, the years are gathered into months, the months into days, the days into hours—all the work of firing and timing resides in a single moment. Though the text is solemnly expounded, its purport is no different; there are not truly six hundred *Fire Records*.

Alas, the world fails to study deeply or ponder the source and flow. If one could but investigate, this single Kan and Li—dark moon and bright sun, kindred dwelling together, sun and moon as the *Changes*, penetrating day and night—would be the supreme, most wondrous Dao. Now that I have awakened through my teacher, I see clearly that the Dao lies before me. I may only discuss it with one or two worthy ones; I dare not lightly commit it to writing. Yet to seal my mouth and bind my tongue would truly risk defying the heavenly Dao and incurring reproach. To fully inscribe it on bamboo and silk, I fear, would leak the celestial treasure and invite transgression.

Caught between advance and retreat, wavering and bowing, I can only borrow the images of the *Changes* to convey the formless workings of the Great Elixir. After exhausting all refinement, I roughly set forth the warp: the tripod, the medicinal substances, and the broad outlines. I gather the firing and tempering, faintly revealing the branches. This is to aid later learners in grasping the meaning and forgetting the words. For writing does not exhaust speech, nor speech exhaust meaning.

The *Can Tong Qi* of the Immortal Elder truly aligns with the Three Sages’ *Changes* in its aim to fully realize nature, reach destiny, and fathom the divine and comprehend transformation—as if tallying halves. Those in the world who are destined to meet a teacher will, upon encountering this single verification, attain clarity!

**Chapter Fourteen: The Law and Image of the Returned Elixir**

With metal as the embankment, water enters and flows freely.
The number of metal is fifteen; the number of water is the same.
Before the furnace, fix the weights and measures—
five parts water, with a surplus.
Take these two as the true,
the metal’s weight as in its origin.
The earth (three) then does not depart (enters),
the two (fire) accompanying it.
The three substances mutually contain and receive,
their transformations appearing divine.
Below lies the qi of Grand Yang,
steaming and seething in an instant.
First liquid, then congealing,
it is called the Yellow Chariot.
When years and months draw to an end,
one’s nature is destroyed, and lifespan harmed.
The body becomes like ash and earth,
its form like dust in a bright window.
Pound, refine, and combine them,
then hold and enter the vermilion gate.
Securely seal their junctures,
striving to make them whole and firm.

Flames blaze below,
the Dragon and Tiger’s cries ceaseless day and night.
At first, the civil is cultivated;
in the end, the martial is accomplished.
Observe with utmost care,
scrutinizing the balance of cold and warmth.
Cycle through the twelve nodes,
and when each is complete, draw closer still.
Qi wanes, life nears its end,
body dies, and soul departs.
The hue shifts to purple,
brilliant—this is called the Returned Elixir.
Ground to a single pellet,
the spatula’s touch is most divine.

This passage fully presents the symbolic method of the Returned Elixir,
serving as the standard for entering the chamber.

Use metal as the embankment,
so water may enter with ease.
The metal’s number is fifteen,
the number of water is the same.
Before the furnace, fix the weights and measures—
five parts water, with a surplus.
Take these two as the true,
the metal’s weight as in its origin.
The earth then does not depart,
the two accompanying it.

This section speaks of the two qi of metal and water as the true seed of the Golden Elixir.
Indeed, the wondrous work of the Returned Elixir, from beginning to end,
lies solely in these two—metal and water.
When established, they become the furnace and cauldron;
when gathered, they become the medicine;
when refined, they become the fire-timing.
Even in withdrawal, addition, and transformation,
all operations depend on them.
Yet the Daoist adept must know the order of precedence—
without first establishing the embankment, one cannot employ metal and water.

The metal within Kan (Water) is originally concealed within,
yet what is within must emerge.
In the work of the Golden Elixir, one must first establish the embankment,
firmly sealing the six gates so the primordial qi does not leak outward.
The water within Li (Fire) easily overflows without,
yet what is without must enter.
Once the embankment is set, overflow is forbidden,
true essence no longer escapes, and naturally flows into the furnace with ease.
Thus it is said: *"Use metal as the embankment, so water may enter with ease."*

Within the two substances of metal and water lie the two earths, Wu and Ji.
The generating number of earth is five, its completing number ten.
The metal within Kan embraces Wu—this is its five of the ten.
The water within Li embraces Ji—this is also its five of the ten.
The two earths combine to form the *gui*,
the qi of the two phases perfectly rounding and fulfilling.
Thus it is said: *"The metal’s number is fifteen, the number of water is the same."*

Once the embankment is set, one may proceed to the furnace.
In the furnace’s pairing, it is still the two—metal and water—
but their weights and measures must not err by even a hair.
True water and true metal must be perfectly balanced,
neither in excess nor deficient.
Thus water is limited to five parts,
guarding against overflow—it must not be excessive.
Metal must also be five parts,
its weight as in its original measure—it must not be deficient.
When metal and water attain their truth,
true earth naturally harmonizes between them.
For within Li, Ji is embraced—its five parts of water are Ji earth;
within Kan, Wu is embraced—its five parts of metal are Wu earth.
Thus, in the two substances of metal and water, true earth is already present.
When Wu and Ji earths converge at the center,
they too attain their five-part base number,
the three families meeting to complete the three and five.
Thus it is said: *"The earth then does not depart, the two accompanying it."*

The meaning of the three and five derives from the *Hetu*:
east three and south two, wood and fire as companions;
north one and west four, metal and water as friends.
Here, only metal and water are mentioned, not wood and fire,
for metal and water are essence and spirit, like a person’s form.
Wood and fire are spirit and soul, like a person’s shadow.
When the form moves, the shadow follows—never apart, step by step.
So too are wood and fire with metal and water.
When essence and spirit unite and transform,
spirit and soul likewise attain wondrous union.
Such is the marvel of the Golden Elixir’s creation.

The three substances mutually contain and receive,
their transformations appearing divine.
Below lies the qi of Grand Yang,
steaming and seething in an instant.
First liquid, then congealing,
it is called the Yellow Chariot.
When years and months draw to an end,
one’s nature is destroyed, and lifespan harmed.
The body becomes like ash and earth,
its form like dust in a bright window.

This section describes the union of Kan and Li, the symbolic method of the Golden Elixir.
The qi of the two phases—metal and water—are nurtured by true earth,
becoming the three substances of one family.
Within them, transformations arise,
their divine workings unfathomable.
Once the embankments fore and aft are firmly set,
allowing no leakage, the true qi in the furnace naturally stirs.
Then the yang within Kan is drawn to fill the yin within Li.
The true fire of Grand Yang in the Northern Sea steams upward,
and in an instant, the true water of Li Palace responds,
first turning to white liquid, then congealing to utmost firmness.
The two unite in the Yellow Chamber, revolving without cease,
forming the image of the Yellow Chariot.
As it is said: *"The Infant and the Maiden emerge together,
only to be led into the chamber by the Yellow Dame."*

Yet before these two unite, one must guide them with true intent;
after their union, one must guard them with true intent.
A speck of yang qi gathers into thick earth,
life’s mechanism turning to destruction—
like the depths of winter, when all things wither and return to the root.
Thus it is said: *"When years and months draw to an end, one’s nature is destroyed, and lifespan harmed."*
At first, spirit enters qi, motionless and still,
like withered wood or dead ashes.
In time, life’s mechanism stirs again,
a speck of true qi faint and indistinct, rising like mist—
like wild horses or dust motes.
Thus it is said: *"The body becomes like ash and earth, its form like dust in a bright window."*
This is the symbolic image of Kan and Li’s first union,
the Great Medicine about to be born.

Pound, refine, and combine them,
then hold and enter the vermilion gate.
Securely seal their junctures,
striving to make them whole and firm.
Flames blaze below,
the Dragon and Tiger’s cries ceaseless.

At first, the civil fire enables refinement;
in the end, the martial fire achieves completion.
Observe with utmost care,
examining closely to regulate warmth and coolness.
Cycle through the twelve nodes,
and when each node concludes, attend to it anew.
When qi is exhausted and life nears its end,
the body dies, and the hun and po dissolve.
Its hue shifts to deepest purple—
brilliant, it is called the Returned Elixir.
Ground to a single pellet,
the *daogui* is most divine.

This passage describes the symbolic image of Qian and Kun’s union,
the method of the Returned Elixir.
Once Kan and Li have conjoined within the Yellow Chamber,
the two substances are fused and refined into one,
nurtured within the Kun furnace.
When the time arrives, the Great Medicine is born—
what is called *"the lead of the Water Realm, but a single flavor."*
Once the Great Medicine emerges, hasten to gather it,
using true intent as the medium,
revolving the wind to blend them,
slowly ascending from the Kun furnace into the Qian tripod,
where it condenses into the Elixir.
Thus it is said: *"Pound, refine, and combine them,
then hold and enter the vermilion gate."*
These two lines encompass countless acts of absorption, licking, gathering, and sealing.
The vermilion gate is the Crimson Palace, the Qian tripod.
Once the medicine ascends into the tripod,
it gradually congeals and coalesces,
then is slowly drawn downward from the Qian tripod
and returned to the Yellow Court.
At this moment, employ the method of firm sealing,
deepening and securing it further,
until emptiness reaches its utmost and stillness its fullness—
only then will the single speck of true yang qi not leak away.
Thus it is said: *"Securely seal their junctures,
striving to make them whole and firm."*
When sealing is absolute,
a single yang stirs beneath the Nine Earths,
bursting forth like a raging fire breaking through a barrier.
At the precise midnight hour, swiftly apply the true fire in response.
In an instant, Qian and Kun open and close,
Dragon and Tiger contend,
and the cries of dragon and tiger resound.
Thus it is said: *"Flames blaze below,
the Dragon and Tiger’s cries ceaseless."*

When the Great Medicine first arises,
use the civil fire to nurture it,
so it may ascend and emerge from the furnace.
Once the Great Medicine is born,
use the martial fire to forge it,
so it may coalesce into a treasure and return to the tripod.
Thus it is said: *"At first, the civil fire enables refinement;
in the end, the martial fire achieves completion."*
In this process, the firing times must not err by even a hair’s breadth.
If civil fire is misapplied with excess vigor, the flames will burn too fiercely.
If martial fire is misapplied with insufficient strength, the flames will grow too cold.
One must gauge the proper balance of gentleness and intensity,
adjusting the rhythm of warmth and coolness,
to achieve the mean.
Thus it is said: *"Observe with utmost care,
examining closely to regulate warmth and coolness."*

At midnight, kindle the fire from the *weilu*,
responding to the first stirring of yang in the *Fu* hexagram—
this is the Root of Heaven.
Continue until the six yang lines fill Qian,
where motion reaches its peak and reverts to stillness.
At noon, withdraw the fire from the *niwan*,
responding to the first stillness of yin in the *Gou* hexagram—
this is the Grotto of the Moon.
Continue until the six yin lines fill Kun,
where stillness reaches its peak and reverts to motion.
Thus it is said: *"Cycle through the twelve nodes,
and when each node concludes, attend to it anew."*
This is the symbolic image of the great union of Qian and Kun.
Motion and stillness generate each other in endless cycles.
Refine ceaselessly, day by day drawing forth lead and adding mercury.
Over time, lead is exhausted and mercury dries,
yin wanes as yang waxes,
until conditioned nature is transformed into true nature,
and the spirit of consciousness into the primordial spirit.
When the dregs of yin are fully purged,
the breath of the corpse vanishes and the root of life severs;
when the yang spirit takes form,
the mortal body dies and the hun and po dissolve.
Thus it is said: *"When qi is exhausted and life nears its end,
the body dies, and the hun and po dissolve."*
This is the moment Guan Yinzi spoke of: *"When not a single breath remains,
the Dao will come to meet you."*

With prolonged refinement,
after extinction comes revival,
the heart dies and the spirit lives,
and the Elixir within the tripod becomes full and luminous,
filling the great void.
Is this not *"Its hue shifts to deepest purple—
brilliant, it is called the Returned Elixir"*?
The Golden Elixir originates from the Qian household,
and returns to Qian—hence it is called the Returned Elixir.
Its color turns purple through the forging of water and fire qi.
The Returned Elixir has qi but no form,
no different from a pellet of powder or a *daogui* of seven parts—
yet its transformations are as divine as this.
From here, one sheds the womb and changes the tripod,
recreating Qian and Kun.
The child gives birth to grandchildren,
transformations beyond measure.
Beyond this point, none can know what lies ahead.
Is this not *"Ground to a single pellet,
the *daogui* is most divine"*?
The *dao* is the metal within water;
the *gui* is the two earths of *wu* and *ji*.
Thus, from beginning to end,
it is only through the transformations of metal, water, and earth
that the Returned Elixir is formed.
As Master Cui’s *Mirror for Taking the Medicine* says:
*"Drink the *daogui*, glimpse the workings of heaven."*
And Lüzu’s *Spring in Qin Garden*:
*"At that time, I drank the *daogui* myself—
who would believe a child was born from nothing?"*
Their meanings are much the same.

This chapter fully reveals the symbolic image of the Returned Elixir,
a pivotal passage in the section on internal alchemy.
First, it speaks of the union of the two substances,
where qi is subdued in the Kun furnace to produce the medicine.
Then, it speaks of the first stirring of yang,
where the spirit is congealed in the Qian tripod to form the Elixir.
The first two sections describe the workings of the Golden Elixir,
while the latter section describes the workings of the Returned Elixir.
As *Mirror for Taking the Medicine* says:
*"Born in Kun, planted in Qian."*
And *Awakening to Reality*:
*"Relying on Kun’s position to form the body,
planted in Qian’s Palace of Mutual Response."*
Both draw from this chapter.
*Chapter Fifteen: The Meaning of the Returned Elixir.*

Expounding the numbers of the Five Phases, it is concise yet not cumbersome.
Raising water to douse fire, suddenly extinguishes its radiance.
Sun and moon mutually eclipse,
ever at the juncture of the dark and new moon.
When water prevails, Kan encroaches upon yang;
when fire wanes, Li dims the day.
Yin and yang consume one another,
their interaction the natural Way.

Names fix their essence,
characters speak of their nature.
When metal returns to the origin of nature,
only then is it called the Returned Elixir.
I dare not speak idly,
but emulate the writings of the sages.
Ancient records depict the Dragon and Tiger,
the Yellow Emperor praised Golden Flowers.
Huainan refined Autumn Stone,
Yuyang added the Yellow Sprout.
The worthy may uphold and practice this,
the unworthy must not partake.
The Dao of past and present is one,
our discourse reveals its counsel.
Scholars, exert yourselves further,
reflect deeply and ponder.
The essential words are fully revealed,
lucid and never deceiving.

This chapter concludes that the meaning of the Returned Elixir lies solely in the nature and essence of water and fire.

Expounding the numbers of the Five Phases, it is concise yet not cumbersome.
Raising water to douse fire, suddenly extinguishes its radiance.
Sun and moon mutually eclipse,
ever at the juncture of the dark and new moon.
When water prevails, Kan encroaches upon yang;
when fire wanes, Li dims the day.
Yin and yang consume one another,
their interaction the natural Way.

This section speaks of the interaction of water and fire—though mutable, it does not lose its constancy.
The essence of the Elixir Way lies in nothing more than one water and one fire.
Water and fire originally emerge from a single source, later dividing into two substances.
Within Qian, a single yang flees into the Kun palace to form Kan, where the true fire of Great Yang resides.
Within Kun, a single yin turns into the Qian palace to form Li, where the true water of Great Yin resides.
The two qi of water and fire mutually conceal their roots, transforming endlessly, the Five Phases fully contained within.
Water generates wood, wood generates fire, fire generates earth, earth generates metal, and metal in turn generates water—
a leftward cycle of mutual generation, the forward sequence of the River Chart.
Fire overcomes metal, metal overcomes wood, wood overcomes earth, earth overcomes water, and water in turn overcomes fire—
a rightward cycle of mutual conquest, the reverse sequence of the Luo Book.
One forward, one reverse; one generation, one conquest—
the myriad transformations of the Five Phases never exceed this scope.
Thus it is said: *"Expounding the numbers of the Five Phases, it is concise yet not cumbersome."*

Heavenly One generates water, water being true yang, yet it falls into the Great Yin of the north, so water is ascribed to yin.
Earthly Two generates fire, fire being true yin, yet it rises to the position of Great Yang in the south, so fire is ascribed to yang.
When yin prevails, it encroaches upon yang;
when water prevails, it can extinguish fire.
For the formless water and fire of the primordial state, their function is mutual support.
For the formed water and fire of the later state, their function is mutual conflict.
Thus it is said: *"Raising water to douse fire, suddenly extinguishes its radiance."*

The sun and moon in the heavens are the water and fire of the world.
The sun belongs to the essence of Great Yang fire, its light never waning.
The moon belongs to the essence of Great Yin water, borrowing the sun’s light.
At the juncture of the dark and new moon, the sun and moon converge along the ecliptic, called the conjunction.
Yet though they share the same longitude, they differ in latitude, so even at conjunction, the sun is not eclipsed.
If they share both longitude and latitude, the moon does not avoid the sun, and the yang light is obscured by the yin spirit—
thus solar eclipses always occur on the new moon.
Thus it is said: *"Sun and moon mutually eclipse, ever at the juncture of the dark and new moon."*

Human life aligns with the workings of creation as if tallying tokens.
People know only that Kan water is the moon, unaware that the single true water within Li is precisely the moon’s essence.
They know only that Li fire is the sun, unaware that the single true fire within Kan is precisely the sun’s light.
At the juncture of the dark and new moon, sun and moon unite, water and fire mutually conceal—
a single true fire of Great Yang sinks to the depths of the northern sea.
As Shaozi said: *"The sun enters the earth, the image of their union."*
In the Elixir Way, this is the meeting of Kan and Li, the moment when yang first stirs.
At this time, one should nurture and refine, the latent dragon must not be lightly employed—
only when the yang light emerges above the earth does it shine brilliantly at midday.
If true yang cannot take command and remains trapped within yin, unable to emerge from the furnace,
this is the image of the northern cold water overwhelming and extinguishing the Great Yang.
Once true fire is submerged by cold water, the sun’s light is heavily obscured by yin—
even at the height of yang’s power at midday, it grows faint and dim, suddenly devoid of radiance.
Thus it is said: *"When water prevails, Kan encroaches upon yang; when fire wanes, Li dims the day."*

Kan resides in the northern, secluded palace, at the midnight position, the image of the new moon.
Li resides in the southern, luminous land, at the noon position, the image of the sun at day.
Only when water and fire are balanced do they interact harmoniously.
If one prevails, they eclipse and bring disaster.
The mutual eclipsing of sun and moon is the meaning of *"raising water to douse fire, suddenly extinguishes its radiance."*
This should be compared with the middle section: *"At the dark and new moon, they eclipse and obscure, overwhelming one another."*
Though this speaks only of their mutability,
if water does not prevail and fire does not wane,
the sun bestows virtue, the moon unfurls its light—
the natural nature and essence of water and fire are the constant Way of yin-yang interaction.
How could eclipses and disasters arise?
Thus it is said: *"Yin and yang consume one another, their interaction the natural Way."*

When sun and moon deviate from their constant Way, it is called eclipse.
When yin and yang follow their nature, it is called mutual nourishment.
Just as the union of the celestial sun and moon has constancy and change,
the interaction of Kan and Li within the body has success and failure—
one must be cautious and meticulous.

Names fix their essence,
characters speak of their nature.

When gold returns to its original nature, it may truly be called the Reverted Elixir.
This passage speaks of gold reverting to its nature—this is the ultimate meaning of the Reverted Elixir.
The primordial essence within Li is the true water of Greater Yin, also called "wood-fluid."
The primordial qi within Kan is the true fire of Greater Yang, also called "gold-essence."
In the Dao of the Elixir, water and fire serve as the body, gold and wood as the function.
Master Guan Yin said: *"Gold and wood are the interaction of water and fire."*
Though gold and wood are two distinct things, their root is but a single gold-nature.
This gold-nature originally emerges from the primordial Qian; before birth, it is pure and refined, indestructible through ten thousand kalpas.
Yet after birth, once the primordial chaos is broken, it enters the Kun palace—this is the gold-essence within Kan, where Qian’s nature transforms and is called "emotion."
The one yang of Qian having become Kan, the one yin of Kun enters within—this is the wood-fluid within Li, where Kun’s emotion transforms and is called "nature."
For wood governs tranquility, and thus it is termed "nature"—as it is said: *"Man is tranquil at birth; this is Heaven’s nature."*
Gold governs movement, and thus it is termed "emotion"—as it is said: *"Stirred by things, the nature’s desires move."*
Though the two emerge as different names, they are like a single person who has both a given name and a courtesy name: though name and courtesy name differ, nature and emotion remain one.
Thus it is said: *"Names fix their essence, characters speak of their nature."*

Originally, the gold within Qian transformed into Kan—this is nature turning into emotion.
Once this turning occurs, all things turn without cease; the cycle of rebirth and inversion lies before one’s eyes—this is the "forward path" that gives rise to humans and beings.
Now, if one retrieves the true gold within Kan and returns it to Qian, this is emotion reverting to nature.
Once this reversion occurs, all things revert without exception; firmness, wholeness, and constancy are suddenly attained, transcending all leakage—this is the "reverse path" that perfects sages and immortals.
If the practitioner of the Dao, after awakening to responsiveness, does not lose that initial state of serene stillness, the Elixir may then be reverted.
Thus it is said: *"When gold comes to return to its original nature, it may truly be called the Reverted Elixir."*
These two lines are not only the key to the entire *Cantong Qi*, but also thread through ten thousand scriptures and a thousand classics.
The *Śūraṅgama Sūtra* states: *"Like gold ore mixed with gold-essence, once the gold is pure, it no longer forms impurity."*
The *Sūtra of Perfect Enlightenment* states: *"Like smelting gold ore, the gold is not produced by smelting; once it has become gold, it does not revert to ore. Through endless time, the nature of gold remains uncorrupted."*
This is the same principle.
Lü Chunyang said: *"Gold, when it floats, reveals its nature; wood, when it sinks, reveals its heart."*
Zhang Ziyang said: *"The golden crucible seeks to retain mercury within cinnabar; the jade pool first receives silver within water."*
This, too, is the same principle.
Thus it is clear: the three treasuries of Buddhist scriptures expound only the two words "gold-nature," while the ten thousand volumes of elixir classics attest only to the two words "Reverted Elixir."
Moreover, when compared with the *Changes* of Fuxi, they align as perfectly as tallying tokens—one may suddenly awaken to this truth.

The symbolic imagery of the Reverted Elixir has already been set forth in the previous chapter; here, only its name and meaning are concluded.
I dare not speak idly, but follow the pattern of the sages’ writings.
Ancient records reveal the dragon and tiger; the Yellow Emperor praised golden blossoms.
Huainan refined autumn stone; Yuyang added the yellow sprout.
The worthy may uphold and practice this; the unworthy should not partake.
The Dao of past and present is one; in conversation, we expound our thoughts.
Let scholars strive earnestly, reflecting deeply and pondering.
The essential teaching is utterly plain, shining clearly and never deceiving me.

This passage speaks of the fundamental principle of the Reverted Elixir, truly transmitted from the ancient sages.
Since the beginning of creation, there has been only this single point of gold-nature.
Attaining this for self-cultivation, one transcends the ordinary and enters sagehood—it is precisely this.
Attaining this to save the world, writing books and establishing teachings—it is also this.

Across a thousand ages past and a thousand ages yet to come, whenever a sage arises, this heart and this principle remain unchanged—far beyond the reach of all empty rhetoric or twisted interpretations. Thus it is said: *"I dare not speak falsely, but emulate the writings of the sages."* The true gold-nature is originally nameless and wordless; ancient sages, in awakening later learners, forcibly assigned names and terms, which vary endlessly. The Way of the Reverted Elixir takes the qi of the two arcs, dragon and tiger, to pair and complete it—hence the ancient alchemical classics reveal the two entities, dragon and tiger. Thus it is said: *"Ancient records reveal dragon and tiger."*

Yet this is not all. Long ago, the Yellow Emperor refined the Reverted Elixir and praised it as "golden blossoms"; Huainan, upon completing the elixir, named it "autumn stone"; Yuyang, upon completion, called it "yellow sprout." Dragon and tiger symbolize one metal and one wood; golden blossoms symbolize metal within water; autumn stone, though originally black, turns white, also symbolizing metal within water; yellow sprout symbolizes metal within earth. In the end, though names differ, their nature and essence are one. This is what is meant by *"concise yet not cumbersome."*

For instance, in this text, terms like dragon and tiger, golden blossoms, and yellow sprout appear repeatedly—sometimes metaphorically representing two substances, sometimes the true seed—all derived from the transmitted writings since the Yellow Emperor’s time. How could these be mere empty rhetoric or twisted interpretations meant to mislead later learners? Yet this practice can only be upheld and pursued by the worthy; it must never be shared with the unworthy. Why? The worthy possess innate wisdom and clarity; with a single enlightening word from a true teacher, they immediately seek the primordial golden qi, refine the Reverted Elixir, and remain unswayed by distractions. The unworthy, dull and easily deluded, upon hearing of dragon and tiger, suspect it to be the external path of furnace and fire; upon hearing of golden blossoms or yellow sprout, they guess it involves the five metals or eight minerals; upon hearing of autumn stone, they think of refining and consuming urine. Mistaking the great Dao of the ancient sages, they stray into the heterodox arts of alchemical forging—how could such individuals be fit for this practice?

Yet all these varied names ultimately refer to nothing but the primordial gold-nature. There is only this one truth; anything beyond it is not genuine. After the ancient sages and worthies grasped the principle and mastered the practice, they wrote books and established teachings. Though each expressed it in their own way, when it came to the essential truth, they spoke as if face-to-face, revealing the most urgent and vital points without a single word of self-deception or deceit toward others. If scholars can seek out a true teacher, delve into the profound mysteries, and strive earnestly with deep reflection, comparing all with this text, they will find not even a hair’s breadth of discrepancy. Then they will realize that the great Way of the Reverted Elixir lies right before them, and the immortal elder has indeed not deceived them. Why hesitate to accept it directly, only to be led astray by doubts?

This is the concluding section of the "Subduing and Ingesting" chapter in the preceding text, specifically summarizing the two characters "Reverted Elixir." Thus Master Wei explains his purpose in writing and urges particular caution.

Yet a doubt arises: Master Wei states that the *Can Tong Qi* was composed by following the *Book of Changes* of the Three Sages, yet here he speaks of emulating the sages, specifically referring to those after the Yellow Emperor—this is the first point of confusion. Furthermore, it is commonly said that Master Wei did not know from whom he received his teachings but composed the *Can Tong Qi* by modeling it after the ancient *Dragon-Tiger Classic*—this is the second point of confusion.

I have privately examined the *Dragon-Tiger Classic* as transmitted in recent times, reading it repeatedly. Not only is its meaning shallow, incomparable to the *Can Tong Qi*, but its chapters and sentences are clearly imitative, even mimicking the tone and style—its path of plagiarism is glaringly obvious. This is likely the work of some meddlesome person who, seeing the line *"ancient records reveal dragon and tiger"* in this text and failing to understand its meaning, fabricated a false book to deceive the world and confuse the masses. Later scholars like Peng Xiao and Wang Dao, lacking discernment, were willingly deceived and even used this book as a basis for composing their own works—how grievously mistaken!

Since Wang and Peng set this precedent, later practitioners of furnace and fire have all revered the *Dragon-Tiger Classic* as their guide, forcibly linking it to this text in an unbreakable bond. As a result, the great Way of the Golden Elixir has degenerated into the heterodox arts of alchemical forging—truly lamentable. Then what does *"emulating the sages"* truly mean? It broadly refers to the various patriarchs since the Yellow Emperor, and *"emulating"* means tracing the origins of the various names like golden blossoms and yellow sprout. It does not specifically point to the *Dragon-Tiger Classic*. If it did, then where do names like golden blossoms and yellow sprout come from? Even if the *Dragon-Tiger Classic* did exist, it would have been an ancient text, vaguely transmitted in Master Wei’s time, but long since lost today—certainly not the spurious *Dragon-Tiger Classic* circulated in recent times.

Then how do we reconcile *"emulating the sages"* with *"following the Three Sages"*? The two are not contradictory. The various names in this text—dragon and tiger, golden blossoms—were passed down in writings since the Yellow Emperor, while the three symbolic methods of medicinal substances, furnace and cauldron, and firing times are unquestionably derived from the *Book of Changes* of the Three Sages. This is how *"governing and subduing"* and *"subduing and ingesting"* form an inner and outer correspondence. The reference to consulting alchemical classics is clearly evidenced in the concluding lines of the middle section: *"Only the ancient sages and worthies refined the nine cauldrons."* The origin from the *Book of Changes* is even more clearly evidenced in the concluding lines of the final section: *"The song recounts the great *Book of Changes*, the transmitted words of the Three Sages."*

The concluding sections of the latter two parts resonate with this chapter, and neither of those sections mentions the *Dragon-Tiger Classic* even once. Thus, it is beyond doubt that *"emulating the sages"* here is a general reference. I do not presume to offer arbitrary interpretations but merely follow what the immortal elder himself has revealed. This is a major unresolved question of ancient times; though my insights are limited, I humbly point it out. Whether others approve or blame me, how could I dare refuse?

The chapters on *fushi* (ingesting the elixir) in this section are even more profound than those in the previous two volumes. With this elucidation, they shine as brightly as the sun at midday.

As for the case of the *Dragon-Tiger Classic*—where the false has mingled with the true—no one has dared to speak of it for over a thousand years. Now that it has been thoroughly exposed, how utterly exhilarating!

**Middle Section of the *Cantong Qi* (Upper Volume, "Governing the Polity," four chapters—this being the foremost of the middle section).**
The first section’s fifteen chapters are divided into three volumes: governing the polity, nurturing nature, and ingesting the elixir, corresponding to the three essentials of medicinal substances, the crucible, and the firing process. The great Dao of the Golden Elixir is already fully expounded here, yet while it presents the overarching framework, there remain subtle operations that have not been exhaustively examined. Fearing that learners might fail to discern these and stray into divergent schools, this section is likewise divided into three volumes, dissecting the distinctions chapter by chapter, each part mirroring and complementing the first section.

Some modern scholars have divided the first section into the "Classic" and this section into its "Commentary," while others have separated the four-character lines as the "Classic" and the five-character lines as the "Commentary." They do not realize that only when the three sections are read as a continuous whole does the *Cantong Qi* form a complete work. A thousand years later, who can arbitrarily decree what is "Classic" and what is "Commentary," merely fragmenting its chapters and verses? All such claims are baseless conjectures, which I reject.

This volume focuses on governing the polity, though the nurturing of nature and the ingestion of the elixir are already implicit within it, its meaning aligning with the first section.

**Chapter Sixteen: The Four Symbols Revolving Within the Center**
*Qian* (Heaven) is firm, *Kun* (Earth) is yielding; they unite and encompass one another.
Yang bestows, yin receives; male and female depend on each other.
Through this, transformation unfolds, and essence and *qi* expand.
*Kan* (Water) and *Li* (Fire) crown the head, their radiance spreading forth.
Mysterious and obscure, beyond depiction in diagrams,
The sages measured and ordered them, arranging the primal foundations.
The four merge into chaos, directly entering the void of non-being.
The sixty hexagrams revolve, spread out like a chariot.
The dragon-horse is harnessed; the enlightened ruler governs the times.
In harmony, all follows; the path is level and not deviant.
Deviant paths are perilous and treacherous, toppling nations and families.

This chapter speaks of the self-transformation of *Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, and *Li*, illuminating the wondrous mystery of the primordial circle within.

*Qian* is firm, *Kun* is yielding; they unite and encompass one another.
Yang bestows, yin receives; male and female depend on each other.
Through this, transformation unfolds, and essence and *qi* expand.
This passage describes *Qian* and *Kun* as the essence of *Kan* and *Li*. For *Qian* and *Kun* are the gates of the *Book of Changes*, the true source from which *Kan* and *Li* emerge. The primal *Qian* is the beginning of Heaven and Earth; the primal *Kun* is the mother of the myriad things. *Qian* moves and is straight, its nature fundamentally firm, thus it initiates and embodies the Way of the Father. *Kun* is still and receptive, its nature fundamentally yielding, thus it nurtures and embodies the Way of the Mother. The two unite and contain all transformations. Hence it is said: *"Qian is firm, Kun is yielding; they unite and encompass one another."*

The father governs bestowal, capable of knowing the great beginning—this is what is meant by *"the male yang disseminates the primal and extends it."* The mother governs reception, capable of completing things—this is what is meant by *"the female yin transforms and envelops the yellow."* Hence it is said: *"Yang bestows, yin receives; male and female depend on each other."* Only when the two depend on each other can transformation be accomplished. *"Transformation"* (造) is the movement from non-being to being; *"change"* (化) is the movement from being to non-being. From non-being to being, true emptiness takes wondrous form—*Qian* conceals *Kun* within. From being to non-being, wondrous being returns to true emptiness—*Kun* conceals *Qian* within. *Qian* concealing *Kun* is the primordial unity of essence; *Kun* concealing *Qian* is the ancestral origin of *qi*. With host and guest inverted, the wonder of transformation is revealed. Hence it is said: *"Through this, transformation unfolds, and essence and qi expand."* This speaks of *Qian* and *Kun* uniting to generate the medicinal substances of *Kan* and *Li*, just as the *Book of Changes* says: *"Heaven and Earth intermingle, and the myriad things are transformed into harmony."*

*Kan* and *Li* crown the head, their radiance spreading forth.
Mysterious and obscure, beyond depiction in diagrams,
The sages measured and ordered them, arranging the primal foundations.
The four merge into chaos, directly entering the void of non-being.
This passage describes *Kan* and *Li* as the function of *Qian* and *Kun*. When *Qian* and *Kun* unite, *Kan* and *Li* form between them. *Li* is the essence of utmost yin; *Kan* is the *qi* of utmost yang. Though they arise after Heaven and Earth in the manifest world, they are born before Heaven and Earth in the primordial. In the workings of transformation, they become the solar spirit and lunar soul, their radiance illuminating all, capable of generating the myriad things. Within our bodies, they become the solar essence and lunar brilliance. When their radiance is gathered, it can produce the great elixir. Is this not what is meant by *"Kan and Li crown the head, their radiance spreading forth"*?

This primal essence and primal *qi*—ethereal and indistinct—are neither being nor non-being, usable yet invisible, difficult to fathom. How could they be transmitted through diagrams? It is entirely thanks to the sages who composed the *Book of Changes* that they were measured in many ways. Using *Qian* as the father and *Kun* as the mother, *Kan* as the son and *Li* as the daughter, the text aligns them with the crucible, the furnace, and the medicinal substances. There is nothing here but the arrangement of the foundations of primal transformation, enabling those who practice inner observation to know where to begin.

If cultivators of the Dao can model the crucible and furnace after *Qian* and *Kun*, and gather the medicinal substances by concentrating *Kan* and *Li*, then the solar essence and lunar radiance will naturally coalesce and revolve within the ancestral aperture, merging into chaos and returning to the primordial void of a single *qi*—the great elixir lies within. Hence it is said: *"The four merge into chaos, directly entering the void of non-being."* This speaks of *Kan* and *Li* uniting and returning to the ancestral aperture of *Qian* and *Kun*, just as the *Book of Changes* says: *"When male and female unite their essence, the myriad things are transformed and generated."*

The sixty hexagrams revolve, spread out like a chariot.
The dragon-horse is harnessed; the enlightened ruler governs the times.
In harmony, all follows; the path is level and not deviant.
Deviant paths are perilous and treacherous, toppling nations and families.
This passage describes the regulation of the firing process. Excluding the four hexagrams *Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, and *Li*—which correspond to the crucible and medicinal substances—the remaining sixty hexagrams are arranged in cyclical order, corresponding to the revolutions of the celestial sphere. In a single day, they align with *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you*; in a single month, with the new moon, full moon, and quarter phases; in a single year, with spring, summer, autumn, and winter. They circulate endlessly, forming the image of a chariot being spread out. Yet where there is a chariot, there must be a horse to harness it.

What is meant by "dragon-horse"? The dragon governs the heavens, mastering ascent and flight. The horse treads the earth, mastering regulation and submission. When compounding the elixir, spirit and qi guard one another, daring not to soar—thus the celestial yang dragon of governance transforms into the earthly kun horse of traversal. Step by step, it follows the compass and square, as a sovereign governs ministers, establishing the web of law and discipline, not daring the slightest laxity. Hence it is said: *"The dragon-horse is harnessed; the enlightened ruler governs the times."*

The method of driving a chariot aligns closely with that of governing the state: one must hold the six reins in hand, harmonizing and attuning them. The chariot follows the horse, the horse obeys the driver, advancing steadily along the grand thoroughfare. It is fitting to remain upright and level, not tilting or slanting. Should it tilt even slightly, peril and obstruction lie ahead, and overturning follows swiftly—just as a ruler who strays from the constant Way endangers the state.

In the Dao of the elixir, the body is the chariot, intention the horse, and the one who governs them is the heart-monarch. At the moment of gathering and union, one relies on the heart-monarch’s steadfastness to curb the waywardness of the intention-horse. The slightest negligence risks damaging one’s nature and harming the elixir—how can one not be vigilant?

In all cases, there is a single master: in the chariot, it is the driver; in governance, the enlightened ruler; in the heavens, the handle of the Dipper; in the Dao of the elixir, the celestial heart—all speak of holding the reins in hand. The passage in the previous chapter on governance, *"The essential Way and the handle of the Plough,"* conveys this same meaning.

The main purport of this chapter corresponds to the opening chapter of the preceding section. The passage on *"qian’s firmness and kun’s yielding"* echoes the discourse on *"the gates of qian and kun."* The passage on *"kan and li at the forefront"* mirrors the discussion of *"kan and li as the encompassing frame."* The passage on *"the sixty hexagrams in cyclical order"* aligns with the teaching on *"turning the hub and aligning the axle, governing the exterior from the center."* The rest may be inferred by analogy, though this is but a rough approximation.

*Chapter Seventeen: Movement and Stillness in Accord with the Times*

When the noble person abides in their chamber and utters words of goodness, they elicit response from a thousand *li* away. This refers to the lord of ten thousand chariots, dwelling in the nine-fold chamber, issuing decrees and commands in accord with the rhythms of yin and yang. Store the vessel and await the proper time; do not transgress the hexagram’s month. *Zhun* employs *zi* and *shen*; *Meng* employs *yin* and *xu*. The remaining sixty hexagrams each have their own days. Here I merely present two images, unable to exhaust their details. Establishing righteousness and enacting punishments, applying benevolence and bestowing virtue—those who defy them meet misfortune, while those who follow them attain good fortune. Follow the calendar and decrees with utmost sincerity and meticulous care. Diligently observe the celestial stems and branches, scrutinizing the waxing and waning. Even the slightest deviation brings regret and harm. At the solstices, the measurements shift; discord and crookedness arise. In the depths of winter, scorching heat; in the height of summer, frost and snow. At the equinoxes, alignment falters, not even a moment’s delay. Floods and droughts contend; wind and rain lose their rhythm. Locusts surge like boiling water; strange omens emerge on all sides. Heaven displays its portents: mountains collapse, the earth splits. The filial child’s devotion moves the imperial standard. Words uttered near at hand flow afar to distant lands. Some bring disaster, some bestow fortune; some usher in great peace, some incite war. These four outcomes arise from the heart’s intent. Movement and stillness follow constancy, adhering to their guiding line. The four seasons proceed in harmony, aligning with the *qi*. Hardness and softness are decisively divided, never encroaching upon one another. The Five Phases guard their boundaries, neither recklessly expanding nor contracting. The *Yi* flows in ceaseless circulation, bending and stretching, reversing and returning. This chapter speaks of the firing times in inner alchemy—every movement and stillness must not lose its proper season.

When the noble person abides in their chamber and utters words of goodness, they elicit response from a thousand *li* away. This refers to the lord of ten thousand chariots, dwelling in the nine-fold chamber, issuing decrees and commands in accord with the rhythms of yin and yang. Store the vessel and await the proper time; do not transgress the hexagram’s month. *Zhun* employs *zi* and *shen*; *Meng* employs *yin* and *xu*. The remaining sixty hexagrams each have their own days. Here I merely present two images, unable to exhaust their details. Establishing righteousness and enacting punishments, applying benevolence and bestowing virtue—those who defy them meet misfortune, while those who follow them attain good fortune. This passage speaks of movement and stillness not losing their proper time, serving as the standard for the firing times. The essential point of refining the elixir lies entirely in the celestial firing times, and the essence of the firing times lies entirely in every movement and stillness. The preceding chapter described the sixty hexagrams spreading out like a chariot’s canopy, revealing that the rhythm of the firing times shares the same pivotal mechanism as a ruler’s governance. The trigger of this mechanism must not be treated carelessly, even by a hair’s breadth. Thus, it is again likened to *"the noble person abiding in their chamber and uttering words of goodness, eliciting response from a thousand *li* away"*—this is the original text of the *Great Commentary on the Yi*. Master Wei further elucidates it, explaining that the lord of ten thousand chariots is the original Celestial Ruler, the nine-fold chamber is the inner chamber of the spirit, and the Celestial Ruler, dwelling in this secluded chamber, remains still and motionless, purifying the mind and withdrawing into concealment. When moved, it responds and communicates, issuing decrees and commands—all in accord with the rhythms of yin and yang, observing the Dao of Heaven and wielding the workings of Heaven.

When yang reaches its zenith and yin begins to arise, this is called the "Moon Grotto," its hexagram being *Gou* (姤), its month positioned at *wu* (午). When yin reaches its zenith and yang begins to arise, this is called the "Celestial Root," its hexagram being *Fu* (复), its month positioned at *zi* (子). If the time is not yet ripe, one must await it in stillness; if the time must not be missed, one must move to meet it. Thus it is said: *"Store the vessel and await the time; do not transgress the hexagram and month."*

When stillness reaches its extreme and movement begins, the myriad transformations sprout forth—this is the image of *Zhun* (屯). The inner body of *Zhun* receives *zi* (子), its outer body receives *shen* (申); water is born in *shen*, taking the meaning of sprouting life. Thus it is said: *"Zhun takes zi and shen."* This is what was referred to in the previous section as *"In spring and summer, rely on the inner body, from zi to chen and si."*

When movement reaches its extreme and stillness begins, the myriad transformations withdraw and conceal themselves—this is the image of *Meng* (蒙). The inner body of *Meng* receives *yin* (寅), its outer body receives *xu* (戌); the fire repository lies in *xu*, taking the meaning of concealment. Thus it is said: *"Meng uses yin and xu."* This is what was referred to in the previous section as *"In autumn and winter, employ the outer body, from wu to xu and hai."*

The two hexagrams alternate in reversal, one day and one night, thereby distinguishing the two solstices of winter and summer. The remaining sixty hexagrams each have their own day-night reversals, which one may extend and apply accordingly. Thus it is said: *"I merely present the two images; I cannot exhaust their details."*

Once the two solstices are established, the two quarters are divided between them. The upper quarter employs the spring equinox, fundamentally belonging to *mao* (卯) Wood, yet within its virtue lies punishment, reversing to the meaning of stern killing. Thus it is said: *"Establish righteousness and set punishment."* The lower quarter corresponds to the autumn equinox, fundamentally belonging to *you* (酉) Metal, yet within its punishment lies virtue, reversing to the meaning of gentle benevolence. Thus it is said: *"When facing benevolence, bestow virtue."* This is what was referred to in the previous section as *"Rewards and punishments correspond to spring and autumn,"* at the time of bathing.

The single cold and single heat of *zi* and *wu*, the single killing and single birth of *mao* and *you*—these are the great divisions of yin and yang, where not even a hair’s breadth may be misaligned. If one aligns with their rhythms, then the external fire and internal tally naturally resonate, just as the lord of ten thousand chariots, enthroned in the nine-fold chamber, issues a single decree and elicits response from a thousand *li* away. Otherwise, a thousand *li* away all will defy. Thus it is said: *"To go against it is ominous; to follow it is auspicious."*

According to the calendar and decrees, utmost sincerity and meticulous care must be observed. Diligently await the days and hours, scrutinizing the waxing and waning of cold and warmth. Even the slightest deviation brings regret and harm.
