If the two solstices alter their measures, leading to discord and distortion, then midwinter may become scorching summer, and midsummer may bring frost and snow. If the two equinoxes deviate, even by a moment’s error, water and drought will contend, wind and rain will fall out of season, locusts will surge like boiling water, and manifold anomalies will emerge. Heaven displays its portents: mountains collapse, the earth splits. The filial son, with devoted heart, moves the August Ultimate.

What issues from one’s own mouth may flow afar to distant lands, bringing either calamity or fortune, either ushering in great peace or inciting warfare. These four outcomes arise from the heart’s intentions. This passage precisely speaks of the rhythms of the firing process—going against them brings misfortune, while following them brings good fortune.

The single stillness and single movement of the firing process are like decrees that must not be transgressed. Those who cultivate the Dao need only follow and traverse them. In all advances and retreats, comings and goings at the boundaries of the two solstices and two equinoxes, the mind must be utterly sincere, the intent wholly focused. Diligently await the days and hours of ascent and descent, scrutinizing the waxing and waning of cold and warmth—this is what the *Mirror of Entering the Medicine* calls *"Only with utmost sincerity does one accord with the natural principle."*

If one slightly transgresses the decrees, even the slightest deviation, regret and harm will converge, damaging the elixir crucible. How can this be verified? Take, for example, the winter solstice, when the first yang arises: by principle, one should advance the fire, yet one must nurture the sprouting of the latent dragon, and the fire must not be excessively intense. At the summer solstice, when the first yin descends: by principle, one should withdraw the fire, yet one must guard against the gradual onset of frost, and the fire must not be excessively cold. If one acts perversely or distorts the proper measures, altering the constant degrees—applying excessive heat when it should not be hot—then midwinter reverts to scorching summer; applying excessive cold when it should not be cold—then midsummer reverts to frost and snow.

As for the spring and autumn equinoxes, yin and yang are evenly balanced, water and fire are in equilibrium. At this point, one must bathe, cleansing the mind and purifying thoughts, harmonizing and blending the center. Only then can the true *qi* within the crucible congeal. If the intent is not focused, scattering in all directions, then even a moment’s error will follow. If water is excessive, it becomes a flood;

If fire is excessive, it becomes a drought; and blind winds with strange rains fail to meet their proper seasons. Not only this—if the moment’s timing is amiss, then in minor cases, locusts arise at once, and the jade crucible and golden tripod boil over; in major cases, mountains and rivers split asunder, the Metal Tiger and Wood Dragon flee in chaos. All these are what is meant by “to go against it is inauspicious.”

This occurs because the Heart-Monarch has grown lax, and the Spirit-Chamber lacks a master, thus summoning calamities and transformations. If the Dao-practitioner can instead turn the light inward and guard it, never straying for even an instant—like a filial son serving his parents, seeing the formless and hearing the soundless—then with such focused intent, the Supreme Ultimate is naturally moved. The Supreme Ultimate is the true sovereign within Heaven, the primordial spirit of the Heavenly Valley within oneself. The precelestial primordial spirit is utterly still, inherently without coming or going, facing or turning away. But once a postcelestial thought stirs, good and ill fortune are immediately felt in response. It is like a filial son serving his parents: though their bodies may be apart, their moments are subtly connected; even a thousand *li* away, aches, itches, and pains are never unshared. Is this not what is meant by “near, it issues from one’s own mouth; far, it flows into distant lands”?

Thus, every moment aligns, and calamities do not intrude—this is what is meant by “to follow it is auspicious.” Here, we see only a single mechanism of resonance: whether one goes against it to summon misfortune or follows it to bring blessing; whether one remains composed to receive the felicity of great peace or acts restlessly to brew the disasters of war. The origins of good and ill fortune, regret and remorse—do they not all arise from the breast of the one who dwells within? For to go against it is inauspicious, to follow it is auspicious; auspiciousness and inauspiciousness stand opposed, with regret and remorse lying between them. Though there is but one auspicious outcome, three others—misfortune, regret, and remorse—dwell among them. How can one not be cautious?

Movement and stillness have their constancy; one must adhere to the guiding line. The four seasons follow their proper order, harmonizing with *qi*. Hard and soft are decisively distinguished, never encroaching upon one another. The Five Phases guard their boundaries, neither recklessly expanding nor contracting. The *Yi* flows in circulation, bending and stretching, reversing and repeating. This passage concludes by stating that movement and stillness have their fixed times, and one must not lose their standard.

For in the Dao of the Elixir, movement and stillness align with the workings of Creation: when movement reaches its extreme, it enters stillness, sinking into profound obscurity—here, one must empty oneself and await the moment. When stillness reaches its extreme, it emerges into movement, arising from the dim and indistinct—here, one must apply intent to gather and refine. If, when stillness is called for, one introduces movement, or when movement is called for, one introduces stillness, this is forced and artificial, losing the constant Way. Hence it is said, “Movement and stillness have their constancy; one must adhere to the guiding line.”

Once the constancy of movement and stillness is understood, at the two solstices, one should advance the fire and retreat the tally; at the two equinoxes, one should nurture with warmth and bathe. Each must be fitting, only then can one correspond with the proper *qi* of the four seasons. Hence it is said, “The four seasons follow their proper order, harmonizing with *qi*.”

Hardness pertains to the martial fire, softness to the civil fire. When body and mind have not yet merged, one must employ martial fire to temper them, never allowing the slightest softness to intrude. When spirit and *qi* have already harmonized, one must employ civil fire to secure and strengthen them, never allowing the slightest hardness to intrude. Hence it is said, “Hard and soft are decisively distinguished, never encroaching upon one another.”

The essentials of the Golden Elixir lie entirely in harmonizing the Four Images and gathering the Five Phases. The Four Images are arrayed in a circle, with the virtue of Earth at the center; east, west, south, and north each have their boundaries—one must neither exceed nor fall short. Hence it is said, “The Five Phases guard their boundaries, neither recklessly expanding nor contracting.”

Where there is the *qi* of yin and yang, there are the qualities of hard and soft; where there are the qualities of hard and soft, there are the times of movement and stillness—this is the true *Yi* within oneself. The true *Yi* flows throughout the body, bending and stretching, reversing and repeating, all in perfect accord. It is like a ruler issuing a decree, and a thousand *li* away, all respond. This chapter elaborates on the measures of the fire-times, mutually illuminating the earlier passages on the early and late stages of *Zhun* and *Meng*, and the cold and heat of spring and autumn.

The earlier passage presented the general outline, thus speaking only of attainment and not of loss; here, the details are expounded minutely, thus both attainment and loss are set forth, so that Dao-practitioners may know what to emulate and what to guard against.

**Chapter Eighteen: The Union of Kan and Li**

Between the dark and new moons, tally and action converge at the center. Primal chaos and vast obscurity—feminine and masculine follow one another. Nourishing fluids moisten and enrich, bestowing transformation and flowing in circulation. The numinous brightness of Heaven and Earth cannot be measured. Employ it to secure the body, concealing form and hiding away.

It begins in the northeast, the region of *Ji* and *Dou*. Turning to the right, it revolves like a wheel, spitting forth sprouts. In the hidden depths, images appear, scattering radiant essence. Above *Ang* and *Bi*, *Zhen* emerges to set forth. Yang *qi* initiates its inception—*Chujiu*, the Hidden Dragon.

Yang is established in threes, yin flows in eights. On the third day, *Zhen* stirs; on the eighth day, *Dui* moves. *Jiu’er*, the Dragon Appears—peaceful and luminous. When the virtues of three and five are perfected, the body of *Qian* is complete. *Jiusan* watches in the evening with trepidation, the divine tally waning and breaking. As flourishing and decline gradually transform, it ultimately returns to its origin. *Xun* inherits its lineage, securing and upholding it.

*Jiusi*, Perhaps Leaping—advancing and retreating, the Way is perilous. *Gen* governs progression and cessation, one must not exceed the proper time. On the twenty-third day, one guards the crescent phase. *Jiuwu*, the Flying Dragon—Heaven’s position brings joy. *Liwu*, Earth submits, binding and encompassing beginning and end. It nurtures the many offspring, becoming the mother of all kinds.

*Shangjiu*, the Exalted Dragon—its virtue battles in the wilds. *Yongjiu*, soaring and fluttering, becomes the compass and square of the Way. The yang numbers are exhausted, and once exhausted, they arise anew. Following emotions to unite with nature, turning and joining together. The revolving jade mechanism ascends and descends, rising and falling. Circulating through the six lines, it is difficult to perceive. Thus, it has no fixed position—it is the ancestral founder of the *Yi*.

This chapter speaks of the union of Kan and Li to produce the elixir, corresponding to the lunar phases of new moon, crescent, full moon, and waning. This is the fire-timing of the minor celestial cycle. Between the dark of the moon and the new moon, the tally is joined and the center traversed. Primal chaos and vast obscurity—female and male follow one another. Nourishing fluids moisten and enrich, bestowing transformation and flowing freely. The numinous brightness of Heaven and Earth cannot be measured. It is fitting to secure the body, concealing form and hiding away.

This passage describes the conjunction of dark and new moon, when the sun and moon meet, forming the root of the great elixir. The marvel of creation lies in the mutual generation of movement and stillness, an endless cycle. Yet without gathering, there can be no dispersion; without hibernation, there can be no growth. Thus, reckoned by the great kalpa, there is *zhen* and then *yuan*; reckoned by a year, there is winter and then spring; reckoned by a day, there is *hai* and then *zi*; reckoned by a month, there must be the dark of the moon before the new moon—this is the image of ending and then beginning anew.

Why is it called the dark and new moon? The moon itself has no light; it receives the sun’s spirit to shine. On the thirtieth night, its light is exhausted and its body concealed—this is called the dark of the moon. At this time, the sun and moon travel together along the ecliptic, their tally joined precisely between dark and new moon. The sun’s essence and moon’s light within our bodies, one south and one north, rely on true intention to draw them together, meeting at last in the central yellow chamber of the spirit. Water and fire achieve harmony precisely between *Xu* and *Wei*, where emptiness reaches its utmost stillness and the numinous arises of itself—this is the true dark and new moon within a single moment. Thus it is said, “Between the dark and new moon, the tally is joined and the center traversed.”

The sun and moon of creation enfold one another with spirit and soul; the sun and moon within our bodies respond to one another with essence and light. When the spirit returns to the qi-cavern, there is neither seeing nor hearing, neither heaven nor earth. The revolving jade mechanism halts for a time, returning to primal chaos and reentering vast obscurity. Within this murky undifferentiation, true yin and true yang naturally pair. Thus it is said, “Primal chaos and vast obscurity—female and male follow one another.”

Within the union of primordial female and male lies the true seed. The primordial qi swirls in profound obscurity, as when the sun’s spirit bestows essence and the moon’s soul receives transformation, allowing essence and qi to flow in hidden communion. Thus it is said, “Nourishing fluids moisten and enrich, bestowing transformation and flowing freely.”

At the moment when sun and moon join their tally, heavenly qi descends into earth, the divine wind falls silent, mountains and seas conceal their clouds. A single point of numinous brightness is enveloped within the chaotic aperture, beyond all seeking. This is the state where a single thought does not arise, and even spirits and gods cannot know it. Thus it is said, “The numinous brightness of Heaven and Earth cannot be measured.”

Heaven enters earth, yang enfolds yin within, returning to the root and reviving the mandate. Profoundly hidden as if empty, it is no less than the hibernation of dragons and snakes across the nine provinces or the concealment of pearls and jade in rivers and marshes. Tan Jingsheng said, “Attaining the gate of vast qi, one returns to the root; knowing the vessel of primordial spirit, one sheathes its light.” This is what is meant. Thus it is said, “It is fitting to secure the body, concealing form and hiding away.”

It begins in the northeast, in the region of *Ji* and *Dou*. Turning to the right, it brings forth the wheel and spits forth the sprout. The hidden abyss reveals its image, emitting and dispersing radiant essence. Above *Ang* and *Bi*, *Zhen* sets forth on its march. Yang qi initiates its inception—*Chujiu* is the hidden dragon.

This passage speaks of *Gen*’s solitary yang reversing to become *Zhen*. People know that the moon loses its brightness on the dark of the moon, yet do not realize it truly begins at the last quarter. The last quarter belongs to *Gen*, which in the Later Heaven arrangement occupies the northeast. Among the twelve earthly branches, it lies between *chou* and *yin*; among the twenty-eight lodges, it corresponds to the degrees of *Ji* and *Dou*. For the Way of Heaven turns left in forward motion, beginning at the center of *zi*. The qi of Earth turns right in reverse motion, beginning between *chou* and *yin*.

To understand Heaven’s forward motion, observe the sequence of a year: its order proceeds from north to east, then to south, and from south to west, returning to the north—from *zi* to *chou*, from *chou* to *yin*, emerging from *Zhen* and completing in *Gen*. This is the forward-moving Five Phases of the Later Heaven.

To understand Earth’s reverse motion, examine the lunar lodges in the *Na Jia* system. In the *Na Jia* cycle, *zi* turns right, moving backward to *wei* and *shen*, from north to west, then from west to south—this is the qi of the first quarter, its image the gaining of companions. *Wu* then turns east, moving backward to *yin* and *chou*, from south to east, then from east to north—this is the qi of the last quarter, its image the loss of companions. The two quarters meet precisely between dark and new moon, where *Bo* in *Gen* reverses to become *Fu* in *Zhen*—this is the reverse application of the Five Phases in the Earlier Heaven.

The Way of the Golden Elixir relies entirely on the Earlier Heaven. The *Na Jia* system shares the same body as the celestial Great Yin. True Water of the Great Yin is born at *wu*, from the single yin of *xun* on the sixteenth to the two yins of *gen* on the twenty-third. Yin comes to strip away yang, leaving only the last fruit. Then, turning from east to north, it arrives precisely at the junction of *chou* and *yin*, at the degrees of *Ji*-Water and *Dou*-Wood, spiraling into *yi* and *gui*. *Gen*’s solitary yang is utterly lost, becoming *Kun*. Within the body, this is the spirit entering qi, the myriad transformations returning to the root—this is the image described as “*wu* turns east, the northeast loses its companions.”

At this time, yin reaches its extreme and yang is born. True Fire of the Great Yang arises at *zi*, for yang has no principle of total destruction. The mutual polishing of sun and moon occurs precisely in the northern land of *Xu* and *Wei*. Once their conjunction is complete, they gradually turn from north to west. The moon’s soul here faintly reveals yang light—this is called “turning to the right, bringing forth the wheel and spitting forth the sprout.” A single point of true fire lies hidden in the depths of the northern sea—this is called “the hidden abyss reveals its image, emitting and dispersing radiant essence.” As the radiant essence gradually emerges, on the first and second day, and by the third day, it arrives precisely at the junction of *wei* and *shen*, at the degrees of *Ang*-Sun and *Bi*-Moon. Above *geng*, at dusk, a hook appears like an upturned bowl. Within *Kun*, a single yang emerges to become *Zhen*. Within the body, this is the first warming of the lead crucible, the tender new sprout of the medicine—this is the image described as “*zi* turns right, the southwest gains its companions.”

Though yang qi has now arisen, its inception remains nascent, its fire-force still faint—precisely corresponding to the *Qian* hexagram’s “first nine: the hidden dragon.” At this stage, one should only gently nurture the *zi*-pearl, not yet apply fierce fire. This passage speaks of the sun and moon uniting as jade disks, giving birth to the great elixir of golden cinnabar—this is the work of the *living zi-hour*. As Master Yin the Perfected said, “To seek the great elixir as the root of cinnabar, one must recognize the living *zi*-hour within the body”—this is precisely its meaning.

Between the dark of the moon and the new moon, *Kan* and *Li* unite to form *Qian*. *Qian* is true metal, hence it is called “golden cinnabar.” Thus, the fire-times of golden cinnabar correspond exclusively to the six yang lines of *Qian*. Yang is established through three; yin communicates through eight. On the third day, *Zhen* stirs; on the eighth day, *Dui* moves. The second nine: the dragon appears—peaceful and luminous. This speaks of the advance of two yang lines to become *Dui*. Three is the position of lesser yang, belonging to *Zhen*; eight is the number of lesser yin, belonging to *Dui*. The *Zhen* hexagram contains yang within yin, hence “yang is established through three”; the *Dui* hexagram carries yin within yang, hence “yin communicates through eight.” On the third day, the moon emerges in the *geng* direction, bearing the image of *Zhen* stirring; on the eighth day, the first quarter moon appears in the *ding* direction, bearing the image of *Dui* moving. When the moon reaches the first quarter, the golden essence in the crucible begins to flourish, the dragon’s virtue stands at its center—thus it is also the image of “the second nine: the dragon appears—peaceful and luminous.” Yet in *Zhen*, the one yang has only just stirred beneath two yin; in *Dui*, the one yin has already moved above two yang—within virtue lies punishment, within generation lies destruction. This is the time of bathing.

When the three and five virtues are perfected, the body of *Qian* is complete. The third nine: vigilant at dusk—diminishing the spirit-tally. Gradual decline and transformation return to the beginning. This speaks of the three yang reaching *Qian*, where yang’s extremity gives birth to yin. When the moon reaches the full-moon day, the virtues of three and five are perfected and the body of *Qian* is complete. At this time, the elixir has ascended the crucible, the golden essence is full and abundant, its light penetrating the great void. Yet at the height of fullness comes decline—one must guard against diminution, hence the image of “the third nine: vigilant at dusk.” This is precisely the time of “all day long, dry and dry again.” The Dao of *Qian* gradually transforms, as *Xun*’s one yin comes to receive the tally. The end of yang is the beginning of yin—this is the time of guarding the city.

*Xun* continues its succession, firmly securing and holding fast. The fourth nine: perhaps leaping—advance and retreat are perilous. This speaks of the one yin’s retreat to become *Xun*. When the body of *Qian* is pure and the yang fire excessive, it must be followed by the yin tally, relying entirely on *Xun*’s one yin to firmly secure and hold fast, gathering and restraining the yang qi. At this time, the “perhaps leaping” of the fourth line of *Qian* has transformed into the “binding sack” of the fourth line of *Kun*. For the fire-times of golden cinnabar take only the three yang of *Qian*; when the three yang retreat, they become three yin. At the extremity of advance comes retreat—one must guard against peril on the path. This is the time of considering danger.

*Gen* governs advance and halt, not to be transgressed in timing. On the twenty-third day, one guards the time of the half-moon. The fifth nine: the flying dragon—heaven’s position brings joy. This speaks of the two yin’s retreat to become *Gen*. With one yang above, the solitary fruit remains—what would advance in yang comes to an abrupt halt. At this time, water and fire are in balance, the yang qi in the crucible gradually congeals, gradually returning to storage. The timing must not be transgressed—precisely on the twenty-third day, one guards the time of the last quarter. The “flying dragon in heaven” of the fifth line of *Qian* transforms into the “yellow skirts, supremely auspicious” of the fifth line of *Kun*—within punishment lies virtue, within destruction lies generation. Thus there is the image of “heaven’s position brings joy.” This is also a time of bathing.

The sixth five of *Kun* receives, binding and encompassing beginning and end. It nurtures and shelters the many children, becoming the mother of its kind for all generations. The ninth above: the exalted dragon—battling virtue in the wilds. This speaks of pure yin returning to *Kun*, where yin’s extremity gives birth to yang. Though the phrase “sixth five” seems to refer specifically to the fifth line of the *Kun* hexagram, it actually encompasses the entire sequence of the moon’s phases from half to full, dark to new. For in the eight trigrams’ correspondence to the heavenly stems, *Qian* and *Kun* bind beginning and end, encompassing the six children within. The six children all rely on *Qian*-father for their inception and on *Kun*-mother for their completion. The creative transformations of a single month comprise three yin and three yang in their entirety. The moon is the great yin—its watery body is pure black, without light—yet it receives and embodies the golden essence of the great yang, borrowing its body to generate light. One yang arises in *Zhen*; from the new moon to the full moon, this is *Qian* borrowing its body. One yin arises in *Xun*; from the full moon to the dark of the moon, this is *Kun*’s true body. Yet from beginning to end, that single point of yang light belongs entirely to the essence of the great yang of *Qian*, merely borrowing *Kun*’s yin-darkness to bear and receive it.

*Qian*-father’s essence relies entirely on *Kun*-mother’s body to encompass and bind it. From the first to the fifth line of *Kun*, the phases of a single month are perfectly complete—hence “the sixth five of *Kun* receives, binding and encompassing beginning and end.” The six children do not exceed the bounds of *Qian* and *Kun*. The three sons and three daughters each follow their kind: the yang souls are always sunlight, belonging to *Qian*; the yin essences are always moonlight, belonging to *Kun*. Yet the three yin are all subsumed under *Qian*—this is the principle that *Qian*’s origin governs heaven, the way of the father. The three yang are all embodied in *Kun*—this is the principle that *Kun*’s origin receives heaven, the way of the mother. Thus *Qian*’s position in the ninth above is called the “line of the ancestral temple,” truly the father of the six children; *Kun*’s position in the sixth above is called the “line of the ancestral temple,” truly the mother of the six children. This speaks from the perspective of *Kun*’s compliance with *Qian*—hence “it nurtures and shelters the many children, becoming the mother of its kind for all generations.”

The great elixir of golden cinnabar originally emerges from the *Kun*-furnace, only then ascending to the *Qian*-crucible. Ascending and then descending, it falls into the Yellow Court. The work of nurturing the fire remains in the *Kun*-furnace, awaiting in stillness the return of the one yang. From beginning to end, the way of the mother is present throughout.

When *Qian*’s upper nine exhausts its change, it becomes *Kun*’s upper six. Yet one must know that yang has no principle of total depletion—its great fruit remains above, towering and unmoved. This is why Jing’s *Fire-Pearl Forest* *Yi* takes the upper line as the ancestral temple, embodying the principle of non-transformation. Thus *Kun*’s upper six line reads: *“Dragons battle in the wilds; their blood is dark and yellow.”* The dragon battling in the wilds is none other than *Qian*’s upper nine, the dragon at its zenith. When yin reaches its extreme, yang contends with it; only after this battle do they achieve harmony. *Kun* is the realm of the limitless, hence the battle is said to occur “in the wilds.” In the later heaven, *Qian* resides in the northwest, the land of utmost yin, so it is also said to battle “in *Qian*.” The *yuan* belongs to *Qian*, the *huang* to *Kun*; through this single battle, *yuan* and *huang* first intermingle, and within them the yang essence is conceived, forming the body of *Zhen*. Thus *Zhen* is called *yuan-huang*: “Within the earth, there is thunder”—the first stirring of the one yang, splitting open the primordial chaos, turning into the dawn of *Fu*!

*“Use the nine, soaring and free, as the compass and square of the Dao.”* The yang number has run its course; when it ends, it begins anew. Pushing forth emotion to unite with nature, they turn and join together. Circling like the turning jade armillary, ascending and descending, rising and falling. Flowing through the six lines, yet difficult to perceive. Thus it has no fixed position, becoming the ancestral origin of the *Yi*. This passage speaks of the ceaseless circulation of the two functions of *Kan* and *Li*, serving as the conclusion to the entire chapter.

*Qian*’s three and *Kun*’s six combine to form nine. *Qian*’s “use of nine” encompasses *Kun*, while *Kun*’s “use of six” cannot encompass *Qian*. Observing the above, the three yangs and three yins are all governed by *Qian*, while *Kun* merely envelops and complies within. Thus when *Qian*’s nine is invoked, *Kun*’s six is already contained within. Moreover, the great Dao of the golden elixir is rooted in *Qian*’s nature. *Qian* is pure yang, and must be refined through nine revolutions before completion. Hence it is said: *“Use the nine, soaring and free, as the compass and square of the Dao.”* *Qian* belongs to the great yang; when yang is exhausted at nine, it transforms into lesser yin. The *Qian* of the prior heaven, in one revolution, becomes the *Li* nine of the later heaven. One becomes nine, and nine reverts to one—originally without beginning or end. Thus it is said: *“The yang number has run its course; when it ends, it begins anew.”* This is what is later called *“the number of one and nine, ending and then beginning again.”*

Within *Kan* lies the essence of metal, residing in the west; within *Li* lies the nature of wood, residing in the east. Separated by the distance between east and west, they have no means to meet—entirely dependent on the Dipper’s handle to revolve between them, so that the metal’s emotion naturally returns to nature. Hence it is said: *“Pushing forth emotion to unite with nature, they turn and join together.”* The ancients established the jade armillary and sighting-tube to symbolize the revolutions of the celestial sphere. Only these two—nature and emotion—emerge with the sun and enter with the moon, now rising, now descending, now ascending, now falling. Longitude is the north-south axis, while latitude becomes the east-west path. North and south are measured by the meridian of *zi* and *wu*; east and west by the parallel of *mao* and *you*. Like the armillary’s ceaseless revolution, its beginning is unfathomable—this is precisely the function of the *mao-you* celestial circuit. Thus it is said: *“Circling like the turning armillary, ascending and descending, rising and falling.”*

From *Zhen* to *Qian*, from *Xun* to *Kun*, the three yangs and three yins wax and wane among themselves, with no visible presence of the *Kan* and *Li* lines. Yet as the sun departs and the moon arrives, and the moon arrives and the sun departs, their ebb and flow, advance and retreat—all are the wondrous functions of *Kan* and *Li*, though nothing is visible. Hence it is said: *“Flowing through the six lines, yet difficult to perceive.”* The sun and moon grasp *Qian* and *Kun*, circulating through the six voids—this is called the *Yi* without form. This formless *Yi* governs the celestial heart, from which the sixty-four hexagrams and three hundred eighty-four lines emerge. Is this not the *Yi* without fixed position, the ancestral origin of the *Yi*?

*Qian*’s primal power governs heaven, completing the nine revolutions—thus the “use of nine” becomes the compass and square of the Dao. The sun and moon are the *Yi*, inherently without fixed form—hence the golden elixir is the ancestral origin of the *Yi*, spoken of in mutual terms. This chapter focuses on the workings of the golden elixir. At first, during the dark and new moon conjunction, *Kan* is drawn to fill *Li*, emotion returns to nature, and the one yang is born—this is the foundation of the golden elixir. Then, when the medicine arises in the *geng* direction, ascending from *Kun* to *Qian* and descending again, the three yangs are matched—this is the function of the golden elixir, known as the “fire times of the lesser celestial circuit.” This is a crucial key in the *Cantong Qi*, yet its wonders are only fully revealed when read together with the following chapter.

**Chapter Nineteen: The Union of Qian and Kun**

At the dawn of the new moon, *Fu* arrives, and the yang qi begins to flow. Its entry and exit are unhurried; the marker is set, subtly firm. The Yellow Bell establishes *zi*, and the omen flourishes. Spreading warmth and softness, the common people thrive in constancy. Tending the furnace and applying the twigs, the path opens and light is born. The radiance gradually advances, and the days grow longer. *Chou*’s Great Chariot sets the balance of high and low. Lifting to form *Tai*, hardness and softness rise together. Yin and yang unite, the small departs and the great arrives. Converging at *yin*, advancing with the season. Gradually passing through *Dazhuang*, flanking the gate of *mao*. Elm pods fall, returning to their root. Punishment and virtue contend, and day and night first divide. *Guai*’s yin retreats, while yang ascends and advances. Washing and cleansing the wings, shaking off the dust of the past. *Qian*’s vigor shines brightly, broadly covering the four neighbors. Yang ends at *si*, centered and interacting. *Gou* begins the sequence, treading frost first. The cold spring at the well’s bottom—*wu* becomes *Linzhong*. The guest submits to yin, and yin becomes the host. Withdrawing from the world and relinquishing position, gathering one’s essence. Cherishing virtue and awaiting the time, dwelling in obscurity. *Pi*’s blockage is impassable; sprouts do not grow. Yin extends while yang contracts, names are damaged and harmed. Observing its measures, examining the mood of mid-autumn. Nurturing the young and tender, the old and withered revive. Shepherd’s purse and wheat sprout, growing beneath the cover. *Bo*’s limbs rot, its form diminishing. When the transformed qi is exhausted, the utmost spirit is lost.

When the Dao reaches its limit, it turns back, returning to the origin of *Kun*. Ever in accord with earth’s patterns, it receives from heaven and spreads forth. The primordial profound and distant, though separated, remain connected. Responding to measure and nurturing life, they are the origin of yin and yang. Vast and indistinct, none know their beginning. Those who lose the track first wander astray, while those who follow become lords. No level ground remains without slope—this is the Dao’s nature. Change and renewal flourish, waxing and waning mutually dependent. *Kun* ends and *Fu* begins, like tracing a linked ring. Emperors and kings ride and govern, enduring for a thousand generations.

This chapter speaks of *Qian* and *Kun* uniting to congeal the elixir, corresponding to a year’s six yang and six yin, the fire-timing of the great celestial cycle. The new moon’s dawn is *Fu*, where yang qi first flows. Entering and exiting without haste, it establishes the marker of subtle firmness. *Huangzhong* aligns with *zi*, and its signs gradually manifest. Spreading gentle warmth, the myriad steams attain constancy.

This passage speaks of the first yang’s movement in *Fu*, the initial foundation of the returning elixir. The preceding chapter described *Kan* and *Li* uniting to produce the great medicine—this is the work of the living *zi* hour, where within a single day’s twelve hours, intent may arrive at any moment. Once the great medicine is produced, the precelestial *nayin* fire-timing of yang ascending and yin descending is employed, called the small celestial cycle. Only after one full cycle is complete, when the true *zi* hour arrives, is the great celestial cycle’s fire-timing used.

What is the true *zi* hour? From *Zhen* to *Qian*, movement reaches its extreme and stills; from *Xun* to *Kun*, stillness reaches its extreme and moves again. Attaining emptiness to the utmost, preserving quiescence to its depth—one speck of true yang lies hidden in the ninefold earth, this is the juncture of *hai* and *zi*. When the time arrives and the mechanism stirs, being arises from nonbeing. Suddenly, at midnight, thunder shakes open the earth’s gate, cleaving the heart of heaven and earth from primordial chaos—this corresponds to the winter solstice’s new moon dawn. Thus it is said, “The new moon’s dawn is *Fu*, where yang qi first flows.” This is what is meant by “where the first yang stirs, before the ten thousand things are born”—this is the true *zi* hour within our bodies.

When the first yang returns, its qi is still faint. At this time, one should nurture the hidden dragon with warmth, not hastily advancing the fire. The former kings, on the solstice, closed the passes, allowing nothing to enter or exit, all to refine the protective barrier and guard this faint yang. Thus it is said, “Entering and exiting without haste, establishing the marker of subtle firmness.” Though the yang qi is faint, its mechanism cannot be restrained. Among the twelve pitches, it precisely corresponds to *Huangzhong*; among the twelve earthly branches, it precisely corresponds to the Dipper’s handle pointing to *zi*—all are images of sprouting, growing, and progressing from faint to manifest. Thus it is said, “*Huangzhong* aligns with *zi*, and its signs gradually manifest.”

The yang fire lies below, warming the lead crucible with gentle heat, naturally harmonizing and softening. Amidst the host of yin, this single speck of yang essence serves as its sovereign. Thus it is said, “Spreading gentle warmth, the myriad steams attain constancy.” The myriad steams, in the hexagrams, are five yin; in humans, they are the body’s essence and qi. “Attaining constancy” refers, in the hexagrams, to the one yang; in humans, to the single speck of yang essence presiding over the ten thousand transformations. This speaks of the first yang’s return, establishing the foundation of the great elixir.

Tending the furnace and applying the twigs, opening the path to generate light. The radiance gradually advances, increasing day by day. *Chou*’s *Dalü* binds the proper balance of low and high. This speaks of the second yang’s advance into *Lin*.

Advancing to the second yang, the furnace’s fire-qi gradually flows smoothly. From here, the path is opened, and light is generated. The radiance gradually progresses, and the solar corona daily lengthens. At this time, the Dipper’s handle points to *chou*, and the pitch corresponds to *Dalü*—first low, then high, an image of advancing the fire.

Looking upward to form *Tai*, firmness and softness flourish together. Yin and yang unite, the small departs and the great arrives. Converging at *yin*, advancing to seize the time. This speaks of the third yang’s advance into *Tai*.

The third yang looks upward, precisely when humans are born in *yin*, the meeting of creation’s opening, where wood’s virtue flourishes and fire is born in *yin*—yin and yang are evenly balanced. Thus it is said, “Firmness and softness flourish together.” At this time, heavenly qi descends and earthly qi ascends; the small departs and the great arrives, yin and yang unite. One must urgently apply fire to correspond—moreover, the first month’s pitch corresponds to *Taicu*, hence the image of converging and seizing the time.

Progressing through *Dazhuang*, flanking the gate of *mao*. Elm pods fall, returning to their root. Punishment and virtue contend, day and night begin to divide. This speaks of the fourth yang’s advance into *Dazhuang*.

The sun rises in the east at the *mao* position, *mao* being the gate of the great yang. In the year, this is the spring equinox; the second month aligns with *mao*, and the pitch corresponds to *Jiazhong*. Thus it is said, “Flanking the gate of *mao*.” Advancing the fire to the fourth yang, the generative qi flourishes, yet within wood’s womb lies metal, life carrying destruction. Thus elm pods fall and return to their root, an image of virtue reverting to punishment. At the spring equinox, day and night begin to equalize, water and fire each half—this is the time of the upper crescent’s bathing.

*Guai* retreats yin, while yang ascends and advances. Washing the wings, shaking off the dust of lodging. This speaks of the fifth yang’s advance into *Guai*.

Five yang ascend, and one yin is about to end—it must be decisively expelled. The third month aligns with *chen*, and the pitch corresponds to *Guxi*—an image of “washing the wings, shaking off the dust of lodging.” Like the great peng preparing to migrate to the southern sea, it stirs its wings, striking the water and soaring upward. The river chariot cannot tarry here—passing this point, it ascends to the summit of Kunlun.

*Qian* is vigorous, flourishing, and bright, broadly covering the four neighbors. Yang ends at *si*, centered and interacting. This speaks of the six yang’s purity in *Qian*.

The fourth month aligns with *si*, and the pitch corresponds to *Zhonglü*. At this time, yang ascends to its peak, above the nine heavens, firelight pervading, golden liquid flowing abundantly—hence the image of “*Qian* vigorous, flourishing, and bright, broadly covering the four neighbors.”

Yet yang reaches its zenith at *si*, and in that very moment, one yin begins to revolve—yin arrives to contend with yang, hence the phrase “centered and interacting.” From the perspective of the six yang, *si* marks their final culmination; yet from the cycle of *Kun*’s end and *Fu*’s beginning, *Qian* stands as the mid-heaven—each interpretation draws from its own meaning.

*Gou* inaugurates the sequence, treading frost as its first sign. The well’s chill spring emerges at *wu*, corresponding to *Rui Bin*. The guest submits to yin, and yin becomes the host. This speaks of the one yin’s retreat into *Gou*. When the six yang reach *Qian*, yang at its peak gives birth to yin, and one must then withdraw fire and advance water. *Xun*’s solitary yin enters to take command. Even at the height of yang’s fiery blaze, the cauldron already harbors yin’s water—just as in the *wu* month of midsummer, the well’s depths yield a cold spring. The warning of treading frost thus attaches to *Kun*’s first line. Yin enters as host while yang retreats as guest; *Gou*’s lunar cavern directly opposes *Fu*’s celestial root. The *wu* month, with its pitch corresponding to *Rui Bin*, also embodies this exchange of host and guest.

Withdrawing from the world and relinquishing rank, one gathers and refines one’s essence. Cherishing virtue and awaiting the hour, one dwells in obscurity and darkness. This speaks of the two yin’s retreat into *Dun*. The sixth month aligns with *wei*, and the pitch corresponds to *Lin Zhong*. As the two yin gradually lengthen, yang’s qi slowly withdraws into the cauldron, like the worthy one who retreats from the world and abides in mountain forests—hence the words “cherishing virtue and awaiting the hour, dwelling in obscurity and darkness.”

Obstruction and blockage prevail, nothing sprouts or grows. Yin advances while yang recedes, names and reputations are ruined. This speaks of the three yin’s retreat into *Pi*. At this time, yang returns to heaven and yin to earth; the two qi fail to interact, and the myriad things do not arise. The seventh month aligns with *shen*, and the pitch corresponds to *Yi Ze*. *Yi* means injury; water born in *shen* can erode and extinguish yang’s fire—hence the image of “yin advancing while yang recedes, names and reputations ruined.”

Observe its measures and weigh its balance, discerning the essence of mid-autumn. The young are nurtured, the old and withered revive. Shepherd’s purse and wheat sprout anew, emerging beneath the cover. This speaks of the four yin’s retreat into *Guan*. The moon rises in the west at the *you* position, marking the autumnal equinox in the year’s cycle, with its pitch corresponding to *Nan Lü*. Metal’s qi is stern and withering, grasses and trees all wither, yet within metal lies the embryo of wood, and within destruction resides generation. Thus, the aged revert to youth, the withered revive, and the sprouts of shepherd’s purse and wheat emerge in their wake—an image of punishment transforming into virtue. At the autumnal equinox, day and night first equalize, water and fire each claim half—this is the time of the lower crescent’s bathing. In the mid-autumn ordinances, measures are standardized and balances leveled—hence the opening phrase “observe its measures and weigh its balance.”

Peeling away the limbs, dissolving the form. The transformative qi exhausted, the utmost spirit lost. This speaks of the five yin’s retreat into *Bo*. The ninth month aligns with *xu*, and the pitch corresponds to *Wu Yi*. Yin arrives to peel away yang, yang’s qi vanishes without remainder, like the limbs of grasses and trees rotting away entirely, leaving only the solitary fruit at the crown—hence the image of “peeling away the limbs, dissolving the form.” *Xu* is the convergence of closed things; through change comes transformation, the spirit and qi turn inward, now present, now absent—hence the words “the transformative qi exhausted, the utmost spirit lost.” Yet know that the form is not truly destroyed—it merely seems to vanish at the extreme of peeling away. The spirit is not truly lost—it merely seems absent at the extreme of returning and concealing. This is the image of the six yin’s return to *Kun*.

When the Dao reaches its limit, it turns back, returning to *Kun*’s origin. Ever in accord with earth’s patterns, receiving heaven’s mandate and spreading it forth. The primordial is distant and boundless, seemingly cut off yet connected. Responding to measure and nurturing the seed—these are the origins of yin and yang. Vast and indistinct, none know its beginning. First lost to the track, later becoming the lord. This speaks of the six yin’s return to *Kun*, the end that is also the beginning. The tenth month, pure yin, aligns with *hai*, and the pitch corresponds to *Ying Zhong*—a time of creation’s closure, when our bodies return to the root and restore life. For humans, *Qian*’s origin is nature, and *Kun*’s origin is life. After birth, the entire body, inside and out, is yin—thus *Kun*’s origin serves as the foundation of life. The initial resurgence of one yang in *Fu* originally emerges from pure *Kun*; when accumulated to *Qian*’s six yang, life fully returns to nature. Then, the one yin of *Gou* again turns back from pure *Qian* to *Kun*’s origin; when accumulated to *Kun*’s six yin, nature fully returns to life. Hence the words “when the Dao reaches its limit, it turns back, returning to *Kun*’s origin.”

Nature having returned to life, the primordial spirit silently retreats into qi, motionless and still, inwardly nurturing the great elixir—just as in the depths of winter, all things without exception lie dormant. Heaven’s qi descends into the earth, and earth’s qi in turn receives and employs it. The end of concealment is the beginning of manifest benevolence; a single spark of heaven’s mechanism generates without cease—hence the words “ever in accord with earth’s patterns, receiving heaven’s mandate and spreading it forth.”

The utmost height of heaven lies eighty-four thousand *li* from the utmost depth of earth. The upper extreme is the primordial void, the lower extreme is profound darkness—seemingly distant and unreachable, yet the sun’s light and the moon’s essence, of the same kind, draw together like a lodestone to iron, not the slightest barrier between them. Hence the words “the primordial is distant and boundless, seemingly cut off yet connected.”

The sun’s light in heaven and the moon’s essence in earth, one yin and one yang, meet in timely union, breathing and nurturing, engendering the true seed—this is the primordial *Qian*’s ancestral qi. Hence the words “responding to measure and nurturing the seed—these are the origins of yin and yang.” At the first union of the primordial womb, the great elixir is about to form—precisely at the midpoint between *hai* and *zi*, between movement and stillness, lies the most wondrous mechanism of heaven, earth, and humanity. Even sages and worthies cannot fathom it, for it is said: “In the indistinct, yin and yang generate transformation; in the interwoven, heaven and earth suddenly revolve.”

Here the fleeting moment of radiant splendor—how could mere words capture its workings? Thus it is said: “Vast and indistinct, none know its beginning.”

At first, when primal chaos remained undivided, the heart of heaven resided at the center, and the primordial yellow was indistinguishable—hence the words “first one loses the track.” Then, as the great flood parted and the root of heaven stirred, the myriad transformations returned of themselves—hence the words “later it becomes the sovereign lord,” echoing the *Kun* hexagram’s judgment: “First one strays, later one finds the master.” At this moment, the first yang revives, turning into the *Zhen* hexagram’s initial nine.

No level ground but has its slope—this is the Dao’s spontaneity. Change and flux grow ever more vigorous, waxing and waning in mutual dependence. The end of *Kun* is the beginning of *Fu*, like tracing an unbroken ring. Emperors and kings who ride and govern by this principle endure for a thousand generations. This passage speaks of movement and stillness begetting one another in endless cycles, serving as the chapter’s conclusion.

As the six yangs ascend and the fire is advanced, the six yins descend and the tally is withdrawn—extreme movement gives rise to stillness, extreme stillness to movement, all the natural workings of heaven’s Dao. Thus it is said: “No level ground but has its slope—this is the Dao’s spontaneity.” Yin and yang alternate, revealing the principles of exchange and transformation. When yang flourishes, yin must wane; when yin dissolves, yang must rest—hence the words “change and flux grow ever more vigorous, waxing and waning in mutual dependence.” Movement and stillness have no beginning, end and inception no limit. The close of *hui* is the start of *shuo*; the end of *hai* is the beginning of *zi*; the end of *Kun* is the start of *Fu*. One meets it yet sees no head, follows it yet sees no tail—thus it is said: “The end of *Kun* is the beginning of *Fu*, like tracing an unbroken ring.”

The subtlety of the firing times aligns above with the workings of creation and below with the human body. Within, it can govern the heart; without, it can govern the world. Emperors and kings who ride this Dao to govern endure for millennia; alchemists who attain this Dao to refine the heart preserve their dharma-body for a thousand kalpas—hence the words “emperors and kings who ride and govern endure for a thousand generations.” This being the final chapter of the middle section on governance, it concludes with imperial rule, corresponding to the preceding section’s final chapter on the *Mingtang* and the enactment of governance.

This chapter elaborates the great celestial cycle of firing times, complementing the previous chapter from beginning to end. Only after *Kan* and *Li* unite is the great elixir produced; once produced, it may be gathered; once gathered and placed in the crucible, it may be refined. The previous chapter spoke of the moment of gathering; this chapter speaks of the moment of refinement. For gathering, one must recognize the living *zi* hour’s operation, awaiting the juncture of *hui* and *shuo*, when the two luminaries merge their essences, the *geng* direction reveals the moon, and metal is born within water—amidst the indistinct and the profound, one then seeks the primordial pearl within the formless, moving true fire within non-action. When the moon is full and the elixir congeals, this is called the golden elixir.

For refinement, one must recognize the proper *zi* hour’s operation, awaiting the first stir of yang between *hai* and *zi*, when fire arises within water. Only then does one apply the method of closing *Ren* and opening *Du*, fanning with the wind of *Xun*, and pumping with the bellows. Seizing this moment of vigorous fire, one drives the river chariot, fully laden with golden liquid, reversing its flow upward from the *Taiyuan* pass to the celestial valley. At the moment of union, the hundred vessels return to the origin, the nine gates are thoroughly opened, golden essence penetrates the crown, and silver waves surge to the heavens—scenes too wondrous to describe in full. Once the union is complete and yang reaches its extreme, yin arises, and one must swiftly open the gates and withdraw the fire, gradually descending the tower of layers. At this juncture, one must guard against danger and peril, purifying thoughts and cleansing the heart, until the elixir is returned to the earthen crucible—this is called “when the union of *Qian* and *Kun* ceases, a single point falls into the yellow court.”

Once the elixir enters the crucible, one must employ the *mao-you* celestial cycle’s firing times to congeal it. When the sacred embryo is formed, further nurturing is required, along with the work of nursing. When the embryo is complete and qi is sufficient, the infant moves to the upper field, and the primordial spirit transforms and emerges, naturally uniting form and spirit, merging with the Dao in truth—this is called the ninefold transmutation of the golden liquid returning elixir.

Yet these two operations—one internal, one external—differ as heaven and earth. From the mouths of past sages, this transmission has been passed down without being committed to writing. Even Master Wei dared not fully disclose the celestial mechanism. He merely likened the waxing and waning of a single month to a single moment in the golden elixir’s workings, and the six yins and six yangs of a year to the nine transmutations of the returning elixir. The true firing times remain secretly embedded within. Those who study the Dao and meet a teacher must grasp the meaning and forget the image.

**Middle Section (Middle Volume on Nurturing Innate Nature, comprising four chapters—this being the central chapter)**

**Chapter Twenty: Returning Innate Nature and Life to the Origin**

To nurture innate nature is to extend life and await its fulfillment. Reflect deeply on what follows, and first consider what precedes. The body one receives is fundamentally empty and void. Primordial essence spreads like clouds, borne by qi at the outset. Yin and yang serve as measures, the dwelling of hun and po. The yang spirit is the sun’s hun; the yin spirit is the moon’s po. Hun and po serve as each other’s chambers. Innate nature presides within, establishing the inner boundary; emotions operate without, constructing the outer walls. When the walls are complete, the people are at peace. At this time, emotions harmonize with *Qian* and *Kun*.

*Qian* moves and extends, qi spreads and essence flows; *Kun* is still and receptive, serving as the Dao’s dwelling. The firm acts and retreats; the yielding transforms and nourishes. Nine returns, seven reversals, eight returns, six dwellings. The male is white, the female red—metal and fire restrain each other. Then water settles fire, the beginning of the five phases. Supreme goodness resembles water, clear and without blemish. The Dao’s form and image are the true one, difficult to depict. When it changes and spreads, each dwells in its own place.

Like a chicken’s egg, white and black correspond, spanning a single inch—this is the beginning. The four limbs, five viscera, sinews, and bones are all complete.

After ten months, it emerges from the womb.
Its bones are soft and pliable, its flesh smooth as malt sugar.

This passage speaks of *xing* (nature) and *ming* (life) sharing a single source—establishing *ming* is precisely how one nurtures *xing*.
To nurture *xing* is to extend *ming* and delay its appointed end.
Examine the latter and consider the former;
one must ponder what comes first.

The body one receives is fundamentally nothingness.
Primal essence spreads like clouds, relying on *qi* to take its first form.
Yin and yang serve as its measure, the dwelling of *hun* and *po*.

This section speaks of the work of nurturing *xing*—one must thoroughly investigate the root of *xing* and *ming*.

What is *xing*?
A single spirit, vast and penetrating, round as the great void—this is the *Qian* origin of all beginnings.
What is *ming*?
A single *qi*, entwined and nurturing, governing the ten thousand transformations—this is the *Kun* origin of all life.
This is the *xing* and *ming* of the primordial, existing before parents gave birth, originally a single undivided thing, inherently unstained, requiring no cultivation or verification.
Once born into existence, the Supreme Ultimate divides: *xing* takes form, *ming* is established—these two must then be cultivated together.
Yet *xing* fundamentally neither comes nor goes, while *ming* may be prolonged or shortened. If one fails to sustain *ming*, the single spirit will suddenly depart forever.
Those who cultivate the Dao and seek to nurture *xing* must begin with the root of *ming*.
Thus it is said: "To nurture *xing*, extend *ming* and delay its end."

What does "delay its end" mean?
Every person’s *ming* has a fixed term—its coming cannot be delayed, nor can its going.
Only those of great cultivation take control for themselves, unbound by the molding of creation.
Once *ming* is firmly established, true *xing* resides within it.

If one does not know where the original true *xing* ultimately returns, and *xing* is the great matter of the end, then to seek a return to the end, one must first trace its origin.
One must repeatedly inquire: from where did this speck of true *xing* come before birth?
By what does it stand after birth?
Then one will understand that the cultivation of *ming* cannot be delayed.
Thus it is said: "Examine the latter and consider the former."

At the moment of conception, one merely receives the father’s essence and mother’s blood, which gather and congeal into an illusory body—this is the formed body, not the true body.
The true body is originally one with the great void, luminous and pure, fundamentally empty of all things.
Thus it is said: "The body one receives is fundamentally nothingness."

When the ten-month gestation is complete, a speck of primal essence from the great void, like clouds moving and rain falling, suddenly attaches itself, entering directly into the central palace of the spirit chamber to become one’s master.
There, it splits open the ancestral aperture—with a single thunderous sound, the *xing* of Heaven’s mandate divides into one yin and one yang.

For the *qi* of later heaven’s creation, without the primal essence of earlier heaven, would have no master and could not become numinous.
For the primal essence of earlier heaven, without the *qi* of later heaven’s creation, would have no place to rely upon and could not stand.
Thus *xing* and *ming* are fundamentally inseparable.
Thus it is said: "Primal essence spreads like clouds, relying on *qi* to take its first form."

Once later heaven’s creation divides into one yin and one yang, the yang spirit becomes the *hun*, which governs the light and pure, belonging to the wood-fluid of the east.
The yin spirit becomes the *po*, which governs the heavy and stagnant, belonging to the metal-essence of the west.
The two dwell within the enclosure of *Kan* and *Li*.
Thus it is said: "Yin and yang serve as its measure, the dwelling of *hun* and *po*."

Since *ming* in humans belongs to later heaven’s creation, it carries with it emotions and consciousness.
Only because the original true *xing* intermingles with the karmic roots from beginningless time do arising-and-ceasing and non-arising-and-ceasing combine to form the eight consciousnesses.
The subtlest aspect of consciousness is thought; the wandering aspect of thought is emotion.
Emotion gives rise to the obstruction of wisdom; thought alters the body’s uniqueness.
Thus true *xing* is inverted, and one vainly enters the wheel of rebirth.

Therefore, those who seek to understand *xing* must first understand *ming*.

The yang spirit is the sun’s *hun*; the yin spirit is the moon’s *po*.
*Hun* and *po* serve as each other’s chambers.
*Xing* governs the interior, establishing boundaries and defenses.
Emotion governs the exterior, constructing walls and ramparts.
When walls and ramparts are complete, the people are at peace.

This section properly speaks of the work of establishing *ming* in later heaven.

In later heaven, the single *hun* and *po* are divided between *Kan* and *Li*.
Since the great yang resides in *mao*, the sun’s *hun* within *Li* is the yang spirit.
Since the great yin resides in *you*, the moon’s *po* within *Kan* is the yin spirit.
Though their bodies dwell separately, the sun’s light in *Li* is precisely the jade hare of the moon—thus the sun’s *hun* returns as a yin spirit.
The moon’s essence in *Kan* is precisely the golden crow of the sun—thus the moon’s *po* returns as a yang spirit.
Thus it is said: "*Hun* and *po* serve as each other’s chambers."

Though later heaven’s two things divide *xing* and *ming*, the original *xing* is entirely entrusted to *ming*.
For once yin and yang divide, all is *ming*.
Moreover, the root of *ming* further transforms into emotion, for the changes and unions of yin and yang are nothing but emotion.

Because *xing* is entrusted to *ming*, the primal essence within *Li* and the primal *qi* within *Kan* are both called *ming*.
Because *ming* transforms into emotion, the wood-*hun* of the sun and the metal-*po* of the moon are both called emotion.
Only the speck of original spirit within the ancestral aperture is the original true *xing*.
The original spirit is the sovereign, settled within the aperture, coming and going without ever leaving its gate.
Is this not "*Xing* governs the interior, establishing boundaries and defenses"?

Essence and *qi* are ministers, strictly establishing embankments, guarding front and back, left and right, cutting off all treachery and evil.
Is this not "Emotion governs the exterior, constructing walls and ramparts"?

When the embankments are firm, the master roams leisurely within the secret chamber, unmoving and unshaken, unstartled and unafraid.
Thus it is said: "When walls and ramparts are complete, the people are at peace."

First, the *xing* within is sufficient to restrain emotion; then, the emotion without naturally returns to *xing*.
Guest and host mutually unite; sovereign and minister follow the Dao.
This is the union of *Kan* and *Li*, the initial foundation of the golden elixir—establishing *ming* is precisely how one nurtures *xing*.

At this moment, emotion harmonizes with Qian and Kun.
Qian moves and straightens, *qi* spreads and essence flows;
Kun is still and contracts, becoming the dwelling of the Dao.
The firm bestows and retreats; the yielding transforms and nourishes.
This passage speaks of the postnatal returning to the prenatal.

The postnatal Kan and Li are none other than the prenatal Qian and Kun. Yet once Qian and Kun are broken, *xing* turns into emotion, and from then on, emotion takes charge—chasing sounds and pursuing forms, unable to return to the origin. Only when the two substances unite, the city walls are complete, and the embankments are established, does emotion return to *xing*. The yin within Li reverts to Kun, and the yang within Kan reverts to Qian.
Thus it is said: "At this moment, emotion harmonizes with Qian and Kun."

The *xing* of Qian is supremely firm: when still, it is focused; when moving, it is direct. The primordial spirit governs essence and *qi* with absolute rigidity and straightness, unyielding.
Thus it is said: "Qian moves and straightens, *qi* spreads and essence flows."
This speaks of the primordial spirit establishing the embankments—what is called "Qian, the origin of beginnings."

The *xing* of Kun is supremely yielding: when moving, it opens; when still, it contracts. The true *qi* of Qian flows and spreads, and Kun compliantly receives it. The primordial spirit, intermingling and transforming, is nurtured within the Yellow Court’s crucible.
Thus it is said: "Kun is still and contracts, becoming the dwelling of the Dao."
This speaks of the primordial spirit’s original womb—what is called "Kun, the source of generation."

The father, Qian, is firm and governs bestowal, yet bestows only a single point of true *qi*;
The mother, Kun, is yielding and governs transformation, requiring constant nurturing in the Central Palace to form the embryo.
Thus it is said: "The firm bestows and retreats; the yielding transforms and nourishes."
This speaks of Kan and Li uniting to produce the prenatal primordial spirit—the wondrous workings of the golden elixir.

Nine returns, seven reverts;
Eight returns, six abides.
Male white, female red—
Metal and fire restrain each other.
Water settles fire, the beginning of the Five Phases.
This passage speaks of the Four Symbols and Five Phases merging into a single *qi*.

Once Kan and Li revert to Qian and Kun, the postnatal Four Symbols and Five Phases all return to their origin.
Why is this so?
Heaven One generates Water, Earth Six completes it—this is the essence of the North.
Earth Two generates Fire, Heaven Seven completes it—this is the spirit of the South.
Heaven Three generates Wood, Earth Eight completes it—this is the soul of the East.
Earth Four generates Metal, Heaven Nine completes it—this is the corporeal soul of the West.
Water, Fire, Wood, and Metal are the Four Symbols; together with the central Earth of Wu and Ji, they form the Five Phases.
Yet what are called the Four Symbols and Five Phases are merely the two substances, Kan and Li.
The Kan trigram emerges from Kun, and the Water of the North belongs to yin; its base number is six, but with the addition of Heaven One’s yang, it becomes seven.
The Li trigram emerges from Qian, and the Fire of the South belongs to yang; its base number is seven, but with the addition of Earth Two’s yin, it becomes nine.
Now, the Kan of the North returns to Qian, and the Li of the South reverts to Kun—is this not the image of "nine returns, seven reverts"?

The One of the North returns to the Seven of the South, totaling eight;
The Two of the South returns to the Six of the North, also totaling eight.
Yet it speaks of "abiding" because once the One of the North returns to the South, only the completion number of Water remains, abiding in its place without shifting—precisely six.
Is this not the image of "eight returns, six abides"?

Moreover, the Four Symbols originally derive from two substances. Since nine returns and seven reverts, eight naturally returns and six abides.
Thus the *Wuzhen Pian* speaks only of "returning and reverting," revealing the marvel of creation.
Two and Seven combine to form the Metal of the West, its color turning white;
One and Six combine to form the Fire of the South, its color turning red.
White belongs to Metal, red to Fire—taking the Metal of the West and refining it with the Fire of the South,
Thus it is said: "Male white, female red, Metal and fire restrain each other."

Heaven One’s Water emerges from the Qian Palace, originally the true fire of Great Yang;
Earth Two’s Fire emerges from the Kun Palace, originally the true water of Great Yin.
Only after one reverts and one returns do Water return to Water and Fire to Fire, restoring their original essence.
Thus it is said: "Water settles fire, the beginning of the Five Phases."

Earlier it spoke of Metal and Fire—why now of Water and Fire?
The marvel of postnatal creation lies solely in Kan and Li, yet its thousand transformations and myriad changes bear different names.
In terms of Kan and Li’s positions, they are called Water and Fire;
In terms of the *qi* of the two phases, they are called Metal and Water;
In terms of the functions of Jia and Geng, they are called Metal and Wood;
In terms of the work of refinement, they are called Metal and Fire.
Their applications are inverted and unfathomable, yet ultimately they are merely the two substances, Water and Fire.
Though postnatal Water and Fire are divided into two, they are fundamentally the single *qi* of the prenatal.
Once Kan and Li revert to Qian and Kun, this is precisely "nine returns, seven reverts, eight returns, six abides"—transforming into the single *qi* of the prenatal.

The highest good is like Water, pure and flawless.
The form and image of the Dao—true unity is hard to depict.
Though it changes and spreads, each abides in its own place.

This passage speaks of the primordial singular *qi* as the foundation of the Great Elixir. The Dao is fundamentally formless and void, yet from this void arises the singular *qi*. This singular *qi*, undifferentiated in the primordial chaos, is the true unity of prenatal Water—not the postnatal Water of tangible form. If a cultivator of the Dao can gather emotions into nature and merge dualities into oneness, they may recover this prenatal true Water. Its source is utterly pure and flawless; at this moment, body and mind fuse into one, unstained and unadulterated, naturally transparent within and without, like a vast expanse of water in a vessel—hence the saying, *"The highest good is like Water, pure and flawless."*

The Dao transcends form and name, inherently without shape or image. Yet when it generates the singular *qi*, it seems possible to describe. However, this true unity of *qi* is subtle and elusive, manifesting in the formless and appearing in the imageless—beyond all conjecture or speculation of the mind. Thus it is said, *"The form and image of the Dao—true unity is hard to depict."*

This true unity of Water is the single point of *yinqiao* in the Central Palace, the so-called "Supreme Unity containing the true *qi*." Combined, it is one *qi*; divided, it becomes two substances; further divided, it becomes the Four Images and Five Phases. When they interact, the Five Phases transform entirely within the center. Yet Wood remains in the East, Metal in the West, Fire in the South, and Water in the North—each abiding in its proper place. Hence it is said, *"Though it changes and spreads, each abides in its own place."*

This section declares that the true unity of Water is indeed the foundation of the Elixir. As *The Mirror for Compounding the Medicine* states, *"The Lead of the Water Realm is but a single flavor."* If the scholar understands the principle of gathering the Five and uniting the Four, merging dualities into oneness, the *yinqiao* will form, and the Sacred Embryo will take shape. It resembles an egg, where white and black correspond, spanning an inch in all directions—this is the initial stage. The four limbs and five viscera, along with sinews and bones, are all present. After ten months, it emerges from its womb. Its bones are supple enough to coil, its flesh smooth as maltose.

This passage particularly reveals the form of the Dharma-body. When the Sacred Embryo first congeals, a single spark of original spirit lies hidden in the chamber of spirit, indistinct and undivided, like primordial chaos before the separation of darkness and light—akin to the state of an egg. Thus it is said, *"It resembles an egg, where white and black correspond."* Within the chamber of spirit, the space is perfectly square and round, exactly an inch in diameter. The Dharma-body dwells within, growing and flourishing, just as an infant in the mother’s womb—hence, *"spanning an inch in all directions, this is the initial stage."*

To nurture the true embryo, one must proceed from the subtle to the manifest. First, an image forms; then, a shape. The four limbs, five viscera, sinews, bones, and joints—all become complete, fully formed yet minute. Thus it is said, *"The four limbs and five viscera, along with sinews and bones, are all present."* Know that the Four Images and Five Phases envelop the Dharma-body like the four limbs and five viscera; as the Dharma-body gradually solidifies, it resembles sinews and bones—yet it is not truly tangible. When the nurturing is complete, after ten months the embryo is fully formed. The infant leaps forth from the Kun furnace, ascending into the Qian cauldron. From here, the furnace and cauldron are reset, and Heaven and Earth are recreated—ushering in a new transformation. Only then does one’s Dharma-body connect with Heaven and Earth, merging with the Great Void. Thus it is said, *"After ten months, it emerges from its womb,"* taking on the appearance of supple bones and flesh smooth as maltose.

This passage describes the form of the Dharma-body as sharing the same creative process as the physical body receiving *qi* in the mother’s womb. Yet while the natural course produces a human, the reverse course yields the Elixir—distinguishing the sacred from the ordinary. This chapter is the first crucial threshold of nurturing nature, corresponding to the previous section on the mutual interaction of the two orifices.

**Chapter Twenty-One: The Mutual Resonance of the Two Qi**

A burning-mirror draws fire—without the sun, it cannot glow.
A moon-pearl gathers dew—without stars or moon, how could it yield water?
The two *qi* are profound and distant, yet their resonance still connects—
How much more so when near, stored within the heart and chest?

Yin and Yang pair with the sun and moon; Water and Fire serve as their signs.
This chapter speaks of the mutual resonance of the *qi* of Water and Fire through like attracting like.

The previous chapter described the soul and spirit as mutual dwellings—these are the two substances, Water and Fire. The Dao of the Golden Elixir takes the sun and moon as its body and Water and Fire as its function. In essence, they interpenetrate; in function, they interact. Without Water and Fire, the sun and moon have no means to manifest their essence; without the sun and moon, Water and Fire have no source for their function. Whether drawn from the body or from external things, the principle is the same.

The burning-mirror is a fire-pearl, shaped like a bronze mirror, solid in its center—symbolizing the single Yang within Kan. This object embodies the fiery essence of the Great Yang. Thus, people use it to draw fire, but it must face the sun to succeed. This is because the spark of true Yang is originally the light of the sun’s soul. The sun is the gathering place of light, while the burning-mirror is the summoner of light. Drawing fire with fire—how could it not be effective? Hence it is said, *"A burning-mirror draws fire—without the sun, it cannot glow."*

The moon-pearl is a pearl from a clam, hollow in its center—symbolizing the single Yin within Li. This object embodies the watery essence of the Great Yin. Thus, people use it to gather water, but it must face the moon to succeed. This is because the spark of true Yin is originally the essence of the moon’s spirit. The moon is the repository of essence, while the moon-pearl is the gatherer of essence. Drawing water with water—how could it not respond? Hence it is said, *"A moon-pearl gathers dew—without stars or moon, how could it yield water?"* This is the principle of the mutual interaction of Kan and Li.

The sun and moon in the sky and Water and Fire in the world are separated by countless miles—truly profound and distant. Yet they resonate across barriers, like a magnet attracting iron. This is because like naturally draws like, so the two *qi* spontaneously resonate and connect.

Drawing from distant things, even the insentient still resonate—how much more so when drawing from the body, where the true Water and true Fire of the sentient lie mere inches apart, utterly void yet supremely numinous, responding in an instant to a single breath? Could the true *qi* of the two celestial arcs fail to resonate and transform?

Thus, the true Water within Li flows forth to meet Wu, while the true Fire within Kan comes to join Ji. By employing symbolic methods to gather the essence of the Great Yin and erecting the crucible to collect the *qi* of the Great Yang, like naturally follows like, coalescing into the *yinqie*. For when true Yin and true Yang mutually abide in their dwelling, they become the sun and moon within one’s own body; when the sunlight and moon essence reciprocally engage, they become the Water and Fire within one’s own body. The gathering and summoning between them rely entirely on the true intent of the Central Yellow—this is the wondrous function of the yang-sundial and moon-pearl within one’s own body. Hence it is said, *"Yin and Yang pair with the sun and moon; Water and Fire serve as their proof."*

This chapter focuses on the mutual resonance of the two things and the affinity of like *qi*, elucidating the fundamental principle of *Xing* and *Qing* in the *Great Change*. For stillness without movement is the essence of *Xing*; response leading to communion is the function of *Qing*. The *Qing* of Li ever resides in the North, while the *Qing* of Kan ever resides in the South—this is how the sun and moon unite as a bi-disc and how Water and Fire intermingle. When the true Water within Li returns to the North and the true Fire within Kan returns to the South, this is how Qian and Kun revert to their origin and how the *yinqie* is established.

The *Upper Canon* of the *Zhouyi* begins with Qian and Kun, taking their fixed positions to establish the essence. The *Lower Canon* begins with *Xian* and *Heng*, taking their interaction to achieve function. The hexagram *Xian* depicts a marsh above a mountain, and Confucius’ commentary on it states, *"The two *qi* resonate and commune,"* and further, *"Heaven and Earth respond, and the myriad things are transformed and born."* Clearly, the world contains only these two *qi*: when they flow in harmony and resonate, they give life to beings; when they flow in reverse and resonate, they form the Elixir. Moreover, the two trigrams Dui and Gen precisely correspond to the upper and lower celestial arcs—employing the interactive function of Dui and Gen to restore the unchanging essence of Qian and Kun is no different from the mutual gathering of the yang-sundial and moon-pearl.

Alas! This is the true *Yi* inherent in every person.

**Chapter Twenty-Two: The Key to the Three Treasures**

The three treasures—ear, eye, and mouth—
Seal them tight, let no passage through.
The true person hides in the deep abyss,
Drifting and roaming, guarding the center’s rule.

Turning and bending to see and hear,
Opening and closing, all in accord.
They serve as the pivot and linchpin for the self,
In movement and stillness, never exhausted.

*Li*’s *qi* receives *rong* and *wei*,
*Kan* then ceases its hearing.
*Dui* closes, not with speech,
Silent words follow the primordial chaos.

Once these three are secured,
Relax the body in an empty chamber.
Entrust the will to emptiness and nothingness,
Take no-thought as the constant state.

Proof is unshaken by change,
The mind focused, not scattered.
In sleep, spirit and self embrace,
In waking, await the signs of presence and absence.

The complexion gradually moistens,
The bones and joints grow stronger.
Repelling all yin evils,
Only then is true yang established.

Practice without cease,
And the *qi* of clouds and rain will flow.
Gentle as spring rain,
Fluid as melting ice,
From head to foot it streams,
Then rises again in fullness,
Coming and going through the boundless void,
Pervading the valleys with subtle breath.

Reversion is the Dao’s verification,
Weakness is virtue’s handle.
Cultivate and clear the long-accumulated filth,
In the finest details, harmony is achieved.
The turbid is the path to clarity,
Prolonged darkness yields to brightness.

This chapter speaks of the key to the three treasures,
Inner reality matching outer response—
The essential work of nurturing one’s nature.

The three treasures—ear, eye, and mouth—
Seal them tight, let no passage through.
The true person hides in the deep abyss,
Drifting and roaming, guarding the center’s rule.

This section summarizes the essential Dao of securing the three treasures.
The cultivator of the Dao possesses inner and outer three treasures:
The inner three are original essence, original *qi*, and original spirit;
The outer three are ear, eye, and mouth.
To return the inner three treasures to truth,
All depends on the outer three not leaking—
As the *Yinfu Jing* states, “The nine orifices’ deviations lie in the three essentials.”
At the outset, one must withdraw hearing and dismiss sight,
Carefully seal the *Dui* mouth,
So the true origin does not leak outward.
Thus it is said: “The three treasures—ear, eye, and mouth—
Seal them tight, let no passage through.”

With the outer orifices unbreached,
The original spirit abides within.
Front and back converge,
And in the center, a positionless true person hides in the deep abyss.
The deep abyss is the North Culmen’s Great Abyss,
The dwelling of the celestial heart—
