The original pass, the one orifice.
The original pass lies at the center of heaven and earth,
The exact middle of the four directions above and below,
A suspended cavity, boundless in vastness,
Infinitesimal in smallness—
This is called the center’s rule.
Within it is a master,
Called the true person.
Guard it without losing,
Called embracing the One.
Yet its subtlety lies entirely in non-action and non-idleness,
Neither forcing nor forgetting,
Possessing the image of drifting—
Thus it is said: “The true person hides in the deep abyss,
Drifting and roaming, guarding the center’s rule.”
These four lines are the essential work of nurturing one’s nature,
The guiding principle of the entire chapter.

Turning and bending to see and hear,
Opening and closing, all in accord.
They serve as the pivot and linchpin for the self,
In movement and stillness, never exhausted.

*Kan* belongs to water, the original gate;
*Li* belongs to fire, the female portal;
*Dui* is the mouth, corresponding internally to the square inch.
When the adept enters the chamber,
They should withdraw sight and return hearing,
Turning the usual into the reverse.
The opening and closing of these gates
All correspond to the inner orifice of the original female.
Thus it is said: “Turning and bending to see and hear,
Opening and closing, all in accord.”

Within *Kan* is received *wu*,
Within *Li* is received *ji*.
*Wu*-earth belongs to yang and governs movement;
*Li*-earth belongs to yin and governs stillness.
Though *Li*’s one yin body is still,
In truth it is easily moved,
Restless and shifting, uncontrollable.
Only by relying on the true yang emerging from *Kan* to restrain it—
Like a door’s pivot, a chariot’s linchpin—
Can opening and closing each have their time,
Movement and stillness each their proper moment,
And the original *qi* not be exhausted.
Thus it is said: “They serve as the pivot and linchpin for the self,
In movement and stillness, never exhausted.”

Within the original orifice, the primordial ancestral *qi*
Originally was undivided chaos.
Alas, it first emerges from *Li*, leaking its brightness;
Then from *Kan*, leaking its hearing;
And from *Dui*, opening its gate.
All three scatter without return—
How much of the primordial *qi* remains?
Only by silently lowering the inner screen,
Repeatedly reversing hearing,
Can the *qi* of *Kan* and *Li* not leak.
Thus it is said: “*Li*’s *qi* receives *rong* and *wei*,
*Kan* then ceases its hearing.”
Guard within, forget speech,
So the *qi* of the *Dui* mouth does not leak.
Thus it is said: “*Dui* closes, not with speech,
Silent words follow the primordial chaos.”
This is what is meant by “The three treasures—ear, eye, and mouth—
Seal them tight, let no passage through.”

The secret here lies entirely in the “mouth.”
This “mouth” is the original pass, the one orifice,
Swallowing and spitting forth heaven and earth.
Since the celestial mechanism cannot be fully revealed,
It is provisionally taken as the *Dui* image—
Not the mouth of eating and drinking in the world.
Only with a true teacher’s guidance can its subtlety be known.

Once these three are secured,
Relax the body in an empty chamber.
Entrust the will to emptiness and nothingness,
Take no-thought as the constant state.

This section details the work of hiding in the abyss and guarding the center.
Once the three—ear, eye, and mouth—are securely sealed,
Not a single thread leaks,
Then one’s true person naturally remains undisturbed and unconfused,
Roaming freely in the deep abyss.
Within, it is empty and vast,
Devoid of all things,
Like an empty chamber.
Thus it is said: “Once these three are secured,
Relax the body in an empty chamber.”

The primordial one *qi* originally comes from emptiness and nothingness.
One must entrust the will to it,
Awaiting it in emptiness.
Only when the six roots are fully stabilized,
Not a single thought arising,
Can resonance be achieved.

Yet this so-called "no-thought" is but constant responsiveness within stillness, never straying beyond the proper measure—far from the dull inertia of wood or stone. This "thought of no-thought" is the true thought; when true thought stands ever-present before one, only then may the primordial one *qi* be attained, and the moment of obtaining the medicine arrive. Thus it is said: "Entrust the will to emptiness and nothingness, take no-thought as the constant state."

This matter is possessed by all, and in itself is no difficult attainment—like raising a pole and seeing its shadow. Yet the difficulty people face in attaining it lies precisely in the mind’s will not being concentrated, shifting moment by moment, scattering in a hundred directions, so that they gaze across the ocean and turn back. If, when entering the chamber, the mind’s will is single and unshifting, free from the malady of scattering, then it may be grasped in a single breath—what difficulty remains in attaining it? Thus it is said: "Attainment is difficult to shift; the mind, concentrated, does not scatter."

Once this mind remains unmoved, throughout the twelve hours—whether in motion, stillness, sitting, or lying—it never strays from the proper measure. Even when retiring at dusk to rest, as the primordial spirit naturally embraces the primordial *qi*, like a seed of fire within a furnace, one must remain ever awake and ever aware, inwardly illuminating the mind to observe the ebb and flow within the proper measure and to watch for the presence or absence of the true seed. Thus it is said: "In sleep, spirit embraces spirit; in wakefulness, one watches for its presence or absence." With such attentiveness, how can one fear the golden elixir will not coalesce or the true person fail to appear? This is the discipline of "the true person hidden in the deep abyss, floating and roaming while guarding the proper measure."

The complexion becomes increasingly moist,
The bones and joints grow ever stronger.
All yin evils are repelled,
And then the true yang is established.
If cultivation is unceasing,
The *qi* will course like clouds and rain.
Gentle as spring’s nourishing rain,
Flowing like melting ice,
From the head it streams down to the feet,
Then ultimately ascends again.
Coming and going through the boundless void,
Pervading the valleys with a subtle presence.

This section speaks of the signs of elixir formation. When the body and spirit, originally inseparable, receive the true seed, the inner and outer respond as one: naturally, the complexion becomes moist and radiant, the bones and joints grow strong, and the postnatal yin evils are expelled, establishing the primordial true yang *qi*. For within the body, all is yin evil; when the primordial yang *qi* arrives, yin evils naturally cannot remain. If cultivation is unceasing, its effects are divine: throughout the nine orifices, eight vessels, three hundred and sixty bones and joints, and eighty-four thousand pores, the great harmony of primordial *qi* circulates. One sees it move like clouds, descend like rain, moisten like spring’s nourishment, and flow like melting ice—from the Kunlun peak it descends to the feet, then from the Yongquan point it ascends back to the head, penetrating head and feet, coming and going through the boundless void without obstruction. For in heaven and earth, mountains, rivers, earth, and stone are all blocked and impassable, while only the hollow heavens and empty valleys are interconnected. The human body is the same: flesh and bones are blocked and impassable, while only the primordial apertures and empty valleys, vessel upon vessel, are interconnected, resembling the hollow heavens of creation. The primordial *qi* comes and goes, boundlessly pervading the empty valleys. Thus it is said: "Coming and going through the boundless void, subtly pervading the valleys."

This resembles the earlier passage: "The yellow center gradually opens its principle, moistening and nourishing the skin." Both describe the natural signs of the golden elixir.

Reversal is the verification of the Dao,
Weakness is the handle of virtue.
Cultivate to clear long-standing filth,
And in the finest details, harmony is achieved.
The turbid is the path to clarity,
Prolonged darkness leads to illumination.

This concludes by speaking of the golden elixir’s transcendence of the ordinary. What is reversal? The common Dao proceeds in accord, while the elixir Dao proceeds in reversal—overturning the Mysterious Female, embracing the One without separation, only then can one return to the root and restore life. Is this not "reversal is the verification of the Dao"? What is weakness? The strong and rigid are followers of death, while the soft and weak are followers of life. Concentrating *qi* to attain softness, becoming like an infant, naturally places the handle in one’s grasp. Is this not "weakness is the handle of virtue"?

Moreover, when yin evils are repelled, all long-standing filth within the body is thoroughly cultivated and removed. Once true yang is established, the primordial *qi* penetrates, and even the finest details are harmonized without obstruction. When the golden elixir first forms, the pulse halts and *qi* settles, returning to primordial chaos, re-entering the womb—this is the great death of the body. After a long time, one revives beyond all expectation, directly witnessing the original face, naturally attaining pure clarity, perfect wisdom, and all-pervading illumination, so that heaven and earth become a crystal palace. Thus it is said: "The turbid is the path to clarity, prolonged darkness leads to illumination."

The previous section spoke of the marvels of form; this section speaks of the marvels of spirit. Only when both form and spirit are marvelous can one unite with the Dao in truth. This chapter focuses on securing the three treasures, which is the first step in guarding the center and embracing the One—nurturing inner nature. It corresponds to the earlier chapter on refining the self and establishing the foundation.

**Appendix:**
Bao Yi Zi said: "When the ears do not listen, the Kan water within settles; when the eyes do not see, the Li fire within is nurtured; when the mouth does not speak, the Dui metal does not sound. Once these three are closed, the true person roams freely within."

He also said: "When the seven gates are returned, one nearly forgets life; all pulses sink, form and *qi* dissolve, strength fails to support, and one becomes as dull and turbid as if drunk. This is the verification of the Dao and the handle of virtue. Darkness is the foundation of brightness, turbidity is the source of clarity. From this point onward, perfect illumination, empty penetration, and spiritual communication all begin from darkness and turbidity."

Yu Yu Wu said: "Reversal means turning back. The verification of elixir cultivation lies in extreme emptiness and profound stillness, merging with heaven and earth, then the primordial *qi* returns from the first yang."

"‘Weak’ means soft and yielding. The handle of elixir cultivation lies in holding fast to one’s resolve and not violently disturbing one’s *qi*, being as soft and yielding as an infant—only then can one return to the root and revert to the origin."

**Chapter Twenty-Three: The Chapter on Heterodox Schools Achieving Nothing (Title follows tradition)**

The world delights in petty arts,
Not discerning the Dao’s depth or shallowness.
Abandoning the true to follow deviant paths,
They seek swift results but meet obstruction and blockage.
Like the blind without a staff to lean on,
Or the deaf listening to gong and shang notes,
Diving into water to hunt pheasants and hares,
Or scaling mountains to seek fish and dragons,
Planting wheat yet hoping for millet,
Or wielding a compass to draw a square—
They exhaust their strength and labor their spirits,
Yet through the years see no achievement.

To know the method of *fushi* (internal alchemy),
It is most concise and not cumbersome.
This chapter decisively declares the futility of heterodox schools.

Those who study the Dao must first understand:
The Dao and mere techniques differ as heaven from earth.
The complete cultivation of *xing* (nature) and *ming* (life),
Returning to the boundless, is called the Great Dao.
A single mechanism, a single trick,
Incapable of self-salvation, is called a petty art.

The Great Dao of the Golden Elixir is hard to encounter yet easy to accomplish;
All heterodox schools and petty arts are easy to encounter yet hard to accomplish.
Alas! The foolish people of the world have minds choked with weeds:
Not only do they fail to distinguish depth from shallowness,
Their vision blurred,
But they also cannot discern the true from the deviant.
Often they turn their backs on light to grope in darkness,
Abandoning the true to follow the false.
Originally seeking swift results,
They instead meet obstruction and blockage,
Forever severing the path to the Dao—
Is this not lamentable?

Unknowing that the precelestial *xing* and *ming* transcend form and instrument,
They mistakenly grasp at postcelestial essence and *qi*,
Groping within the body,
Lost and without trace,
Tossed about by others’ delusions,
Utterly without discernment—
This is like the blind without a staff to lean on,
Or the deaf listening to gong and shang notes.

Failing to realize that precelestial yin and yang are of the same kind as oneself,
They guess them to be worldly men and women,
Seeking them externally,
Drifting into licentiousness and depravity,
Harming life and corrupting virtue—
Nothing is more grievous.
This is like entering water to hunt pheasants and hares,
Or scaling mountains to seek fish and dragons.

Not considering that precelestial lead and mercury are originally without substance or form,
They go about burning thatch and playing with fire,
Refining mercury to transmute copper,
Deceiving and bewildering the foolish,
Ruining themselves and destroying their families—
This is like planting wheat yet hoping for millet,
Or wielding a compass while vainly seeking a square.

These heterodox schools expend a lifetime of effort,
Toiling year after year,
Yet in old age achieve nothing.
Instead, they slander the ancestral masters as liars,
Unknowing that the method of the Golden Elixir’s *fushi*
Is most simple and essential:
With *youwei* (action) to trace back to the origin,
And *wuwei* (non-action) to grasp the end,
Its function is one with the creation of heaven and earth.
Even the dullest of men, upon obtaining it,
Instantly ascends to sagehood—
How can it be mentioned in the same breath as heterodox schools and petty arts?

This discussion of the faults of heterodox schools merely outlines the general points.
To exhaustively examine them:
The Chan school has ninety-six kinds of external paths,
The Yuan teachings have three thousand six hundred heterodox schools—
A thousand differences, ten thousand divergences,
Impossible to enumerate fully.
Thus, the Patriarch of Zhengyang composed the *Song of the True Dao*,
The Perfected of Cuixu wrote the *Luofu Lament*,
And extending to Li Qing’an’s *Nine Grades Discourse*
And Chen Guanwu’s *Song of Resolving Delusion*—
All meticulously detail the errors of heterodox schools and external paths,
To awaken the deaf and blind of the world.
Alas! The world does not awaken,
And these deluded traditions continue to spread,
Increasing without end—
Truly pitiable.

The above merely sketches the broad strokes.
One should select the essential sayings of the perfected,
And compile a separate work, *Guide for the Lost*,
To be studied together with like-minded seekers.

This scroll focuses exclusively on *fushi* (subduing and ingesting), wherein the governance of governance and the nurturing of nature are already implicit—its meaning aligns with the preceding section.

**Chapter Twenty-Four: Flowing Pearl and Golden Flower**

The flowing pearl of the Great Yang ever seeks to depart from man.
Suddenly obtaining the golden flower, it turns and interrelates,
Transforming into a white liquid, congealing to utmost firmness.

The golden flower takes the lead; in but a moment,
It dissolves and transforms into water—jade-like horse’s teeth,
The Yang then moves to harmonize, nature and emotion in spontaneity.

Pressing upon the timely Yin, restraining and enclosing the forbidden gate,
The compassionate mother nurtures, the filial son repays kindness,
The stern father issues commands, instructing and admonishing descendants.

The Five Phases vie for dominance, each relying on the other to arise;
Fire’s nature melts metal, metal fells wood to flourish.

The Three and Five unite as one—the utmost essence of Heaven and Earth.
This may be transmitted orally, yet is difficult to convey in writing.

You must turn to the right, at noon rotate eastward;
Mao and You form the boundary, host and guest assume their names.

The dragon calls to the tiger, the tiger inhales the dragon’s essence,
Both feed upon each other, compelled to merge,
Then interlock and swallow, chewing and devouring one another.

When Yinghuo guards the west and Taibai traverses the heavens,
Wherever the killing qi descends, what can remain unoverturned?
The foxhound guards the rat, the sparrow fears the kestrel—
Each fulfills its nature; how dare they utter a sound?

This chapter speaks of the wood-nature and metal-emotion spontaneously uniting to accomplish the work of *fushi*.

The flowing pearl of the Great Yang ever seeks to depart from man.
Suddenly obtaining the golden flower, it turns and interrelates,
Transforming into a white liquid, congealing to utmost firmness.

This passage describes the union of the natures and emotions of two substances to form the golden elixir.

The primordial body is nature and life—this is Qian and Kun.
The post-celestial function is nature and emotion—this is Kan and Li.
Since Qian and Kun shattered into Kan and Li,
The function of nature and emotion became manifest while the body of nature and life receded.
Following this, one becomes ordinary.
Only when Kan and Li re-unite as Qian and Kun,
Using the function of nature and emotion to restore the body of nature and life,
Reversing this, one becomes sagely.

Within the post-celestial Kan and Li, body and function further divide:
True Yin and True Yang constitute the body, which pertains to water and fire;
The qi of the two meridians constitutes the function, which pertains to metal and wood.
This distinction must not be confused.

Qian pertains to the True Nature of the Great Yang, originally quiescent and unmoving.
Yet upon merging with Kun’s single Yin, nature transforms into emotion,
Becoming the wood-mercury within Li.
From this point, Yin essence prevails,
Li’s light flows outward in compliance,
Flickering and unstable, assuming the form of a flowing pearl.
Once Qian becomes Li, its single Yang enters the Kun palace.
Kun pertains to the Primordial Life of the Great Yin.
Upon receiving Qian’s single Yang, life transforms into nature,
Becoming the metal-lead within Kan.
This speck of golden qi’s essence lies hidden within Kan’s water,
Profound and unfathomable, assuming the form of the golden flower.

Li’s numinous substance constantly circulates, inherently prone to flee and difficult to grasp.
The more urgently one seizes it, the swifter it departs.
Fortunately, Kan’s single True Lead reverses its course to restrain it.
Upon encountering True Lead, True Mercury no longer flees.
Thus it is said: “The flowing pearl of the Great Yang ever seeks to depart from man.
Suddenly obtaining the golden flower, it turns and interrelates.”

When lead enters mercury, mercury relies on lead’s restraint,
While lead also gains mercury’s transformative power.
The two substances meet in the Yellow Chamber, merging into a single qi.
This qi first becomes liquid, then congeals,
Hence it is said: “Transforming into a white liquid, congealing to utmost firmness.”
White is the color of metal; utmost firmness is the nature of metal.
When metal returns to nature, the elixir is already formed.
This is the guiding principle of the entire chapter.

The golden flower takes the lead; in but a moment,
It dissolves and transforms into water—jade-like horse’s teeth.
The Yang then moves to harmonize, nature and emotion in spontaneity.

This passage describes the two substances spontaneously uniting and harmonizing in turn.
The Kan male takes the lead, the Li female responds.
Kan’s single Yang is inherently difficult to extract from the furnace,
Yet when the time arrives, it emerges in but a finger-snap.
Thus it is said: “The golden flower takes the lead; in but a moment.”
Metal born within water can itself transform into water—
The liquid of the golden flower is the True One’s water.
Vibrant and dynamic, formless yet generating form,
It gradually solidifies, resembling the jade-like teeth of a horse.
Thus it is said: “Dissolves and transforms into water—jade-like horse’s teeth.”

Once Kan’s golden liquid ascends, Li’s wood liquid follows to harmonize with it.
East and west, long separated,
Fortunately, True Intention draws them to meet in the Yellow Chamber.
Wood-nature loves metal, metal-emotion yearns for wood.
One leads, the other responds, arising from the spontaneity of nature and emotion,
Not something that can be forced by human effort.
Thus it is said: “The Yang then moves to harmonize, nature and emotion in spontaneity.”
The Yang here refers to the flowing pearl of the Great Yang mentioned above.
Externally Yang yet internally Yin, it readily follows things and flees,
Responding rather than leading.
Only when paired with the True Yang of the golden flower as husband and wife
Does it cease to flee.
At this point, it has transformed into True Yin,
Thus possessing the marvel of the wife’s path overturned—
This must not be overlooked.

Pressing upon the timely Yin, restraining and enclosing the forbidden gate,
The compassionate mother nurtures, the filial son repays kindness,
The stern father issues commands, instructing and admonishing descendants.

This passage describes restraining the two substances to meet in the Central Palace and produce the true seed.
Once Kan’s golden flower ascends, Li’s flowing pearl descends.
The qi of the two meridians unite in a single moment—
The time must not be lost.
One must press upon it with True Intention.
When the two substances unite, precisely at the midpoint between Xu and Wei,
One should seal the earthly gate and gather the true qi,
Allowing not the slightest leakage.
Thus it is said: “Pressing upon the timely Yin, restraining and enclosing the forbidden gate.”
Once the true seed returns to the earthen cauldron,
It relies entirely on the Kun mother of the Central Palace for warmth and nourishment.
Initially, the mother attends to the child, like a hen brooding her eggs, constantly embracing them.
Later, the child yearns for the mother, like the kind bird returning food, never parting for a moment.
Thus it is said: “The compassionate mother nurtures, the filial son repays kindness.”

Once the true seed resides in the Central Palace,
The outer defenses must be strictly guarded—
Firmly sealing the eight gates, encircling and closing them tight,
Not allowing the slightest slack.
This is like a child in infancy:
Nurture depends on the compassionate mother,
While discipline relies entirely on the stern father.
Thus it is said: *“The stern father issues commands, instructing descendants.”*

The compassionate mother is likened to the civil fire,
Warming and nourishing within the chamber of spirit;
The stern father is likened to the martial fire,
Fortifying the gates and barriers.
The filial child is the true seed—
The golden flower and flowing pearl,
The two substances conjoined.

From the pressing of timely Yin to this point,
All pertains to the work of the golden elixir,
Accomplished in a single moment.
The five phases intermingle in sovereign reversal,
Mutually sustaining to generate life—
Fire’s nature melts metal, metal’s edge fosters wood’s flourishing.
Three and five merge as one,
The essence of heaven and earth.
This may be conveyed through oral transmission,
But cannot be set down in writing.

This passage speaks of the marvel of the five phases in inversion during elixir-making.
In the common Dao, the five phases follow sequential generation—
Metal engenders water, wood engenders fire—
Flowing unchecked, vital qi inevitably scatters,
Bringing one ever closer to death,
Turning life’s mechanism into one of destruction.
This is what is meant by *“the living are the root of death.”*

In the Dao of the elixir, the five phases are entirely reversed:
The flowing pearl, originally the wood dragon,
Is drawn from the fire of Li;
The golden flower, originally the metal tiger,
Is drawn from the water of Kan.
Water and fire interpenetrate, metal and wood invert—
Only then can one return to the root and restore life,
Preserving it for kalpas without end,
Turning the mechanism of destruction into one of generation.
This is what is meant by *“the dead are the root of life.”*
Thus it is said: *“The five phases intermingle in sovereign reversal, mutually sustaining to generate life.”*

*Sovereign reversal* means the north-south axis of Zi and Wu,
Interchanging as the fundamental framework;
*Mutual sustaining* means the east-west alignment of dragon and tiger,
Establishing the weft of Mao and You.
By the logic of the common Dao,
Metal lies dormant in ore, unable to emerge on its own;
Wood, laden with yin qi, how could it flourish?
First, one must employ the fire within southern wood
To smelt the metal within northern water,
Refining the ore to reveal the golden flower.
Then, one must use the metal within western water
To restrain the wood within eastern fire,
Cutting away yin qi so that wood’s essence may flourish.
Thus it is said: *“Fire’s nature melts metal, metal’s edge fosters wood’s flourishing.”*
This is the essence of *“the five phases intermingle in sovereign reversal, mutually sustaining to generate life.”*

East’s three and south’s two combine to form one five,
North’s one and west’s four combine to form one five,
The true earth of the central palace naturally forms one five—
This is called *“three fives merge, south and north unite, east and west gather within the central earth.”*
This is what is meant by *“one, three, and five unite as one—
The most refined and wondrous principle of creation.”*
Grasping heaven and earth, encompassing the River and Luo,
Its workings must be transmitted orally by a true teacher—
How could they be committed to writing?
Thus it is said: *“Three and five merge as one, the essence of heaven and earth.
This may be conveyed through oral transmission,
But cannot be set down in writing.”*

This passage speaks of inverting the two substances,
So that the five phases return to oneness.
The *Model and Image* chapter of the final section states:
*“Originally but two things—
In the end, three and five.
Three and five merge as one—
All gather in a single place.”*
The verification is clear.

Zi turns to the right, Wu rotates to the east,
Mao and You form the boundary,
Giving rise to the names of host and guest.
This passage speaks of the separation between metal and wood,
And the need to apply the work of bathing.
By the logic of the common Dao’s five phases,
Wood is born in Hai, the thunderous wood arises from Kan water—
This is called *“the dragon emerges from water.”*
Metal is born in Si, the joyous metal is produced from Li fire—
This is called *“the tiger is born from fire.”*

In the inverted Dao of the elixir, it is otherwise:
From Zi, turning right to Wei,
From north to west, and thence to south,
With You metal concealed within,
The golden flower is produced in Kan,
Becoming the qi of the upper arc—
This is called *“the tiger is born from water.”*
From Wu, rotating left to Chou,
From south to east, and thence to north,
With Mao wood concealed within,
The flowing pearl is drawn from Li,
Becoming the qi of the lower arc—
This is called *“the dragon emerges from fire.”*

Yet when Zi is south and Wu is north,
At the moment water and fire intermingle,
One metal and one wood stand as boundaries between them.
Wood’s nature, in the east, serves as host;
Metal’s disposition, in the west, serves as guest.
Inevitably, nature and disposition become estranged,
Host and guest fall into discord.
At this time, one must apply the method of bathing—
Emptying all conditions, leaving not a single thread,
Preserving true intention within the compass,
Harmonizing metal’s disposition with wood’s nature.

When metal returns to the east, becoming host,
And wood returns to the west, becoming guest,
Host and guest exchange their names,
And the qi of the two arcs finally unite as one.

These two—nature and disposition—
In their mutual resonance, are husband and wife;
In their mutual generation, are mother and child;
In their mutual restraint, are father and son;
In their mutual exchange, are host and guest.
Ever shifting, unfathomable—
This is the essence of heaven and earth’s most refined principle.

The dragon calls to the tiger,
The tiger inhales the dragon’s essence.
The two feast upon each other,
Forcing their union,
Then interlock and swallow,
Chewing and devouring one another.
This passage speaks of the merging of the two substances.

Once the five phases are mutually sustained
And host and guest have exchanged places,
The wood dragon, now occupying the position of You,
Calls forth the qi of the black tiger;
The metal tiger, now occupying the position of Mao,
Inhales the essence of the red dragon.
Thus it is said: *“The dragon calls to the tiger, the tiger inhales the dragon’s essence.”*
Thereupon, their natures and dispositions become entangled,
Indulging in unrestrained union,
Feasting upon each other,
Merging as one.
At the moment of their union,
They interlock and swallow,
Taking into the mouth and forming the elixir the size of a millet grain.

This passage speaks of the mutual union of the two substances, emerging from the preceding discussion of their interdependence, and specifically expounds the meaning of "consumption." When Mars guards the west and Venus traverses the heavens, the killing qi descends—how could anything not collapse? The wildcat watches the mouse, and birds fear the goshawk; each follows its nature—how dare they make a sound?

This section describes the mutual restraint of the two substances. As the five phases clash in dominance, fire’s nature can melt metal, thus fire enters the western metal domain, forming the image of Mars guarding the west. Metal’s nature can fell wood, thus metal seizes the eastern wood position, forming the image of Venus traversing the heavens. Fire overcomes metal, and metal in turn overcomes wood, completing a rightward cycle where nothing escapes conquest—yet this is achieved through counter-restriction, employing only the mechanism of destruction. Hence it is said: *“When the killing qi descends, how could anything not collapse?”* When wood encounters metal and metal encounters fire, their natures and dispositions are naturally subdued, like the wildcat seizing the mouse or birds fearing the goshawk—at first sight, they are captured, and both fall silent without a sound. This is not forced silence but their inherent nature. Once each has attained its restraining nature, how dare they make a sound?

This passage speaks of the mutual binding and restraint of the two substances, emerging from the preceding discussion of the five phases’ conflicting dominion, and specifically expounds the meaning of "submission." For only when they unite without hindering their mutual restraint does the vital mechanism reside within the destructive mechanism. And only through restraint can they achieve union, thus reversing the destructive mechanism into the vital mechanism. The alternation of submission and consumption fulfills the great meaning of "submission and consumption" in the *Returning Elixir* chapter—this is abundantly clear, far removed from the so-called "ingestion techniques" of heterodox schools.

**Appendix:**
Master Baoyi Zi said: *“Human life resides in Mao. The sun rises in Mao, and the myriad things depend on it to live—thus all things rely on the essence of the great yang to establish their lives. The flowing pearl of the great yang is the treasure of life. Yet this life-treasure, when lodged in spirit, becomes restless and scattered in thought; when lodged in essence, it is preserved but difficult to sustain. Hence it is said to ‘constantly depart from man’—it must be subdued by the golden flower.”* (From *Chapter Twenty-Five: Discerning Authenticity and Falsehood*)

Without grasping its principles, one should not speak recklessly. Exhausting family wealth, leaving wives and children hungry and impoverished—from ancient times to the present, countless millions have pursued this path, yet few have met with success, and even fewer have achieved completion. Seeking rare medicines far and wide only leads one astray from the Dao.

If, however, one truly encounters the Way and perceives its beginnings and threads, then by analogy one may weigh the nature of things from start to finish. The five phases overcome one another, alternating as father and mother. The mother contains the nourishing fluid, while the father governs bestowal and reception; essence congeals and form flows, enduring like metal and stone without decay. If one discerns the true and does not leak it, one attains the correct Dao—like a pole casting its shadow at once, or a voice echoing through the valley. How can this not be wondrous? It is the supreme image of Heaven and Earth.

Yet if one takes but an inch of wild aconite or a tael of croton seed, swallowing it will cause one to collapse, unable to rise or bow. At such a moment, even if King Wen cast the yarrow stalks, Confucius interpreted the hexagrams, Bian Que wielded his needles, or Wu Xian beat his drums, how could they revive the fallen or restore them to swift movement?

This chapter speaks of the Dao’s truth and falsehood, urging one to discern the false and preserve the true.

Without grasping its principles, one should not speak recklessly. Exhausting family wealth, leaving wives and children hungry and impoverished—from ancient times to the present, countless millions have pursued this path, yet few have met with success, and even fewer have achieved completion. Seeking rare medicines far and wide only leads one astray from the Dao. If, however, one truly encounters the Way and perceives its beginnings and threads, then by analogy one may weigh the nature of things from start to finish. This passage speaks of the truth and falsehood in "submission and consumption," which those who study the Dao must discern early on.

The great Dao of the Golden Elixir encompasses Heaven and Earth, embracing the images of the *Yi*; its principles are vast, profound, and subtle. One must thoroughly understand yin and yang and deeply penetrate the workings of creation before grasping its mysteries. How could those who fail to grasp its principles dare to speak or act recklessly?

Those who speak and act without understanding often drift into the arts of furnace and fire, depleting their family wealth and leaving their wives and children starving, yet still they do not awaken—truly pitiable. From ancient times to the present, those who have loved the Dao number in the thousands of millions, but those who love it do not necessarily encounter it, and those who encounter it do not necessarily complete it. Seekers of the Dao are as numerous as ox hairs, while those who achieve it are as rare as rabbit horns. This is because they abandon their own nature and life, instead entering the mountains to seek mercury or digging in the earth for lead, collecting the five metals and eight minerals, mistaking them for the medicine of immortality—thus straying entirely from the great Dao.

When students seek teachers and inquire into the Dao, they must first possess a discerning eye. If they encounter a potential master, they must examine the origins and threads of their teachings to determine whether they are true or false. If the teacher is genuine, they will certainly have a thorough understanding of yin and yang and a deep grasp of creation’s workings. Simply inquire into the root of nature and life, the principle of like attracting like, and the reversal of the five phases from beginning to end—if not a single point is unclear, then the authenticity of the medicine can be weighed, and the authenticity of the teacher’s lineage can likewise be determined. Thus it is said: *"By analogy one may weigh the nature of things from start to finish."*

The five phases overcome one another, alternating as father and mother. The mother contains the nourishing fluid, while the father governs bestowal and reception; essence congeals and form flows, enduring like metal and stone without decay. If one discerns the true and does not leak it, one attains the correct Dao—like a pole casting its shadow at once, or a voice echoing through the valley. How can this not be wondrous? It is the supreme image of Heaven and Earth.

This passage speaks of the reverse overcoming of the five phases to conjoin the Great Elixir—precisely the visible thread of the true method. In the common Dao, the five phases take mutual generation as father and mother; in the Elixir Dao, they instead take mutual overcoming as father and mother. For without overcoming, there can be no generation—killing is precisely where life resides. Take metal overcoming wood: when metal stirs, it immediately generates water. Wood qi, greedy for water’s life, forgets metal’s overcoming. The overcomer becomes the father, and that which overcomes yet generates becomes the mother. Extend this to the five phases, and none is not so. Thus it is said: *"The five phases overcome one another, alternating as father and mother."*

The mother’s way belongs to *kun*, governing nurturance, its virtue lying in quiescent gathering. At the moment of union, having received the true seed from the *qian* father, it nurtures within the Central Palace, gradually forming the infant. Thus it is said: *"The mother contains the nourishing fluid."* The mother embodies the principle of greed for life and forgetfulness of overcoming—this is the *"compassionate mother’s nurturance"* spoken of in the previous chapter. The father’s way belongs to *qian*, governing inception, its virtue lying in dynamic directness. At the first encounter, qi spreads and essence flows, bestowing generative qi upon the *kun* mother—this is the true seed. Thus it is said: *"The father governs bestowal and reception."* The father embodies the principle of overcoming as generation—this is the *"stern father’s decree"* spoken of in the previous chapter.

One generates, one overcomes: bestowal congeals the essence of inception, while nurturance flows forth the form of sustenance. When these two wondrously unite, the true embryo is formed—this is the *"five phases interlocked in sovereignty, mutually sustaining life"* spoken of in the previous chapter. At this stage of practice, advancing without cease, the dharma-body attains endurance like metal and stone, imperishable. Only by discerning the true and remaining utterly steadfast, without the slightest leakage, can one achieve the supreme Dao.

From beginning to end, it is simply taking overcoming as generation that reveals the marvel of the five phases’ inversion. Once this marvel is grasped, the Great Elixir is immediately accomplished. It is like a pole casting its shadow at once, or a voice echoing through the valley—this is the natural image of creation’s workings. How could it not be supremely efficacious and verifiable? These are the proofs of the true Dao, its thread visible to the eye—how could heterodox arts and false techniques be confused with it?

Consider the wild aconite of an inch or the croton seed of a tael: once swallowed, the body stiffens, unable to bend or rise. At such a time, even if Zhou Wen were casting yarrow stalks, Confucius interpreting omens, Bian Que wielding needles, or Wu Xian beating drums, could they revive the dead and restore them to running and walking? This passage shifts to another analogy to demonstrate the wondrous efficacy of the ingested elixir.

People know only that poison taken by mouth kills and cannot be revived, yet do not realize that the Golden Elixir taken by mouth grants life that cannot die. Poison taken by mouth, though even sages cannot revive the dead, yet when the Golden Elixir enters the mouth, could even the Creator cause one to die again? Alas that people are enlightened to the one yet utterly benighted to the other! Once the Golden Elixir has entered the mouth, even if one were fed wild aconite or croton seeds, they could not kill. Thus the marvel of the five phases’ overcoming, congealing essence and flowing form, and the ingested elixir’s efficacy is like a pole casting its shadow at once, or a voice echoing through the valley—how can it be equated with false paths lacking the true seed?

This chapter specially distinguishes the true and false of elixir ingestion, opening a single eye for learners of the Dao through the ages, lest they be deceived by blind teachers.

**Chapter Twenty-Six: Lead and Mercury Conjoin**

The River Maid is numinous and most spirit-like. When fire is applied, she flies away, leaving no trace of dust. Like a ghost hidden or a dragon concealed, none know where she abides. To restrain her, the Yellow Sprout serves as root.

Things lacking yin and yang violate Heaven and contravene the Origin. A hen hatching her own egg—her chick will not be whole. Why is this? Union is not yet complete; the Three and Five have not merged; hardness and softness remain apart. The way of transformation follows Heaven and Earth’s nature. Fire moves upward in flame, water flows downward in moisture—not by any master’s guidance do they behave thus. The governance of inception is correct and cannot be altered.

Observe the moment of male and female union: hardness and softness bind inseparably, attaining their proper rhythm without any craft or control. The male crouches at birth, the female lowers her body, receiving qi at the outset within the womb—not only at birth is this visible, but even at death, they replicate it. This is not taught by parents; it is determined at the moment of union, fixed at the very beginning.

This chapter speaks of true lead and true mercury, two substances restraining and employing one another.

The River Maid is numinous and most spirit-like. When fire is applied, she flies away, leaving no trace of dust. Like a ghost hidden or a dragon concealed, none know where she abides. To restrain her, the Yellow Sprout serves as root.

This passage speaks of using lead to restrain mercury—this is the work of the Golden Elixir. *Li* is originally the *qian* body of Grand Yang, the origin of nature. Within it lies a single yin, the true water of *kun*—this is true mercury. As it encloses the feminine within the masculine, it is also called the River Maid. *Kan* is originally the *kun* body of Grand Yin, the origin of life. Within it lies a single yang, the true metal of *qian*—this is true lead. As it generates metal within water, it is also called the Yellow Sprout.

The River Maid symbolizes the postnatal heart. The prenatal nature is originally quiescent and unmoving; transformed into the postnatal heart, it responds to stimuli, pervading Heaven and Earth with utmost numinosity and spirit. In an instant, it traverses the four directions, past and future, without limit. Thus it is said: *"The River Maid is numinous and most spirit-like."* In terms of celestial divisions, *wu* belongs to the Three Rivers’ region, the abode of *li* fire. It also embodies the meaning of desire flowing with the current. The human heart is inherently numinous, but entangled with postnatal emotions and perceptions, it readily chases after things. Thus, when encountering circumstances, it stirs; when meeting conditions, it arises—ceaselessly turning, never pausing. This is just like common mercury: at the sight of fire, it immediately flees, leaving no shadow or trace, impossible to grasp. Thus it is said: *"When fire is applied, she flies away, leaving no trace of dust."* At the moment of flight, like a hidden ghost or a concealed dragon, even a sage cannot fathom its comings and goings—this is what Confucius meant by *"entering and exiting without fixed time, none know its dwelling."*

The River Maid’s division continues...

Thus it is said: *"Hidden as a ghost, concealed as a dragon—none know where it abides."* The River Maid is the yin within *li*, hence she takes the image of a ghost; the yin within *li* originally pertains to Wood Mercury, thus she also takes the image of a dragon. Such is the ease with which numinous mercury is lost and the difficulty of holding it—one must seek the method of restraint and subjugation. Only true lead from *kan* will suffice, for the single yang within *kan* originally emerges from pure gold, being of the same kind as oneself. Following it leads to the flow of emotions, but reversing it turns it into nature. When metal returns to nature, one reverts to the root and restores the origin. The Yellow Sprout then unites with the River Maid, like a ruler governing a minister or a husband restraining a wife, naturally remaining unmoved. As Zhang Pingshu said: *"One must seek the Metal Elder for restraint."* Thus it is said: *"To restrain her, the Yellow Sprout is the root."* This speaks specifically of the mutual restraint between two substances, sharing the same purport as the earlier "flowing pearl and golden radiance."

Things devoid of yin and yang violate Heaven and defy the Origin; a hen hatching eggs on its own will not produce complete chicks. Why is this so? Because union is incomplete, the Three and Five do not merge, and hardness and softness remain apart. The Way of transformation follows the spontaneity of Heaven and Earth. This passage speaks of the error in cultivating a single substance alone.

The Dao is called the interplay of one yin and one yang. Any thing that is purely yin without yang or purely yang without yin violates the principle of *qian* as the source of origination and *kun* as the source of generation. Hence it is said: *"Things devoid of yin and yang violate Heaven and defy the Origin."* When a hen broods over eggs, it first infuses a single point of true yang within, gradually incubating it to form a chick. But if there is only the female without the male, the chick will never form. Why is this? Because solitary yin lacks yang, and union remains incomplete.

In the Dao of elixir refinement, it is likewise: the East’s three and the South’s two must unite with the North’s one and the West’s four, the Four Symbols merging into two substances, which then converge at the Central True Earth. Only when like seeks like and the three and five combine is the sacred embryo formed. If the qi of the three and five fail to merge, it remains but solitary yin and orphaned yang, one hardness and one softness, each sundered and scattered—how then could the true embryo take shape?

For yang governs the bestowal of essence, while yin governs its transformation—this is the natural Way of Heaven and Earth, the interplay of one yin and one yang. Whether in the formation of a mortal embryo or a sacred one, the workings of creation are the same; it is self-evident that one cannot cultivate a single substance alone. Yet this very interplay of yin and yang is none other than the original true nature and true destiny of *qian* as the source of origination and *kun* as the source of generation. Only when both are cultivated and realized together is it called the Great Way of the Golden Elixir. To cultivate destiny without cultivating nature, or nature without cultivating destiny, is to violate Heaven and defy the Origin—such deluded paths often lead to the practice of "absorption and replenishment," which is no different from fleeing drowning only to leap into fire. Alas!

Fire moves and flames upward; water flows and moistens downward. Without a teacher’s guidance, they act thus of themselves. The source of origination governs correctness, and it cannot be altered. This passage speaks of the two substances merging, each returning to its original nature.

The functions of true yin and true yang are best exemplified by water and fire. Fire’s nature is yang and governs movement, and movement must flame upward; water’s nature is yin and governs flow, and flow must moisten downward. How could these be like sentient beings, who follow a teacher’s instruction to act thus? From the very beginning of their origination, the moistening nature of water and the flaming nature of fire were each firmly established in their correctness—how could they be altered later? Observe creation, and you will understand your own body.

The fire within *kan* in our body constantly seeks dryness and flames upward, inheriting the nature of the Father *qian*; the water within *li* constantly flows toward moisture and moistens downward, inheriting the nature of the Mother *kun*. As we have received these natures, so too must we return them to their origin. This is why the *kan* male and *li* female each return to their root, and why the Father *qian* and Mother *kun* each restore their beginning.

As *The Mirror of Entering the Medicine* says: *"Water can flow, fire can flame—within the body, this can be verified."* This is what it means.

Observe the moment when male and female unite: hardness and softness bind together inseparably, attaining their proper rhythm without any artifice to control them. The male lies prone, the female bends her body—this is how they receive the initial qi in the womb, not merely at birth when it becomes visible. Even in death, they replicate this pattern, yet this is not something parents teach them. It is determined at the moment of union, fixed from the very beginning.

This passage uses the union of man and woman to illustrate the return of *kan* and *li* to their root. To understand the principle of water flowing and fire moving, observe it in the ways of the world: when a man and woman unite, naturally the hard is above and the soft below, binding as firmly as if sealed by tally, unalterably fixed. How could this be the result of some skilled craftsmanship? It has been thus from the moment of their birth.

When a man is born, his body must lie prone—proneness signifies a nature and disposition invariably turned inward. When a woman is born, her body must recline—reclining signifies a nature and disposition invariably turned outward. From the moment of receiving *qi* within the parents’ womb, one hard and one soft, their physical constitutions are already fixed, merely becoming visible after birth. Yet this is not only apparent at birth; it remains so even in death. When people drown, men still lie prone and women still recline—this is not because parents have diligently instructed them to do so. Rather, at the moment of their parents’ union, the hard occupied the upper position, forming the image of *qian*-Dao becoming male; the soft occupied the lower position, forming the image of *kun*-Dao becoming female. The positions of male and female were already determined in the womb, and after birth, their reclining and proneness remain fixed, no different from the moment of their union.

Once one understands the ways of the world, one grasps the application of the Dao. The *qian* above and *kun* below in the primordial state are the father and mother of our bodies; the *li* above and *kan* below in the later state are the male and female of our bodies. Fire’s nature is to flame upward—this is the disposition of the *kan*-male. Water’s nature is to moisten downward—this is the disposition of the *li*-female. The dispositions of the *kan*-male and *li*-female are the dispositions of the *qian*-father and *kun*-mother. *Qian* is inherently fixed in the upper position, *kun* in the lower. When *qian*-father and *kun*-mother unite to form *kan* and *li*, their positions shift, but their dispositions remain unchanged. Thus, the fire within *kan* still strives to flame upward, and the water within *li* still strives to moisten downward, each seeking to return to its root and revert to its kind. When the *kan*-male and *li*-female unite once more, they naturally restore the constant order of heaven above and earth below, and the primordial *xing*-nature and *ming*-life are recovered.

The union of *qian* and *kun* to form *kan* and *li* is like the initial birth of male and female, one reclining and one prone—this is the inherent endowment. The reunion of *kan* and *li* to form *qian* and *kun* is like the death of male and female, still one reclining and one prone—this is the return to the origin. As it is said, “Each receives its beginning and assumes its proper place, never to be altered again.”

**Chapter Twenty-Seven: Refining the Hun and Po**

The *kan*-male is the moon; the *li*-female is the sun. The sun bestows virtue; the moon unfurls its light. The moon receives the sun’s transformation, and its body suffers no harm.

When yang loses its harmony, yin encroaches upon its brightness. At the dark and new moons, they press close and eclipse, covering and overwhelming one another. Yang’s form diminishes, and yin’s encroachment brings calamity. Male and female depend on each other, inhaling and exhaling to nourish. When the feminine and masculine intermingle, they seek their kind.

Metal transforms into water, and water’s nature flows freely. Fire transforms into earth, and water cannot move. The male moves and extends outward; the female is still and conceals within. If the measure overflows or the rhythm is transgressed, the female restrains it. The *po* governs the *hun*, preventing excess.

Neither cold nor hot, advancing and retreating in due time, each attains harmony and together they manifest the sign. This chapter speaks of the mutual regulation of the sun’s *hun* and moon’s *po* to form the Golden Elixir.

The *kan*-male is the moon; the *li*-female is the sun. The sun bestows virtue; the moon unfurls its light. The moon receives the sun’s transformation, and its body suffers no harm. This passage reveals the profound mystery of the sun and moon’s union and their inverted mutual employment.

In the alchemical Dao, *kan* and *li* are the medicinal substances—namely, the sun’s *hun* and the moon’s *po*. In the workings of creation, the sun and moon reflect each other, mutually containing the celestial *hun* and terrestrial *po*. In the human body, water and fire achieve harmony, mutually drawing the sun’s light and the moon’s essence. The principle of their mutual regulation is one and the same.

The previous chapter used the male’s prone birth and the female’s reclining body as an allegory for the two entities, *kan* and *li*. The male is positioned externally yet faces inward; the female is positioned internally yet faces outward. The marvel of their inverted images is already contained within. *Kan* belongs to the true water of the north, corresponding to the moon in the heavens. The moon is the essence of *tai*-yin water, an image of *kun*—it should be called female, yet it contains the *qian*-household’s true fire of *tai*-yang within its *po*, its *hun* within its *po*, taking the image of the Jade Hare, and so it is instead male. *Li* belongs to the true fire of the south, corresponding to the sun in the heavens. The sun is the essence of *tai*-yang fire, an image of *qian*—it should be called male, yet it contains the true water of the *kun*-palace within its *hun*, its *po* within its *hun*, taking the image of the Golden Crow, and so it is instead female. This is the marvel of inversion described in the *Wuzhen Pian*: “The sun resides in the *li* position yet becomes female; the moon is paired with the Toad Palace yet is male.”

The *li* body is originally *qian*. The *qian*-father moves and is positioned externally, yet when transformed into the *li*-female, its disposition turns entirely inward. Thus, though the sun’s light primarily functions externally, it constantly reflects back with *tai*-yin, gathering a single yang light within the yin *po*. The *li* body turns outward into inward, hence it is said, “The sun bestows virtue.” The *kan* body is originally *kun*. The *kun*-mother is still and positioned internally, yet when transformed into the *kan*-male, its disposition turns entirely outward. Thus, though the moon’s essence is primarily concealed within, it constantly responds to the *qi* of *tai*-yang, and the entire body of yin *po* borrows the yang *hun* for its light. The *kan* body turns inward into outward, hence it is said, “The moon unfurls its light.”

In terms of inversion, that which enters within is female, and that which extends outward is male. In terms of their original bodies, that which emits essence is male, and that which receives transformation is female. Though the two entities, *kan* and *li*, are inverted, they do not lose their original bodies. Thus, at the conjunction of the dark and new moons, the sun and moon meet along the ecliptic, merging in primordial chaos, forming a sphere of primal yellow. The sun’s *hun* enters the moon’s *po*, and the moon’s *po* receives it to form an embryo. When the sun’s light and moon’s essence unite in due time, matching their tally, the original luminous body suffers no loss. Hence it is said, “The moon receives the sun’s transformation, and its body suffers no harm.” This is the constant Dao of the sun and moon’s mutual influence.

The Dao of elixir cultivation follows the same principle. Within our bodies, the sun’s light and moon’s essence reflect upon one another moment by moment. When the sun and moon conjoin as a single disk, they give birth to the toad’s radiance, which becomes the root of the golden elixir. If yang loses its tally, yin encroaches upon its brightness; at the dark and new moons, eclipses occur, with obscuration and overpowering in mutual contention. Yang’s form is consumed, and yin’s calamities arise. This passage speaks of the loss of constancy in their mutual influence, akin to the earlier statement: *“When water waxes, it encroaches upon yang; when fire wanes, daylight dims.”*

At the conjunction of the dark and new moons, the sun and moon merge. If the yang *hun* can restrain the yin *po*, though it dwells within yin’s body, its luminous essence remains intact. But if the yang light cannot take command, sinking into the Northern Sea with no means of self-emergence, it loses the tally of their union, inevitably suffering encroachment by yin and the loss of its brightness. Thus it is said: *“If yang loses its tally, yin encroaches upon its brightness.”* Once yang is encroached upon by yin, it leads to the calamity of eclipses.

At the time of the dark and new moons, a single yang essence sinks into the depths of the cavernous void. With the fire’s power still weak and the water’s force waxing, yin prevails and obscures yang, while the surging water overwhelms fire, contending and seizing in mutual struggle. The great yang, which should shine at midday, becomes dim. Thus it is said: *“At the dark and new moons, eclipses occur, with obscuration and overpowering in mutual contention.”* The light of the great yang, born of metal’s nature, is originally round, bright, and universally illuminating, never diminishing. Yet once it suffers encroachment by yin qi, its form temporarily fades, and the calamity of eclipses arises. Thus it is said: *“Yang’s form is consumed, and yin’s calamities arise.”* This describes how the loss of the Dao in the sun and moon’s mutual influence immediately summons disaster. In human cultivation, this corresponds to the initial union of *kan* and *li*, when a single yang sinks to the sea’s depths. If, in movement and stillness, the slightest misstep occurs, the true fire becomes trapped in water, unable to emerge from the furnace, manifesting the phenomenon of eclipses. (See Chapter Fifteen of the preceding section for details.)

Male and female depend on one another, inhaling and exhaling to nurture growth. Yin and yang intermingle, seeking their kind. Metal transforms into water, and water’s nature flows unrestrained; fire transforms into earth, and water cannot course freely. The male moves and extends outward, while the female remains still and stores within. If the measure is exceeded, it is constrained by the female. The *po* restrains the *hun*, preventing excess. Neither cold nor heat, advancing and retreating in due time, each attains harmony, and both emit their tally’s proof. This passage speaks of the Dao achieved in their mutual influence, akin to the earlier statement: *“Yin and yang consume one another, and their mutual influence follows the natural Dao.”*

The *kan* male and *li* female depend on one another for function. The moon’s *po* draws in the golden crow’s essence from without; the sun’s *hun* exhales the jade rabbit’s marrow from within. Inverting host and guest, one inhales and one exhales, and the true seed thus flourishes. Thus it is said: *“Male and female depend on one another, inhaling and exhaling to nurture growth.”* *Qian* is originally old yang, yet transforms into the primordial maiden within *li*; *kun* is originally old yin, yet transforms into the yellow male within *kan*. Though *qian* and *kun* shatter their bodies, exhibiting the intermingling of yin and yang, the true fire within *kan* still seeks to ascend and return to *qian*, while the true water within *li* still seeks to descend and return to *kun*. Only through refining the shattered body into a pure one is this achieved—this is the principle of *“water flows to the damp, fire tends toward the dry,”* each following its kind. Thus it is said: *“Yin and yang intermingle, seeking their kind.”* Within our bodies, this is the work of *wu* flowing to *ji*, where like attracts like.

*Li* is originally the great yang of *qian* metal, but when a single yin emerges within it, the yang metal transforms into yin water—this is the so-called *“pearl flowing from the great yang.”* Its nature is to flow and wander, uncontrolled, inevitably flooding and circulating without restraint. Thus it is said: *“Metal transforms into water, and water’s nature flows unrestrained.”* Once the water within *li* floods, it comes to restrain the true fire within *kan*. Fortunately, the true fire within *kan* transforms into *wu* earth, which in turn can control water—this is the so-called *“yellow sprout as root.”* The *wu* earth within *kan* and the *ji* earth within *li* unite and settle in the central palace, so that the circulating water finds its embankment and dares not recklessly flow in all directions. Thus it is said: *“Fire transforms into earth, and water cannot course freely.”*

The moon’s essence of *kan wu* is originally dark and stored within, yet its great yang true fire inherits the nature of the *qian* father. Fire’s nature is to move, and movement should extend outward in function. Thus it is said: *“The male moves and extends outward.”* The sun’s light of *li ji* is originally ethereal and functions externally, yet its great yin true water inherits the nature of the *kun* mother. Water’s nature is to be still, and stillness should withdraw and conceal within. Thus it is said: *“The female remains still and stores within.”* This is the marvelous inversion and reversal of the earlier statement: *“The sun extends its virtue, and the moon unfurls its light.”*

Yet when the two unite, movement and stillness must each follow their measure. If yang moves to join yin and becomes excessively immersed, entering but unable to emerge, the great yang true fire suffers encroachment by the flooding water qi. This is like the union of male and female: if desire is excessive, the man becomes constrained by the woman. Thus it is said: *“If the measure is exceeded, it is constrained by the female.”* This is the verification of the earlier statement: *“If yang loses its tally, yin encroaches upon its brightness,”* and the omen of eclipses.

The yin within *li* belongs to the *po*, yet because it is the body of the great yang, it is instead called the yang spirit, the sun’s *hun*. The yang within *kan* belongs to the *hun*, yet because it is the essence of the great yin, it is instead called the yin spirit, the moon’s *po*—this is the so-called *“hun and po serve as each other’s dwellings.”* Now, with fire transforming into earth to restrain the unrestrained water, the *po* can control the *hun* and prevent excess. Thus it is said: *“The po restrains the hun, preventing excess.”* With *hun* and *po* mutually restraining, water and fire in balance, when a single yang moves to advance fire and retreat water, it does not err toward excessive cold.

When a single yin settles and advances water, retreating fire does not err toward excessive heat. Thus it is said: *“Neither cold nor hot, advancing and retreating in due season.”* When water flourishes yet does not exceed in cold, and fire flourishes yet does not exceed in heat, their qi harmonizes in equilibrium, forever free from the calamity of eclipses and obscurations. Thereupon, the sun’s radiance and the moon’s essence merge and converge, reaching the region of *geng*. The metal’s essence emits its light, and as the single yang receives the tally, the great elixir of golden cinnabar is produced. Thus it is said: *“Each attains its harmony, both emit the tally of verification.”* *Verification* means confirmation; *tally* means correspondence—rightly answering the sense of *“tally”* in the preceding text.

This chapter speaks of refining and tempering the *hun* and *po*, harmonizing water and fire, and the subtleties of inversion and reversal—these are the operations of the golden elixir at the crucible. It should be read in conjunction with Chapters Eleven and Fifteen of the preceding section.

**Chapter Twenty-Eight: The Three Families Meet**

Cinnabar and wood-essence merge when they obtain metal; where metal and water unite, wood and fire become companions. These four, in primordial chaos, are arrayed as dragon and tiger. The dragon, born of the odd number of yang, is yang; the tiger, born of the even number of yin, is yin.

The liver, blue-green, is the father; the lungs, white, are the mother. *Li*, red, is the daughter; the kidneys, black, are the son—the son is the beginning of the Five Phases. The spleen, yellow, is the ancestor. The three substances form one family, all returning to *wu* and *ji*.
*(The line “the son is the beginning of the Five Phases” was misplaced below “the spleen, yellow, is the ancestor” in common editions; it has now been corrected according to ancient texts.)*

This chapter speaks of the three families of body, heart, and intent uniting as one to form the elixir. Man is the heart of Heaven and Earth, thus able to establish the Three Powers and partake of Heaven and Earth. At the moment of receiving qi at birth, the primordial ancestral qi first enters the Central Palace, whole and undivided, in the chaos of the Great Ultimate—this is what is called the heart of Heaven and Earth. After the first cry, the Great Ultimate divides from the womb: above, the Celestial Pass is established, concealing the *qian* nature; below, the Earthly Axis is established, concealing the *kun* destiny. The empty valley lies between Heaven and Earth, concealing the original spirit. From the One Center, creation divides, and the Three Powers are thus fixed. Once the Three Powers are established, the Four Images immediately separate.

For *qian* is the ancestral nature of the prior Heaven, which breaks open to become *li* and transforms into the heart of the posterior Heaven. *Kun* is the original destiny of the prior Heaven, which solidifies to become *kan* and transforms into the body of the posterior Heaven. As for the prior Heaven’s *li*, it further transforms into *zhen*, with wood within fire—this is the image of the *hun* lodging in the heart. The prior Heaven’s *kan* further transforms into *dui*, with metal within water—this is the image of the *po* concealed in the body. From the One Qi, two bodies divide; from the two bodies, the Four Images further divide.

Once the Four Images are established, wood and fire of the east and south dwell together in the yang region, while water and metal of the west and north reside jointly in the yin position. The southern *li* fire, red in color, bears the image of cinnabar, within which true mercury is concealed—this is the wood-essence. It is like the northern *kan* water, where black lead conceals the metal-essence. People know only that fire contains wood, yet fail to recognize that wood contains metal. For wood flourishes in *mao*, and the qi of metal is already gestating within *mao*. The yang *hun* must obtain the yin *po* before its spirit has a place to return—metal does not depart from wood.

People know only that water contains metal, yet fail to recognize that metal contains wood. For metal flourishes in *you*, and the qi of wood is already gestating within *you*. The yin *po* cannot obtain the yang *hun*, so where would its spirit attach? Wood does not depart from metal. Though metal and wood are divided between the two arcs, *hun* and *po* are truly merged as one body. Thus it is said: *“Cinnabar and wood-essence merge when they obtain metal.”*

Heaven’s One generates water, whose image is the Dark Warrior; in man, it pertains to the essence within the kidneys, manifesting through the ears. Earth’s Four generates metal, whose image is the White Tiger; in man, it pertains to the *po* within the lungs, manifesting through the nose. Essence and *po* are both tied to the body, hence: *“where metal and water unite.”*

Earth’s Two generates fire, whose image is the Vermilion Bird; in man, it pertains to the spirit within the heart, manifesting through the tongue. Heaven’s Three generates wood, whose image is the Azure Dragon; in man, it pertains to the *hun* within the liver, manifesting through the eyes. *Hun* and spirit are both tied to the heart, hence: *“wood and fire become companions.”*

In ordinary people, the heart is merely the heart, and the body merely the body—water and fire do not interact, metal and wood remain separate, and so they drift ever farther from the Dao. Those who study the Dao, if they can in every double-hour contain their gaze, concentrate their hearing, regulate their breath, seal their tongue, and still the Four Great, causing spirit, essence, *hun*, and *po* to gather in the Central Palace, and water, fire, wood, and metal to merge along the Yellow Path—this is the image of *“the four in primordial chaos.”* Within this chaos, one must be able to merge the Four Images into one body, and also to divide the one body into Four Images.

Originally, wood and fire were companions, and from *li* the wood-fluid is born—this is the dragon emerging from fire. Originally, metal and water were united, and from *kan* the metal-essence is produced—this is the tiger arising from water. Thus appears the image of *“arrayed as dragon and tiger.”* As Zhang Pingshu said: *“The Four Images do not depart from the two bodies.”*

The dragon is born of Heaven’s Three, wood—is its number not odd? Odd pertains to yang, hence it is called the yang dragon. The tiger is born of Earth’s Four, metal—is its number not even? Even pertains to yin, hence it is called the yin tiger. This speaks of the original bodies of dragon and tiger. If the Five Phases are inverted, then the dragon becomes yin, and the tiger becomes yang.

The Five Phases of the elixir path are not tied to the five viscera. Master Wei, fearing the leakage of celestial secrets, spoke of the mother while concealing the child, and provisionally assigned the Five Phases to the five viscera within the body. In the common Five Phases, wood can generate fire, and metal can generate water—what generates is called father and mother, hence the image of *“the liver, blue-green, as father; the lungs, white, as mother.”*

Wood’s three and metal’s four—one yin, one yang. What they generate are called sons and daughters, hence the image of *“Li’s red as daughter, the kidneys’ black as son.”* Water’s one and fire’s two—likewise one yin, one yang. Why then is it said, *“The child is the beginning of the Five Phases”*? It is because Heaven’s one generates water, receiving it first of all. Heaven opens at *zi*, thus occupying the due-north position of *zi*, and is truly the source of the Five Phases. Thereafter wood, fire, earth, and metal are generated in sequence. Hence it is said, *“The child is the beginning of the Five Phases.”* The *qi* of the ancestral origin is concealed within Kun earth, from which metal, wood, water, and fire all emerge, hence the image of *“the spleen’s yellow as ancestor.”* Since water is the source of the Five Phases, it takes the meaning of beginning, which is also the ancestral aperture within our body. Earth is the mother of the Five Phases, thus it takes the image of ancestor, which is the center of our body’s ancestral aperture. The myriad transformations return to the One, and the One in turn returns to the Center—here one may awaken to the work of returning to the root and restoring life.

The *hun* of liver-wood and the *shen* of heart-fire both issue from the heart within *Li*, forming the true emptiness within the wondrous existence of the original state—these are one thing, the so-called *“east’s three and south’s two together make five.”* The *po* of lung-metal and the *jing* of kidney-water both issue from the body within *Kan*, forming the wondrous existence within the true emptiness of the original state—these are one thing, the so-called *“north’s one and west’s four share it.”* Within *Kan* lies *wu*, within *Li* lies *ji*; together they form the central earth, solitary and without counterpart—this is true intent. True intent is the original *qi* of the primordial *qian*, and is yet another thing, the so-called *“wu and ji return from the generative number five.”*

The households of body and mind are by nature difficult to unite; fortunately, true intent acts as the go-between, blending north and south, merging east and west, meeting in the yellow-earth crucible of the Center to congeal a single grain of golden elixir—the so-called *“the three families meet to form the infant.”* When the three things gather, they return to the One, and the One in turn returns to the Center—this is the Dao of returning to the root, restoring life, and reverting to the origin. Thus it is summarized: *“The three things are one family, all returning to wu and ji.”*

The postnatal body and mind are none other than the prenatal nature and life—this is the image of the Two Modes. The postnatal body, mind, and intent are none other than the prenatal original essence, original *qi*, and original spirit—this is the image of the Three Powers. The postnatal true earth is none other than the prenatal ancestral earth of *Fuli*—this is the image of the Supreme Ultimate. When the three things return to one family, it is the image of the Supreme Ultimate containing the Three as One. Has one not attained the embodiment of the Dao at this point? Standing between heaven and earth, one is utterly at one with the heart of heaven and earth. If one can take yet another step at the hundred-foot pole and thoroughly realize the original face before the birth of the body, one will know that though heaven and earth may decay, this does not decay; though emptiness may end, this is endless. Alas! Who can truly know this?

The author of this chapter has already slightly revealed the celestial mechanism; the commentator has fully opened a new perspective. May the reader possess a discerning eye and take care not to enter the mountain of treasure only to return empty-handed.

**Chapter Twenty-Nine: The Reversal of Punishment and Virtue**

Hard and soft alternate in succession, traversing the divisions of the phases. The dragon in the west and the tiger in the east establish the weft of *mao* and *you*. Punishment and virtue meet in joyful union: punishment governs restraint and killing, while virtue governs generation and arising. In the second month, the elm scatters its flowers as the *Kui* star arrives at *mao*; in the eighth month, wheat sprouts as the *Tian’gang* star occupies *you*. *Zi* in the south and *wu* in the north serve as mutual guiding principles. The numbers one and nine end and begin anew. Within *Xu* and *Wei*, essence is sown at *zi*.

This chapter speaks of the profound mystery of the dragon and tiger’s two phases and the mutual employment of punishment and virtue. In the elixir path, water and fire are the essence, while metal and wood are the function. *Zi* and *wu* fix the north-south meridian, *mao* and *you* move the east-west weft. Interweaving and interlocking, they correspond to the degrees of the celestial sphere. In terms of the constant Dao of creation, the heavenly Dao has one yin and one yang, the earthly Dao has one soft and one hard. Once the Two Modes are established, they intermingle to form the Four Images: *zi*-water resides in the north, *wu*-fire in the south, *mao*-wood in the east, and *you*-metal in the west. From *zi* to *si* is yang-hardness, moving through the southeast; from *wu* to *hai* is yin-softness, moving through the northwest. Divided into the twelve branches and further into the twenty-eight constellations, the 365 degrees of the celestial sphere each have their fixed positions. Only when the handle of the Dipper shifts in the center of heaven does *zi* rotate to the right, *wu* revolve to the east, hardness reverse to softness, and softness reverse to hardness—all fixed positions become unfixed at this point. Hence it is said, *“Hard and soft alternate in succession, traversing the divisions of the phases.”*

*Zhen*-wood is the dragon, originally dwelling in the east at the *mao* position; *Dui*-metal is the tiger, originally dwelling in the west at the *you* position. Yet when traversing the divisions of the phases, the dragon reverses to *you*, and the tiger reverses to *mao*. East and west form the weft of south and north, hence it is said, *“The dragon in the west and the tiger in the east establish the weft of mao and you.”*

The dragon bears the generative *qi* of the east, an image of virtue; yet when the dragon turns to the west, the *qi* of wood transforms to follow metal, and virtue reverses to punishment. The tiger bears the killing *qi* of the west, an image of punishment; yet when the tiger turns to the east, the *qi* of metal transforms to follow wood, and punishment reverses to virtue. The intermingling of metal and wood occurs in a single moment. If one understands the mechanism of reversal, then harm naturally gives rise to grace, and host and guest meet in joyful union. Hence it is said, *“Punishment and virtue meet in joyful union.”*

People only know that punishment governs killing, yet do not realize that the mechanism of killing is precisely concealed within the mechanism of generation. People only know that virtue governs generation, yet do not realize that the mechanism of generation is precisely hidden within the mechanism of killing. Hence it is said, *“Punishment governs restraint and killing, while virtue governs generation and arising.”*

At the time of the second month, when *mao*-Wood flourishes in full vigor, myriad plants burst into bloom—why then do the elm’s pods suddenly fall?
