This is because *mao* forms a union with *xu*, and *xu* is about to become the *River Chariot* of the west; the *River Chariot* now occupies the *mao* position, bearing generation within killing, thus manifesting the response of the elm’s pods. This is precisely the image of the killing mechanism lying concealed, where virtue reverses to punishment.

At the time of the eighth month, when *you*-Metal reigns in full strength, a hundred grasses wither—why then do shepherd’s purse and wheat suddenly sprout? This is because *chen* forms a union with *you*, and *chen* is about to become the *Celestial Pivot* of the east; it now holds the *you* position, bearing killing within generation, thus manifesting the image of wheat’s sprouting. This is precisely the image of the generative mechanism hidden within, where punishment reverses to virtue.

Once one thoroughly comprehends the mechanism of creation and transformation, one immediately understands the creation and transformation within one’s own body. Those who cultivate the Dao, at the time when the two crescents merge as one, must revolve the *Celestial Pivot* in reverse, rotate the handle of the *River Chariot* against its course, externally guard the six gates, and internally seal the cinnabar latch.

The cinnabar latch sealed, one washes the heart and bathes in purity—all in but a moment—naturally reversing punishment into virtue, killing into generation, merging the natures and dispositions of the two things into one.

East at *mao*, west at *you*; south at *wu*, north at *zi*—these are the warp and woof of the celestial sphere. In the cinnabar Way, the handle of the Dipper is rotated in reverse, so that the east-west weft, already contrary to the norm, forces the north-south warp likewise to exchange places. How is this known?

Yang is born at *zi*, thus the fire embryo resides in *zi*; yet the true fire of great yang within *kan* originally emerges from the south. Now, with *zi* turning right, it returns to the south. Yin is born at *wu*, thus the water embryo resides in *wu*; yet the true water of great yin within *li* originally comes from the north. Now, with *wu* rotating east, it returns to the north. One water, one fire—presence and absence intermingle. Though called mutual assistance, this is in truth a reversal of their origins.

With north and south exchanged, every celestial pattern and image in the heavens follows in inversion. Hence it is said: “*Zi* south, *wu* north, mutually serving as warp and woof.” The post-heaven Five Phases are employed in reverse, entirely based on the *Luoshu*. The numbers of the *Luoshu* begin at one and end at nine: the north’s *kan* position holds one, while *qian* in the northwest truly initiates the sequence, so the one yang of *qian* lodges within *kan*, and the one of *kan* is the beginning of *qian*. The south’s *li* position holds nine, while *kun* in the southwest truly concludes the sequence, so the one yin of *kun* lodges within *li*, and the nine of *li* is the end of *kun*. Now, with *zi* south and *wu* north, their positions exchanged, *kan* becomes the end and transforms into *kun*; *li* becomes the beginning and transforms into *qian*. One becomes nine, nine reverts to one—endless circulation. In the *Yi*, this is the image of *qian*’s primal yang employing nine, the host of dragons without a head; in the cinnabar Way, it is the work of nine revolutions. Hence it is said: “The numbers of one and nine end and begin anew.”

Heaven’s one generates water; the north’s *kan* position precisely aligns with the degrees of *Xu* and *Wei*, the root-source of creation and transformation. The two constellations *Xu* and *Wei* in the heavens lie between *hai* and *zi*, the place where sun and moon unite as one; in the human body, they correspond to the junction of *Ren* and *Du*, where water and fire simultaneously arise. *Xu* belongs to the sun, *Wei* to the moon—here true water and true fire mutually conceal their essence. As Bai Ziqing said: “Creation is soundless; fire arises within water—its wonder lies in the *Xu-Wei* cavity.” If the scholar of the Way can attain emptiness and guard stillness, return the light of the south’s *li* to illuminate the north’s *kan* earth, so that the primal essence within *li* and the primal *qi* within *kan* naturally nurture one another, then, upon reaching the utmost emptiness and profound stillness, heaven and human respond in unison. A single speck of true yang is born in the Northern Sea, and can be gathered as the foundation of the great elixir. Hence it is said: “Embracing the primal in *Xu-Wei*, sowing essence in *zi*.” This speaks of water and fire achieving harmony to produce the great medicine—it is the same work as the earlier merging of metal and wood.

For *zi* south and *wu* north mutually serve as warp and woof—this is the body of sun and moon. The dragon in the west and tiger in the east establish the weft at *mao* and *you*—this is the function of the two crescents. Yet the marvel of their merging lies entirely in the mechanism of mutually concealing generation and killing. It depends solely on the momentary reversal of bathing, instantly perfecting the work of harmonizing the four images. This should be studied in conjunction with the chapter on the three families meeting.

**Chapter Thirty: The Mutual Resonance of Yin and Yang**

*Guan guan* cry the ospreys,
on the islet in the river.
Gentle and graceful is the maiden,
a fine mate for the noble man.

The male does not dwell alone,
the female does not live in solitude.
Black Warrior of turtle and snake,
coiled dragons supporting one another—
thus are yin and yang made clear,
each naturally requiring the other.

Were two women to share a chamber,
their beauty most fair,
with Su Qin speaking persuasively,
Zhang Yi arranging the match,
their tongues sharp as braids,
eloquent in fine words,
opening their hearts in harmony,
vowing to be husband and wife—
even with hair worn out and teeth decayed,
they would never truly know one another.

If the medicinal substances are not of the same kind,
their names and categories different,
their proportions uneven,
their warp and woof lost—
though the Yellow Emperor tend the furnace,
Taiyi hold the fire,
the Eight Lords pound and refine,
Huainan blend and harmonize,
pavilions raised and altars erected,
steps and terraces of jade,
unicorn jerky and phoenix preserved meat offered,
registers held and long kneeling performed,
prayers and sacrifices to spirits,
pleas to the ghosts,
bathing and fasting observed,
vain hopes entertained—
it would be like mending a cauldron with glue,
applying brine to a wound,
adding ice to dispel cold,
using hot water to remove heat,
making turtles fly and snakes dance—
only to see things grow more perverse.

This chapter speaks of true yin and true yang, of like kind resonating with like, to accomplish the great Way of the golden elixir.

*Guan guan* cry the ospreys,
on the islet in the river.
Gentle and graceful is the maiden,
a fine mate for the noble man.

The male does not dwell alone,
the female does not live in solitude.
Black Warrior of turtle and snake,
coiled dragons supporting one another—
thus are yin and yang made clear,
each naturally requiring the other.

This passage speaks of the mutual resonance of yin and yang, each according to its kind.

“One yin, one yang—this is called the Dao,” as Confucius wrote in the *Appended Judgments*. “Partial yin, partial yang—this is called illness,” as Qibo wrote in the *Plain Questions*. For all the sages and teachers of old employed substances of true yin and true yang, of the same kind, to transcend the ordinary and enter sainthood. Thus the *Yi* begins with *qian* and *kun*, clarifying the unchanging body of yin and yang; the *Book of Songs* begins with the “Guan Ju,” illustrating the interchangeable function of yin and yang. Even in worldly matters: the osprey’s paired mating and the ode to a fine mate, the resonance of one female and one male; the turtle and snake, coiled dragons forming the Black Warrior, the mutual requirement of one yin and one yang. The turtle and snake pairing with the north’s Black Warrior is indeed the image of *kan*; the osprey pairing with the south’s Vermilion Bird is certainly the image of *li*. The union of heaven’s primal and earth’s receptive in our bodies, the coupling of *kan*’s man and *li*’s woman—how is this different? If one thoroughly understands the laws of the worldly realm, one immediately knows the laws beyond the world.

Should two women share a chamber, their beauty most fair, with Su Qin offering persuasive words and Zhang Yi brokering the match—flaunting eloquent tongues and weaving honeyed speech, baring their hearts to harmonize, vowing to wed as husband and wife—yet even with hair worn thin and teeth decayed, they would never truly know one another. This passage illustrates the folly of cultivating but a single element, for it is not the Dao.

In the Dao of the *Yi*, Kun pairs with Qian, Li with Kan, Xun with Zhen, Dui with Gen—each a union of one yin and one yang, each finding its counterpart, only then achieving the work of mutual resonance. Yet when fire ascends and marsh descends, Dui meets Li, two yins following one another, this is called the *Kui* hexagram. The Master’s commentary on it says: “Two women dwelling together, their wills do not move as one.” Thus, two women sharing a chamber, yin seeking yin—even if they deploy the tongues of Su and Zhang to broker a marriage—will never achieve lifelong harmony. This is what is meant by “cultivating but a single element,” an orphaned yin.

If the ingredients are not of the same kind, their categories disparate, their proportions misaligned, their guiding principles lost—even if the Yellow Emperor tends the furnace, Tai Yi wields the flame, the Eight Lords pound and refine, Huainan harmonizes the blend; even if they erect towering altars with jade steps, offer unicorn jerky and phoenix cured meats, prostrate themselves with registers in hand, pray to the spirits, beseech the ghosts, bathe in purity and fast in reverence, harboring vain hopes—it would be like mending a cauldron with glue, salving a wound with brine, adding ice to dispel cold, or using hot water to quench heat. To demand a tortoise fly or a snake dance would only make their perversity more glaring. This passage makes clear that disparate substances cannot fuse to form the elixir.

What, then, are the same kind? The life-stem within Li, the nature-root within Kan—only when one yin and one yang unite do true lead and true mercury emerge. Yet people fail to realize that true lead and true mercury arise in the primordial, possessing no form or substance. Instead, they seek the dregs of the posterior heaven: the Three Yellows, Four Spirits, Five Metals, Eight Stones—nothing they do not try. This is what is meant by “ingredients not of the same kind, their categories disparate.” Even if they recognize the ingredients, they do not know the methods of gathering, refining, and firing—this is what is meant by “proportions misaligned, guiding principles lost.”

Such benighted fools, refusing to seek a true teacher’s guidance, remain ignorant of the great Way of ingestion and refinement. They vainly believe that the false arts of the furnace and flame might by chance produce the elixir. Year after year, they toil in vain, squandering their wealth, even erecting altars to sacrifice to ghosts and pray to spirits, hoping for aid from the unseen. Yet even if divine sages were to tend the furnace for them, immortals to pound and refine on their behalf, every attempt would end in failure. Those external alchemical arts, their ingredients not of the true seed, their combinations not of the same kind, are like mending a cauldron with glue or salving a wound with brine—utterly dissimilar. Moreover, everyone knows that nothing is colder than ice, hotter than boiling water, that tortoises cannot fly, nor snakes dance. Yet to dispel cold by adding ice, to quench heat with hot water, to seize a tortoise and demand it fly, or a snake and force it to dance—how much more do such actions violate the mutual containment of water and fire, the restraining dynamic of tortoise and snake? Their perversity and contrariness defy description. How is the error of using the wrong seed any different?

The great Dao does not depart from yin and yang; yin and yang are nothing but nature and life. These two emerge as one yet bear different names, fundamentally the same path. In the *Yi* of Fuxi, they are one Kan and one Li; in the scripture of Laozi, they are one Nonbeing and one Being. At the root, a single phrase suffices to clarify. Yet later alchemical texts and philosophical works, with their myriad roundabout metaphors, have only opened the floodgates to confusion, leading many astray into heterodox paths.

The Dao of the elixir sometimes compares the process to man and woman, for Qian’s Dao gives rise to man, Kun’s Dao to woman—these are the numinous father and sagely mother within oneself, not the men and women of the mundane world. At other times, it compares them to lead and mercury, for the primordial essence within Li and the primordial qi within Kan are one’s true lead and true mercury, not the lead and mercury of the material world. Alas, the world is filled with those greedy for wealth and lustful for beauty, who either fall for the delusions of “gathering and supplementing” or the deceptions of “burning and refining.” Compounding the problem, the charlatans they encounter fabricate ever more bizarre claims, falsely citing alchemical texts to deceive the unlearned.

Those deluded by “gathering and supplementing” commit errors too numerous to list, but in essence, they mistake men and women for yin and yang, indulging their lustful desires. Those deluded by “burning and refining” stray in countless ways, but in essence, they mistake cinnabar and mercury for the ingredients, indulging their greed for wealth. Such heretical arts slander the great Dao of the ancient sages and sever the true cultivation of later worthies. Though they claim to study the Dao, they are in fact creating karma—undoubtedly planting the seeds of hell.

There are also those who grasp the principles somewhat more clearly and harbor slightly purer intentions. Fortunate to avoid the two perverse paths, they turn their search inward to body and mind. Yet they do not realize that the body is not the body of the Four Great Elements, but wondrous existence within true emptiness; the mind is not the mind of the fleshly mass, but true emptiness within wondrous existence. Body and mind are one, seamlessly merged—provisionally called the elixir. Alas, without encountering a true teacher, seekers remain blind to the great Dao. They inevitably mistake the illusory combination of the Four Great Elements for the body, the fleeting shadow of the fleshly mass for the mind.

Those attached to the illusory body often fixate on guarding essence and circulating qi, leaning toward contrived effort. Their error lies in seeking the body outside the mind, unaware that true emptiness *is* the body—and even the body they guard is not the true one. Those attached to the phantom mind often cling to seated meditation and entering samadhi, leaning toward non-action. Their error lies in seeking the mind outside the body, unaware that wondrous existence *is* the mind—and even the mind they grasp is false.

Yet they fail to realize: cultivating life without awakening to nature leaves one a fool whose lifespan matches heaven and earth; awakening to nature without cultivating life dooms the eternal yin spirit to struggle endlessly toward sagehood. How much less, then, can the illusory body and phantom mind—along with whatever single thing they cling to—be considered real?

Generally, each school clings to its own doctrine, failing to harmonize with others, all suffering from the illness of partial yin or partial yang, not the great Dao of balanced yin and yang. Master Wei composed the *Cantong Qi*, exhaustively exploring the nature and emotions of the *Great Change*, borrowing the imagery of furnace and fire, and sealing the宗旨 of the Huang-Lao tradition. It reveals nothing but two entities of shared origin yet differing names, enabling all beings on earth to fulfill their nature and realize their life-destiny, directly transcending to the other shore.

Yet one must guard against heterodox arts disrupting the true path, and carefully distinguish them; one must correct the deviations of the Hīnayāna, which loses the truth. Though I have earnestly expounded this in the final chapter on nurturing nature, I fear the world remains unenlightened. Thus, I reclarify the true seed here, shattering all devious bypaths, so that learners of the Dao across the ages may abandon the false and return to the true, discard illusion and attain reality—thereby matching the功用 of the Three Sages who unfolded the *Yi*, Huang-Lao, and scriptures.

**Chapter Thirty-One: The Success of Reclusive Ingestion**

Of old, the sagely and worthy, embracing the mysterious and holding fast to the real, refined the Nine Cauldrons in reclusion, concealing their traces and vanishing from the world. They contained essence and nurtured spirit, aligning their virtue with the Three Luminaries. Essence overflowed into the pores, sinews and bones grew firm; all perverse influences were dispelled, the upright *qi* ever abided. Through long accumulation, they transformed their form and became immortals.

Moved by compassion for later generations—those who love the Dao—they gathered scattered teachings, traced ancient texts, and composed diagrams and books to enlighten posterity. Yet they revealed only branches, concealing the root; they assigned various names and obscured the true meaning of texts. Scholars who obtained these works stored them in cabinets for lifetimes. Sons inherited their fathers’ pursuits, grandsons followed their ancestors’ paths, passing down confusion through generations, never gaining insight. Thus, officials abandoned their posts, farmers neglected their plows, merchants forsook their goods, and aspirants fell into poverty.

Deeply grieved, I have definitively recorded this text—concise in words and easy to ponder, sparing in affairs and free from tedium. I lay out its principles, verifying them for observation; their measures are precise, allowing for mutual progression. Thus, I employ disordered phrases to open its gates; the wise will reflect carefully and deliberate upon them.

Diligently practice this, day and night without rest. After three years of reclusive ingestion, you will ascend lightly and wander afar, unscathed by fire, undampened by water. You will master presence and absence, ever joyful and free from care. When the Dao is perfected and virtue accomplished, you will conceal yourself and await the time. The Supreme Unity will summon you, relocating you to the Central Land. When your merit is complete, you will ascend, receiving the register and talismans.

*(The fourteen lines beginning with "Diligently practice this" were mistakenly included in the previous chapter, "Discerning the False and Upholding the True," in common editions; I have now corrected this.)*

This chapter comprehensively presents the success of reclusive ingestion, serving as the summation of the middle section of the *Cantong Qi*.

Of old, the sagely and worthy, embracing the mysterious and holding fast to the real, refined the Nine Cauldrons in reclusion, concealing their traces and vanishing from the world. They contained essence and nurtured spirit, aligning their virtue with the Three Luminaries. Essence overflowed into the pores, bones and sinews grew firm; all perverse influences were dispelled, the upright *qi* ever abided. Through long accumulation, they transformed their form and became immortals.

This speaks of how the ancient sages attained the great Dao through reclusive ingestion. "Of old, the sagely and worthy" refers to the Yellow Emperor, Laozi, and all the transcendent ones who ascended in antiquity. "Embracing the mysterious and holding fast to the real" denotes the work of guarding the center and embracing the One, returning to the root and restoring life—this is the task of nurturing nature. Yet nurturing nature cannot proceed without the effort of reclusive refinement. In the Dao of the elixir, nine transformations complete the work, hence "refining the Nine Cauldrons." "Concealing their traces and vanishing from the world" refers to examples like the Yellow Emperor, whose elixir was perfected and who ascended at Mount Dinghu, or Laozi, who departed through the pass and manifested transformations in the West.

The original essence, *qi*, and spirit of humans correspond to the sun, moon, and Northern Dipper in the heavens. When these three are complete, they harmonize their virtue with the Three Luminaries. Thus it is said: "They contained essence and nurtured spirit, aligning their virtue with the Three Luminaries." The Yellow Center penetrates the principles, moistening and reaching the skin—hence "Essence overflowed into the pores, sinews and bones grew firm," describing the marvel of the form. Preserving the Great Harmony, nature and life each find their proper place—hence "All perverse influences were dispelled, the upright *qi* ever abided," describing the marvel of the spirit. After nine years of wall-facing, when practice is complete and merit fulfilled, one suddenly transcends the realm of form and *qi*, becoming a true person. Thus it is said: "Through long accumulation, they transformed their form and became immortals"—this is the state of both form and spirit being marvelous, merging with the Dao in reality.

Moved by compassion for later generations—those who love the Dao—they gathered scattered teachings, traced ancient texts, and composed diagrams and books to enlighten posterity. Yet they revealed only branches, concealing the root; they assigned various names and obscured the true meaning of texts. Scholars who obtained these works stored them in cabinets for lifetimes. Sons inherited their fathers’ pursuits, grandsons followed their ancestors’ paths, passing down confusion through generations, never gaining insight. Thus, officials abandoned their posts, farmers neglected their plows, merchants forsook their goods, and aspirants fell into poverty.

This passage describes how the ancient sages composed books to awaken the world, yet later generations lost their intent. The ancient sages possessed vast and magnanimous hearts, refusing to be mere self-liberated individuals. Having attained liberation themselves, they inevitably sought to liberate others. Compelled by circumstance, they wrote books and established teachings—such as the Yellow Emperor’s *Three Hundred Characters of the Yin Talisman*, Laozi’s *Five Thousand Words of the Dao and Virtue*, and all the alchemical classics and texts transmitted by the various transcendents. All were composed out of compassion for later generations of Dao-seekers who could not find the gate to enter. They specifically pointed out the root of nature and life, providing various supports and tracing them into diagrams and books to guide posterity and lead them through the gate.

Yet fearing the leakage of celestial secrets, they spoke of the mother without revealing the child, exposing the branches while hiding the root. Terms like the Three Thieves and Five Bandits, the Mysterious Female, the Bellows and Key, as well as Dragon and Tiger, Yellow Sprout, Golden Flower, and myriad other names—these are what is meant by "assigning various names and obscuring the true meaning of texts." Their true intention was for later scholars to repeatedly investigate and ponder, grasping the meaning while forgetting the images.

Alas, among learners, the deluded are many while the enlightened are few. They refuse to humbly seek a master’s true instruction, like bright pearls and great cowries locked deep in a chest, never to see the light—condemned to lifelong poverty, passing ignorance from father to son, from ancestor to descendant, age after age in delusion’s endless cycle, ever more lost, ever more confused, with no hope of awakening. Not recognizing the treasures of their own house, when will their poverty ever end? It is like an official who never serves, a farmer who abandons the plow, a merchant who discards his goods—thus the determined scholar suffers endless hardship at home. This is the parable of the *Śūraṅgama Sūtra*: a jewel sewn into one’s robe, unknown to its wearer, who begs for alms elsewhere.

Yet this is not the fault of the ancient sages. In writing their books to awaken the world, they wished for all to attain enlightenment—how could they have foreseen such delusion? As the saying goes: “The rivers and lakes bear no ill will toward people; it is people who cannot cross them and so blame the rivers and lakes. The patriarchs never deceived anyone; it is people who cannot penetrate the teachings and so resent the patriarchs for deceiving them.” If one wishes not to be deceived, one must seek a great guiding master.

I grieve deeply for this and have therefore recorded this text—its words concise and easy to ponder, its matters streamlined and unburdensome. Its principles are laid out in order, verifiable and observable, their measures precise and interlinked. Thus, I have employed disordered phrases to obscure, yet left openings in the gate. Let the wise reflect carefully and contemplate its intent.

This passage expresses my intent in transmitting the ancient sages’ writings to awaken the world. Later learners fail to grasp the great Dao of the ancient sages simply because they cannot find the gate to enter. The Immortal Elder, moved by compassion for later learners, composed the *Cantong Qi* (*The Seal of the Unity of the Three*), expanding upon the images of Qian and Kun, Kan and Li from the *Book of Changes*, borrowing the alchemical terms of Dragon and Tiger, Lead and Mercury, and returning to the essence of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi’s teaching on fulfilling one’s nature and perfecting life. Its language is simple and essential, hence its words are concise and easy to ponder; its purport is unified, hence its matters are streamlined and uncluttered. “Laying out its principles in order” means dividing the one Dao into three schools, thus revealing the branches. “Verifiable and observable” means the three schools originally stem from one Dao, thus concealing the root. Yet the artistry of its composition lies in revealing without fully revealing, concealing without fully concealing—each measure and weight precisely balanced, all drawn from the ancient sages’ texts and refined through careful consideration, making it easier for learners to explore.

If too much is revealed, the celestial mechanism may be leaked; thus, disordered phrases must be used. If too much is concealed, the celestial Dao may be obstructed; thus, openings in the gate must be left. Should there be a person of discerning eyes in the world who, through repeated study of these three chapters, penetrates to the openings within, they will realize that the great Dao lies hidden in the chest before their eyes—possessed by all, with no one rich or poor.

The Immortal Elder’s compassion for later learners truly aligns with the Yellow Emperor, Laozi, and all the supreme sages across ages. The *Cantong Qi*, compared to the *Three Hundred Words of the Yin符* or the *Five Thousand Characters of the Dao and Virtue*, is even more detailed in its elaboration. Diligently practice it, day and night without rest. After three years of refining the elixir, you will ascend and travel afar, walk through fire unscorched, enter water undrenched, able to appear or vanish, ever joyful and free from care.

When the Dao is perfected and virtue accomplished, remain hidden and await the time. The Supreme One will summon you, and you will be moved to the Central Continent. When your merit is complete, you will ascend, receive the registers, and be granted the talismans.

This passage speaks of the scholar who delves into the profundities of the *Cantong Qi*, refines the elixir, and attains immortality. The great Dao requires both knowledge and practice to be fully realized. Initially, one may be benighted and unaware; once aware, one may fail to practice; once practicing, one may lack diligence. When a learner receives true instruction from a master and thoroughly understands the principles of elixir refinement, they should gather companions, enter the furnace, and forge with unwavering resolve. As Laozi said: “The superior person, hearing the Dao, diligently practices it.” Master Ma Danyang said: “The debt to the master’s kindness is deep and hard to repay; I vow to refine within the furnace until the true is attained.” Thus, “day and night without rest” is what constitutes diligent practice.

The work of refining the elixir may yield the pill in a single moment, yet establishing the foundation generally takes a hundred days, gestating the embryo roughly ten months, and nurturing it with warmth and care approximately three years. As Chen Cuixu said: “A moment’s effort may suffice for cultivation, but maturity requires three years.” Yet one must not rigidly fix the three-year limit—it depends on the diligence or indolence of one’s practice. Once the nurturing is complete, the sacred embryo is perfected, and one can ascend and travel afar.

From this point, the dharma-body is liberated, moving freely and unobstructed. Fire cannot burn it, water cannot drown it. It may conceal or manifest, appear or vanish—appearing, it takes form; vanishing, it leaves no trace. Unhindered in coming and going, how can one not be “ever joyful and free from care”? Embracing the primordial and holding the true is called the Dao; accumulating merit and piling up deeds is called virtue. Only when both are fully realized can one roam playfully among mortals, awaiting the time of ascension. Thus it is said: “When the Dao is perfected and virtue accomplished, remain hidden and await the time.”

Beyond the world of dust lie the Four Seas; within the Four Seas are the Three Isles; within the Three Isles are the Ten Continents. The upper isles are Penglai, Fangzhang, and Yingzhou; the middle isles are Furong, Langyuan, and Yaochi; the lower isles are Chicheng, Yuan’guan, and Taoyuan. At their center lies a continent called the Purple Court, the abode of the Supreme One, the sovereign who oversees the merits and deeds of immortals. When one sheds the mortal shell and ascends as an immortal, they first meet the Supreme One, who examines their merits and deeds before granting them gradual ascent. Thus it is said: “The Supreme One will summon you, and you will be moved to the Central Continent.”

When merits fill the Three Thousand of the Great Veil, one becomes an immortal; when deeds fulfill the Eight Hundred of the Great Veil, one becomes a guest. Then, drifting upward, they "receive the register and accept the talisman," attaining the rank of the Supreme Perfected. Thus it is said: "When merits are complete, one ascends; receiving the register and accepting the talisman."

Though this is but a provisional analogy to guide the middle and lower streams, lest they fall into nihilistic views. In truth, the Central Continent is one’s own cinnabar chamber; the Supreme One is one’s own primordial spirit; ascent is one’s own heaven; receiving the register and accepting the talisman is the restoration of one’s original celestial countenance, no longer drifting or sinking with the kalpa’s fire. If one thoroughly comprehends the essentials of refining spirit to merge with emptiness and refining emptiness to unite with the Dao, then all ascents and talisman-receiving may be likened to floating clouds, entrusted to the vastness of space. Is this not the unspoken intent of Master Wei?

Though this chapter concludes the success of the "embryo-ingestion" practice, it serves as the summation of the entire middle section. For the "Governing the Polity" chapters merely expound the imagery of creation and transformation, without touching upon the subtleties of nature and life; the "Nurturing Nature" chapters only point to the gates of nature and life, without exhausting the functions of elixir-making. From "The Flowing Pearl of the Great Yang" onward, seven chapters fully set forth the work of embryo-ingestion—some speak of gathering, some of conjoining, some of refinement. What the first section left unsaid is here revealed without remainder.

As the section draws to a close, the author states his purpose in writing: to seal the wisdom of the ancient sages above and enlighten later worthies below. Those who follow it shall instantly become immortals and patriarchs—how could this not be firmly credible? Here, the meaning echoes the closing lines of the first section, "I dare not speak falsely, but emulate the sages’ words," confirming this as the summation of the middle section. Yet common editions mistakenly place it at the start of the lower section.

As for the passage on diligent practice, it rightly concludes this chapter, though common editions misplace it at the end of the "Discerning the Orthodox and Heterodox" section in the first part—an even greater error. Here, it is restored to its proper place according to the ancient text.

**The Lower Section (Also titled *The Three Categories* or *Supplementing Omissions and Filling Gaps*)**
The first and middle sections each divide into three parts—"Governing the Polity," "Nurturing Nature," and "Embryo-Ingestion"—though their structure appears scattered and disjointed. This section unifies their threads, bringing the three into one category. The first chapter expounds the marvelous workings of the crucible; the second reveals the full efficacy of the firing times; the third clarifies that the Three Ways arise from one, showing how divergent paths return to the same source; the fourth directly points to the return of the Four Images to emptiness, thereby grasping the primordial, limitless Daoist principle; the final chapter recounts the author’s intent in writing, concluding with a veiled name. The five chapters form a coherent whole, and the great meaning of the *Three Categories* becomes clear. What was missing or incomplete in the first two sections is here resolved without remainder. Readers should study this alongside the previous sections to grasp the underlying structure and avoid the flaw of incompleteness.

**Chapter Thirty-Two: The Marvelous Workings of the Crucible**
Round as three and five, a tenth of an inch in division;
The mouth, four by eight, the lips two inches wide.
Twelve inches long, thickness evenly measured.
The belly aligned with three, seated and warmly suspended.
Yin above, Yang rushing below.
Hard at the start and end, gentle in the middle.
Beginning with seventy, ending at thirty days,
Two hundred and six—skillfully harmonized.
Yin fire white, lead of the yellow sprout,
Two sevens gathered, aiding the human.
Gaze at the brain’s principle, determine the ascending mystery.
The child dwells within, secure and preserved.
Coming and going in play, never leaving the gate.
Gradually growing great, nature and emotion pure.
Returning to the One, restoring the original source.
Cherish reverence, like lord and minister.
After one cycle, the toil is intense.
Guard it closely, lest confusion arise.
The path is distant, again profound and obscure.
If you attain this, you fathom Heaven and Earth.
The elixir’s touch purifies the hun and po.
Attaining longevity, dwelling in the immortal village.
Those who delight in the Dao seek its root.
Examine the Five Phases, fix the weights and measures.
Contemplate it deeply—no need for discourse.
Guard it in secrecy, do not transmit the text.
Ride the white crane, mount the dragon’s scales,
Roam the Great Void, visit the immortal lords,
Receive the celestial charts, be titled a Perfected One.

This chapter, though speaking of the wondrous workings of the crucible and furnace, already encompasses the ingredients and firing times within it—thus serving as the summation of the entire *Cantong Qi*.

The marvel of the golden elixir lies wholly in the crucible and furnace. Only by recognizing the crucible and furnace can one gather the ingredients; only by recognizing the ingredients can one apply the fire for refinement. These three are originally of the same thread and mutually interconnected. In the preceding two sections, they were separately addressed as governing governance, nurturing nature, and subduing consumption, with the three concealed within. Yet their meanings were scattered and not yet unified, so Master Wei specially composed this song to bind them together.

**"Round the Three and Five, one inch and a fraction,
Mouth of Four and Eight, lips of two inches,
Twelve inches long, thickness evenly balanced."**

This passage reveals the symbolic forms of the crucible and furnace. Their use has two aspects:

In terms of the golden elixir, the frame of *Li* (☲) is the suspended-embryo crucible, and the frame of *Kan* (☵) is the inverted-moon furnace. The central palace of the spirit-chamber is the human position—this is the symbolic form of the small crucible and furnace.

In terms of the returned elixir, the *Qian* (☰) position above serves as the crucible for congealing the elixir, while the *Kun* (☷) position below serves as the furnace for producing the medicine. The Yellow Court of the central palace is the human position—this is the symbolic form of the great crucible and furnace.

Generally, each has three layers—upper, middle, and lower—to correspond with the Three Powers of Heaven, Earth, and Humanity. Once the crucible and furnace are established, the Two Modes, Four Images, Five Phases, Eight Trigrams, twelve earthly branches, twenty-eight constellations, and the 365 degrees of the celestial circuit all emerge from within them.

The furnace and crucible take their model from *Qian* and *Kun*: round to symbolize Heaven, square to symbolize Earth.

**Round to symbolize Heaven**—this is the principle of the circular vessel. Roundness has a diameter of one and a circumference of three, derived from the *River Chart*. The *River Chart* has no corners in its circumference: east (3) and south (2) combine to form one five, north (1) and west (4) combine to form another five, while the central *wu-ji* naturally forms a third five. Together, the Three Fives form a circle. The Three Fives encircle and return to the center, where the empty position of the center has a diameter of no more than an inch—this is the chamber where the Heart of Heaven resides. Within this inch, *Qian* and *Kun* are divided, as Shaozi said: *"Heaven, from the One at the center, divides the workings of creation."* Hence it is said: **"Round the Three and Five, one inch and a fraction."**

**Square to symbolize Earth**—this is the principle of the square vessel. Squareness has a diameter of one and a circumference of four, derived from the *Luo Writing*. The *Luo Writing* has four cardinal and four diagonal directions: east, south, west, and north are the four cardinals (the Four Images), while southeast, southwest, northeast, and northwest are the four diagonals. The four cardinals together with the four diagonals form the Eight Trigrams. The division between *zi* and *wu* separates north and south—the Two Modes. The opening in the center of the square vessel resembles a mouth; the boundary between the upper and lower cauldrons resembles lips. The Four Images and Eight Trigrams are arranged around the four sides, corresponding to the four seasons and eight solar terms of creation. *Qian* above and *Kun* below evenly divide the Two Modes, corresponding to the north and south poles of creation—all derived from the division of the One at the center. Hence it is said: **"Mouth of Four and Eight, lips of two inches."**

Once the Two Modes are divided, from *zi* to *si* are the six yangs, corresponding to the spring and summer of creation—this is the time for advancing the fire. From *wu* to *hai* are the six yins, corresponding to the autumn and winter of creation—this is the time for retreating the fire. The cycle of a year corresponds to that of a month, and the cycle of a month to that of a day. Hard and soft are not biased; cold and heat align with the seasons—just as the earlier section states: *"Revolving through the twelve nodes, when the cycle ends, renew the closeness."* Hence it is said: **"Twelve inches long, thickness evenly balanced."**

The workings of the crucible and furnace are drawn from afar in creation and from near in our own bodies—both belong to the natural symbolic forms. All heterodox schools, ignorant of the wondrous subtleties, vainly seek the crucible and furnace outside the body—this is no different from scaling a mountain ten thousand *li* away!

**"Abdomen aligned with three, seated to maintain warmth.
Yin above, yang rushes below.
Beginning and end with martial fire, the middle with civil.
Starting at seventy, ending at three decades,
Two hundred and six, skillfully harmonized."**

This passage speaks of the time when the medicine is born in the furnace and the fire must be carefully regulated.

Within the square inch of the central chamber lies an opening, vast and boundless, resembling an abdomen. When the water and fire *qi* converge in the central palace, the Three Families meet. At the moment of their union, one should sit and guard the central yellow, neither forgetting nor forcing, awaiting the spontaneous arrival of spirit illumination. Only when the water and fire *qi* are harmoniously balanced does warmth naturally arise, and the true seed spontaneously grow. Hence it is said: **"Abdomen aligned with three, seated to maintain warmth."**

The fire of *Li* is originally above, yet the true water within *Li* constantly flows downward to return to *wu*. The water of *Kan* is originally below, yet the true fire within *Kan* constantly rushes upward to join *ji*. All depends on the true earth in the middle for regulation. Hence it is said: **"Yin above, yang rushes below."**

This describes the state of water and fire in mutual completion, when the great medicine is about to be produced. The medicine in the furnace relies entirely on the fire for refinement, yet the firing times include both martial and civil fires. Martial fire governs refinement, while civil fire governs bathing—these two uses are as far apart as heaven and earth.

At the *zi* hour, the tail of yin and the head of yang, one should advance the fire and retreat the water. At the *wu* hour, the tail of yang and the head of yin, one should advance the water and retreat the fire—both employ martial fire. Only during the *mao* and *you* hours in the middle, at the time of bathing, is civil fire used alone. The beginning and end, evenly dividing *Kan* and *Li*, harmonize the two families without departing from the true earth in the middle. Hence it is said: **"Beginning and end with martial fire, the middle with civil."**

At the winter solstice, the first yang stirs—this is truly the beginning of the six yangs. Stillness reaches its extreme and gives birth to movement, resembling the seven-day return. Hence it is said: **"Starting at seventy."** At the summer solstice, the first yin settles—this gradually leads to the end of the six yins. Movement reaches its extreme and returns to stillness, resembling the cycle from new moon to dark moon. Hence it is said: **"Ending at three decades."**

At the beginning, one must engage in "wild battle"; at the end, one guards the city—both employ martial fire, which is precisely what is meant by **"beginning and end with martial fire."** The full three hundred sixty days correspond to the degrees of the celestial revolution. Excluding the seventy-three ten-day periods (a total of one hundred days) at the beginning and end, the remaining two hundred sixty days are divided equally into yin and yang over two hundred days. One *zi* and one *wu* correspond to the winter and summer solstices, and together with one beginning and one end, they make three hundred days, exactly matching the ten-month gestation period for the embryo’s completion. The remaining sixty days correspond precisely to the two months of *mao* and *you*, one *mao* and one *you* aligning with the spring and autumn equinoxes—this is the time for bathing. Hence it is said: **"Two hundred six, well-balanced."** "Well-balanced" means neither cold nor hot, gently harmonized to the mean, which is what is meant by **"the middle with civil fire."**

One must understand that martial fire for refinement enters at the juncture of one south and one north, while civil fire for bathing is entirely a matter of inner guarding within the central palace. Thoughts must not arise, and intention must not scatter—the subtlety of the fire timing lies in this very moment. The Perfected One of Purple Yang said: **"The fire timing is not bound by hours; the winter solstice is not confined to *zi*. As for the bathing method, *mao* and *you* are but empty comparisons."** This is what is meant.

Yin fire is white; the yellow sprout is lead. The two sevens gather, assisting and aiding the person. Gazing at the brain’s principle, one stabilizes the ascent of the mysterious. At *zi*, abiding in the center, one attains peace and preservation. Coming and going in roaming, yet never leaving the gate. Gradually growing great, emotions and nature become pure. Returning to the One, one restores the original source.

This passage speaks of the initial formation of the Golden Elixir and the work of gentle nurturing within the crucible. The true mercury within *Li* (Fire) is the yin fire, yet it emerges transformed from the vastness of dry metal—an image of whiteness containing blackness. Thus it is said: **"Yin fire is white."** The true lead within *Kan* (Water) is the yellow sprout, yet it bursts forth from the womb of *Kun* (Earth)—an image of lead giving birth to gold. Thus it is said: **"The yellow sprout is lead."**

Seven is the complete number of fire. Since the flowing pearl within *Li* is called yin fire, the yellow sprout within *Kan* is called yang fire. When the two fires converge, they nurture the true person within the divine chamber, as if providing aid and wings. Thus it is said: **"The two sevens gather, assisting and aiding the person."**

When the great medicine first arises, it is born in the *Kun* crucible. When the time is ripe and the mechanism stirs, it must ascend to the *Qian* cauldron. The *Qian* cauldron lies within the Heavenly Valley at the Gate of the Brain, the aperture where all meridians converge—the alchemical classic’s meaning in **"If you wish not to age, return essence to replenish the brain."** As the medicine is born, one must use true intention to gather it, gazing upward in hesitation, sending it forth with spirit, guiding it straight to the Heavenly Valley. Thus it is said: **"Gazing at the brain’s principle, one stabilizes the ascent of the mysterious."**

Once the true seed ascends to the Heavenly Valley, it descends in turn to the Yellow Court, embodied yet subtle, like an infant at peace within the Yellow Court, roaming freely and at ease, attaining permanence in the attainment. Thus it is said: **"At *zi*, abiding in the center, one attains peace and preservation."** The infant dwells securely within the cauldron, its surroundings sealed and closed. There is no coming or going, nor any exit or entry—even if there were, it would never leave the Gate of the Original Female. Thus it is said: **"Coming and going in roaming, yet never leaving the gate."** Initially but a millet-grain pearl, with sufficient nurturing, it gradually grows from subtle to manifest, filling and expanding. Emotions return to nature, becoming purely refined. Thus it is said: **"Gradually growing great, emotions and nature become pure."**

This speck of true seed originally came from the Great Ultimate. From One it divided into Two, becoming two things; from Two it divided into Three, becoming the Three Families; further dividing into the Four Images, Five Phases, Eight Trigrams, and Nine Palaces—this is the Dao of descending from root to branch, the natural generation of things. Now, the two things unite, merging Three into One, so that the Four Images, Five Phases, Eight Trigrams, and Nine Palaces all return to the One. This is the Dao of returning to the root and restoring the origin, the reversal that forms the Elixir. Thus it is said: **"Returning to the One, one restores the original source."** This **"One"** can unify the Three Teachings. The *Daodejing* says: **"Attain the One, and all affairs are complete."** The *Huangting Jing* says: **"The Five Phases mutually impel and return to the One."** Even Confucius’ **"One that pervades all"** and Śākyamuni’s **"All dharmas return to the One"** refer to this same principle. This passage consistently advocates guarding the center and embracing the One, the essential teaching of deep-rooted stability. For when the crucible contains the treasure, this work of gentle nurturing must not be lacking.

Cherish and revere it as lord and minister. Through one cycle, it is most arduous. Guard it closely, lest you fall into confusion. The path is distant, again profound and mysterious. Should you attain this, you will comprehend Heaven and Earth. The *daogui* touches, purifying soul and spirit. Attaining longevity, you dwell in the immortal village.

This passage speaks of the work of guarding against danger and considering risk. The primordial ancestral *qi* is the lord; postnatal essence-*qi* is the minister. Once the crucible has obtained the primordial One *qi*, it relies on postnatal essence-*qi* to nourish and protect it, like a minister revering the lord and the lord cherishing the minister—between lord and minister, there is perfect harmony. Thus it is said: **"Cherish and revere it as lord and minister."** In the Dao of the Elixir, nine transformations complete one cycle. Ten months form the embryo, three years nurture it—during this time, the work of drawing and adding must not be neglected in the slightest. Thus it is said: **"Through one cycle, it is most arduous."** Once the original spirit abides within the Elixir’s gate, one must guard it with true intention, closely protecting and sustaining it without the slightest lapse. If true intention ever leaves its place, there is the danger of confusion and loss. Thus it is said: **"Guard it closely, lest you fall into confusion."** The original spirit is swift without haste, arrives without moving, ascending to heaven and descending to earth in an instant—yet it is also dark and indistinct, leaving no trace. Thus it is said: **"The path is distant, again profound and mysterious."**

The Dao of the Elixir has two aspects. In terms of the Golden Elixir, when *Kan* and *Li* unite, the true seed is obtained. But in terms of the Returned Elixir, one must refine essence into *qi*, refine *qi* into spirit, reset the crucible and cauldron, and re-create Heaven and Earth—there is further work above. Thus it is said: **"Should you attain this, you will comprehend Heaven and Earth."** The millet-grain medicine is called *daogui*. When the *daogui* touches the mouth, the yin *po* is entirely dissolved, and the yang *hun* also becomes obscure. Thus it is said: **"The *daogui* touches, purifying soul and spirit."** This echoes the earlier passage: **"When the body dies, forget soul and spirit—the *daogui* is most divine."** Once soul and spirit are purified, one’s original nature stands alone and unshaken, no longer drifting with the kalpa’s fire, the form lodged in the dust while the spirit dwells in the Great Void. Thus it is said: **"Attaining longevity, you dwell in the immortal village."** This passage consistently conveys the meaning of careful protection, linked like strung pearls with the previous work of nurturing.

Those who delight in the Dao seek its root. Examine the Five Phases, determine the precise measures. Contemplate it deeply—no need for discussion. Guard it in deep concealment, do not transmit it in writing. Ride the white crane, mount the dragon’s scales, roam the Great Void, visit the Immortal Lords, receive the celestial charts, and be named a Perfected One.

This passage speaks of the verification of spiritual transformation after shedding the embryo. The Dao has its root—it lies solely in embracing the One. This is what Laozi meant by **"returning to the root and recovering destiny."**

People of the world seek everything among the branches and leaves, drifting ever farther from the root and growing more distant from the Dao. Thus it is said: **"Those who delight in the Dao seek its root."** The marvels of creation do not transcend the Five Phases. The Five Phases move in sequence or reversal—sequential movement produces the mundane, the creation of the worldly realm; reversal produces the sacred, the creation of the transcendent realm. Yet the principle of reversing the Five Phases is most profound and abstruse. If the slightest measure or fraction is miscalculated, the elixir will surely fail to form. Thus it is said: **"Examine the Five Phases, determine the precise measures."**

The secret of the elixir path lies entirely in the firing process. From the ancient sages, transmission must be heart-to-heart. Those who study the Dao may only hold it in the mind, not speak it aloud; they may only silently comprehend, not commit it to writing. Thus it is said: **"Contemplate it deeply—no need for discussion. Guard it in deep concealment, do not transmit it in writing."** When the firing process is complete and the sacred embryo is fully formed, at the moment of shedding the shell, one may ride a white crane or mount a fiery dragon, soaring beyond the Great Void to pay homage to the Three Realms’ Most Revered. From then on, one receives celestial registers and charts, attains the rank of Great Brahma Heaven Immortal, and is named a Perfected One.

Yet this is no external phenomenon—it is an inner vision. The dragon and crane are one’s own primordial qi; the Great Void is one’s own primordial aperture; the Immortal Lords are one’s own primordial spirit; the celestial charts are the primordial chaos of red script since time immemorial; the Perfected One is one’s original face before birth. As the *Vajracchedikā Sūtra* states: **"All conditioned phenomena are like dreams, illusions, bubbles, shadows—if you see all phenomena as non-phenomena, you see the Tathāgata."** What Buddhism calls the Tathāgata, our Dao calls the Perfected One. If those who study the Dao but recognize the face of the Perfected One, all names and forms may be set aside and need not be discussed.

Yet the meaning of "Perfected One" has two aspects: in the realm of ordinary beings, it is called the Dharmakāya, which all possess; in the realm of sages, it is called the Reward Body, known only through realization. Ultimately, the Reward Body realized by sages is the Dharmakāya possessed by all beings. Though all possess it, because they refuse to accept it directly, they drift through life and death in vain, cycling through the Six Paths—how can this be blamed on the Creator?

This chapter, though it expounds the marvels of the cauldron and furnace, fully encompasses the ingredients and firing process. It is the summation of the Three Essentials of the Golden Elixir. Yet it must be read together with the following chapter to fully grasp the subtleties of the Three Categories.

**Chapter Thirty-Three: The Firing Process in Full Achievement**

No image is greater than heaven and earth—
The dark ravine spans ten thousand miles.
The River Drum nears the Star Marker,
And the people all tremble in fear.
The sundial’s shadow advances and retreats in error,
Nine years of misfortune befall.
The sovereign observes and takes heed—
The king retreats and reforms himself.
The key has its rises and falls,
And harmful qi scatters in flight.
The Yangtze and Huai run dry,
Their waters flowing into the sea.

Heaven and earth are the female and male,
Wandering between *zi* and *wu*;
*Yin* and *yang* originate in *yin* and *shen*,
Emerging and entering, ever beginning and ending.
Following the Dipper’s handle in its sweep,
Holding the balance to fix the primal order.
Rising and roasting on Mount Zeng,
Fierce fire blazes below.
The White Tiger leads the way,
The blue liquid harmonizes behind.
The Vermilion Bird soars and plays,
Its five-colored radiance flying.
Trapped in a net cast upon it,
Pressed down, it cannot rise.
Its wails are mournful,
Like an infant longing for its mother.
Overturned into the boiling cauldron,
Its feathers and wings are crushed and broken.
Before half the marked time has passed,
Dragon scales bristle and rise.
Five colors dazzle and gleam,
Transformations without constant master.
The cauldron seethes and surges,
Boiling violently without cease.
Layer upon layer, piled high,
Jagged as interlocking canine teeth.
In form like midwinter ice,
Railing-like stalactites emerge.
Towering and jumbled together,
Crossing and propping one another.
*Yin* and *yang* find their match,
Serene and abiding in harmony.

The Green Dragon dwells in the sixth chamber of *Fang*,
Spring blossoms in the east at *Mao*;
The White Tiger resides in the seventh star of *Mao*,
Autumn’s sharpness in the west at *You*;
The Vermilion Bird is in the second star of *Zhang*,
True *yang* in the south at *Wu*.
All three come to pay homage,
Their families and kin as dear companions.
Originally but two substances,
In the end they become three and five.
Three and five merge into one,
All gathered in a single place.
Refine them according to the method above,
Counting the days to the measure.
First white, then yellow,
Red color permeates inside and out.
This is called the First Cauldron,
Its nourishment like a grain of millet.

This is the work of nature,
Not some devious or false path.
Mountains and marshes exhale their qi,
Rising as clouds to become rain;
Dried mud turns to dust,
Extinguished fire becomes earth;
Shoots dyed yellow,
Like indigo forming green silk.
Hides boiled into glue,
Fermented grain transformed into wine.
Things of the same kind are easily worked upon,
Those of different kinds are hard to craft.
Only this wondrous art—
Examine it carefully, it does not deceive.
Passed down to countless generations,
Its brilliance is self-evident.
Shining like the stars across the Milky Way,
Glowing like the sea’s ancestral waters.
Ponder it until it is thoroughly familiar,
Review it again and again, above and below.
After a thousand cycles, it shines resplendently;
After ten thousand repetitions, it becomes visible.
Suddenly the spirit enlightens one,
The heart’s numen awakens in sudden realization.
Trace the beginning, seek its thread,
And you will surely find the gate and door.

The Dao of heaven favors no one,
Yet it is always transmitted to the worthy.

Yet within the "Fivefold Round of Three" chapters, much is said of the workings of golden elixir and the functions of warming, nurturing, and preserving its coalescence—while the operations of returning elixir, the imagery of union and refinement, remain incompletely expounded. Thus this chapter follows closely to fulfill that intent.

No image is greater than that of heaven and earth—
The Dark Gully spans tens of thousands of miles.
The Drum of the River stands over the Star Marker,
And all the people are struck with terror.
The shadow’s reckless advance and retreat—
Nine years of calamity ensue.
The August Emperor gazes upon it,
And the king retreats to amend his ways.
The pivot has its highs and lows—
Harmful qi scatters and flees.
The Jiang and Huai run dry,
Their waters flowing into the sea.

This passage speaks of the workings of fire-tending, modeled on heaven and earth—one cannot but be vigilant. The preceding chapter unfolded the imagery of the crucible and furnace: the Qian crucible emulates heaven, the Kun furnace mirrors earth. Thus the human body fully embodies a heaven and earth, and heaven and earth are but one vast crucible and furnace within me. The marvels of their transformations are wholly congruent.

The utmost height of heaven lies eighty-four thousand miles from the utmost depth of earth. The Milky Way within heaven is the Dark Gully, rising between the *wei* and *ji* constellations (stars of the Twenty-Eight Mansions) in the *chou* and *yin* positions, extending to the boundary of the *xing* and *liu* stars in the *wu* and *wei* positions—severing the celestial plate for who knows how many tens of thousands of miles. Within my body, the Celestial Pass and Earthly Axis are likewise separated by eighty-four thousand miles, the space between them forming the Dark Gully, demarcating above and below with the imagery of metal and wood in alternation. Hence it is said: “No image is greater than that of heaven and earth— / The Dark Gully spans tens of thousands of miles.”

The Drum of the River comprises three stars: the central one is the Great General, the left is the Left General, and the right is the Right General. With their radiant edges, they govern the sounds of military drums. Located north of the Ox Mansion, they rest directly upon the Star Marker of the Milky Way, corresponding to the *chou* position among the twelve earthly branches—precisely where the Milky Way traverses. The Drum of the River is not inherently a star of the *chou* division; its untimely appearance over the Star Marker signals disorder among the constellations within the Milky Way, foretelling the rise of water disasters—no wonder it evokes terror. At the juncture of *zi* and *chou* within my body lies the very ground where yang fire arises. If one acts recklessly before the proper time, the essence and qi of the entire body scatter in disarray, and the hundred vessels fall into chaos—is this not the very image of the people struck with terror? Hence it is said: “The Drum of the River stands over the Star Marker, / And all the people are struck with terror.”

The *mian* cap and its counterpart pertain to the sun’s shadow—here borrowed to describe the degrees of celestial bodies’ advance and retreat. Within the body, this corresponds to the increments of advancing and retreating fire. Advancing fire is “advance,” retreating fire is “retreat.” To advance when one should not, or retreat when one should not—whether excessive or deficient—is like the solstices and equinoxes failing to align with the water-clock’s measures, thereby summoning floods and droughts. According to the above, the Drum of the River’s position over the Star Marker signifies an improper advance of fire, leading to water disasters. Yao suffered nine years of flooding, hence it is said: “The shadow’s reckless advance and retreat— / Nine years of calamity ensue.” Nine years correspond to the imagery of nine revolutions. If the advance of fire is misaligned in the first revolution, all nine are lost—is this not the gravest of calamities?

The August Emperor refers to the Lord on High, the king to the human sovereign. “Gazes upon it” means making the warning manifest to the sovereign, for celestial portents serve as admonitions—precisely the “calamity” mentioned above. “Retreats to amend his ways” means correcting the errors of “advance and retreat” so that progression and regression align with proper measure. The August Emperor symbolizes the prenatal nature, the king the postnatal heart—their essence is one, though their functions differ. Nature governs non-action, abiding in stillness, resting in the chamber of spirit. The heart governs action, responding immediately to stimuli, revolving the celestial warp. Thus the advance and retreat of fire-tending will invariably hit the mark. Hence it is said: “The August Emperor gazes upon it, / And the king retreats to amend his ways.”

The pivot of the Dao of heaven lies entirely in the north and south poles. The north pole rises thirty-six degrees above the earth, the south pole sinks thirty-six degrees below—an image of low and high. The celestial sphere revolves day and night without cease, yet though the north and south poles govern its rotation, they never depart from their positions. Thus the warp and weft proceed in order, and harmful qi does not arise. Within my body, the Celestial Pass and Earthly Axis likewise rise and fall, corresponding to the north and south poles. When tending the fire, the pivot must be secure—this is to hold the Celestial Pass in hand and embody the Earthly Axis in form and heart. At this stage, the yin qi of the entire body naturally sheds away without residue. Hence it is said: “The pivot has its highs and lows— / Harmful qi scatters and flees.”

Heaven generates water, which inundates the great earth. Only with the vast sea as its destination can flooding be averted. Within the human body, inside and out, nothing is not yin dregs—the confluence of all waters. At the summit of Kunlun lies the Primordial Sea, the gathering place of all waters. Only when the pivots of the north and south poles are secure, compelling the hundred vessels to return to the origin, does qi naturally flow back to the Primordial Sea—like the Jiang and Huai rivers converging in the sea without causing floods. Hence it is said: “The Jiang and Huai run dry, / Their waters flowing into the sea.”

This passage begins by likening the celestial Dark Gully to the imagery of the crucible and furnace, then uses the movements of celestial bodies to illustrate the standards of fire-tending. Misalignment invites the disaster of floods, while proper alignment brings the blessing of returning to the origin. Gain and loss—here lies the measure of fire-tending. This is the guiding principle of the entire chapter.

The female and male of heaven and earth—
They linger between *zi* and *wu*.
The *yin* and *shen* are the ancestors of yin and yang,
Exiting and entering, repeating their beginning and end.
Following the Dipper’s swaying handle,
Grasping the balance to fix the primal order.

This passage speaks of the union of Kan and Li, the imagery of their conjoining. *Zi* is the head of the six yang, corresponding to the winter solstice...

*Wu* marks the head of the six *yin*, corresponding to the summer solstice. The two phases of *zi* and *wu*—one *yin*, one *yang*—serve as the north-south axis, the very ground where water and fire meet. When the sun and moon reach this point, they linger, neither advancing nor retreating in haste. Thus, when the sun stands at high noon, the ancients called it *tingwu* ("pausing at *wu*"), signifying this very hesitation. In the alchemy of the Elixir, the ascent and descent of water and fire occur solely within the *zi* and *wu* phases. When the true fire within Kan ascends, the first *yang* stirs anew, its *qi* still faint—one must close the gates to nurture the sprouting of the latent dragon. When the true water within Li descends, the first *yin* arrives in union, its *qi* just budding—one must fasten the crossbar to guard against the first frost’s encroachment. The marvel of creation lies entirely between *wu*-after and *zi*-before, where true intent must linger in hesitation. Thus it is said: *"The female and male of heaven and earth— / They linger between *zi* and *wu*."*

Though the embryo of *yang* fire takes form at *zi*, it is not born until *yin*; though the embryo of *yin* water takes form at *wu*, it is not born until *shen*. The sun, infused with the essence of fire, rises at *yin* and sets at *shen*; the moon, infused with the essence of water, rises at *shen* and sets at *yin*. Clearly, *yin* and *shen* are the ancestral homeland of *yin* and *yang*, the gate through which creation enters and exits. The Dao of the Elixir mirrors this: though the first *yang* within Kan revives at *zi*, it is not until the *yin* position that the true fire emerges from the earth; though the first *yin* within Li unites at *wu*, it is not until the *shen* position that the true water attains growth. One emerges, one enters—endlessly repeating their beginning and end—only then does one witness the true *yin* and true *yang*, born of the same ancestral source yet bearing different names. Thus it is said: *"The *yin* and *shen* are the ancestors of *yin* and *yang*, / Exiting and entering, repeating their beginning and end."*

The star *Zhaoyao* lies north of *Genghe*, its rays sharp as blades; when its rays stir, war is sure to follow. The fifth star of the Northern Dipper is named *Heng* ("Balance"), the Dipper’s handle, governing the administration of the celestial center and the regulation of the four directions. Whether pointing to *zi* and *wu* or to *yin* and *shen*, it fixes the positions of wood, metal, water, and fire, and divides the seasons of spring, autumn, winter, and summer. Though *Zhaoyao* tends toward reckless motion, if it follows the Dipper’s handle in its movement, its motion will align with the seasons, never losing its guiding principle. The Dao of the Elixir emulates heaven, relying entirely on the turning of the Celestial Heart and the shifting of the Dipper’s handle. The Celestial Heart abides motionless at the North Pole, observing only the direction of the Dipper’s handle. When the handle points to *zi* and *wu*, water and fire linger in hesitation; when it points to *yin* and *shen*, metal and wood converge in union. This is akin to *Zhaoyao* following the Dipper’s movement to establish the guiding principles of the celestial sphere. Thus it is said: *"Following the Dipper’s swaying handle, / Grasping the balance to fix the primal order."*

This passage explains how water and fire unite, how metal and wood merge—all through the turning of the Dipper’s handle. Such is the initial work of the union of Kan and Li. When Kan and Li conjoin, the true seed is born. Only when the first *yang* stirs and the Dipper’s handle aligns with *zi* may the work of refinement begin!

Rising to be refined upon the steamer-mountain,
With blazing fire arrayed below.
The White Tiger leads the way in front,
The Azure Fluid harmonizes behind.
The Vermilion Bird soars and plays,
Its five-colored radiance in flight.
Yet it encounters the spread of nets,
Pressed down, unable to rise.
Its cries are mournful and sorrowful,
Like an infant longing for its mother.
Overturned into the seething cauldron,
Its feathers and wings crushed and broken.
Before half the clepsydra’s measure has passed,
Dragon scales and manes begin to bristle.
Five hues shimmer and dazzle,
Transforming without constancy.
The cauldron boils and surges,
Violently churning without cease.
Layer upon layer, piled in heaps,
Jagged as interlocking canine teeth.
Its form resembles midwinter ice,
With stalactites jutting forth.
Towering and jumbled,
Intertwined and propped against one another.
When *yin* and *yang* find their match,
They rest in tranquil harmony.

This passage speaks of the union of Qian and Kun, the imagery of their refinement. Earlier, the union of Kan and Li gave birth to the true seed. Now, with further work of conjoining, the Great Medicine of the Golden Elixir is nurtured within the Kun furnace—hence the term "refinement," the very "pivot of refinement" mentioned in the previous section. Once the furnace’s nurturing is complete and the first *yang* stirs, precisely at the hour of *zi*, one must swiftly apply fire in response. This requires fierce refinement and extreme forging, along with the work of *shi* (licking), *cuo* (gathering), and *bi* (closing), compelling the golden fluid within the furnace to ascend. Seizing the fire’s momentum, one drives the River Chariot from the *Weilü* cavity, reversing its flow to ascend the Kunlun peak—this is the image of "rising to be refined upon the steamer-mountain." As the *Cuixu Pian* states: *"At the hour of *zi*, when *qi* reaches the *Weilü* Pass, / The River Chariot crosses the steamer-mountain."* Such is the meaning here. Thus it is said: *"Rising to be refined upon the steamer-mountain, / With blazing fire arrayed below."*

The metal essence of the West is the White Tiger; the wood fluid of the East is the Azure Dragon. The dragon, *yang*, leads; the tiger, *yin*, harmonizes. Now, the tiger reverses to lead in front, while the dragon reverses to harmonize behind—this is the image of the Five Phases moving in reverse, *yin* and *yang* overturned. Thus it is said: *"The White Tiger leads the way in front, / The Azure Fluid harmonizes behind."* This marks the great union, where one seals the senses, closes the gates, and employs the work of blowing, inhaling, and absorbing spirit—a practice distinct from the earlier union of Kan and Li. One must discern this carefully.

The Vermilion Bird is the fire essence of the South, stationed in the Li palace—the very "blazing fire" mentioned above. Its nature is flighty and restless; at the slightest encounter with illusory sensory forms, it soars away, beyond control.

Thus it is said:
*"The Vermilion Bird soars and sports,
Its five-colored radiance in flight."*

The Vermilion Bird’s nature is inherently soaring and buoyant—nothing can restrain it—until it encounters the Dark Warrior of the North, whereupon it surrenders and submits. At the moment of Qian and Kun’s union, fire ascends from below while water descends from above; the Dark Warrior seizes the Vermilion Bird, binding it fast in mutual restraint, refusing to release even unto death, as though trapped in a net, its wings pressed down and unable to lift. Thus it is said:
*"Encountering the net’s spread,
Pressed down, it cannot rise."*

Fire, by nature, flames upward; once suppressed by water, its temperament grows frantic, longing to ascend—like an infant parted from its mother, wailing in sorrowful yearning, its cries plaintive. Thus it is said:
*"Its wails so mournful,
Like an infant’s longing for its mother."*

With fire ascending and water descending, host and guest reverse; the Vermilion Bird and the Dark Warrior devour each other, sealed within the crucible, unable to escape—like feathers broken, never again to take flight. Thus it is said:
*"Overturned into the seething cauldron,
Feathers crushed and broken."*

Once water and fire seize and restrain each other, the dragon and tiger must also submit. The four images—Metal, Wood, Water, and Fire—converge within the crucible, firmly sealed without leakage. In but a moment, the elixir coalesces. With the five phases’ *qi* gathered in the crucible, transformation arises spontaneously, like a divine dragon traversing the sky, its scales stirring and mane tossing, its five-colored brilliance dazzling and indescribable in its shifts. Thus it is said:
*"Before half a *lou-ke* has passed,
Dragon scales and manes rise.
Five hues in dazzling display,
Transformations without fixed master."*

As the crucible seethes and roils, dragon and tiger contend, shaking heaven and earth. In an instant, golden essence pierces the crown, silver waves surge like a tide, as though the steamer’s rice nears readiness, the crucible’s waters boiling without cease, surging forth in torrents. Thus it is said:
*"The crucible’s bubbling rush,
Violent surging without rest."*

Just as the boiling reaches its peak, the fire’s force is redoubled, layered and continuous, steaming without end—until the fire is full and the *qi* complete. Within the crucible, true *qi* naturally suffuses and intermingles, like interlocking canine teeth. Thus it is said:
*"Layer upon layer piled,
Like interlocking fangs."*

Once the union is complete, the golden crucible’s waters grow warm, the jade furnace’s fire disperses, and a single drop falls into the Yellow Court—first as liquid, then congealing, gradually solidifying into firmness, like midwinter’s ice, or stalactites bursting from the lattice of stone. Thus it is said:
*"Form like midwinter’s ice,
Latticed stone yielding stalactites."*

The crucible’s true fluid and *qi* circulate: the light and pure congeal in the Mud Pill Palace, while the heavy and turbid return to the *qi* cavern, presenting an image of towering peaks interspersed; the true seed having congealed, the formless generates form, like intersecting supports propping one another. Thus it is said:
*"Towering yet intermingled,
Interlocking like propping beams."*

All the above are the phenomena of obtaining the medicine and forming the elixir in that moment. For when Qian and Kun unite in great congress, true *yin* and true *yang* pair without flaw, hence the signs described. From this point, the war ceases, and the city is guarded with gentle fire—neither forgetting nor forcing—quietly tending the Central Yellow, as in the saying: *"Send it to the earthen cauldron, sealed and secured."* Thus it is said:
*"Yin and yang find their match,
Abiding in tranquil harmony."*

This passage concerns the moment of Qian and Kun’s union: the preceding speaks of the refining method, the middle of the coalescing imagery, and the end of the nurturing work—it is the pivotal point of the entire chapter.

The Azure Dragon dwells in House Six,
Spring’s blossoms at the eastern *mao*.
The White Tiger resides in Pleiades Seven,
Autumn’s awns at the western *you*.
The Vermilion Bird in Zhang Two,
True yang at the southern *wu*.
All three come to court,
Kin as intimate companions.
Originally but two things,
Yet in the end, three and five.
Three and five merge as one,
All gathered in a single place.
Refine them as prescribed above,
Counting the days to completion.
First white, then yellow,
Red suffusing inside and out.
Named the First Crucible,
Its measure like a great millet grain.

This section speaks of the four images and five phases merging into one—such are the phenomena of elixir formation. Earlier, at the moment of great union, the Azure Dragon, White Tiger, and Vermilion Bird were all overturned and moving in reverse; here, they return to their proper positions.

The Azure Dragon’s true position is in the East. The eastern constellation House belongs to Wood, its number eight, yet here called "House Six"—for six is Water’s completion number, and Wood is born in *hai*; Wood’s essence originally flows from Kan Water, as the *Ru Yao Jing* calls it: *"Lead Dragon."* The eastern dragon corresponds to spring, the trigram Zhen, the hour *mao*; Wood flourishes in *mao*, when grasses and trees blossom into flowers. Thus it is said:
*"The Azure Dragon dwells in House Six,
Spring’s blossoms at the eastern *mao*."*

The White Tiger’s true position is in the West. The western constellation Pleiades belongs to Metal, its number nine, yet here called "Pleiades Seven"—for seven is Fire’s completion number, and Metal is born in *si*; Metal’s essence is originally forged from Li Fire, as the *Ru Yao Jing* terms it: *"Mercury Tiger."* The western tiger corresponds to autumn, the trigram Dui, the hour *you*; Metal flourishes in *you*, when grains ripen and awns form.

Thus it is said:
*"The White Tiger dwells in Pleiades Seven,
Autumn’s awns at the western *you*."*
The Vermilion Bird’s true position is in the South. The southern constellation Zhang belongs to Fire, and two is Fire’s generative number. The southern Vermilion Bird corresponds to summer, the trigram Li, the hour *wu*; Fire flourishes in *wu*, capable of burning Wood and smelting Metal. Thus it is said:
*"The Vermilion Bird in Zhang Two,
True Yang at the southern *wu* of Li."*

At the time of their convergence, one from the East, one from the West, one from the South—all come to pay homage and encircle the North Star, the Pole of Heaven. The three families gather into one, disparate bones become kin, rejoicing in great peace. Thus it is said:
*"When the three come to pay homage,
