Their households become intimate companions."*

Here, the three symbols of Wood, Metal, and Fire correspond precisely to the preceding passage. The earlier and later sections both omit the Dark Warrior, for the Dark Warrior’s true position lies in the North, directly aligned with the pivot of the Dipper. Since the three come to encircle the North Star, the Dark Warrior is already within them—this is the meaning of the middle section’s *"nine returns, seven reversions, eight returns, six abodes."*

Originally, it is the union of true Yin and true Yang. Yet one Dragon, one Tiger, and the Fire of the South together form the three families. Wood and Fire become companions, Metal and Water become friends; when the Earth of the Center is included, the Five Phases are complete. Tracing their root and stock, they are but two substances that branch into three families and five phases, combining to form the number fifteen of three and five. Thus it is said:
*"Their root is but two things,
Their branches become three and five."*

In the beginning, from root to branch, they originally issue from a single stock: one divides into two, two into three, three into five—this is the ordinary Dao’s forward sequence. In the end, from branch back to root, they are gathered again into a single stock: five returns to three, three to two, two to one—this is the Reversed Dao of the Elixir. Thus it is said:
*"Three and five merge into one,
All gather to return to the One Place."*

"Merge into one" refers to the primordial One Qi, the true seed. "Return to the One Place" refers to the Central True Position, the Yellow Court. When three and five become one, it is the transmission from the hearts of the supreme sages, like statutes that cannot be violated. Following this in cultivation, the Elixir is certain to form—yet it is not the work of a single day. Only through accumulation over months and years is accomplishment attained. Still, one must take the first turning, the initial point of the true seed, as the foundation. Thus it is said:
*"Cultivate it according to the statutes above,
Counting the days from the very beginning."*

"Counting the days" refers to the three years of refining and ingestion. "Beginning" refers to the first turning, the starting point. The Elixir’s initial coagulation is inherently pure gold. When the seed is further planted in the Qian Palace, its color is purely white. By the time it descends to the Yellow Court and is sent into the Earth Cauldron, nurtured by the Qi of the Kun Mother, it gradually transforms into a yellow hue. From beginning to end, refined by the Fire of Li in the South, its color blazes red through and through—this is called the Returned Elixir. Thus it is said:
*"First white, then yellow,
Its red color permeates inside and out."*

Each turning of the Elixir corresponds to one tripod; nine tripods correspond to nine turnings. Yet within one turning are already nine turnings, so the work of nine tripods is wholly contained in the first tripod. After Qian and Kun unite in *gou*, with bathing and gentle nurturing, the millet-pearl naturally coagulates in the tripod. The *Scripture of Salvation* states: *"The Primordial Beginning suspended a precious pearl, large as a millet grain, within the Empty Origin... Heavenly beings looked up and saw only a surging brilliance entering through the pearl’s opening."* This is the meaning here. Thus it is said:
*"Named the First Tripod,
Its ingestion like a great millet grain."*

This passage speaks of the four symbols and five phases merging into one, the proof of the Elixir’s coagulation. It is the work of nature itself, not some deviant or false path. As the Qi of mountains and marshes steam together, clouds arise and become rain; when mud dries and crumbles, it turns to dust; when fire extinguishes, it transforms into earth. Like the yellow dye extracted from the *huai* tree, or the green silk woven from indigo—leather boiled becomes glue, yeast and grains ferment into wine. Work is easily done among those of the same kind, but skill avails little with the wrong seed. This section describes the Elixir’s success, which arises from the Dao of nature itself.

All the above processes of union and coagulation are but the mutual matching of true Yin and true Yang, returning to my primordial Empty-Origin One Qi. Though it is spoken of as "having action," it is in truth "non-action," all emerging from the natural mechanism of Heaven. It is not like the heterodox minor arts—manipulation, absorption, and various strange fabrications used to deceive the world and delude the masses. This is what the Most High meant by *"the Dao follows nature."* The marvel of the Elixir’s natural Dao accords with the transformations of Heaven and human affairs without discrepancy. When the two Qi interact and steam together, they transform into true liquid—just as the Qi of mountains and marshes naturally steam to form clouds and coalesce into rain. Thus it is said:
*"As the Qi of mountains and marshes steam together,
Clouds arise and become rain."*

Mud’s nature is heavy and stagnant, seemingly unlike dust—yet when it dries and crumbles, it naturally transforms into dust. Fire’s nature is soaring and scattering, seemingly unlike earth—yet when its flames fade and smoke cools, it naturally transforms into earth.

Just as the sprouts of a stump are inherently yellow, when dyed, they naturally become yellow; indigo is inherently green, when woven into silk, it naturally becomes green. Leather is the very source of glue, naturally boiled to form glue; yeast and sprouts are the essence of wine, naturally fermented to become wine. All these are of the same kind, each returning to its origin.

Thus, the workings of transformation are natural, without the slightest trace of artificial contrivance. The Reverted Elixir is no different: the true fire within *Kan* originally emerges from *Qian*, its nature ever desiring to ascend and return to *Qian*; the true water within *Li* originally emerges from *Kun*, its nature ever desiring to descend and return to *Kun*. Moreover, when the dragon intones, clouds arise of themselves; when the tiger roars, wind stirs of itself. As the two *qi* interact, each follows its kind—how could it not be efficacious?

This is what is meant by: *"To refine the immortal elixir, one must use those of the same kind."* How could solitary yin or orphaned yang—all that are not of the same kind—compare? Thus it is said: *"With the same kind, effort is easily applied; without the proper seed, skill is difficult to achieve."*

This passage speaks of the cause of success in the Elixir’s Dao, which lies solely in the two characters *natural* and *same kind*. Only the true seed is originally of the same kind, so their interaction arises from the natural—this cannot be unknown. Such is the marvel of this art, examined in truth and without deceit. Transmitted to the myriad ages to come, its brilliance will be self-evident for all to verify.

Radiant as stars traversing the Han River, resplendent as waters converging in the sea. Ponder it thoroughly, turning it over to examine above and below. After a thousand cycles, its brilliance shines resplendent; after ten thousand repetitions, it may at last be perceived. Suddenly the numinous reveals itself to one, and the heart’s spirit awakens in sudden comprehension. Tracing its beginnings and seeking its threads, one must find its gate and door.

The Dao of Heaven admits no favoritism, yet it is ever transmitted to the worthy. This passage serves as the conclusion to the entire chapter. It speaks of the marvelous Dao of like seeking like and natural interaction—passed down from the ancient sages through the mind-seal of the master, silently realized in secret, without a single word falsely set forth. Thus it is said: *"Such is the marvel of this art, examined in truth and without deceit."* The Great Dao knows no past or present, no before or after; a thousand ages above or a thousand ages below, this heart and this principle remain ever in accord. To read its text is as if to meet its author in person. Thus it is said: *"Transmitted to the myriad ages to come, its brilliance will be self-evident for all to verify."*

The secrets of the firing times are fully recorded herein: in Heaven, they correspond to the stars, like the host of stars traversing the River of Stars; on Earth, they correspond to the tides, like the myriad waters converging in the great sea—without the slightest deviation, not a drop lost. Thus it is said: *"Radiant as stars traversing the Han River, resplendent as waters converging in the sea."* These two lines echo the earlier passage: *"The River Drum and Star Records, the Yangtze and Huai flowing to the sea."*

Within this text, the firing times must be studied not only by recitation but by contemplation; not only by contemplation but by embodiment. The yang fire and yin tally within the body circulate ceaselessly, ascending and descending in every moment. Yet one must not see with the eyes but with the spirit—only then is it attained. Thus it is said: *"Ponder it thoroughly, turning it over to examine above and below."*

From one cycle to endless cycles, from one repetition to ten thousand, the turning continues without cease—*"ever revolving thus, through a thousand, a hundred million scrolls,"* not merely one or two. Thus it is said: *"After a thousand cycles, its brilliance shines resplendent; after ten thousand repetitions, it may at last be perceived."*

Guanzi said: *"Ponder it, ponder it again, and ponder it once more; if still not grasped, the spirits will make it known."* In all ordinary inquiry, one should proceed thus. How much more so with this sacred firing record, where one must not see with the eyes but with the spirit?

After a thousand cycles and ten thousand repetitions, the heart’s spirit suddenly awakens, and wisdom’s nature becomes naturally luminous. All matters, worldly and transcendent, are seen with perfect clarity, as if the spirits themselves had come to reveal it. Thus it is said: *"Suddenly the numinous reveals itself to one, and the heart’s spirit awakens in sudden comprehension."*

The Great Elixir’s Dao has its beginnings and threads, its gates and doors. True yin and true yang, like seeking like—these are its beginnings and threads. Kan and Li unite to produce the medicine; Qian and Kun interact to form the Elixir—one inner, one outer, twofold in function—these are its gates and doors. If later students can trace and seek them, once the threads are grasped, they may glimpse the gates and doors of the Great Dao. Thus it is said: *"Tracing its beginnings and seeking its threads, one must find its gate and door."* Here, the term *"gate and door"* precisely echoes the opening of the first chapter: *"Qian and Kun are the gates and doors of the Changes."*

This matter is originally complete in every person, perfectly realized in each. Yet the Great Dao is transmitted but once in ten thousand kalpas, and one must choose the right recipient. To meet someone and not transmit it is to incur the fault of obstructing the Dao of Heaven.

To transmit it to an unworthy recipient is to incur the blame of divulging Heaven’s treasure. Only those of loyal, filial, pure, luminous, benevolent, and upright character—who can moreover sever worldly attachments of affection and cast off all mundane toils—are fit to receive this Dao. As the saying goes: *"The Way of Heaven shows no partiality; it is ever transmitted to the worthy."* The Immortal Elder, having expounded the essentials of the firing process, repeatedly and earnestly emphasizes the need to choose the right recipient, revealing a heart both vast and prudent.

Though this chapter describes the firing process and its symbolic correspondences, it serves in truth to conclude the entire work. The preceding chapter was the general summation; this is its closing exposition. The two chapters form a complete whole, and the essential principles of governance, nurturing nature, and ingesting the elixir are all contained within. Each passage may be cross-referenced and verified—precisely what is meant by *"the threefold correspondence."* The discerning will naturally understand.

**Chapter Thirty-Four of the Three Ways Converging in One**
The *Cantong Qi* presents an outline but lacks unity, its discourse diffuse, its finer points incomplete, its details vague and fragmentary. Here, I have compiled and supplemented omissions, refined obscure passages, linked scattered threads, aligned the intent, and ensured the direction remains unerring. Thus, I have composed this chapter and named it *"The Threefold Correspondence."* In this way, the nature and feelings of the *Great Change* are fully revealed.

The nature and feelings of the *Great Change* each adhere to their proper measure. The teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, when thoroughly investigated, may be applied in practice. The work of the furnace and fire has a true basis. The Three Ways converge in one, all emerging from the same path. Branches, stems, flowers, and leaves—fruit hangs in abundance—yet all depend on the root, never losing their original essence. What is spoken here is sincere, examined, and without error.

This chapter explains that governance, nurturing nature, and ingesting the elixir—though distinct paths—ultimately return to the same Dao. The *Cantong Qi* presents an outline but lacks unity, its discourse diffuse, its finer points incomplete, its details vague and fragmentary. Here, I have compiled and supplemented omissions, refined obscure passages, linked scattered threads, aligned the intent, and ensured the direction remains unerring. Thus, I have composed this chapter and named it *"The Threefold Correspondence."* In this way, the nature and feelings of the *Great Change* are fully revealed. This explains the origin and convergence of the Three Ways in One.

The *Cantong Qi* originally derives from the River Chart and Luo Scripture, expounding the trigrams of Fuxi to reveal the precelestial mind and the *Changes*. Yet it must be grounded in the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, employing the symbolic correspondences of the furnace and fire. Only when the Three Schools are mutually referenced and converge in one does it fully accord with the great Dao of realizing nature and destiny.

However, the first two chapters artificially divide the single Dao into three schools, resulting in uneven prose and obscured principles. Therefore, this later chapter is composed to explicitly articulate the idea of correspondence. The chapters *"The Marvelous Function of the Crucible"* and *"The Complete Achievement of the Firing Process"* have already clarified its structure. Yet the principle of the Three Ways converging in One remains unexposed. The Master Wei further explains that in the *Cantong Qi*, the first two chapters merely present an outline, lacking unity, with much diffuse language, while finer subtleties are often omitted or unclear. Hence, this supplementary chapter could not be omitted.

Thus, obscure passages in the earlier text are refined; scattered elements are linked and drawn together. In this way, the principles of the Three Schools return to one, and their direction does not deviate. Governance, nurturing nature, and ingesting the elixir—when summarized, they are called *"the threefold correspondence."* In a single phrase, they are simply *"the nature and feelings of the Great Change."*

For the *Changes* are but the sun and moon—Kan and Li, two things: one yin, one yang; one nature, one feeling. Ultimately, they reduce to the two words *body* and *mind*. If one can harmonize body and mind with the true intent of the Central Yellow, uniting the two in the center, this alone suffices to embody the Dao of all under Heaven. What the Yellow Emperor and Laozi nurture is this alone; what the furnace and fire refine is this alone.

This is why they are called *"the threefold correspondence."* This is why the *threefold correspondence* constitutes the *Triple Concordance.* Observing the "Furnace and Cauldron" chapter, it speaks only of furnace and cauldron, yet the medicinal substances and firing times are already fully presented. In the "Firing Times" chapter, it speaks only of firing times, yet the medicinal substances, furnace, and cauldron are also entirely encompassed—thus one comprehends the great purport of the *threefold correspondence!*

In the *Great Change,* feelings and nature each accord with their measure. In the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, investigation is applied, and governance becomes possible. The work of furnace and fire is truly grounded in evidence. The Three Ways emerge from one, all proceeding from the same path. Branches, stems, flowers, and leaves—fruits hang in abundance—yet all reside in the rootstock, never departing from their original simplicity.

What is spoken here arises from sincere intent, carefully considered and without error. This passage precisely elucidates how the Three Ways return to one. Yet世人 merely see that the *Triple Concordance* is divided into three sections, demarcating governance, nurturing nature, and ingesting the elixir, and thus believe these to be three distinct schools. When they speak of the *Great Change,* they take it as mere yin-yang cosmology and drift into sexual arts. When they speak of nurturing nature, they fixate on the bodily form and mind, clinging to preservation. When they speak of furnace and fire, they take it as refining cinnabar and mercury, descending into mere alchemy. The three schools cling to their own views, each failing to communicate with the others—truly a millennia-long night! For fourteen or fifteen hundred years, the *Triple Concordance* has lain buried in the depths, unseen by the light of day.

Heaven does not withhold the Dao; now it is dissected and made clear. The *Great Change’s* nature and feelings precisely refer to the two things Kan and Li. The sun and moon are the *Change*; true essence mutually conceals itself. The two words *feeling* and *nature* are one metal and one wood, one water and one fire, one hun and one po, one dragon and one tiger, one man and one woman—yet in truth, they are but one body and one mind. Body and mind, these two, are naturally paired, forged into one—are they not the medicinal substances of the golden elixir? Thus it is said: *"In the Great Change, feelings and nature each accord with their measure."*

As for the Yellow Emperor and Laozi’s nurturing of nature, it may seem to refer to their principle of purity and non-action. Yet they knew that the head contains nine palaces, with the Yellow Court at the center, the dwelling of the Central Yellow Lord. The *Yellow Court Scripture* states: *"The Central Lord governs the Hall of Light."* The Yellow Court is the central yellow position, also called the *Abode of Spirit Virtue,* the *Dao’s Dwelling Furnace,* the *Great Abyss,* or the *Regulating Center*—roughly the primordial ancestral aperture. Recognizing the ancestral aperture, the original spirit has a place to return, and one understands the application of nurturing nature. Its function relies entirely on true intent. With this true intent, body and mind are harmonized, the handle held in hand—is this not the furnace and cauldron of the golden elixir? Thus it is said: *"In the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, investigation is applied, and governance becomes possible."*

As for the work of furnace and fire, it borrows external imagery to illustrate internal practice. Once the medicinal substances enter the furnace, fire must be applied to refine them. Whether paired as dragon and tiger, mercury and lead, flowing pearls and golden blossoms, or yellow sprouts and maiden, these various names all refer to the two things of body and mind. To restrain them is called *"subduing,"* to unite them is called *"ingesting"*—still, it is the harmonizing of body and mind with true intent. One subjugation and one ingestion complete the work of the golden elixir’s furnace and fire, its principle firmly grounded—is this not the firing time of the golden elixir? Thus it is said: *"The work of furnace and fire is truly grounded in evidence."*

Without medicinal substances, there can be no furnace and cauldron; without furnace and cauldron, there can be no firing times. These three are of the same thread, mutually entailing. Mention one, and all three are fully present. Though divided into three sections, their function is never separate. Know that the various names for medicinal substances in the text all refer to one thing; the various names for furnace and cauldron all refer to one place; the various names for firing times all refer to one moment. If one understands this one thing, one knows that all living beings are but this one thing. If one understands this one place, one knows that mountains, rivers, and the great earth all reside in this one place. If one understands this one moment, one knows that kalpas and epochs are but this one moment. For this one thing is this one place, this one place is this one moment, and this one moment is this one thing.

This is called *"uniting the three in one"* and *"grasping the one, all affairs are complete."* Thus it is said: *"The Three Ways emerge from one, all proceeding from the same path."* Originally, there was but one Way, yet when analyzed, it becomes three paths—like plants and trees that sprout stems and branches in spring, bloom with flowers and leaves in summer, and bear fruit in autumn. Yet the source of their growth lies solely in the rootstock. Only when winter strips them bare, returning to the root, does the marvel of the fruit’s generative power become manifest. Thus it is said: *"Branches, stems, flowers, and leaves—fruits hang in abundance—yet all reside in the rootstock, never departing from their original simplicity."* This *simplicity* is the *primordial simplicity,* the meaning of returning to the origin.

From one Way branching into three paths, there is the image of stems, branches, flowers, and fruit—this is what is meant by *"revealing the branches."* From three paths returning to one Way, there is the image of the rootstock—this is what is meant by *"concealing the root."* Though the previous two chapters are divided into three sections, appearing like branches, the rootstock’s oneness never fails to run through them. What is spoken arises from sincere intent, but the reader must examine it repeatedly, pondering it thousands of times until enlightenment suddenly dawns. Only then will one know that the Three Ways truly emerge from one, and not be misled by heterodox paths. Thus it is said: *"What is spoken here arises from sincere intent, carefully considered and without error."*

This chapter is the crux of the *threefold correspondence.* Master Wei feared that people might mistake one Way for three paths, or mistake three paths for not being one Way. Thus he explicitly pointed out the root source, emphasizing the two phrases *"all reside in the rootstock"*—but ultimately, what *is* the rootstock?

At the first stirring of the solitary Yang, the heart of Heaven and Earth is revealed, and the marvel of creation-and-transformation resides within it—this is why the three Ways, though diverging in path, converge in return, and the single phrase of the *Cantong Qi* suffices to encompass them. Thus, immediately following the chapter *"Like the Midwinter Season,"* according to the common edition, this chapter originally included a *"Fivefold Correspondence Diagram,"* forcibly aligning it with the *Hetu*’s five positions, each attaining harmony in its own way. This tradition began with Peng Xiao and others, becoming an unshakable convention. Yet a close examination of Master Wei’s original intent reveals that this chapter clearly speaks of the three Ways—governing affairs, nurturing nature, and refining the elixir—emerging from one, hence *"threefold correspondence,"* not *"fivefold correspondence."* For the east’s three and the south’s two unite as one family, the north’s one and the west’s four unite as another, while the central five and ten form their own family. In the threefold correspondence, the marvel of the five positions attaining harmony is already inherent—why add superfluous limbs? Moreover, the three Ways arising from one not only run through the preceding and following chapters but are the very crux of the entire work. How could there be any extraneous meaning to interpose? Furthermore, their so-called *"floating left, sinking right,"* *"generational gold, generational silver,"* and other forced interpretations all veer into the collateral paths of furnace-and-fire, utterly contradicting the work’s grand meaning. They disrupt the coherence of the text’s bloodline, and collation with ancient editions reveals no such diagram. It is clear that this was Peng Xiao’s fabrication, not Master Wei’s original text, and thus it is excised.

**Chapter Thirty-Five: The Four Phases Return to the Root**
*"Like the Midwinter Season,"*
Grasses and trees all wither and break.
Assisting Yang, it questions merchants and travelers;
The lord of men deeply conceals himself.
Emulating the season, following its decree,
Seal the mouth, refrain from speech.
The Way of Heaven is vast and boundless,
The Great Origin without form or shape.
Vacuous and quiescent, unseen,
Its frame dissolves into dissolution.
Errors lose the thread of affairs,
Words rebound to self-defeat.
This sequence of the four phases
Illumines the blind of later generations.

This passage speaks of the four phases merging and returning to the Ultimateless, directly revealing the supreme and most authentic Dao. People know only the *postcelestial* four phases but not the *precelestial* four phases. Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li are the postcelestial four phases. When these four return to chaos and revert to emptiness, this is the precelestial four phases. The postcelestial four phases have form and name, and their words can be exhausted; they are precisely what is called *"branches, stems, flowers, and leaves."* The precelestial four phases are formless and nameless, and their words cannot be exhausted; they are precisely what is called *"the rootstock."* The *Cantong Qi* expounds Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li throughout, nearly exhausting their meaning. Master Wei feared that people might ascend the branches and forget the root, so at the end of the work, he specially revealed to them the wordless words and imageless intent, continuing from the previous chapter’s *"residing in the rootstock, not losing its simplicity."*

In the world, all grasses and trees grow branches and stems in early spring, spread flowers and leaves in midsummer, and bear fruit in autumn. Though these three are present, they have not yet returned to the root. Only when the midwinter season arrives, when Heaven and Earth are closed and sealed, and the bitter cold congeals, do all branches, stems, flowers, and fruits fall away without remainder, leaving only the rootstock. In creation-and-transformation, this is the convergence of hidden potential; in our bodies, it is the time of returning to the root and restoring life. Thus it is said: *"Like the Midwinter Season, grasses and trees all wither and break."*

At the first stirring of the solitary Yang, the myriad things have not yet arisen. Though it moves, it has not departed from stillness—this is what Shaozi called *"the supreme marvel between one movement and one stillness, the marvel of Heaven, Earth, and humanity."* At this moment, the heart of Heaven and Earth is deeply concealed in the ninefold abyss, its mechanism secure and tight. What is within does not emerge; what is without does not enter—this is the image of *"the solstice closing the passes, merchants and travelers not moving, the latter not inquiring of the directions."* Thus it is said: *"Assisting Yang, it questions merchants and travelers; the lord of men deeply conceals himself."* Merchants and travelers, rushing about in movement, are a metaphor for the activation of the ears and eyes. The lord of men, composed and inactive, is a metaphor for the True Person’s retreat and concealment. The True Person, abiding in the deep abyss, neither emerges nor enters, all restless seeking thoughts forever stilled and never arising again—like merchants and travelers, questioned and thus not daring to proceed. The image of closing the passes corresponds to the winter solstice, though moving, not departing from stillness, following the season’s natural decree. At this time, one should only seal the *dui* and embrace the One, guarding the center. How could one violate the prohibition against excessive speech and the exhaustion of words? Thus it is said: *"Emulating the season, following its decree, seal the mouth, refrain from speech."*

The great Way of the Golden Elixir shares the same creative transformation as the Way of Heaven. The Way of Heaven has *yuan, heng, li,* and *zhen,* cycling endlessly, vast and profound, beyond exhaustive inquiry. *Yuan* and *heng* govern generation and growth, the outward mechanism of creation-and-transformation, what is called *"manifesting through benevolence."* *Li* and *zhen* govern return and concealment, the inward mechanism of creation-and-transformation, what is called *"concealing in function."* At the time of return and concealment, above there is no longer color, below there is no longer abyss. Meeting it, there is no head; following it, there is no tail—this is what is called *"the Primordial Darkness, difficult to fathom, impossible to depict."* Thus it is said: *"The Way of Heaven is vast and boundless, the Great Origin without form or shape."*

Heaven and Earth are the true womb of the Great Void; the sun and moon are the true breath of the Great Void. At the midwinter season, at the juncture of *hai* and *zi,* Heaven and Earth unite in essence, and the sun and moon hold fast. The true breath of the sun and moon is concealed within the true womb of Heaven and Earth—unseen, unheard. The turning mechanism halts, returning to chaos. At this moment, there is no Heaven, no Earth, no sun, no moon, no gates of Qian and Kun, no frame of Kan and Li. All dissolve into a single expanse of the Great Void—this is true emptiness, this is wondrous being, this is the *Yi* before Fu Xi drew it, this is the nameless Dao of Laozi, this is *"what Heaven bears, without sound or scent,"* this is the original face before the arising of sound.

Thus it is said: *"The void and quiescence cannot be beheld; the frame and bounds have dissolved and vanished."*

Within chaos, Heaven and Earth are but Qian and Kun; within chaos, the sun and moon are but Kan and Li. The formless form is the true form; the wordless word is the ultimate word. Those with clear vision may from this contemplate the precelestial *Xin Yi*—indeed, they may dispense with all images and spare all words!

Yet this Dao is such that only those of the highest faculties and keenest instruments grasp it upon contact. The rest, of middling and lesser capacities, know only the *Yi* of forms—how could they know the *Yi* without form? They know only Qian and Kun, Kan and Li with shape and name—how could they know Qian and Kun, Kan and Li without shape or name?

If one were to remain silent and say nothing, one might well risk obscurity and carelessness, leaving distinctions unclear and functions unexplored, causing later learners to fall into all manner of errors—what evidence would they have? Yet if one were to recklessly invent extraneous details, one might only pile heads upon heads, mount donkeys to seek donkeys, leading later learners into all manner of forced interpretations, inevitably harming their very roots.

Thus it is said: *"Errors lose the thread of affairs; words return to self-defeat."*

After repeated reflection, I could do no other than divide this work into three sections, each further divided into chapters, distinguishing and ordering them thus: this is the Gate of Qian and Kun; this is the Frame of Kan and Li; this is the Crucible and Cauldron of Qian and Kun; this is the Medicinal Substance of Kan and Li—these are what is meant by the *Xing* and *Qing* of the Great *Yi*.

When these are brought together and penetrated, then what the Yellow Emperor and Laozi nurtured is none other than this Qian and Kun, Kan and Li; what the furnace fire refines is none other than this Qian and Kun, Kan and Li. All this is but pointing the way for the blind, expending every effort in circumlocution. Yet if one were speaking to those with clear eyes, there would be no need for such manifold distinctions!

Thus it is said: *"Distinguish and order these Four Images to enlighten later generations of the blind."* Since it is called the Four Images, it is no longer the root and trunk! Since it is called "distinguish and order," it is already the root and trunk broken into branches, stems, flowers, and leaves. How could it compare to the supreme subtlety of the undifferentiated, wordless state?

This chapter contains the final earnest admonitions of the *Cantong Qi*, of utmost importance, yet rife with errors through the ages—it cannot but be clarified. Chen Xianwei’s annotated edition moves this passage before the "Taiyang’s Flowing Pearl" chapter, where "the *zi* turns right, the *wu* rotates east," linking the *zi-wu-mao-you* to the Four Images. Yu Yuwu’s edition relocates it beneath the "Confucius Praises Hongmeng" chapter, where "the yang *qi* fades and hides," connecting the numbers seven, eight, nine, and six to the Four Images. Little do they realize that *zi-wu-mao-you* and seven, eight, nine, six all belong to the postcelestial Four Images of form and name—what relevance do they have to the profound concealment, the vanishing of the frame?

Chen Guanwu’s edition comes closest to the correct order, yet it mistakenly merges this with the preceding chapter on the *Xing* and *Qing* of the Great *Yi*. The earlier chapter clearly speaks of the Three Ways, while this one clearly speaks of the Four Images. Though the text flows continuously, their meanings are entirely distinct—how can they be conflated? If these scholars have not yet grasped the meaning of the words and sentences, how can they claim to have attained the author’s intent?

Throughout the *Cantong Qi*, there is no passage that does not elucidate the Four Images. Yet the Four Images, being of form and name, have already fallen to the second level of meaning. Fearing that later learners might pluck the branches and leaves while forgetting the root, the primordial *Xin-Yi* would nearly be extinguished! Thus, in his final words, Master Wei directly points to the undifferentiated, returning to the supreme, single vehicle of the Dao. For by tracing the Four Images back to the Two Modes, the Two Modes back to the Supreme Ultimate, and the Supreme Ultimate back to the Non-Ultimate—whether called the Great Origin, emptiness and quiescence, profound concealment, or the vanishing of the frame—all these expressions, appearing in layers, ultimately reveal the return to the root and restoration to the origin, the subtle workings prior to birth.

When one grasps this subtle working, one realizes that before the brush touched the page, a complete *Cantong Qi* already existed throughout heaven and earth, in the Qian and Kun, Kan and Li, manifest everywhere. Open your eyes and you see it; close your eyes and it is still not unseen. Incline your ear and you hear it; cover your ears and it is still not unheard. This is what the *Daode Jing* means by *"There is a thing, undifferentiated, born before heaven and earth"*; what the *Xici Zhuan* means by *"The spirit has no direction, the *Yi* has no form"*; what Master Shao means by *"Before the first stroke, the *Yi* was already there"*; what Master Zhou means by *"The Supreme Ultimate is fundamentally the Non-Ultimate."*

Nor is this all. Confucius devoted his life to editing, revising, and expounding, yet he said, *"I wish to speak no more—what does Heaven say?"* Is this not the principle that *"words return to self-defeat"*? Śākyamuni taught for forty-nine years, yet claimed *"I have not spoken a single word,"* and in the end transmitted only the wordless gong’an of the flower held aloft. Is this not the meaning of *"keeping silent and not speaking"*? Why then doubt the *Cantong Qi*?

The patriarch composed this work and established these images to enlighten later generations of the blind. Yet for over fourteen or fifteen hundred years, though the book has been transmitted, the blind remain as they were. Some falsely invoke the yin and yang of the Great *Yi* to practice sexual arts; others mistake the nurturing of *xing* in the Yellow Emperor and Laozi for solitary cultivation; still others misconstrue the furnace fire’s sublimation of elixirs as mere alchemy. The blind leading the blind, they plunge together into the fire pit. Even if they encounter a true teacher’s guidance, they remain stubbornly unconvinced. How lamentable!

In his final words, the patriarch, with earnest concern, sought to seal his tongue and forget speech, for he foresaw the many blind men of later ages. These are the *Cantong Qi*’s ultimate, all-encompassing lines—who would dare reveal them to this extent? Truly, *"Heaven does not begrudge the Dao!"* Reader, bring your single eye—lest you pass through in vain.

**Postface to the Opening Chapter, Chapter Thirty-Six**

A rustic from the state of Kuai,
A withered scholar from a secluded valley.
Clinging to plainness and simplicity,
I take no joy in power or glory.
Dwelling in obscurity and remoteness,
I disregard profit and fame.
Holding fast to tranquility and indifference,
I seek peace in my time.
At ease in leisure,
I composed this text.
Singing of the Great *Yi*,
I transmit the words of the Three Sages.
Examining their purport,
I find a single thread running through all.
My aim is to follow principle,
To illuminate the spirit.
Let the numinous transformation flow freely,
Bringing peace to the four seas.
I set it forth as a calendar,
For ten thousand generations to follow.
Use it to govern,
And its practice will be effortless.
Draw from it to nurture *xing* within,
In the natural way of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi.
Abiding in profound virtue,
Return to the root and restore the origin.
It is near in my heart,
Not apart from this body.
Embrace the One and never let go—
Thus may you endure forever.
Pair it with the sublimation of elixirs,
Arranging the feminine and masculine.
Discard the martial cinnabar of Wudu,
Abandon the eight minerals.
Discern their use to perfect things,
Though the world may prize them.
I array three methods,
Their branches and stems interconnected.
Though they emerge differently named,
All proceed from a single gate.
Not merely piling up phrases,
I harmonize this text in parallel.
There is indeed truth here,
Like pebbles that may yet be admired.
If I were to fabricate falsehoods,
I would only incur superfluous blame.
Thus I name it *Cantong Qi*.
Glancing at its beginning,
The words are few, the Dao vast—
Later generations should revere it.

Entrusting the times to shed harm,
I take refuge in hills and mountains.
Wandering the vast and boundless,
I dwell as neighbor to spirits.
Transforming form to ascend as immortal,
I sink into silence without sound.
A hundred generations hence,
I roam among men.

Spreading wings to the east and west,
To the south and the verge of north,
Tang met with dire straits,
Water and drought kept apart and conjoined.
Branches and leaves withered yellow,
Losing their flourishing splendor.
The fortunate ride upon blessings,
Abiding in peace, attaining long life.

This chapter recounts Wei Gong’s intent in composing this work,
Concealing his name to await later ages.

A rustic from the humble state of Kuai,
A withered life in a secluded valley,
I cling to plainness and simplicity,
Unmoved by power or glory.
Dwelling in obscurity and remoteness,
I disregard profit and fame.
Holding fast to tranquility,
I long for the peace of the times.
At ease in leisurely seclusion,
I compose this text.

This passage speaks of Wei Gong’s intent in secluding himself to write.

According to the *Liexian Zhuan*: "The Perfected One, Wei Boyang, was a native of Shangyu in Kuaiji. Though his family had long worn the cap and sash of office, he alone did not serve, cultivating the true and dwelling in silence. Nurturing his will in emptiness and non-being, he was broadly learned in literature and versed in the weft and warp of prophecy. Serene and plain, he followed only the Dao, regarding the trappings of rank as chaff and husks. From the Perfected Yin Changsheng, he received the Great Dao of the Golden Elixir. Following its method, he refined and attained perfection, then drew upon the *Zhouyi* to compose the *Cantong Qi* in three chapters."

This autobiographical passage closely mirrors the account in the tradition, and thus may be considered a faithful record. Though Wei Gong was originally from Kuaiji, his reference to the ancient state of Kuai was likely a veiled allusion.

The *Song* expounds the *Great Change*,
The legacy of the Three Sages.
Examining its purport,
All is discussed in unity.
The task lies in aligning with principle,
Radiating the spirit.
Spiritual transformation flows unimpeded,
Bringing peace to the four seas.
Marking it as a calendar,
It may be followed for ten thousand generations.
Ordering it to govern,
Its practice is effortless.

This passage states that the *Cantong Qi* is fundamentally rooted in the *Great Change*, embodying the principle of governance. The *Change* passed through three sages—its trigrams, appended judgments, and *Wings*—all aimed at revealing the work of exhausting one’s nature and fulfilling one’s destiny. Wei Gong, discerning its essential purport, externally aligned with the transformations of creation and internally mirrored the mind and body. Summarizing and examining it, all proceeds from the twofold use of Kan and Li. Its essence is nature and destiny; its function is spirit. When the principle of nature and destiny is aligned, the function of spirit is complete. Thus it is said: "The task lies in aligning with principle, radiating the spirit." To fathom the spirit and know transformation is the wonder of the *Change*. By the One, there is spirit; by the Two, there is transformation. Applying this to cultivate the mind, spirit and transformation are stored in the Central Yellow, yielding the proof of universal principle. Applying this to govern the world, spirit and transformation spread across the four seas, manifesting the achievement of peace. Thus it is said: "Spiritual transformation flows unimpeded, bringing peace to the four seas." Zi to the south and Wu to the north serve as the warp and woof; establishing Wei at Mao and You, the jade armillary turns in cycles—this is the origin of the calendar. Thus it is said: "Marking it as a calendar, it may be followed for ten thousand generations." The lord practices non-action; the minister, action. When the Bright Hall enacts governance, the state suffers no harm to the Dao—this is the standard for ruling the world. Thus it is said: "Ordering it to govern, its practice is effortless." The Dao of the *Change* is the Dao of governance; the Dao of governance is the Dao of the Elixir. Inner sageliness and outer kingship are threaded as one. This passage specifically concludes the guiding principle of governance, which is what is meant by "the nature and feelings of the *Great Change*, each in accord with its measure."

Drawing inward to nurture nature,
Following the naturalness of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi.
Abundant in virtue and power,
Returning to the root, reverting to the origin.
It is near in my heart,
Not apart from this body.
Embrace the One and never let go—
Thus may you endure forever.

This passage speaks of the principle of nurturing nature through naturalness. Externally, the Dao of purity and non-action derives from the Yellow Emperor and Laozi. Internally, within the nine palaces of the human body lies the cinnabar gate of the Yellow Court, governed by the Yellow Emperor and Laozi of the Center, harboring the ancestral nature—heavenly and true, naturally so. What is called nurturing nature is simply nurturing this. Thus it is said: "Drawing inward to nurture nature, following the naturalness of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi." The ancestral nature is the supreme virtue. Originally, it is neither lost nor gained, neither diminished nor increased. If the practitioner thoroughly understands this nature, they may immediately return to the root and revert to destiny, reverting to the origin. Thus it is said: "Abundant in virtue and power, returning to the root, reverting to the origin." The ancestral nature is originally one body, but when divided into two uses, it pertains to the two entities of body and mind. Yet the mind is not the fleshly heart but the true emptiness within original wondrous being; the body is not the four great elements but the wondrous being within original true emptiness. These two are possessed by all, and all cultivation and realization do not depart from this very substance. Thus it is said: "It is near in my heart, not apart from this body." The ancestral aperture is the true center. When the two families of body and mind return to the ancestral aperture, this is the true One. If one can guard the center and embrace the One without ceasing for even a moment, the Dao of long life and enduring vision is attained. Thus it is said: "Embrace the One and never let go—thus may you endure forever." This passage specifically concludes the guiding principle of nurturing nature, which is what is meant by "the Yellow Emperor and Laozi’s thorough investigation, refined and governable."

Pair it with the sublimation of elixirs,
Arranging the feminine and masculine.
Discard the martial cinnabar of Wudu,
Abandon the eight minerals.
Discern their use to perfect things,
Though the world may prize them.

This passage speaks of the principle of furnace and fire in the sublimation of elixirs. Internally, it is the Great Dao of true nature and true destiny, the one yin and one yang. Externally, it is likened to true lead and true mercury, the two entities of one feminine and one masculine. In terms of the restraint of hun and po, it is called subduing with dragon and tiger; in terms of mutual absorption, it is called ingestion—this is the work of the golden liquid returning elixir, far removed from the so-called ingestion of side paths. Thus it is said: "Pair it with the sublimation of elixirs, arranging the feminine and masculine." When the world hears of furnace and fire, they invariably assume it refers to the five metals and eight minerals; when they hear of feminine and masculine, they invariably take it to mean orpiment and realgar. Yet these are all post-heaven dregs of form and substance, discarded and unused by the perfected.

Thus it is said: *"Cast aside the ores of Wudu, discard the eight minerals."* Having discarded them, why then pair them with the practice of ingestion? It is simply that the arts of smelting lead to dry mercury and transmuting copper into gold have long existed—worldly men prize such techniques but not the Dao. They invariably hold these in the highest esteem. The patriarchs, having repeatedly weighed this, knew that what the world most treasures are the substances of alchemy. Thus they borrowed the false to reveal the true, embedding the wondrous workings of the golden elixir and ingestion within allegory, so that many would believe and follow. Hence it is said: *"Carefully employ what the world holds dear."*

Wudu lies thousands of *li* west of Liangzhou, yielding orpiment and realgar. The Master Wei states that his *"arrangement of the feminine and masculine"* does not refer to the minerals produced in Wudu. The *Awakening to Reality* says, *"Cease refining the three yellows and four spirits,"* which conveys the same meaning. This passage specifically elucidates the function of ingestion—what is called *"the work of the furnace and fire, grounded in truth."*

Three principles are arrayed, their branches and stems interconnected. Though bearing different names, they emerge from a single gate. These are not mere accumulations of phrases, nor rhymed couplets for literary effect. Rather, they contain profound truth, like a vein of jade hidden in rough stone—once unearthed, its brilliance is revealed. Should I expound falsehoods, I would only incur superfluous faults.

This book is titled *The Kinship of the Three*—precisely because the three teachings intermingle, all issuing from one gate, thereby uniting with the supreme and perfect Dao. If scholars trace its origins, they will see that though its words are few, its Way is vast, encompassing the full path of fulfilling one’s nature and destiny. How could later disciples fail to uphold it? This section particularly reveals the meaning behind the title *The Kinship of the Three*—that *"three Ways arise from one, all following the same path."*

Abandoning the times to avoid harm, I take refuge in the mountains. Roaming the boundless expanse, I dwell among ghosts. Transforming my form into immortality, I sink into silence. A hundred generations hence, I wander among men. Spreading my feathered wings, I traverse east, west, south. In Tang’s time of peril, floods and droughts divided the land. The leaves of the catalpa wither and yellow, losing their splendor. The virtuous ride upon their burdens, secure in everlasting life.

This final passage conceals the author’s name within the text, awaiting discovery by later ages. The sixteen lines, when combined, reveal the immortal’s name: The first four—*"Abandoning the times to avoid harm, I take refuge in the mountains. Roaming the boundless expanse, I dwell among ghosts"*—form the character *Wei* 魏. The next four—*"Transforming my form into immortality, I sink into silence. A hundred generations hence, I wander among men"*—form *Bo* 伯. The following four—*"Spreading my feathered wings, I traverse east, west, south. In Tang’s time of peril, floods and droughts divided the land"*—form *Yang* 阳. The last four—*"The leaves of the catalpa wither and yellow, losing their splendor. The virtuous ride upon their burdens, secure in everlasting life"*—form *Zao* 造. Thus, the entire *Kinship of the Three* was composed by Wei Boyang.

The immortal was a recluse who did not wish to attach his name to the work, yet neither did he desire complete obscurity. Thus he wove ambiguous phrases and hidden allusions, half-concealing and half-revealing, so that later generations might come to know him. This mirrors how the root of a single truth lies concealed within the branches of the three chapters, subtly emerging to await the enlightenment of future readers.

The immortal’s concealment of his name echoes the earlier admonition: *"Seal your lips and speak not of it."* Those who grasp its meaning may encounter it at dawn or dusk, though they may miss it in passing. The first chapter’s closing lines—*"I dare not speak falsely, emulating the sages’ writings"*—and the middle chapter’s—*"I grieve deeply and thus record these words"*—both express the intent to leave teachings for posterity. Yet the meaning remains incomplete until the final chapter, where the author’s purpose is clarified: elucidating how the three Ways arise from one, making *The Kinship of the Three* a bridge between the sages of old and later worthies, a standard for exhausting principle, fulfilling nature, and realizing destiny.

Thus this chapter not only unites the three correspondences but indeed serves as the summation of the entire work. *The Kinship of the Three* is a text most difficult to read, for at its outset, one line is divided into three chapters, and in the end, three chapters are fused into a single line—yet the line itself is no line at all. Within this division and fusion, wondrous transformations unfold; even one with the eyes of Li Zhu would seldom avoid dizziness, nor one with the ears of Shi Kuang escape deafness. Now, with my teacher’s full revelation of its hidden depths, it is as though a lamp illuminates a chamber dark for a thousand years. Is this not the compass of Fuxi’s *Changes*, the torch of wisdom for *The Kinship of the Three*?
